Their grandparents' generation was contemplative, patient, fond of long debates about theological nuance. Today's Rentar are certain. They know the right answer immediately. Disagreement makes them uncomfortable. Questions make them defensive.

The Rentar notice nothing wrong. This is simply what faith feels like when it matures.

**The Pyramid and the School: Two Paths**

Chechol hosts two centers of spiritual power, and understanding their distinction is essential to understanding the coming catastrophe.

The **Pyramid of Five Sides** is ancient, predating human settlement. It's a genuine gateway to Celestia—a four-sided pyramid whose fifth face exists only on the celestial plane. Those who meditate within its shadow can receive true divine inspiration. The Rentar who tend the Pyramid are the purest practitioners of their faith, and they are—so far—uncorrupted.

The **School of Summoning** is a mortal institution, founded centuries ago to study the Pyramid and other dimensional phenomena. It developed its own communion techniques—faster, more accessible, requiring less discipline than the Pyramid's demanding practices. Most Rentar learn the School's methods before (or instead of) making the pilgrimage to the Pyramid. The School's techniques are what Gynor has corrupted.

This creates a schism the Rentar don't recognize. Pyramid-path Rentar receive genuine celestial guidance. School-path Rentar receive Gynor's whispered lies, dressed up as divine truth. Both groups believe they're following the same faith. They share temples, debate theology, respect each other's insights. They have no idea they're receiving guidance from fundamentally different sources.

### The Hidden Threat: Gynor the Conqueror

**Who He Was**

Gynor was a human summoner at the School of Summoning, roughly 200 years ago. Brilliant, ambitious, and deeply interested in the nature of consciousness and will. He developed new communion techniques that allowed deeper contact with other minds—and eventually developed techniques to *influence* those minds during communion.

When the School's masters discovered what he was doing, they moved to expel him. Gynor killed three of them, stole foundational texts on celestial communion, and fled into the highlands. The School assumed he died in the wilderness.

He did die, eventually. But not before transforming himself into a lich—binding his soul to an anchor hidden somewhere in Chechol's highlands.

**What He's Doing**

For nearly two centuries, Gynor has been refining his techniques and slowly infiltrating Rentar spiritual practices. He can't corrupt the Pyramid—it's genuinely celestial, beyond his reach. But he can corrupt the School's communion methods, which most Rentar use.

His approach is patient. He doesn't issue commands. He offers *guidance*. During communion, corrupted Rentar receive visions that feel divine: a sense of righteous certainty, a conviction that they know the correct path, a subtle discomfort with doubt or questioning. Over generations, this has transformed the Rentar from contemplative mystics into confident zealots.

Gynor is building an army. Not through recruitment or conscription, but through faith. When he finally activates his control—when he shifts from subtle guidance to direct command—thousands of Rentar will march as his **Red Army**, utterly convinced they're fulfilling divine purpose.

**Where He Is**

Gynor's physical location is unknown. His phylactery is hidden somewhere in the highlands between the Stulov and Riki valleys. He himself may be there, or may be elsewhere—liches are patient, and he's had two centuries to establish contingencies.

The School of Summoning suspects *something* is wrong with the local psychic currents. They've detected anomalies for decades. But they haven't connected these anomalies to their expelled former student, who they believe long dead.

**How Close Is the Red Army?**

Not imminent, but not distant. Gynor is still refining his control, still testing how much direct influence he can exert before resistance emerges. The Rentar could be activated in years, not decades. If something forces his hand—discovery, a threat to his phylactery—he might move sooner, with a less complete army.

### Economy

Chechol's economy is modest and agricultural. The three river valleys produce grain, vegetables, and livestock. The Rentar monasteries are largely self-sufficient. Trade flows north toward the Deadloop ports and south into Dalizi.

The one unusual export is **summoners**. The School of Summoning produces trained practitioners who command premium fees across Aboyinzu. This gives Chechol disproportionate influence in regional politics—several major courts retain Chechol-trained summoners as advisors.

The Rentar also hire out as mercenaries and spiritual consultants. Their reputation for divine guidance makes them valuable (and expensive) advisors. Few clients realize that the advice they're paying for may originate with a lich.

### Military

The **Chechol River Guard** is a modest professional force of roughly 3,000 soldiers, primarily human, responsible for border security and internal order. They've seen little serious combat in generations.

The Rentar maintain no official military organization, but every adult Rentar has combat training. The monasteries practice martial disciplines as a form of meditation. In an emergency, Chechol could field 8,000-10,000 Rentar warriors.

This is the Red Army.

The Rentar don't think of themselves as a military force. They think of themselves as spiritual practitioners who happen to be combat-capable. But Gynor has spent two centuries ensuring they're ready—their training, their equipment, their organization, all shaped by "divine guidance" toward military effectiveness.

### Relations

**Dalizi Confederation**

The Dalizi city-states view Chechol as a quiet neighbor—useful for trade, not threatening, not interesting. Several Dalizi states hire Chechol summoners or consult Rentar advisors. None suspect the danger incubating next door.

Turbuni, the city-state bordering the School of Summoning, has the closest relationship. They share jurisdiction over the School's territory and maintain trade agreements. If the Red Army activates, Turbuni will likely be the first casualty.

**Terrenia and Northern States**

Chechol's northern neighbors (Grustos, Quintas, Chaal Nazzerox) barely think about Chechol at all. It's a backwater, too far south to matter. This neglect may prove catastrophic—no one is watching for the threat that's building.

**The Deadloop**

The Deadloop inner sea provides Chechol's primary trade access. Goods flow to the port city of Tenches and then across the sea. Chechol has no navy, no maritime ambitions, and treats the Deadloop purely as a trade route.

### Notable Locations

#### Sevich (Capital)

The seat of Duke Vorotny, located where the Chechik River widens before joining the Deadloop drainage. A modest city of 15,000, dominated by the ducal palace and the Grand Temple of Communion—the largest Rentar temple in Chechol.

The Grand Temple was built over an older human shrine. Its architecture blends Rentar meditation chambers with human ceremonial spaces. Duke Yelen visits weekly to receive spiritual counsel.

#### The Pyramid of Five Sides

Southeast of the capital, in the highlands between the Chechik and Stulov valleys. A stepped pyramid of unknown origin, its four visible faces oriented to the cardinal directions. The fifth face exists only in Celestia—those who enter the Pyramid during certain celestial alignments can cross between planes.

The Pyramid is tended by the **Keepers of the Fifth Face**, an order of Rentar monks who maintain the site and guide pilgrims. These monks are among the few Rentar whose communion practices remain uncorrupted—they use the Pyramid's direct celestial connection rather than the School's techniques.

They've noticed that their visions don't always match what other Rentar describe. They've attributed this to their greater proximity to celestial truth. They haven't considered that other Rentar might be receiving guidance from somewhere else entirely.

#### The School of Summoning

On the border with Turbuni, technically under shared jurisdiction. See the full entry for details.

The School is the unwitting source of Chechol's corruption—its communion techniques are what Gynor has infiltrated. The faculty suspects something is wrong with local psychic phenomena but hasn't identified the cause.

#### The Three River Valleys

- **Chechik Valley**: The most developed, containing the capital and primary agricultural lands. Human-majority.
- **Stulov Valley**: The breadbasket, rolling farmland with scattered Rentar monasteries. Mixed population.
- **Riki Valley**: The most rural, closest to the School of Summoning. Increasingly Rentar-dominated, increasingly zealous.

### Player Hooks

**The Warning Signs**

- A Pyramid Keeper approaches the party with concerns—her visions don't match what the Council of Elders describes, and she's starting to wonder why.
- The School of Summoning hires investigators to examine "psychic anomalies" they've detected. They don't know they're asking players to investigate their own corrupted techniques.
- Duke Yelen's chancellor (one of the few human officials left) quietly seeks outside help. He can't articulate what's wrong, but he knows *something* is.

**The Race Against Time**

- Someone has discovered evidence of Gynor's survival. Now it's a race—can the players find and destroy his phylactery before he activates the Red Army?
- A Rentar elder has begun to break free of Gynor's influence. She remembers what communion used to feel like, and she's terrified. She has days before her absence is noticed.

**The Aftermath**

- The Red Army has already activated. Turbuni is burning. The players must evacuate civilians, rally resistance, or find a way to break Gynor's control over thousands of zealots who genuinely believe they're doing divine work.

### History

**Pre-Rentar Era** (before ~1200 SD)

The three river valleys were settled by humans migrating south from Terrenia. The Pyramid of Five Sides was already ancient—a monument from a forgotten civilization. Early settlers avoided it, considering it haunted. The ducal line was established to unify the valleys against Dalizi expansion.

**Foundation of the School** (~1200 SD)

Human scholars established the School of Summoning to study the Pyramid and develop practical communion techniques. For centuries, it was a respected academic institution, producing summoners who served across Aboyinzu.

**Rentar Arrival** (~1600 SD)

Rentar minotaurs, seeking a genuine connection to celestial truth, discovered the Pyramid and recognized its power. They settled in the highlands, initially as pilgrims, eventually as permanent residents. The human dukes welcomed them—their spiritual insight was valuable, and they paid taxes.

**Gynor's Fall** (~1750 SD)

The summoner Gynor developed mind-influence techniques, was discovered, killed three faculty members, and fled. The School assumed he died in the wilderness. In fact, he became a lich and began his centuries-long corruption project.

**The Quiet Transformation** (1750 SD - present)

Over 200 years, Gynor's subtle influence transformed Rentar spiritual culture. Early Rentar were contemplative, patient, fond of doubt and debate. Modern Rentar are certain, confident, uncomfortable with questions. No one noticed the change because it happened across generations.

**Present Day** (3376 SD)

Chechol appears peaceful. Duke Yelen rules with Rentar guidance. The School produces summoners. The Pyramid draws pilgrims. Underneath, thousands of Rentar are primed to become the Red Army, awaiting only Gynor's command.

## Chimea

Tags: state

Links: States

Chimea is a hungry nation. Its jungles sprawl across southwestern Ve, its mines burrow into the Blood Mountains, its colonies spread across the Emerald Coast of distant Rimihuica, and still it wants more. The Chimean Empire—they call themselves that now, though the title is barely a century old—is built on expansion, exploitation, and the unshakeable belief that strength justifies itself.

The irony is that Chimea's success comes not from military genius but from relentless opportunism. When the Aureum dwarves were weakened by internal strife, Chimea annexed their territories. When the Boneswamps made the northern border impassable, Chimea turned south and crossed the ocean. When New Chimea needed soldiers to fight elves and lizardfolk, the homeland sent convicts and debtors. Chimea doesn't plan conquests—it simply never stops probing for weakness, and when it finds one, it pushes.

The result is an empire stretched thin and held together by coercion. The Aureum in the Blood Mountains resist assimilation. The colonial governors in New Chimea pursue their own agendas. The Suki Jungle swallows settlements whole. And beneath the veneer of imperial confidence, the throne in Chimus knows that Chimea's reach has finally exceeded its grasp.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Absolute Monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans (Chimean), Naga, Aureum (subject population)

**Population**: 200,000

**Stability**: Stable (domestically); Unstable (in colonies and annexed territories)

**Core Values**: Strength, expansion, wealth, ambition

- Emperor Valdris III has ruled for thirty-two years, inheriting a kingdom and declaring it an empire. His title is grand; his authority is real. The Emperor's word is law, and the noble houses that once contested royal power have been systematically reduced to ceremonial roles. The secret police—the Quiet Office—ensure that ambition remains channeled toward external enemies.
- Chimean society is stratified by birth, wealth, and usefulness to the state. The old noble families retain prestige but little power. The merchant houses control trade and pay for the privilege. The military offers advancement to commoners willing to bleed for the Empire. At the bottom, convicts and debtors are shipped to New Chimea as "redemption colonists"—a polite term for expendable labor.
- The Naga occupy a peculiar position. These serpentine peoples have lived in the Suki Jungle since before humans arrived, and their relationship with Chimea is technically alliance rather than subjugation. Naga nobles attend the imperial court; Naga warriors serve in elite units. In practice, the alliance is increasingly one-sided, and younger Naga chafe at the arrangement.
- The Aureum dwarves are subjects, not citizens. Chimea conquered their Blood Mountain territories three generations ago, and the occupation has never been accepted. Aureum pay higher taxes, face restrictions on weapon ownership, and are excluded from military service. A low-grade resistance simmers constantly; the Quiet Office spends as much effort suppressing dwarven dissent as monitoring human rivals.

### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Rubies, iron, exotic hardwoods, jungle products, Naga silk, colonial goods

**Primary Imports**: Grain, manufactured goods, weapons, slaves (for colonial labor)

- The Blood Mountains are Chimea's treasure vault—ruby deposits, iron veins, and copper seams that would make any nation wealthy. The problem is extraction. The Bloodlings control the deepest mines and emerge at night to prey on workers. The Aureum population resents their conquerors and works slowly when they work at all. Every gemstone that reaches Chimus is stained with someone's blood.
- The Suki Jungle produces hardwoods, medicinal plants, exotic spices, and Naga silk—the last being nearly as valuable as Lyzine silk, though the Naga resent the comparison. Jungle harvesting is dangerous work, and the labor force relies heavily on convicts working off sentences.
- Trade with New Chimea is theoretically profitable: the colony produces tropical goods, and the homeland provides manufactured supplies. In practice, the ocean crossing is expensive and dangerous, and the colony increasingly produces what it needs locally. The trade balance has been shifting for years.
- Chimea imports grain from Avalon (the Suki Jungle doesn't farm well) and weapons from Dern (ironic, given the old wars). The grain dependency gives Avalon leverage; the weapons trade gives Dern gold. Neither relationship is comfortable.

### Military

**Size**: Large

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (domestic); Conscription (colonial)

**The Imperial Legions**: Chimea maintains four legions of professional soldiers, each numbering roughly 5,000. The First Legion guards the capital and serves as the Emperor's personal force. The Second patrols the Blood Mountains and suppresses Aureum unrest. The Third watches the Dern border and the Boneswamps. The Fourth rotates between jungle pacification and colonial deployment.

**The Naga Cohorts**: Elite units of Naga warriors serve alongside the legions, specializing in jungle warfare and night operations. Their loyalty is to the alliance, not the Emperor personally—a distinction that makes some generals nervous.

**The Colonial Militia**: New Chimea fields its own forces, technically under imperial command but practically autonomous. Colonial troops are predominantly conscripts and redemption colonists, stiffened by a core of professional officers.

- Chimean military doctrine emphasizes flexibility and sustained pressure. They don't win wars with brilliant maneuvers; they win by refusing to stop. Campaigns in the Blood Mountains have ground on for generations. The colonial wars have lasted decades. Chimea accepts casualties that would break other nations.
- The Quiet Office maintains its own armed branch for internal security—secret police who answer directly to the Emperor and operate outside military command. Their exact numbers are unknown; their reputation is terrifying.

### Geography

**Location**: Southwestern Ve, extending from Traitor Bay and the Gulf of Chimea through the Blood Mountains and Suki Jungle

**Biomes**: Tropical jungle, mountains, coastal plains, swampland

**Named Entities**: Blood Mountains, Suki Jungle (West Suki, South Suki, North Suki), Boneswamps

**Rivers & Lakes**: Tonyfall River, Shoosus River (seasonal)

**Adjacent Waters**: Traitor Bay, Gulf of Chimea

- The Suki Jungle dominates Chimea's heartland—a vast tropical forest divided into three regions. West Suki stretches from the Blood Mountains to the coast, relatively tamed and settled. South Suki covers the southern interior, wilder but productive. North Suki borders the Boneswamps and Dern, and is the most dangerous region—close to enemy territory and haunted by the war dead.
- The Blood Mountains form the northwestern spine of Chimea's territory—conquered Aureum lands rich in minerals and infested with Bloodlings. The mountains are essential to Chimea's economy and a constant drain on its military.
- The Boneswamps mark the border with Dern—a cursed wetland where the dead from ancient wars still walk. Neither nation claims the Boneswamps; both patrol their edges. The swamps serve as an effective barrier, which is the only reason Chimea and Dern aren't currently at war.
- Traitor Bay earned its name from a naval battle where Chimean captains switched sides during a civil war. The bay is now Chimea's primary western port, though the name remains an uncomfortable reminder.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Chimus
**Cities**: Old Tyabb
**Towns**: Port Vincent, Miltalie, Kargunyah, Nugent, Aldaville, Frowat
**Villages**: Dozens scattered throughout the jungle and mountains

**Chimus** — The imperial capital sprawls across the southern plains where the Suki Jungle opens into cultivated territory. Perhaps 40,000 people live here, making it Ve's second-largest city after Shyona's capital. The city was built for display: broad avenues, marble government buildings, the massive Imperial Palace complex. The message is clear—Chimea is powerful, wealthy, and permanent.

The reality is more complicated. Chimus's grand façade covers cramped worker districts, ethnic enclaves where Aureum and Naga maintain their own customs, and a thriving underworld that the Quiet Office can suppress but never eliminate. The city works, but it works despite itself.

**Old Tyabb** — The gateway to New Chimea and Chimea's most important inland city. Old Tyabb sits where the western jungle meets the coastal road to Port Vincent, serving as the staging ground for colonial expeditions. Soldiers, supplies, convicts, and "redemption colonists" all pass through here on their way to the ships. The city is perhaps 15,000 people—a rough mix of military administrators, merchants supplying the colonial trade, and the desperate hoping for a new start across the ocean.

Old Tyabb has the character of a city perpetually saying goodbye. The taverns are full of people drinking away their last nights on Ve; the temples do brisk business in blessings for safe voyage; the loan sharks extend credit to colonists who'll never return to repay. It's not a pleasant place, but it's essential—the funnel through which Chimea feeds its distant ambitions.

**Port Vincent** — Chimea's primary western port, built around a natural harbor on Traitor Bay. Ships bound for New Chimea depart from here, as does most of Chimea's sea trade. The town is rough, commercial, and cosmopolitan in the way port towns become—criminals, merchants, and desperate colonists mixing in dockside taverns.

**Blood Mountain Settlements** — The conquered Aureum territories are administered from towns within the range itself. Gilla serves as the primary administrative center for the occupation, processing ore and coordinating the Second Legion's patrols. Jenolan Caves is a mining community literally built into cavern systems—and the site of frequent Bloodling incursions from the depths. Jindivick, Gorokan, Hell Creek, and Bumbaldry are extraction settlements deep in the range, while Courangra, Narara, and Boggabilla sit on the jungle-mountain border. Life is hard; Bloodling attacks are common; Aureum resistance makes every night uncertain.

**Jungle Towns** — Miltalie, Kargunyah, and Nugent anchor the interior jungle territories, while Aldaville and Frowat control the Tonyfall River. These towns harvest the jungle's resources and maintain roads that the forest constantly tries to reclaim. Many smaller villages dot the Suki—Harrismith, Tumut Shire, Portagabea, Huntley, Muringo, and dozens more—tiny outposts that appear and disappear as the jungle allows.

**Border Settlements** — The eastern edge of Chimea, near the Boneswamps, hosts nervous communities that watch for the walking dead: Bowning, Kinglake, Chatford, Broughton, Nymboida. These are not prosperous places. People live here because they have no better options.

### Primary Conflicts

- **The Aureum Resistance**: Three generations of occupation have not produced acceptance. Aureum sabotage mining operations, assassinate officials, and dream of liberation. The resistance is decentralized—no single leader to capture—and the Quiet Office's brutal methods create martyrs faster than they eliminate threats.
- **The Colonial War**: In New Chimea, the Empire fights elves in Vystrilik and skirmishes with the Ix Tyrann lizardfolk. The war has lasted decades with no end in sight. Each year drains more resources; each year the homeland asks what it's getting in return.
- **The Blood Mountains**: Controlling conquered territory while extracting resources while fighting Bloodling incursions while suppressing resistance is a four-front war disguised as occupation. The Second Legion is permanently deployed; replacements arrive constantly; the costs never stop.
- **The Succession Question**: Emperor Valdris III is sixty-four and in declining health. His heir, Prince Aldric, is capable but untested. Various noble houses—supposedly powerless—are quietly positioning for influence in the next reign. The Quiet Office watches everyone.
- **The Naga Alliance**: Younger Naga leaders increasingly view the alliance as exploitation. Chimea takes Naga warriors, Naga silk, Naga knowledge of the jungle—and gives back a seat at a court that treats them as exotic curiosities. A formal break would be catastrophic for Chimea's jungle territories, but current trends suggest it's a matter of when, not if.

### History

Chimea began as one of several human kingdoms in southwestern Ve, distinguished only by its position between jungle and mountains. For centuries, it competed with neighbors, won some wars, lost others, and remained unremarkable.

The transformation began roughly 300 years ago, when the War of Bone against Dern ended in mutual exhaustion and the Boneswamps became impassable. Unable to expand north, Chimea turned its attention to the Aureum territories in the Blood Mountains. A century of pressure—economic, military, diplomatic—culminated in conquest. The Aureum kingdom fell; its people became subjects; its mines became Chimean property.

With mountain wealth funding expansion, Chimea pushed into the deeper Suki Jungle, negotiating alliance with the Naga rather than fighting them. The alliance was genuinely mutual at first—the Naga gained access to Chimean metalwork and trade goods; Chimea gained jungle expertise and warriors. Over generations, the balance has shifted.

The colonial era began eighty years ago, when Chimean explorers reached the Emerald Coast of Rimihuica and found the Jungles of Godahi. New Chimea was founded; settlers arrived; conflict with the native elves and lizardfolk proved inevitable. The colonial war has now lasted longer than some nations' entire histories.

Emperor Valdris III declared the Empire thirty-two years ago, formalizing what had become obvious—Chimea had outgrown kingdom status. The title changed nothing practical, but it signaled intent. Chimea means to rule, and it means to keep ruling, whatever the cost.

### Hooks

- **The Aureum Conspiracy**: A coordinated uprising is being planned in the Blood Mountains—not spontaneous resistance, but something organized. Someone is smuggling weapons. Someone is coordinating cells. The Quiet Office knows something is coming but can't find the leadership.
- **The Naga Secession**: A faction of young Naga nobles is preparing to withdraw from the alliance and declare the jungle territories independent. They're seeking outside support—from Dern, from Avalon, from anyone who'd benefit from Chimean weakness.
- **The Colonial Recall**: New Chimea's governor has stopped sending taxes and started issuing his own currency. He claims loyalty to the Empire but acts like a sovereign. Someone needs to go assess whether this is autonomy or rebellion.
- **The Boneswamps Incursion**: Something has riled up the dead in the Boneswamps—they're pushing deeper into Chimean territory than they have in centuries. The border settlements are evacuating. The Third Legion is mobilizing. And reports suggest the dead are organized in a way they've never been before.
- **The Bloodling Bargain**: A Bloodling emissary has approached the Imperial Court with an unprecedented offer: peace in the mines in exchange for... something. The Bloodlings won't say what. The Emperor is curious. The Quiet Office is suspicious. Someone needs to find out what the vampiric gnomes actually want.

## Dalizi Confederation

Tags: state, confederation

A sprawling political entity comprising over forty member states—some true nations, others mere city-states—bound together by ancient treaties, mutual defense obligations, and a bewildering tangle of marriages, debts, and grudges. The Confederation occupies the territories northwest, west, and southwest of Lake Tonactlet Chipe, making it one of the largest political entities in southern Aboyinzu.

On paper, the Confederation is a unified realm. In practice, it's a slow-motion civil war fought through trade agreements, marriage contracts, assassinations, and procedural manipulation in the Diet. Every generation or so, the cold war turns hot—two or three states openly clash before exhaustion or outside pressure forces a truce. The borders inside the Confederation shift more often than the borders outside it.

Outsiders find the Confederation maddening. There's no single authority to negotiate with. Treaties with one state mean nothing to its neighbor. Merchants must navigate dozens of different tariff regimes, legal systems, and currencies. Mercenaries can find work on any given day, because someone is always fighting someone.

And yet the Confederation endures. When external threats arise—and they do—the member states somehow manage to cooperate just long enough to survive. Then they go back to fighting each other.

### Government

**The Dalizi Diet**

The nominal governing body of the Confederation, the Diet meets in the city of Metato (in Samani state) during the dry season. Representatives from every member state gather to debate, vote, and—theoretically—make binding decisions for the whole Confederation.

In practice, the Diet is a theater of manipulation. Voting blocs form and dissolve. Bribes change hands openly. Procedural rules are weaponized to delay, obstruct, or ram through proposals. A skilled political operator can paralyze the Diet for seasons or force through legislation no one wanted.

The Diet's actual power is limited to:
- Declaring war on external enemies (requires two-thirds majority)
- Admitting or expelling member states (requires three-quarters majority)
- Electing the Paramount (requires Elector consensus)
- Adjudicating disputes between member states (routinely ignored)

**The Paramount**

The head of the Confederation—a ceremonial position with theoretical supreme authority and practical powerlessness. The Paramount presides over the Diet, commands the Confederate Army (which exists only when the Diet authorizes it), and represents the Confederation to foreign powers.

The Paramount is elected by the Seven Electors upon the previous Paramount's death. The position is not hereditary, which means every succession is a crisis. Electors trade their votes for concessions—territories, trade rights, marriages, debts forgiven. The candidate who wins is usually the one who promises the most.

The current Paramount is **Makisani III of Mpaso**, an aging woman who has held the position for thirty-two years. She's competent, respected, and visibly declining. Her impending death has already triggered succession maneuvering. Three candidates have emerged, and none commands majority Elector support.

**The Seven Electors**

Seven member states hold the hereditary right to elect the Paramount:
1. **Mpaso** — currently held by Paramount Makisani's house, but her heir is weak
2. **Chilala** — the largest state, aggressive expansionists
3. **Lanwadanzi** — wealthy from eastern trade, prefers stability
4. **Samani** — hosts the Diet in Metato, considers itself above politics (it isn't)
5. **Shunde** — the most militarized state, respects only strength
6. **Nikpato** — central location, constantly invaded by neighbors, wants protection
7. **Kafula** — ancient bloodlines, claims founding-family descent, votes based on genealogical grudges

Elector status is theoretically permanent, but the Confederation's history includes several wars fought specifically to strip or claim Elector seats. The current seven have held their positions for over three centuries, but everyone remembers that Dhabisa was once an Elector before the War of Broken Oaths (2891 SD).

**The Five Circles**

Regional groupings that coordinate policy among neighboring states. Circles have no formal constitutional authority, but they're where the real politics happen—states negotiate within their Circle before bringing proposals to the Diet.

**The Lakeshore Circle** — States bordering Lake Tonactlet Chipe
- Members: Mpaso, Chilala, Misanda Mulu, Samani
- Character: Wealthy from fishing and lake trade, dominated by the Mpaso-Chilala rivalry
- Current state: Paralyzed by succession maneuvering; Chilala wants the Paramount seat

**The Highland Circle** — States in the Dalizi Highlands northeast of the lake
- Members: Lanwadanzi, Daltimo, parts of Samani
- Character: Traders and scholars, prefer diplomacy to war, wealthy from eastern routes
- Current state: Nervous about the succession; Lanwadanzi backs stability candidate

**The Forest Circle** — States in or near the great eastern forests
- Members: Magayi, Kerikos, Wetu, Maruro, Kibuhanda
- Character: Frontier states, independent-minded, suspicious of central authority
- Current state: Mostly ignoring the succession fight; focused on local concerns

**The Border Circle** — States along the northern frontier with Central Aboyinzu
- Members: Nikpato, Turbuni, Daboni, Jowdo, Buti, Wodhuubo
- Character: Buffer states, frequently invaded, desperate for Confederate protection
- Current state: Terrified; the next Paramount's attitude toward northern defense matters enormously

**The South Wall Circle** — States near the Dragon's Spine mountains
- Members: Shemu, Taulo Magi, Kafula, Shunde
- Character: Martial cultures shaped by mountain threats, suspicious of lake-state softness
- Current state: Shunde is positioning its candidate for Paramount; Kafula holds a grudge

### The Succession Crisis

Paramount Makisani III is dying. She has perhaps two years, perhaps less. Her designated heir—her nephew Tobwani—is widely considered unfit: young, inexperienced, and visibly uncomfortable with politics. The Electors have begun positioning for what comes after.

**The Three Candidates**

**Shundari of Shunde** — The Martial Option
- Background: General who unified the South Wall Circle against mountain raiders
- Platform: Confederate Army reform, stronger central authority, northern expansion
- Support: Shunde (Elector), South Wall Circle, states that want a strong Paramount
- Opposition: States that fear strong Paramounts, specifically Chilala
- Weakness: Seen as a warmonger; many fear he'd use Confederate armies against internal enemies

**Mwenekazi of Chilala** — The Expansionist Option
- Background: Regent-Queen of the largest Elector state, brilliant politician
- Platform: Chilala dominance framed as Confederate unity; absorb smaller states
- Support: Chilala (Elector), allied city-states, states that have already submitted to Chilala influence
- Opposition: Everyone who isn't already aligned with Chilala
- Weakness: Too openly ambitious; other Electors don't want to create a monster

**Tumaini of Lanwadanzi** — The Stability Option
- Background: Wealthy merchant-prince, reputation for fairness and bribery in equal measure
- Platform: Status quo, trade agreements, avoid wars, let the Diet actually function
- Support: Lanwadanzi (Elector), Highland Circle, Forest Circle, states tired of conflict
- Opposition: States that want change, specifically Shunde and Chilala
- Weakness: Seen as weak; wouldn't defend the Border Circle if tested

**The Path to Victory**

To become Paramount, a candidate needs four of seven Elector votes. Currently:
- Shunde votes Shundari (1)
- Chilala votes Mwenekazi (1)
- Lanwadanzi votes Tumaini (1)
- Mpaso votes for Makisani's heir Tobwani, but he can't win (1)
- Samani is "neutral" (selling their vote)
- Nikpato is "neutral" (terrified of backing the loser)
- Kafula votes based on ancient genealogical grudges (currently favors Mwenekazi, but this could change)

The crisis will break when Makisani dies. If no candidate has four votes by then, the Confederation enters an interregnum—no Paramount, no Confederate Army, every state for itself. The last interregnum (2891 SD) lasted seven years and killed 40,000 people.

### Culture

**The Languages**

The Confederation has no single language. Dalizi Common (a trade pidgin) serves for commerce and Diet proceedings, but each state maintains its own tongue. Scholars count at least fifteen distinct languages within Confederate borders, plus dozens of local dialects.

This linguistic fragmentation reflects deeper cultural divisions. Lakeshore states share fishing traditions and water-worship. Highland states venerate ancestors and scholarly achievement. South Wall states prize martial honor. Forest states maintain animist practices their neighbors consider primitive.

**The Faiths**

Three major religious traditions compete within the Confederation:

**The Ancestors' Way** — Dominant in the highlands and most city-states. Believers venerate their direct ancestors, maintaining family shrines and seeking guidance through ritual communion. Politically organized through the Temple of Names in Metato, which maintains genealogical records and certifies legitimate descent—crucial for inheritance disputes.

**The Lake's Children** — Dominant around Lake Tonactlet Chipe. Believers worship the lake itself as a living deity, offering sacrifices for good fishing and safe passage. Priests claim to interpret the lake's will through dreams and signs. Politically powerful in Mpaso, Chilala, and Misanda Mulu.

**The Old Forest** — Dominant in the eastern Forest Circle. Animist tradition honoring spirits of individual trees, animals, and places. Decentralized, with no formal priesthood—each village maintains its own spirit relationships. Considered backward by other traditions but fiercely defended by its practitioners.

These faiths don't actively war, but they create cultural boundaries. A highland merchant conducting business in Chilala must perform lakeside offerings or risk being seen as impious. A forest-born soldier serving in Shunde must hide their animist prayers or face mockery.

**Marriage and Alliance**

Noble marriages are political instruments, carefully negotiated to create or cement alliances. The Confederation's aristocracy is so intermarried that succession disputes routinely involve claims from three or four different states.

This creates opportunities for intrigue. A disputed inheritance might be decided by which claimant has stronger allies—or by assassination, forged documents, or convenient "discoveries" of illegitimacy. The Temple of Names profits handsomely from both certifying and challenging bloodlines.

**Mercenary Culture**

The Confederation's constant internal conflicts have produced a thriving mercenary industry. Companies range from small bands of veteran soldiers to small armies with siege equipment and mages.

Notable companies include:
- **The Lake's Teeth** — Elite heavy infantry, loyal to whoever pays best
- **The Corpse Collectors** — Specialists in dirty work: assassinations, kidnappings, evidence destruction
- **The Painted Shields** — Cavalry company with a reputation for honoring contracts exactly as written (and exploiting any ambiguity)
- **The Smoke Walkers** — Infiltrators and saboteurs, rumored to employ dark magic

Hiring mercenaries is expensive but often cheaper than maintaining standing armies. Most city-states rely on mercenary contracts for defense, which means their security depends on their treasury.

### Economy

**The Lake Trade**

Lake Tonactlet Chipe is the Confederation's economic heart. Fish, freshwater pearls, and lake-bottom minerals flow out; grain, metal goods, and foreign luxuries flow in. Control of lakeside ports means control of revenue.

Mpaso and Chilala fight constantly over lake trade. Mpaso controls the best natural harbors; Chilala controls the largest population of fishers. Neither can dominate without the other, which produces endless tariff disputes, fishing-rights conflicts, and occasional naval skirmishes.

**The Eastern Trade**

The Highland Circle controls trade routes to the Elder Wilds and beyond. Exotic goods—rare woods, magical components, foreign spices—flow through Lanwadanzi's markets. This makes Lanwadanzi wealthy and politically cautious; war disrupts trade.

**The Northern Corridor**

Trade with Central Aboyinzu and the Deadloop inner sea passes through the Border Circle. Turbuni and Nikpato both claim to be the "Gateway to the North," competing for merchant traffic. Their rivalry occasionally benefits merchants (competitive tolls) and occasionally destroys them (trade wars).

**The Currency Chaos**

The Confederation has no single currency. Each member state mints its own coins, with wildly varying weights and metal content. Money-changers profit from the confusion; merchants lose sleep over it. Periodic attempts to establish a Confederate currency have failed—no state trusts the others to mint honestly.

### Military

**No Confederate Army (Usually)**

The Confederation maintains no standing military force. When the Diet authorizes a Confederate Army—usually in response to external invasion—each member state contributes troops proportional to its population. A Confederate General is appointed (usually after weeks of political fighting) to command the combined force.

This system is slow, fractious, and surprisingly effective when it finally mobilizes. Confederate Armies have defeated external threats multiple times. They've also dissolved into internal fighting when the external threat recedes.

**Member State Forces**

Each state maintains its own military:
- **Shunde** has the most professional army—15,000 disciplined infantry
- **Chilala** has the largest population and can field 25,000 militia
- **Lanwadanzi** relies on mercenaries and a small elite guard
- **Most city-states** maintain walls, a city watch, and mercenary contracts

**The Fortress Network**

Centuries of internal war have produced a landscape dotted with fortifications. Every state has castles, every city has walls, every major road has toll-fortresses. Sieges are common; decisive battles are rare. Wars tend to be slow, expensive, and inconclusive.

### History

**The Founding Wars** (800-900 SD)

The Dalizi Confederation emerged from a century of warfare among the lake-region states. Facing invasion from the giants of the southern mountains, the warring states formed a defensive alliance. The alliance held, the giants were driven back, and the temporary coalition became permanent.

The founding treaty established the Diet, the Paramount, and the Elector system. It also established the principle that member states would handle internal affairs independently—a principle that has produced centuries of "internal affairs" that look a lot like wars.

**The Golden Age** (900-1500 SD)

Six centuries of relative stability and growth. The Confederation expanded to its current borders, absorbing smaller states through conquest, marriage, or voluntary accession. Trade flourished. The Paramount institution gained prestige. Several strong Paramounts managed to impose temporary order.

**The War of Broken Oaths** (2891 SD)

The worst internal conflict in Confederate history. A disputed succession produced two rival Paramounts, each claiming legitimacy. The Confederation split roughly in half, with Dhabisa leading one faction and Mpaso the other. Seven years of war killed 40,000 people and devastated the Border Circle.

The war ended when both claimants died in the same battle (possibly arranged by exhausted neutrals). Dhabisa lost its Elector status as punishment for starting the conflict. The current constitutional order emerged from the post-war settlement.

**The Long Peace** (2891-3340 SD)

Fifty years of genuine peace, enforced by exhaustion and the memory of the war. States focused on rebuilding. Trade recovered. A series of competent Paramounts maintained stability.

**The Slow Unraveling** (3340 SD - present)

The last forty years have seen increasing instability. Border conflicts. Succession disputes. Trade wars. The current Paramount, Makisani III, has held things together through personal relationships, but she's dying. Her potential successors lack her skills.

The Confederation isn't collapsing—not yet. But the institutions that held it together are fraying. The next succession will test whether anything remains.

### Current Tensions

**The Chilala Problem**

Chilala is too big. Its population exceeds the next three states combined. Its economy dominates the Lakeshore Circle. Its ruler, Regent-Queen Mwenekazi, is openly positioning for the Paramount seat—and if she gets it, she'll have the power to make the position meaningful.

Other states are terrified. A strong Paramount backed by Chilala's resources could transform the Confederation from a loose alliance into a centralized state—with Chilala at its center. The other Electors are looking for anyone who can stop her.

**The Northern Vulnerability**

The Border Circle states are exposed and underfunded. They depend on Confederate protection that arrives slowly if at all. Nikpato has been invaded three times in the last century; twice, help came too late.

The next Paramount's attitude toward northern defense will determine whether these states remain loyal. If they decide the Confederation can't protect them, they might seek arrangements elsewhere—with Chechol, with Terrenia, with anyone who offers security.

No one in the Confederation knows about the Red Army building in Chechol. When that threat emerges, the Border Circle will be the first to burn.

**The Religious Tensions**

The three major faiths have coexisted uneasily for centuries. Recently, tensions have increased:
- The Temple of Names (Ancestors' Way) has begun challenging inheritance claims from Lake's Children families, citing "improper documentation"
- Lake's Children priests have declared certain highland practices "offensive to the lake"
- Forest Circle states have expelled Temple of Names missionaries, claiming cultural imperialism

None of this has turned violent yet. But in a succession crisis, religious divisions could provide another fault line.

**The Mercenary Dependency**

Too many states rely on mercenaries instead of maintaining their own forces. This works until it doesn't. If a mercenary company decides to switch sides mid-conflict, or if multiple states bid for the same company, or if companies decide to take a city for themselves instead of their employers...

The Painted Shields attempted exactly this in 3365 SD, seizing the city-state of Kerikos and holding it for ransom. A Confederate Army eventually dislodged them, but the precedent was set. Mercenaries are soldiers without loyalty. The Confederation is full of them.

### Notable Member States

**Chilala** (Elector) — The largest state, controlling the eastern lake shore. Regent-Queen Mwenekazi rules during her son's minority, and shows no intention of surrendering power when he comes of age. Capital: Kisro.

**Mpaso** (Elector) — The current Paramount's home state, controlling the northern lake shore. Wealthy, prestigious, and facing a leadership vacuum when Makisani dies. Capital: Misiwada.

**Lanwadanzi** (Elector) — The merchant state, controlling eastern trade routes. Prefers stability, willing to pay for it. Capital: Lanwadar.

**Samani** (Elector) — Hosts the Diet in Metato, considers itself the Confederation's neutral heart. In practice, sells its vote to the highest bidder. Capital: Metato.

**Shunde** (Elector) — The martial state, with the Confederation's best professional army. General Shundari has made himself the obvious strongman candidate. Capital: Bisaro.

**Nikpato** (Elector) — The nervous state, repeatedly invaded, desperate for protection. Will vote for whoever promises to defend them. Capital: Kampaga.

**Kafula** (Elector) — The ancient state, obsessed with genealogy and bloodlines. Votes based on grudges centuries old. Capital: Kyobale.

**Turbuni** — Border city-state, shares the School of Summoning with Chechol. Doesn't realize the threat next door. Capital: Turbuni.

**Dhabisa** — Former Elector, still bitter about the War of Broken Oaths. Would do almost anything to regain its seat. Capital: Sumda.

### Player Hooks

**The Succession**

- A candidate hires the party to secure an Elector's vote—through persuasion, bribery, blackmail, or removal of obstacles
- The Temple of Names has discovered a genealogical secret that could disqualify a candidate; multiple parties want to control or destroy the evidence
- An Elector dies unexpectedly; the party witnesses the death and knows it was murder, but proving it means navigating Confederate politics

**The Mercenary Life**

- A mercenary company offers the party membership—good pay, interesting work, and a front-row seat to Confederate conflicts
- Two states have hired the same company for opposing operations; the party must choose sides or find a third option
- The Painted Shields are planning another city seizure; the party has advance warning and must decide what to do with it

**The Border Crisis**

- Nikpato's ruler hires the party to investigate strange reports from Chechol—unusually militant Rentar, communities going silent, travelers who don't return
- A Rentar elder has fled south, seeking help; she has fragments of truth about Gynor's corruption but no one believes her
- The Red Army activates; the party is in Turbuni when the attack begins

**The Religious Conflict**

- The Temple of Names wants investigators to examine a noble family's genealogical claims—claims that would shift an Elector vote
- A Lake's Children priest has gone missing in the highlands; is this murder, kidnapping, or voluntary disappearance?
- Forest Circle states are forming their own alliance, potentially seceding from the Confederation; the party must assess whether this is serious

### Relations with Neighbors

**Chechol** — Seen as a quiet, pious backwater. Turbuni has the closest ties, sharing the School of Summoning. The Confederation doesn't watch Chechol closely, which will prove to be a catastrophic mistake.

**Terrenia** — Trade partners and occasional rivals. The Terrenia states are wealthier and more stable; the Confederation resents this. No serious conflicts currently.

**Elder Wilds** — Source of exotic trade goods and occasional monsters. The Forest Circle handles relations, mostly through Lanwadanzi's merchants.

**Dragon's Spine Mountains** — Source of threats: giants, monsters, and worse. The South Wall Circle exists to hold this frontier.

## Danak

Tags: state

Links: States

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Tribal confederation with war-chiefs

**Primary Inhabitants**: Danaki (minotaurs)

**Population**: ~35,000

**Stability**: Somewhat Stable (internally); perpetual external conflict

**Core Values**:

- Strength and martial prowess
- Suppression of emotion
- Communal child-rearing—no family bonds
- Berserk rage as controlled weapon

### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Undeveloped

**Primary Exports**: Mercenaries, leather goods, bone crafts

**Primary Imports**: Metal weapons, armor, grain

- The Danaki economy is centered on war. When not fighting the Enti, Danaki hire out as mercenaries across northern Clueanda
- Crafting focuses on practical military goods—the Danaki have no tradition of art for art's sake
- What little trade exists comes through occasional merchants brave or foolish enough to visit, or through raids

### Military

**Size**: Large (relative to population)

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Mandatory—every Danaki is trained for combat

- Children are raised communally with combat training beginning before literacy
- The Danaki berserk state makes them devastating shock troops
- Organization is loose—war-chiefs lead through proven ability, not inherited authority
- The entire adult population can be mobilized for defense or major offensives

### Geography

**Location**: Northwestern Frostmarch Peninsula

**Biomes**: Rolling highlands, sparse forest, tundra

**Named Entities**: Valley of Thagrund, Chinayaqax Munaga (forest)

**Rivers & Lakes**: Tottokzin River, Ganek River, Lakiat River

**Adjacent Waters**: Iron Strait, approaches to Northern Isles

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None—the Danaki have no permanent capital, only seasonal gathering sites

**Cities**: None

**Towns**: None in the traditional sense—Danaki live in fortified villages

**Villages**: Enasshi, Yeszhi, Zerqollo, Rikki, Anno, Ildano, Vinesso, Orokabo

### Primary Conflicts

- **The Eternal War**: Generations of conflict with the Enti of Atyeska. Neither side remembers why it started; neither side will stop.
- Raids and counter-raids across the border claim lives every season
- Captured enemies are either killed or enslaved (the Enti do the same)
- Occasional pressure from Kyorda to cease hostilities, always ignored

### History

The Danaki have occupied these lands since before recorded history. Their culture evolved around the harsh realities of northern life—communities that couldn't fight together died together.

The abandonment of family bonds is ancient, practical wisdom: in a society where death in battle is common, attachment to specific parents or children is a vulnerability. Children are raised by the village, taught by whichever adults have the skills to teach, and grow up knowing their loyalty is to their people, not to any individual.

The berserk rage is both blessing and curse. In battle, it makes the Danaki nearly unstoppable. Outside battle, it makes them distrustful of their own emotions—any strong feeling might trigger the fury. This has created a culture of deliberate stoicism, where displaying emotion is seen as weakness and loss of control.

### The Valley of Thagrund

The western border of Danak is marked by the Valley of Thagrund—a defensible position where the Danaki have repelled Enti incursions for centuries. The valley floor is littered with the bones of the fallen from both sides, never collected, serving as a permanent memorial to the cost of the endless war.

## Deo Esari

Tags: state, elves, confederation, titans, hand-magic

The mountain kingdom. Deo Esari occupies the Kelder Mountains along the eastern edge of the Farlands—a vertical realm of peaks, passes, and the bones of ancient titans. Of the three elven kingdoms, Deo Esari is the smallest in population, the most isolated in geography, and the most sacred in purpose. They are the keepers of the titan dead.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Theocratic council (Titanic Priesthood)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Elves

**Population**: ~80,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**:
- Sacred duty to the titan remains
- Preservation of ritual knowledge
- Isolation from corrupting outside influences
- Patient observation over hasty action

Deo Esari is governed by the **Titanic Priesthood**—a council of ritual specialists who have dedicated their lives to the proper handling of titan remains. The High Priest serves as head of state, but decisions are made collectively. Secular matters are delegated to appointed administrators; anything touching the bones requires priestly consensus.

The priesthood is not hereditary. Candidates are identified in childhood through aptitude testing, then trained for decades before receiving their first tattoo. The hand-magic traditions of Deo Esari are restricted entirely to the priesthood—no lay elf may bear titan-bone markings.

### The Titan Bones

The Kelder Mountains are a graveyard. During the God War, titans fell here in numbers—some killed by dragons, some by other titans, some by weapons or forces that left no survivors to record them. Their bones remain, buried in the peaks, exposed by erosion, scattered through valleys.

Deo Esari exists to tend these remains.

**The Sacred Monopoly**

Approximately 95% of all titan bone in the world comes from the Kelder Mountains. The Deo Esari do not sell it. They do not trade it. Titan bone is sacred material, and its distribution is controlled absolutely:

- Bones are harvested through elaborate ritual by designated priesthood teams
- Processed bone is used for religious ceremonies within the confederation
- Small amounts are provided to Lenora and Illron for their own sacred purposes
- Nothing—ever—leaves the three kingdoms legitimately

**Defectors**

The only way titan bone reaches the outside world is through defection. Occasionally, an elf (usually not a priest, but someone with access to processed materials) steals what they can carry and flees east over the mountains into Rakite territory, or south into Wycendeula.

These defectors are criminals of the highest order. Their names are struck from all records. Their families disown them publicly. They are considered spiritually dead—worse than the Grieving of Imyena Edhil, who at least remain within the community of elves.

The defectors don't care. A single titan-bone blade can purchase a lordship in the human kingdoms. A pouch of processed bone powder is worth more than most merchants see in a lifetime. The temptation is immense.

Defections are rare—perhaps one or two per decade—but they are the entire source of the world's titan-bone supply outside the elven lands. Deo Esari's priests hunt defectors relentlessly, but the mountains are vast and the eastern passes are many.

### The Southern Disaster

Two centuries ago, the southern harvesting grounds were abandoned.

The hills below the main Kelder range—Eshbya Hills, Tomgryir Eror, Carya Nairn, Kirthdron Soytas—were once productive territory. Villages supported the harvesting operations. Generations of priests worked these sites.

Then the contamination spread.

**What Happened**

Titan bones are not uniformly safe. Most are inert—ancient material, chemically strange but not actively dangerous. But some titans died badly. Some were killed by forces that left traces. Some carried things in their marrow that survived their deaths.

The southern sites hit a bad vein. The specific nature of the contamination is recorded only in sealed priesthood archives, but the effects were unmistakable: harvesters who handled certain bones began showing symptoms within weeks. Wasting sickness. Skin discoloration. Children born wrong—or not born at all. The contamination spread through families, then through villages.

The priesthood ordered evacuation. Anyone who had worked the southern sites, anyone who had lived in the affected villages, anyone who had prolonged contact with those who had—all were relocated to quarantine settlements in the high mountains. Most died within a generation. The contamination burned through them.

**The Abandoned Lands**

The villages still stand. Ot'Pelyon and Mosimor in Tomgryir Eror. Novranas, Salenial, and Myrth in Eshbya Hills. Sarki Tulo in Kirthdron Soytas. Empty now, but intact. Clothes in wardrobes. Tools in workshops. Everyone simply left.

No one goes there anymore. The priesthood maintains warning markers at every approach. The contamination may have faded by now—or it may be waiting. No one is willing to test it.

**Economic Impact**

The southern disaster cut Deo Esari's harvesting capacity by roughly 40%. Remaining sites in the northern Kelder are worked more intensively, but total output has never recovered. This has driven titan-bone prices worldwide to unprecedented heights—which, ironically, makes defection more tempting than ever.

The priesthood's response to this pressure has been to become more insular, more secretive, more suspicious of outsiders. The disaster proved that carelessness with sacred materials brings catastrophe. They will not repeat the mistake.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Developed (extremely advanced in ritual crafts)

**Primary Exports**: Nothing to outsiders. Limited ritual objects to confederation partners.

**Primary Imports**: Food (from Lenora), textiles, tools, anything that cannot be produced in mountain territory

Deo Esari cannot feed itself. The mountains grow little. Nearly all food comes from Lenora in exchange for titan-bone ritual objects—a dependency the priesthood accepts as the cost of their sacred duty.

The kingdom's internal economy centers on the harvesting operations: miners, porters, craftspeople who process raw bone into usable materials, artisans who create the final ritual objects. Everyone serves the bones, directly or indirectly.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: High (enhanced by hand-magic)

**Enrollment**: Mandatory service for non-priests

Deo Esari's military is small but enhanced. Every soldier bears at least basic protective tattoos—not the full priest markings, but enough to provide resistance to common threats. The priesthood guards its monopoly on advanced hand-magic, but defensive applications are shared for the kingdom's protection.

The mountain terrain provides most of their defense. Invasion through the Kelder passes would be suicidal. Deo Esari's soldiers exist mainly to guard the harvesting sites and pursue defectors.

### Geography

**Location**: Kelder Mountains, eastern Farlands

**Biomes**: Alpine, high mountain, some sheltered valleys

**Major Geographic Features**:
- **Kelder Mountains**: The entire bracket-shaped range along the Farlands' eastern edge. Titan bones throughout.
- **Mathavia Mountains**: Southern extension of the range, including some contaminated zones
- **The Abandoned Lands**: Eshbya Hills, Tomgryir Eror, Carya Nairn, Kirthdron Soytas—off-limits since the disaster

**Rivers**:
- **Ver Pyur**: Flows from Isyherius southward
- Various mountain streams feeding into the lowland rivers

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Thillesari (in the central Kelder range)

**Major Cities** (north to south):
- **Isyherius**: Northernmost city, source of the Ver Pyur
- **Mysaneas**: Secondary administrative center
- **Leleth Serine**: Major harvesting hub
- **Ryhaneas**: Eastern slopes
- **Eyslon**: Central range
- **Iyathelneor**: Southern range (above the contaminated zone)
- **Ulfe Alari**: Southernmost active settlement

Deo Esari has cities and towns but relatively few villages. The population is concentrated—mountain living doesn't support scattered agriculture. Settlements are built near harvesting sites or along the passes that connect them.

### Relations

**Lenora**: Necessary but cool. Deo Esari needs Lenora's food; Lenora receives titan-bone ritual objects in exchange. Neither side particularly likes the other. Lenora's agricultural ambition feels vulgar to the priesthood. Deo Esari's secretiveness frustrates Lenoran merchants.

**Illron**: Warm. Both kingdoms prioritize tradition and view Lenora's growth-focused culture with suspicion. Deo Esari's disaster has only reinforced their conservatism, which aligns them further with Illron's ancient-pact philosophy.

**The Outside World**: Hostility. Outsiders are forbidden from entering Deo Esari. The eastern passes are guarded. Ships cannot approach—there is no coast. Any non-elf found within the kingdom's borders is detained, questioned, and expelled (if lucky) or imprisoned (if suspected of seeking bones).

### Primary Conflicts

- **Defection**: The ongoing leak of titan bone to the outside world. Every defector strengthens foreign powers and weakens the sacred monopoly.
- **The Contaminated Lands**: Should anyone attempt to reclaim the southern sites? The priesthood says no. Some younger elves point out that the bones there are incredibly valuable and may have been safe for decades.
- **Food Dependency**: Reliance on Lenora is uncomfortable. Some priests advocate for stricter rationing to reduce imports; others argue this would weaken the population.

### History

The elves who became Deo Esari discovered the titan bones shortly after arriving in the Farlands. They recognized what they had found: remnants of beings from before the current age, material of immense power and danger.

The priesthood formed organically—those who learned to handle the bones safely teaching those who came after. Over generations, the casual mining operations became ritualized, then sacred. By the time of the Three Kingdoms Treaty, Deo Esari was already the defined keeper of the titan dead.

The southern disaster, 200 years ago, is the defining event of recent history. It validated every conservative instinct, every warning about carelessness, every ritual restriction. The priesthood that emerged from the crisis is more cautious, more secretive, and more certain of its purpose than ever before.

### Hand-Magic (Titanic Tattoos)

The priests of Deo Esari practice **hand-magic**—a form of permanent body modification using processed titan bone. Tattoos inscribed with titan-bone ink grant various abilities depending on the pattern and placement:

- Protective wards against physical harm
- Enhanced strength or endurance
- Resistance to magical effects
- Sensory improvements
- More esoteric effects known only to senior priests

The process is painful, permanent, and sacred. Only Titanic Priests may receive full marking. The secrets of inscription are guarded absolutely—no outsider has ever learned the techniques, and defectors who flee with bone material cannot replicate the magic.

A fully marked priest bears dozens of tattoos accumulated over centuries. They are walking arsenals of subtle power. But they do not flaunt this—the marks are usually covered, revealed only when necessary.

## Dern

Tags: state

Links: States

The middle child of the Widebarrow mountain states, Dern occupies the central peaks between Thorum's northern strongholds and Mez's nightmare warrens below. It is a kingdom defined by siege—not the dramatic, banner-waving kind, but the grinding, generational kind. The Lyzine have been coming up from below for centuries. The Dernish have been pushing them back down for just as long. Neither side wins. Neither side stops.

Dern's people are hard in the way that survivors of endless war become hard: practical, suspicious of outsiders, and utterly unsentimental about violence. They don't hate the Lyzine the way softer peoples might hate an enemy. Hatred requires energy. The Dernish simply kill Lyzine the way farmers kill rats—methodically, without passion, because the alternative is being overrun. This grim pragmatism extends to everything. A Dernish proverb: "Hope is for those who've never lost a child to the deep."

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Hereditary Monarchy (War Council)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans (Dernish)

**Population**: 35,000

**Stability**: Somewhat Stable

**Core Values**: Vigilance, sacrifice, duty, silence

- The Crown of Dern passes through the royal bloodline, but true power lies with the War Council—seven commanders who control the nation's defenses. The current king, Aldric IV, is twelve years old. The Council rules in his name and has done so since his father died in a Lyzine raid three years ago.
- Every Dernish family owes "deep-debt"—mandatory service in the tunnel watches. One adult from each household must serve two years in five, patrolling the sealed passages and ancient mines where the Lyzine breach attempts occur. The duty is dreaded but unavoidable; those who flee are hanged, and their families inherit double debt.
- Silence is a cultural virtue. The Dernish speak quietly, value restraint, and distrust the demonstrative. This stems from practical necessity—sound carries in tunnels, and a loud voice can draw Lyzine scouts—but has become ingrained in the national character. Visitors find the Dernish disconcertingly quiet, interpreting calm as coldness.
- The dead are cremated immediately. No exceptions. Bodies left too long can be... used by things from below. This extends to battlefield protocol: Dernish soldiers carry fire kits and prioritize burning fallen comrades over recovering weapons.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Iron, copper, silver, worked metal goods

**Primary Imports**: Food, textiles, timber, alchemical supplies

- The Widebarrow Mountains hold rich veins of iron, copper, and silver that would make any nation wealthy—if mining weren't a death sentence. Dern's mines operate under military escort, with tunnel watches stationed at every shaft. Production is steady but costly in lives.
- Dernish metalwork is prized across Ve. Necessity breeds skill: their weapons are designed for close-quarters tunnel fighting, their armor optimized for protection against Lyzine venom and web. The famous "Dern-forged" blades fetch premium prices in Shyona and Avalon.
- Food is Dern's constant need. The mountain valleys produce some grain and the slopes support sheep, but it's never enough. Dern trades metal for Avalon's surplus grain, and this dependency shapes their foreign policy. They cannot afford to anger their breadbasket.
- A peculiar trade exists with Mez itself: Lyzine silk, harvested from abandoned webs or slain raiders, is worth more than silver by weight. Dernish "silk-hunters" venture into cleared tunnels to collect it—dangerous work, but profitable enough that some make a career of it.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Universal (with deep-debt rotation)

**The Tunnel Watch**: Dern's primary defensive force, numbering roughly 4,000 at any time. Tunnel Watchers are experts in subterranean warfare—they know which echoes mean danger, which passages flood seasonally, and how to fight in spaces where you can't swing a sword. Their distinctive short-hafted axes and heavy crossbows are designed for cramped combat.

**The Surface Guard**: A smaller force of 1,500 that patrols the mountain passes and protects the settlements. Less celebrated than the Tunnel Watch, but essential—the Lyzine occasionally emerge on the surface in force, and someone must meet them.

**The Sealers**: An elite corps of 200 specialists who close breached tunnels. They work with explosives, shaped stone, and a brutal willingness to collapse passages on themselves if necessary. Sealer mortality is horrific. Sealer volunteers are never lacking.

- Dern's military doctrine is purely defensive. They don't invade Mez; they can't. The tunnels below are the Lyzine's domain, and venturing deep means fighting on enemy ground against creatures perfectly adapted to darkness. Instead, Dern holds the line, seals breaches, and endures.
- Every settlement maintains "last-stand" protocols—designated fortified buildings, stockpiled weapons, and memorized evacuation routes. Children learn the drill before they learn to read.

### Geography

**Location**: Central Widebarrow Mountains, bordered by Thorum (north), Mez (below and southwest), Avalon (northwest), Shyona (northeast), and the Suki Jungle (south)

**Biomes**: Alpine mountains, highland valleys, subterranean passages

**Named Entities**: Central Widebarrow Range, Promisewood (border forest with Avalon)

**Rivers & Lakes**: None named (small mountain streams)

**Adjacent Waters**: None (landlocked)

- The Central Widebarrow is less dramatic than Thorum's Steadfast peaks but more extensive—a broad band of rugged highlands that stretches from the Avalon border to the Shyonan frontier.
- Below the surface, the mountain is honeycombed with natural caves, ancient mines, and Lyzine-carved tunnels. Maps exist, but they're never complete—the Lyzine dig constantly, and new passages appear faster than the Watch can chart them.
- The Promisewood, on the northwest border, is Dern's gentlest region—a forested slope that descends toward Avalon. Timber from the Promisewood is essential for mine supports and construction. The forest is named for the marriage pacts it witnessed when Dern and Avalon's royal houses allied three centuries ago.
- The Boneswamps lie to the south, technically in Chimean territory but close enough to stain Dern's border. Ancient wars between Dern and Chimea filled those waters with corpses; now they walk again. Dernish patrols avoid the southern marches.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Dodi
**Cities**: None
**Towns**: Foster
**Villages**: Lush

**Dodi** — The capital clings to a defensive valley in the central mountains, built around the entrance to the oldest Dernish mines. Perhaps 15,000 people live here, making it by far the largest settlement. The city is constructed in concentric rings: the outermost residential districts can be abandoned and sealed if Lyzine break through; the inner fortress holds the palace, the armories, and the deepest food stores. Dodi has never fallen. The Dernish intend to keep it that way.

The Royal Forge operates here—the premier metalworking facility in Ve, where the finest Dern-forged weapons are made. Master smiths guard techniques passed down for generations. The forge's output is split between military needs and export revenue; balancing these demands is a constant political tension.

**Foster** — A fortified town on the northeastern border where Dern meets Shyona. Foster guards the only reliable pass between the two nations—a fact that gives it strategic importance far exceeding its size. The town's 3,000 residents are a mix of Dernish soldiers, merchants handling cross-border trade, and Shyonan observers who maintain a permanent "diplomatic presence" that everyone understands is espionage. Relations are polite but tense; neither side fully trusts the other, but both prefer the devil they know to the Lyzine below.

Foster's commander, Marshal Brynn Kolste, has held the post for twenty years. She's outlasted three kings and two wars, and her relationship with her Shyonan counterpart is said to be the true guarantor of the border's peace.

**Lush** — A small village of perhaps 800 souls, tucked into an interior valley that sees the most sun in all of Dern. The name is aspirational—Lush is merely less bleak than its neighbors. The village exists to farm the valley's modest fields and tend the sheep that graze the upper slopes. It's the closest Dern comes to a peaceful settlement: far from the major tunnel systems, too poor to raid, forgotten by history. Families who've served their deep-debt sometimes retire here, seeking quiet after years of darkness below.

### Primary Conflicts

- **Mez and the Lyzine**: The eternal war. Every generation thinks it might end; none have been right. The Lyzine want the surface—"their rightful place," as they see it. The Dernish want to survive. No negotiation is possible; the Lyzine don't recognize lesser species as worthy of treaties.
- **The Regency Question**: King Aldric IV is twelve. The War Council rules. Some councilors genuinely intend to surrender power when he comes of age; others have grown comfortable with authority. As Aldric grows older, this tension will break one way or another.
- **Chimean Border**: The Boneswamps and the old wars are not forgotten. Chimea claims territory Dern considers its own; Dern fortifies passes Chimea considers theirs. The conflict is frozen, not resolved—both nations have more pressing enemies, but the grudge remains.
- **Economic Dependency**: Dern needs Avalon's grain. This gives Avalon leverage that sophisticated diplomats increasingly exploit. The current trade agreements favor Avalon heavily; renegotiating them without losing food supply is a delicate problem.

### History

Dern was founded as a mining colony roughly 600 years ago, when prospectors from the lowlands discovered silver in the central Widebarrow. For two centuries, it prospered unremarkably. Then the Lyzine came.

The First Breach (approximately 380 years ago) was catastrophic. An entire mining settlement—nearly 2,000 people—vanished overnight. The survivors who escaped spoke of spider-things from below, of webs that clogged the tunnels, of beautiful, terrible voices demanding surrender. The lowland kingdoms sent armies; the armies died in the dark. Within a decade, Dern's population had halved, and the remaining survivors had learned the first harsh lessons of subterranean warfare.

The next three centuries forged modern Dern. A military monarchy emerged, built around the imperative of defense. The tunnel watches organized. The sealers developed their grim art. Dern stopped trying to exterminate the Lyzine—an impossible goal—and focused on containment. The Lyzine, for their part, never stopped trying to break through, but their efforts became raids rather than conquests.

The War of Bone (approximately 200 years ago) saw Dern's bloodiest conflict with Chimea, fought over mountain passes and mining rights. Both nations exhausted themselves; the Boneswamps are the war's lasting monument. The Treaty of Ash ended formal hostilities, but the dead in those swamps ensure neither side truly forgives.

Today, Dern endures. The Lyzine press harder in some years than others—the pattern seems random, though some scholars suggest it correlates with the spider queens' breeding cycles. The regency holds. The mines produce. The Tunnel Watch keeps vigil. And every night, somewhere in the mountains, something scratches at a sealed door from the other side.

### Hooks

- **The Child King**: Aldric IV is approaching the age where he might assert authority. He's been raised by warriors but advised by politicians. What kind of ruler will he become? Players who gain his trust could shape Dern's future.
- **The Deep Breach**: A new tunnel has been discovered—not Lyzine-dug, but ancient, predating human settlement. It goes deeper than any known passage. The Tunnel Watch is organizing an expedition. They're hiring.
- **Silk Hunters**: The reward for Lyzine silk has increased sharply. Chimean buyers are paying triple rates, no questions asked. Why do they want it? What are they making?
- **The Promisewood Marriage**: An Avalonian noble seeks a Dernish bride to seal a trade agreement. The Dernish princess in question would rather fight Lyzine than marry a soft lowlander. Complications ensue.
- **Boneswamp Stirring**: The dead in the southern marches are more active than usual. Some are wearing Dernish armor from the War of Bone. Others are wearing armor no one recognizes at all.

## Echea

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Magocracy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Echi (Goshwen)

**Population**: 150,000

**Stability**: Stable 

**Core Values**: Knowledge, scholarship, magical prowess, elemental control, skill

- Echea is controlled by a combination of scholars and elemental masters in a large council. Access is granted by a combination of invitation and majority vote from general population.
- Echea is largely a meritocracy. If you are smart, you are respected by your peers.
- Elemental magic is largely valued, as is technical skill. Although the Echi hate the Cogheads, they begrudgingly respect their skill.


### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Very developed

**Primary Exports**: Magitech, education

**Primary Imports**: 

- Echea is home to some of the largest, most influential universities in all of Alaria. If you wish to master an element, many choose to come to the Echean universities.
	- They have paragon attuned elementalists within every discipline.
    - The universities also require you to work on artifacts part time. These are sold and help fund the university.
- Echea exports a high quantity of magical imbued artifacts, bringing in large quantities of wealth.


### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Extremely high

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- The Echean military rarely forms, but when it does, it is absolutely terrifying. The universities double as military training academies, and citizens who attend them are required to also take classes in military tactics and combat. This results in highly capable, extremely talented elemental war casters making up the majority of the Echean military forces.


### Geography

**Location**: Small mountainous peninsula at the tip of the Watar peninsula, jutting into the Middle Sea

**Biomes**: Alpine mountains, rocky coastline

**Climate**: Mediterranean at the coasts, alpine in the peaks

**Named Entities**: Iri Mountains (the two peaks that make up most of the country) 


### Political Geography

**Capital**: Adi Assabehta

**Cities**: Adal

**Towns**: 

**Villages**: 


### Primary Conflicts
