- Echea is frequently the trophy of military endeavors, since it is seen as a wellspring of wealth and education to be harnessed. However, invaders have never made it to the capital.
- Echea houses many children of foreign royalty who wish to see some of the knowledge of the Echean universities rub off on their offspring, with variable success.
- Despite hundreds of years passing, the vicious hatred between Echeans and Cogheads continues. Echea conveniently ignores the Coghead genocide only 50 years ago that killed all the cogheads in echea.
- A mad scientist scholar hypothesized about the existance of a flip site of the Material Plane. Unbeknownst to anyone else, he managed to open a portal to the other side, binding the entire city of Adi Assabehta to Zylogmus Eternus. Now, people are mysteriously dying throughout the city, and some people are fleeing. Many people don't think this mysterious death thing is real (or think it's caused by other reasons), so most stay. The scholar was the first to die (freak coincidence), and no one suspects anything. Portal undiscovered. The capital will slowly empty out over the next few years as people realize that there is something causing people to mysteriously die.

### History

Two centuries ago, Cogheads and Echi worked together in the mountains. The cogheads created the machinery, and the Echi created the magic. The mountains were jointly ruled over by the two races. A friendly, but fierce rivarly between the two races existed over which technology was better—magic or machines. The Echi declared victory when they had created infinite resources from their elemental artifacts, but the Cogheads countered by developing a machine allowing them to steal the very elemental attunement out of another creature's body. They used this to say that they had in fact won. Of course, this was disputed by the gnomes. In retaliation, the cogheads sucked the extremely potent elemental attunement right out of a Echi figurehead. This was seen as an act of war, and the Echi massacred and drove the Cogheads out of the mountains. The Cogheads have never forgiven the Echi for the brutal massacre, and the Echi believe it was totally justified. This conflict has spread across ethnic lines, and many gnomes and goblins who had nothing to do with the history now have variably extreme views towards the other.

## Edari

Tags: state, ve, korel

Edari is not a state in the conventional sense—it has no government, no cities, no taxes, no laws. It is a geographic region claimed entirely by the Korel, the Wildborn Koren, who tolerate no permanent settlement within their territory.

### Government

None. The Korel organize into hunting packs of 10-40 individuals, each led by whichever member has proven strongest or most cunning. Packs are fluid—members leave and join based on personal loyalty, mating, or disputes. There is no overarching Korel authority, no council of packs, no paramount chief.

This makes diplomacy with "Edari" effectively impossible. One pack might grant safe passage; the next might hunt the same travelers for sport.

### Culture

The Korel embrace their predatory nature where their Kor cousins suppress it. They live by instinct, valuing cunning, strength, and pack loyalty above all. Their camps are temporary—hide tents, fire pits, drying racks for meat—abandoned when the game moves on.

They have no written language and little use for trade goods. What they want, they take. What they need, they hunt. Outsiders are viewed as either potential prey, potential threats, or (rarely) potential pack members. The last category requires proving oneself through trials that few non-Korel survive.

### Economy

Subsistence hunting. The Korel have no currency, no markets, no exports. They occasionally trade with Sestran merchants at the edge of their territory—furs and bones for metal tools and weapons—but such exchanges are informal and sporadic.

### Military

Every adult Korel is a hunter and fighter. Their warfare is ambush, pursuit, and overwhelming force at chosen moments. They don't hold territory in a military sense—they simply make occupying it so costly that no one tries.

Organized armies have entered Edari before. None have conquered it. The Korel simply fade into the grassland, strike supply lines, pick off stragglers, and wait for the invaders to starve or retreat.

### Relations

- **Sestros**: Uneasy coexistence. Sestran merchants trade at the border; Sestran settlers die if they cross it.
- **Shyona**: Minimal contact. The Turquish Bay and Coquray Sound provide a water barrier.
- **Others**: Edari is too remote and too dangerous for most powers to care about.

### Player Hooks

- **Crossing the Savannah**: The party needs to reach the northern coast. Do they negotiate with Korel packs, try to sneak through, or find another way?
- **Pack Politics**: A Korel pack is in conflict with neighbors. Outsiders who prove themselves might tip the balance—and earn passage.
- **Wind-Singer Territory**: Someone needs something from deep in Howlwood. The Korel won't go—the wind-singers that live there can kill a hunting party. But outsiders might be desperate or foolish enough to try.
- **Hume Une**: The abandoned Kor colony on the Estri Islands contains records and artifacts untouched for centuries. The Korel consider entering it spiritual contamination. An outsider with a boat could walk right in—if they can get past Korel waters first.

## Eidlsandres

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Hidden magocracy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Gnomes

**Population**: 15,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Secrecy, knowledge, self-sufficiency, privacy

- Eidlsandres is ruled by a council of master illusionists—the most skilled handmagic practitioners among the gnomes. Succession is based purely on demonstrated magical ability.
- The city-state doesn't officially exist. No maps show it. No trade routes lead to it. Visitors aren't welcome because visitors don't know there's anywhere to visit.
- The gnomes here have maintained total isolation for centuries. They grow their own food in the hidden valleys of the Bygone Hills, craft their own goods, and want nothing from the outside world.
- Every citizen learns the basics of illusion magic. The children's games involve creating false paths and phantom obstacles. By adulthood, most can sustain minor illusions indefinitely.

### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Very Developed

**Primary Exports**: None

**Primary Imports**: None

- Eidlsandres is completely self-sufficient. The Bygone Hills contain everything the gnomes need—arable land, fresh water, timber, stone, and the air leyline that powers their illusions.
- Internal economy exists but operates differently than outsider systems. Status comes from magical skill and contribution to the collective deception, not wealth.
- The gnomes produce sophisticated magical items—primarily illusion-based—but these never leave the city. The knowledge of how to make them is considered the city's most precious resource.
- No currency from outside is accepted because no outsiders are allowed to exist within the borders.

### Military

**Size**: None (traditional)

**Quality**: Extremely High (magical defense)

**Enrollment**: Universal (illusory training)

- Eidlsandres has no standing army. It doesn't need one.
- The entire region is laced with layered illusions of staggering complexity. False paths lead in circles. Phantom cliffs appear over safe ground. Real hazards are hidden behind images of meadows. The Bygone Hills themselves are one continuous trap.
- Goblins from the surrounding plains know only that their people who enter the hills never return. They don't know why—they've never found bodies, never found evidence of what killed them. They simply vanish.
- The truth is simpler and more elegant: they get lost. They walk in circles until they starve, fall into hidden ravines, or simply wander back out with no memory of where they were trying to go. The illusions don't kill; they redirect.
- In the rare event that someone penetrates the outer defenses, the gnomes can deploy more aggressive magic. But it's never been necessary.

### Geography

**Location**: The Bygone Hills, east of Glidwyn Forest, surrounded by goblin plains

**Biomes**: Hidden valleys, forested hills, agricultural terraces, mountain springs

**Named Entities**: The Bygone Hills, the Air Leyline

**Rivers & Lakes**: Springs and streams flowing from the hills, all concealed

**Adjacent Waters**: None visible

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Eidlsandres (the city itself)

**Cities**: Eidlsandres

**Towns**: Several hidden settlements throughout the hills, unnamed to outsiders

**Villages**: Agricultural hamlets in concealed valleys

### Primary Conflicts

**Maintaining the Veil**

The illusions that protect Eidlsandres require constant maintenance. The air leyline provides power, but the spells must be tended, adjusted, updated. As generations pass, some young gnomes question whether total isolation is worth the effort. So far, the elders' answer has been emphatic: yes.

**The Occasional Penetrator**

Every few decades, someone gets further into the hills than they should. A particularly perceptive traveler, a mage who senses the illusions, an accidental stumble onto a real path. When this happens, the gnomes must decide what to do. Usually, they simply erase the intruder's memory and redirect them outward. Occasionally, the situation is more complicated.

**The Air Leyline**

The leyline that runs through Eidlsandres is the source of their power—and a potential vulnerability. If it were disrupted, damaged, or drained, the illusions would fail. The gnomes guard the leyline's physical manifestation sites jealously, and study constantly to ensure they understand every fluctuation in its flow.

**The Outside World's Encroachment**

The goblin plains are chaotic but stable. The forests are steady. But the world beyond is changing—wars, migrations, magical catastrophes. Eidlsandres has survived by being irrelevant, not worth finding. If the region ever becomes strategically important, isolation may become harder to maintain.

### History

Eidlsandres was founded by gnomes fleeing persecution—the specific details lost even to them, so many generations ago that the original threat has probably ceased to exist. They found the Bygone Hills and its air leyline, recognized the potential, and began the great work of vanishing.

The first generation built the physical city. The second mastered the leyline. The third perfected the outer illusions. Each generation since has refined the techniques, adding layers, patching weaknesses, extending the protected zone further into the surrounding hills.

The gnomes remember that they came from somewhere, that they had enemies, that they chose to disappear. They've preserved this history as a warning: the outside world is dangerous, unpredictable, and best avoided. Better to tend your gardens, practice your craft, and let the chaos pass by unnoticed.

For centuries, the system has worked. The goblins of the plains treat the Bygone Hills as cursed, avoiding them instinctively. The Eloweir to the west know something strange exists to their east but have never investigated seriously. The rest of the world doesn't know the Bygone Hills contain anything more interesting than rocks and scrub.

Eidlsandres intends to keep it that way.

## Eloesi

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Democratic republic

**Primary Inhabitants**: Eloweir (Sea Elves)

**Population**: 80,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Freedom, tradition, excellence, community

- Eloesi is governed by elected councils—a system the Eloweir brought with them when they first settled these shores, long before human memory. The councils are notoriously deliberative, but their decisions carry weight.
- The culture blends apparent contradictions: sailors who party through the night and fishermen who observe strict protocols about when, where, and how to harvest. Freedom within structure.
- Outsiders often mistake Eloweir casualness for softness. The sea elves are skilled soldiers and masters of poison—their relaxed demeanor comes from confidence, not weakness.
- The Eloweir have lived here longer than anyone can remember. They say their ancestors followed the tides from somewhere else, fleeing a calamity they don't name, and found this coast waiting. Whether that's history or myth, none can say.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Pearls, fish, ships, poisons, dyes, salt

**Primary Imports**: Timber, grain, metals, luxury goods

- Oyster Bay's pearls are legendary—thousands harvested each season, each one valuable, the finest worth small fortunes. The Eloweir guard the bay jealously and have refined pearl cultivation to an art form.
- Fishing and shipping form the economic backbone. Eloweir ships are built to last, designed for the unpredictable coastal waters, and command premium prices.
- Poison-craft is a respected trade here, not a shameful secret. Eloweir alchemists produce venoms for hunting, warfare, and medicine with equal skill.
- Trade flows through Kair to the goblin plains, the United Free Nations, and the wider Middle Sea. Eloesi serves as the gateway between Ofrenia and the southern interior.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- The Eloweir maintain a professional military of exceptional quality. Every soldier trains for years before active duty, and the standards are unforgiving.
- Naval power is paramount. The Eloweir fleet patrols the coast, protects trade routes, and can project force across the region when needed.
- Land forces are smaller but elite—skilled with spear, bow, and the traditional curved blades the Eloweir favor. Many carry poisoned weapons as a matter of course.
- The military serves the councils, not individual commanders. Political ambition among officers is viewed with deep suspicion.

### Geography

**Location**: Northwestern coast, where Ofrenia connects to the southern interior via the plains

**Biomes**: Rocky coastline, temperate forests, rolling hills, sheltered bays

**Named Entities**: Seawatch Mountains, Oyster Bay, Elessi Bay, Urchin Bay

**Rivers & Lakes**: Several rivers flowing from the Seawatch Mountains to the coast

**Adjacent Waters**: The Middle Sea, Elessi Bay, Oyster Bay, Urchin Bay

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Kair

**Cities**: Kair

**Towns**: Issasmir, Brachly, Dematir, Oakdale, Trailsfor, Zessos, Golun, Holckots, Krakersport

**Villages**: Numerous fishing villages along the coast

### Primary Conflicts

**The Goblin Chaos**

The Shiverplains and Jinxz Plains to the east and south are home to Scalawag goblin broods locked in perpetual, chaotic conflict. The goblins occasionally raid Eloweir settlements, more for sport than strategy. The Eloweir respond with punitive expeditions that accomplish little—the goblins simply scatter and regroup. It's an annoyance rather than a threat, but a persistent one.

**The Urchin Bay Question**

Urchin Bay, south of Oyster Bay, is too dangerous to harvest safely. Giant urchins and worse lurk in its waters, and ships that enter rarely return intact. Rumors persist of treasure in the depths—wrecks accumulated over centuries. Every few years, someone organizes an expedition. Most fail.

**Old Talaria's Shadow**

The fallen empire to the southwest radiates strangeness. Wild magic seeps across the border, and creatures that shouldn't exist occasionally wander into Eloweir territory. The councils maintain a standing watch along the Glass Forest border, but containment is difficult when the threat is so unpredictable.

**The Bygone Hills Mystery**

Goblins who wander into the Bygone Hills east of Glidwyn Forest never return. The Eloweir know something lurks there but have never investigated seriously—the hills aren't their territory, and whatever kills goblins efficiently is doing them a favor. Still, some councillors worry about what might emerge if it ever decides to expand.

### History

The Eloweir came to these shores in an age before human record, following currents and tides from somewhere across the sea. They speak of a homeland lost to catastrophe—some say flood, others say war, still others say both—but the details have faded into myth. What remains is the journey: generations of sea elves sailing toward a coast they'd never seen, guided by currents they trusted to lead them right.

They found a good land. Rocky coasts full of sheltered harbors. Forests for timber. Hills for grazing. And Oyster Bay, with its wealth waiting to be harvested. The Eloweir built Kair on a natural harbor, established their councils as they had in the old country, and began the slow work of making this place home.

For most of recorded history, Eloesi has been stable, prosperous, and largely uninvolved in the wars that wrack other nations. The councils prefer trade to conquest, diplomacy to battle. When threats arise—goblin raids, pirates, the occasional ambitious neighbor—the Eloweir respond with precise, professional force, then return to their nets and ships.

The fall of Old Talaria, centuries ago, changed the neighborhood permanently. Where once a powerful magical civilization bordered Eloesi to the southwest, now there's only the Glass Forest and the wild things within it. The Eloweir watched the cataclysm from a safe distance, grateful their ancestors had never trusted Talarian magic. Whatever the wizards attempted, it ended badly for everyone except the fae.

## Emblydium

Tags: state

Populated by Nydor. Located in the northwest tip of the Dragon's Spine Mountains, with the Devil Peaks to its south, the Bleeding Mountains to its north, and the Haunted Hills further north and west. Southwest of Shunda and Dhabisa in Dalizi.

## Enapay

Tags: state

An ancient Dwelyn monarchy occupying the Green Wilds at the edge of the Ishnit Jungles. For as long as the Dwelyn have existed as a people, Enapay has been their home—and for nearly as long, Surrey Mahaila has been their protector. Now the dragon sickens, and the nation faces its first true crisis in generations.

### Geography

Enapay sprawls across the Green Wilds, a transitional zone where the dense Ishnit Jungle gives way to more open woodland. The terrain is still wild—massive trees, thick undergrowth, dangerous fauna—but navigable in ways the deep jungle is not. Rivers cut through the territory, providing trade routes and defensive boundaries.

The capital, **Lise**, sits where the Ishtar River delta meets the edge of the Green Wilds, roughly thirty miles inland from the Gulf of Norag.

### Government

Enapay is ruled by a hereditary queen whose authority derives from three sources: bloodline, martial prowess, and—most importantly—her bond with Surrey Mahaila.

**The Dragonbound Queen**: The current queen, **Teyara Greenmantle**, inherited the throne and the dragon bond from her mother, who inherited it from hers. This matrilineal succession has continued unbroken since before written Dwelyn history. The queen is believed to share some portion of Surrey Mahaila's consciousness—what the dragon knows, the queen can learn, and vice versa.

**Clan Chiefs**: Below the queen, the Dwelyn organize into clans led by chiefs who earn their position through combat and the accumulation of war trophies (the beads that signify Dwelyn influence). Chiefs manage their clan's territory and contribute warriors to the queen's campaigns.

**The Empty Throne Problem**: With Surrey Mahaila ill, Queen Teyara's authority has weakened. The dragon bond still exists but feels muted, distant. Some chiefs whisper that if the dragon dies, so does the queen's legitimacy—and succession by combat might replace succession by blood.

### Culture

The Dwelyn combine elven grace with orcish pragmatism. They value both the beauty of the kill and its efficiency.

**Bead Status**: Social standing is literally worn. The more beads woven into a Dwelyn's hair—crafted from the remains of defeated enemies—the greater their influence. A heavily-beaded warrior can challenge a poorly-beaded chief; a chief with few beads rules only at the sufferance of those with more.

**Jungle Craft**: Despite living at the jungle's edge, the Dwelyn have mastered its ways. Their hunters track prey through dense undergrowth, their warriors use the terrain for ambush and escape, and their shamans brew medicines from jungle plants that outsiders have never catalogued.

**The Dragon Cult**: Surrey Mahaila is not worshipped as a god, but revered as a living ancestor. The Dwelyn believe she chose them in the time before time, and their bond with her is what separates them from mere beasts. Her illness is not just a strategic problem—it's a spiritual crisis.

### The Dragon's Sickness

Surrey Mahaila has grown weak. The emerald dragon, once capable of devastating enemy armies and inspiring terror across the region, now rarely leaves her roost. Her scales have dulled. Her mind, once sharp enough to coordinate with Queen Teyara across miles, feels foggy and slow.

The cause is known but not understood: **the Faesong has been poisoned nearby**.

Faesong—the ambient magical melody of Melera that flows through creation—runs strong through the Green Wilds. Surrey Mahaila, a dragon of darkness, water, and earth, has always been attuned to this harmony. Something has corrupted the local Faesong, and the dragon suffers for it.

What poisoned it? Theories abound:
- Shadow emergence from Ekomorn tainting the magical currents
- Demonic influence spreading from Demonwatch
- Deliberate sabotage by enemies (the Naruaghin? The snake dragons?)
- Natural magical phenomenon that will pass in time
- Something older and stranger, awakened by some unknown trigger

Queen Teyara has sent agents throughout Alaria seeking answers. Healers, druids, fae scholars, anyone who understands Faesong has been invited (or compelled) to examine the dragon. So far, nothing has worked.

### The War

Enapay's expansion into East Naruaghin territory began three generations ago and seemed destined to succeed. With Surrey Mahaila's support, Dwelyn armies pushed deep into the jungle fringe, establishing settlements and claiming the best hunting grounds.

Then the dragon sickened.

The war has shifted. Dwelyn forces fight defensively now, protecting what they've taken rather than seizing more. East Naruaghin raids strike deeper into Dwelyn territory each season. Queen Teyara must balance the need to maintain pressure against the need to conserve forces for when—if—the dragon recovers.

### Economy

Enapay trades in jungle goods: exotic feathers, rare medicines, ironwood, and the products of skilled Dwelyn craftsmanship. Their curved scimitars and bone-and-fur armor fetch high prices from merchants brave enough to reach the Green Wilds.

The nation has no port—the Gulf of Norag coast is too dangerous, and the Naruaghin control the shoreline. Trade flows overland or upriver, making commerce slow but relatively secure.

### Notable Locations

**Lise**: The capital. See separate entry.

**The Dragon's Roost**: Surrey Mahaila's lair, built into a colossal jungle tree near Lise. Once the dragon's presence radiated power; now it radiates illness.

**Ishtar Ford**: The contested border crossing, currently held by Dwelyn forces but constantly threatened.

**The Green Marches**: The newest Dwelyn settlements, carved from jungle claimed in the last century. Most vulnerable to Naruaghin counter-attack.

### Player Hooks

**Cure the Dragon**: The ultimate quest hook. Anyone who could restore Surrey Mahaila would earn the eternal gratitude of the Dwelyn queen—and the enmity of the Naruaghin.

**Faesong Investigation**: What poisoned the local Faesong? Investigating could lead to Ekomorn, Demonwatch, or somewhere else entirely.

**War Profiteering**: Both sides need weapons, information, and deniable assets. There's coin to be made in the middle of a conflict.

**Dragon Succession**: If Surrey Mahaila dies, what happens? Could another dragon be bonded? Would the nation tear itself apart in succession crisis?

## Enavadi

Tags: state, fishing, human

A poor nation of fishermen along the Seacleft Coast, Enavadi is remarkable for two things: the skill of its people on the water, and their absolute disinterest in anyone who isn't one of them. The Enavadi are not hostile to outsiders—merely indifferent. They will trade, they will provide harbor in storms, they will even be polite. But they will never consider you one of them, and they will never explain how they do what they do.

### Government

Enavadi has no king, no council of lords, no formal constitution. The closest thing to a central authority is the **Tide Council**, which meets in Edkovic when the tides are at their highest. This schedule is deliberate: only working fishermen can reliably attend meetings timed to the sea's rhythms. Anyone whose life isn't built around the water finds the schedule impossible, which is exactly the point.

The Tide Council handles matters that affect all Enavadi—disputes between villages, responses to external threats, the rare diplomatic contact with foreign powers. Its members are senior fishermen from each village's **net-councils**, the local bodies that govern daily life. Net-council membership is informal: the respected elders of each fishing family, gathering to settle disputes and coordinate the fleet. No votes are taken. Consensus emerges through discussion, and those who cannot build consensus find their opinions carry less weight over time.

This system appears chaotic to outsiders. In practice, it works because everyone shares the same values, the same rhythms, the same deep knowledge of what matters. Enavadi governance is invisible because it rarely needs to be visible.

### Culture

The Enavadi are human, with the same range of appearance found throughout the Seacleft Coast. What distinguishes them is attitude: a bone-deep certainty that their way of life is correct, complete, and in no need of outside input.

Children are on boats before they can walk. They learn the names of currents and fish before they learn to read—indeed, many Enavadi never learn to read at all, finding the skill unnecessary for a life on the water. Literacy is for record-keepers and merchants; a fisherman needs only to know the sea.

Marriage happens within the community, almost exclusively. An Enavadi who marries an outsider faces no formal sanction, but their spouse will never be fully accepted. The couple's children might be, after a generation or two, if they prove themselves on the water. Bloodlines matter less than belonging.

Religion is private and unnamed. The Enavadi acknowledge no foreign gods. They have their own understanding of the sea—its moods, its hungers, its gifts—but they do not discuss it with outsiders. Offerings are left on certain rocks. Songs are sung at certain tides. No temples are built, no priests are ordained. When a missionary arrived three centuries ago and built a temple in Edkovic, the Enavadi let him. They simply never entered it.

### The Gift of the Waters

Outsiders who visit Enavadi return with stories of impossible luck. Fish that swim into nets unprompted. Storms that split around Enavadi boats. Catches so reliable that failure seems impossible.

Ask an Enavadi fisherman about this, and they'll credit skill. Generations of training, intimate knowledge of local waters, techniques refined over centuries. This is true—the Enavadi are genuinely masterful fishermen, their knowledge accumulated and passed down with religious devotion.

But the Enavadi themselves know it's not the whole truth. Their luck is *too* good. They don't discuss it openly, but among themselves, they acknowledge that something responds to their offerings, their songs, their careful observation of taboos. They call it "respecting the sound."

What they're respecting, exactly, no one living can say with certainty. The arrangement predates memory. The terms come due so rarely that most Enavadi live their whole lives without seeing the cost. The ones who have seen it don't speak of it. The fish keep coming. The storms keep splitting. And the deep waters east of the Floss Islands remain unfished, by tradition older than any record.

### Economy

Fish. Enavadi's entire economy is fish.

The catches from Edko Sound and Prince's Bay feed markets across the Seacleft Coast. Fresh fish goes to nearby ports; preserved fish—salted, smoked, or dried by the specialists in Dijana village—travels further. Enavadi boats work year-round, varying their targets with the seasons, their techniques refined for each species and condition.

The Enavadi do not trade directly with most foreign merchants. Outsiders buy from the Stranger's Quarter in Edkovic, dealing with designated traders who serve as intermediaries. These traders are Enavadi, but ones willing to dirty their hands with foreign contact—respected for their sacrifice, slightly pitied for their burden.

### Relations

**With Linas**: Cold indifference. Linas wants shellfish from the Starfish Isles, not fish from Enavadi waters. Their interests rarely overlap, and when they do—in the northern fishing grounds—the Enavadi consider Linas harvesters clumsy amateurs who mistake money for skill.

**With Mirko**: Official disgust, practical cooperation. Mirko represents everything Enavadi culture rejects—noise, chaos, foreigners, crime. But Dijana village does quiet business with Mirko smugglers, and Enavadi preserved fish travels far through Mirko's distribution networks. The Tide Council pretends not to know. Everyone benefits.

**With Everyone Else**: Enavadi deals with the outside world through its Stranger's Quarter, accepting what trade is necessary and ignoring everything else. Foreign titles impress no one here. Foreign threats are met with polite confusion—why would anyone want to conquer a stretch of poor coastline?—followed by a quiet demonstration of how unpleasant occupation would be for the occupiers. Enavadi has never been conquered. Not because it's strong, but because conquering it would gain nothing worth the trouble.

### Notable Locations

- **Edkovic**: The capital, such as it is. A sprawling fishing port with the Great Docks, the Smoking Halls, and the Stranger's Quarter.
- **Vukaden**: Northern village known for boat-building.
- **Krizan**: Central village known for net-making and maintaining the ancient tide-charts.
- **Dijana**: Southern village known for fish preservation and quiet Mirko connections.
- **Prince's Bay**: The large southern bay, named for either a shipwrecked noble or the local slang for a big catch.
- **Edko Sound**: The sheltered waters where most fishing happens, protected by the Floss Islands.

### Player Hooks

- **The Tide-Charts**: Krizan's ancient records contain more than fishing knowledge. Patterns in the charts hint at something deeper—cycles that match no known astronomical phenomena, annotations in archaic script that speak of "the arrangement." Someone wants to study them. The Enavadi would prefer they didn't.

- **The Empty Temple**: Three centuries abandoned, the temple in Edkovic might still hold traces of whatever the missionary tried to establish. Or perhaps it holds something else now—something that moved in when the building was forgotten.

- **Prince Aldric's Heir**: A Veldoraine noble has rediscovered the old claim to "Prince Aldric of the Enavadi Shore." They want their ancestor's remains returned. They want acknowledgment. They might even want territory. The Enavadi find this amusing, for now.

- **The Deep Taboo**: What lives in the waters east of the Floss Islands? Why do the Enavadi never fish there? Someone foolish, desperate, or curious enough might try to find out.

## Enymu

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Hereditary monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans (majority), Craven (substantial minority)

**Population**: ~350,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**: Honor, hard work, loyalty, simplicity, the old ways

- Enymu is ruled by **King Aldric IV**, the latest in a dynasty that has held the throne for over four hundred years. The Aldric kings are known for two things: their honor and their naivety. They keep their word, treat their subjects fairly, and somehow never quite understand that others don't share these values.
- The population is predominantly human farmers, with a substantial Craven minority. Unlike Nektuna, where Craven often face discrimination, Enymu has a long tradition of treating Craven as equals—a policy that began centuries ago when a Craven saved an Aldric king's life.
- Enymu is the breadbasket of Tarkhon. Its farms feed Nektuna's cities, its grain fills Tarkhetan's warehouses, its wool clothes the empire's soldiers. The Enymese do the work. Nektuna takes the profit. This has always been the arrangement.
- The Enymese are proud of their honesty, their work ethic, and their king. They're also quietly resentful of Nektuna's wealth, Tarkhetan's arrogance, and the sneaking suspicion that they're being exploited. But they're loyal. For now.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Simple (agricultural)

**Primary Exports**: Grain, wool, livestock, timber

**Primary Imports**: Metal goods, luxury items, manufactured products

- Enymu's economy is almost entirely agricultural. Vast farms spread across the central lowlands, producing grain, vegetables, and fodder. Sheep graze the northern hills. Timber comes from the eastern forests (carefully managed—the Enymese remember what happened to the Stone Forest).
- The wealth flows out. Enymu sells cheap and buys dear. Nektuna's merchants pay what they want for Enymu grain, then charge what they want for Enymu's imports. The Enymese know they're being squeezed. They accept it because the alternative is worse.
- There are no leylines in Enymu, no magical resources, no strategic chokepoints. The land is all they have. They work it hard.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Conscription with professional core

- Enymu maintains a respectable army—not as professional as Nektuna's, but larger than its wealth would suggest. The Enymese take defense seriously.
- The core is a professional force of ~5,000 soldiers, supplemented by militia that can raise another 15,000 in emergencies. Every village maintains a trained band. Farmers drill during the slow seasons.
- This military tradition stems from Enymu's position: bordered by the Stone Forest (which must be guarded), the Wurn Mountains (which occasionally produce trouble), and Nektuna (which must be reminded that Enymu is not helpless).
- Enymu also provides conscripts for Ekfusy fortress on Midton Island—a duty the Enymese resent but fulfill.

### Geography

**Location**: Northern Tarkhon, east of Nektuna, south of the Wurn Mountains

**Biomes**: Fertile farmland, rolling hills, managed forests, the Stone Forest (forbidden)

**Climate**: Temperate continental—warm summers, cold winters, reliable rainfall

**Named Features**: The Stone Forest (western border), Wurn Mountains (northern border), the Greenway (main road to Tarkhetan)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Aldrichold

**Major Cities**: None (Aldrichold is barely a city)

**Towns**: Numerous farming communities, none particularly large

### Aldrichold

The capital of Enymu is less a city than a large town that happens to contain the king's residence.

Aldrichold sits at the junction of the Greenway (the main road to Tarkhetan) and the river that waters the central farmlands. Perhaps 15,000 people live here—merchants, craftsmen, administrators, and the royal household.

The palace is modest by Tarkhon standards—a fortified manor house that has been expanded over centuries, comfortable rather than grand. The Aldric kings have never seen the point of ostentation. The money is better spent on roads, granaries, and the army.

Visitors from Nektuna often find Aldrichold quaint. The Enymese find Tarkhetan vulgar. Neither opinion changes anyone's behavior.

### King Aldric IV

The current king is forty-one years old and has ruled for eighteen years. He is, like his ancestors, honorable, hardworking, and dangerously naive.

Aldric genuinely believes that people generally mean what they say. He trusts written agreements. He assumes that if he treats others fairly, they will treat him fairly in return. This makes him an excellent ruler for his own people and a terrible negotiator with Nektuna.

**Appearance**: Tall, weathered, with the calloused hands of someone who still works his own fields occasionally. Brown hair, honest eyes, a face that can't hide what he's thinking.

**Family**: Married to Queen Sera (a minor Enymese noble). Three children: Prince Aldric (the heir, 17), Princess Maren (14), Prince Tomlen (11).

**Governing style**: Aldric rules personally, meeting with subjects, settling disputes, and generally trying to be the king he thinks his ancestors were. His advisors handle the details he finds boring—which unfortunately includes most diplomacy with Tarkhetan.

**Greatest weakness**: He believes King Selron II is his friend. Selron finds this useful.

### The Royal Advisors

**Lord Councillor Beren Thatch** — The king's chief advisor, an elderly man who has served three Aldric kings. Wise, cautious, and quietly terrified of what happens when Aldric IV dies and the untested prince takes over. He handles most negotiations with Tarkhetan and has learned to mistrust everything Nektuna's envoys say.

**Marshal Kira Stoneheart** — Commander of Enymu's forces, a Craven woman who rose through the ranks on pure merit. She's the reason Enymu's military is as good as it is. Fiercely loyal to the Aldric dynasty, which gave her opportunities no Craven would have in Nektuna.

**High Warden Gellert** — Keeper of the Stone Forest. His family has held this position for generations, maintaining the pact that keeps people out. He knows more about the forest than anyone living—and he's deeply worried about recent interest from Tarkhetan scholars.

### The Stone Forest

On the western border between Enymu and Nektuna stands one of Alaria's great mysteries: an entire forest turned to stone.

Every tree, every branch, every leaf has been petrified—transformed into gray stone that preserves perfect detail. Animals caught in the transformation remain frozen mid-step, mid-flight, mid-hunt. The forest is silent, motionless, eternal.

**The Pact**: Entry to the Stone Forest is forbidden by ancient agreement between Enymu and Nektuna. The pact is "long forgotten" in the sense that no one remembers the exact terms or who made it—but everyone knows the rule: stay out.

**The Titan Crystals**: In the roots of the stone trees lie strange formations called titan crystals. These crystals, when touched by sentient beings, ring out at a frequency only titans can hear—a sound that may cause **Hykravones** (slumbering titans buried deep in the earth) to stir.

**The Wardens**: Enymu maintains a force of Stone Wardens who patrol the forest's borders, turning away the curious, the greedy, and the foolish. They don't enter the forest themselves. They just make sure no one else does either.

**Recent Concerns**: Scholars from Tarkhetan have shown increased interest in the Stone Forest. They want to study it, catalog it, perhaps extract samples. High Warden Gellert has refused all requests, citing the pact. Lord Councillor Beren backs him. But the scholars have King Selron's ear, and Selron has been hinting that perhaps the pact is... outdated.

### The Craven of Enymu

Unlike in Nektuna—where Craven are second-class citizens at best—Enymu has a long tradition of equality between humans and Craven.

The tradition began four centuries ago, when a Craven farmer saved King Aldric I from assassins. The king declared that any people who would risk their lives for the crown deserved full citizenship. His descendants have honored this decree.

Today, Craven hold positions throughout Enymese society: farmers, merchants, soldiers, even nobility (the Stoneheart family was ennobled two generations ago). Marshal Kira Stoneheart is the most prominent example, but she's not unique.

This equality makes Enymu unusual in Tarkhon and creates interesting dynamics:
- Craven from Nektuna sometimes emigrate to Enymu for better treatment
- Nektuna's human nobility finds Enymu's policies distasteful
- Enymu's Craven are among the crown's most loyal supporters

### Primary Conflicts

**The Exploitation**

Enymu feeds Tarkhon but sees little of the wealth. Nektuna's merchants set the prices, Nektuna's ships carry the goods, Nektuna's banks collect the profits. The Enymese work, and Nektuna takes.

This has always been true. What's changed is awareness. Younger Enymese are asking questions their parents didn't: Why do we accept these terms? What would happen if we demanded better? The king's advisors worry about where these questions lead.

**The Stone Forest Pressure**

Tarkhetan scholars want access to the Stone Forest. They've written papers about the "scientific value" of studying the petrification effect. They've proposed "carefully controlled expeditions" to extract titan crystals for research.

High Warden Gellert and Lord Councillor Beren have refused, citing the ancient pact. But King Selron has been pressuring King Aldric, suggesting that surely old superstitions shouldn't stand in the way of knowledge. Aldric, who trusts Selron, is wavering.

If someone enters the Stone Forest and touches the titan crystals... the consequences could be catastrophic.

**The Succession**

Prince Aldric is seventeen, untested, and not obviously suited to rule. He has his father's honesty but perhaps not his father's character. Lord Councillor Beren worries that the prince might be easier to manipulate than the king—and that Nektuna knows it.

**The Northern Border**

The Wurn Mountains occasionally produce trouble—bandits, monsters, and sometimes organized raids from the Stone Men who live in the high peaks. Enymu's army handles these threats, but resources are stretched. Every soldier watching the mountains is a soldier not watching the Stone Forest.

### History

**The Founding** (~400 years ago): The Aldric dynasty established Enymu as a distinct state within the growing Tarkhon Empire. They negotiated favorable terms—or what seemed favorable at the time.

**The Craven Decree** (~400 years ago): King Aldric I granted full citizenship to all Craven after one saved his life. This policy has continued ever since.

**The Stone Forest Pact** (date unknown, predates Enymu): At some point, Enymu and Nektuna agreed to forbid entry to the Stone Forest. The exact terms and reasons have been lost. The prohibition remains.

**The Long Peace**: Enymu has never fought a war against Nektuna or any other Tarkhon state. The Aldric kings have always preferred negotiation. Whether this represents wisdom or weakness depends on who you ask.

**The Present**: King Aldric IV maintains the dynasty's traditions of honor, hard work, and loyalty to Tarkhon. But pressures are building—economic exploitation, Stone Forest interest, succession concerns—that may force choices his ancestors never faced.

### The Turning Question

The note in the old records says Enymu "could be persuaded to turn on Nektuna, but only with some difficulty."

What would it take?

- **Proof of betrayal**: If Aldric learned that Selron had been deliberately deceiving him for years, his sense of honor might demand response.
- **Stone Forest violation**: If Nektuna violated the pact and caused a catastrophe, Enymu would never forgive.
- **Craven persecution**: If Tarkhetan tried to impose Nektuna's anti-Craven policies on Enymu, the crown would resist.
- **The right manipulator**: Someone who understood Aldric's honor might be able to twist it against Nektuna—making him believe that loyalty required defiance.

For now, Enymu remains loyal. But loyalty built on naivety is fragile. And someday, Aldric IV will learn what Selron II really thinks of him.

## Eoga

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Elected council

**Primary Inhabitants**: Caerine, dark skinned, beautiful, intimidating

**Population**: 10,000

**Stability**: Very stable

**Core Values**: Peace, isolation, forgiveness, free thought, creativity

- The are well hidden within the jungle, as they don't wish to be found.
- Council members are democratically elected once every 20 years.


### Economy

**Size**: Very small

**Tech**: Very developed

**Primary Exports**: None

**Primary Imports**: None

- The Eoga have been around for millenia and have developed an absurdly close connection with nature, which serves them in all ways. Animals do anything they are asked, their houses are built into the trees, their tools naturally repair themselves, and so on.


### Military

**Size**: Very small

**Quality**: Extremely high

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- A small, elite fighting force exists within Eoga. They are rarely deployed to do anything, but they are both diplomats, fighters, emmisaries, and healers. 
- Training with a bow begins in early childhood and never stops. Nearly everyone of adult age is also a very capable archer.


### Geography

**Cities**: 

**Towns**: 

**Villages**:

### History

Eoga and Vystrilik were once one nation, before Hykravones came and destroyed the world. After the titan destroyed the world, the Eoga broke away from Vystrilik, in shame. They were disappointed that they had not died as well, with the humans and other races of the world. This shame has remained with them this day. Although they are virtually unfindable within the jungle, should a child of Gaiea find them, the Caerine would gladly give them anything they wanted.

## Erasnus

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Feudal, House of Nobles and House of Commerce

**Primary Inhabitants**: Belenstrope (Halflings)

**Population**: 100,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Wealth, prestige, intelligence, vision, entrepreneurship

- Erasnus is jointly ruled by two parliamentary houses—the House of Nobles and the House of Commerce. These consist of the highest-ranking members of society within the two most important areas of Erasnun life.
- Nearly every village, town, and city is home to multiple nobility, as well as their extended family. Practically everyone has at least a cousin who's distantly related to some noble family.
- Belenstrope halflings have transformed commerce into high art. They trade in influence, information, and favors with complexity that makes seasoned diplomats dizzy. Every Belenstrope maintains a mental ledger of social debts tracked across generations.

### Economy

**Size**: Very large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Textiles, trade services, financial instruments

**Primary Imports**: Everything

- Members of the Aldriktch Trade Alliance—and its most profitable member. Erasnus is desperately trying to hold the alliance together as it fractures.
- Home to the best overland merchants in Alaria. Their ability to haggle is unparalleled, their eye for a good deal uncanny.
- Lending, business, entrepreneurship, and traveling merchant are all extremely common professions.
- Super close trading partners with Watar, and strong relationships with all Aldriktch members.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Low

**Enrollment**: Mandatory

- Military enrollment is mandatory, but there are a thousand ways to wiggle out of it—meaning it consists primarily of the poor and less influential.
- Working in the military is seen as a job for idiots who can't turn a profit on their own.
- The population is largely non-violent. If threatened with a sharp stick, most Erasnuns would throw up their hands and hand over their money. Much easier to make money trading than risk getting hurt.

### Geography

**Location**: Central Middle Sea region, bordered by Camaran (east), Ubrik (southeast), Myorna (northeast), Hephake mountains (north), Anarak (west, barely), and the Crags of Geth (west)

**Biomes**: Rolling farmland, river valleys, temperate forests, trade roads everywhere

**Climate**: Temperate—good growing weather, mild seasons

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Erdene

**Cities**: Erdene, Minoz, Adnar, Traduci, Simonsor

**Towns**: Countless market towns and trading posts along the major routes

### Primary Conflicts

**The Alliance Collapse**

The Aldriktch Trade Alliance is fracturing. Camaran is isolating, Ubrik is collapsing under the silvertongue outbreak, Watar and Bestacia are fighting over the strait. Erasnus profits most from the alliance and is desperately trying to hold it together through diplomacy, trade incentives, and sheer nervous energy.

**The Myorna Problem**

Myorna, to the northeast, is a backstabbing mess of elven politics. They were barely let into the Trade Alliance—pushed through by Bestacian diplomats hoping it would deter war. Now Myorna's internal chaos is spilling outward, and Erasnus is caught dealing with unreliable partners and shifting alliances.

**The Novglim Displacement**

In the Novglim Forest (northeast, just south of Myorna), native gnome tribes have been displaced by expanding settlements and logging operations. The situation is uncomfortable—Erasnus benefits from the resources but knows the displacement is wrong. Mostly they try not to think about it.

**The Goblin Posturing**

West of Erasnus, across the Crags of Geth, lies Griselia—a collection of goblin city-states. The goblins are perpetually posturing, threatening invasion, and making dramatic proclamations. They will absolutely never actually invade. Everyone knows this. Trade continues regardless, punctuated by theatrical diplomatic incidents that both sides secretly enjoy.

**Military Resentment**

The military is disrespected, underfunded, and staffed by those who couldn't escape service. But military leadership does control real power, and some officers are tired of being scorned by nobles and merchants who've never held a sword. If things get bad enough, a military coup isn't impossible.

### History

Erasnus grew wealthy by being in the middle of everything. While other nations fought wars, the Belenstrope traded with all sides. While empires rose and fell, Erasnun merchants maintained their routes.

The Aldriktch Trade Alliance was largely Erasnun diplomacy—convincing rival states that open trade benefited everyone (especially Erasnus). For decades, it worked beautifully. The alliance made everyone richer and wars less likely.

Now the alliance is crumbling. Erasnus has no army worth mentioning, no walls that could stop a real invasion, and no plan for a world where trade doesn't solve every problem. The merchants are getting nervous.

## Ethadia

Tags: state, kingdom, shacklands, maritime

The kingdom of clean hands and dirty money. Ethadia occupies the Strymün Isles—a volcanic archipelago in the Gindrik Sea—and has survived for eight centuries by making itself useful to everyone and threatening to no one. Their ships carry Kuzagt salt to markets that would never trade with Bonnetaz directly. Their banks hold deposits from Gorathi merchants and escaped slaves alike. Their diplomats speak every language, follow every custom, and commit to nothing.

This is not neutrality. Neutrality implies principle. Ethadia practices profitable cowardice—and has grown rich doing so.

To the older generation, this is wisdom: survival through accommodation. To the younger generation, increasingly, it is shame.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Aristocratic republic (Crown + Council of Families)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans (majority), mixed maritime populations

**Population**: ~70,000 (50,000 in Regina, 15,000 in Sivirine, 5,000 scattered)

**Stability**: Stable but aging (succession concerns)

**Core Values**: Discretion, commerce, neutrality, pragmatism

#### The Crown and the Council

Power in Ethadia divides between two institutions that spend as much energy fighting each other as governing.

**The Crown** is hereditary within the Aldric dynasty, currently worn by **Queen Morvena III** (age 67, ruling for 31 years). The Crown controls the royal fleet, sets harbor fees, conducts foreign policy, and maintains the appearance of unified rule. In practice, the Crown's power extends only as far as its revenue—and the Families control most of the revenue.

**The Council of Families** represents twelve noble houses, each holding their seat by hereditary right. These families own the merchant houses that generate Ethadia's wealth. They set trade policy, control banking, and check every royal initiative that threatens their interests. Conflict between Crown and Council is constant, bitter, and carefully constrained—neither side wants to break the system that enriches both.

The current queen has maintained balance for three decades through a combination of strategic marriages, selective bribery, and the threat of exposing each Family's dirty secrets. She is aging, her designated heir is young, and several Families smell opportunity.

#### The Twelve Families

Each Family specializes in specific aspects of Ethadia's economy:

- **House Valdren**: Primary handlers of Bonnetaz trade; the Family most implicated in slavery-adjacent commerce
- **House Mircalis**: Gorathi connections; timber, hardwoods, and the ugly business of moving cargo Gorath prefers to keep quiet
- **House Petrani**: Banking; rumored to hold deposits from half the criminals in the Gindrik Sea
- **House Thorne**: Fishing and food distribution; least politically ambitious, most practically essential
- **House Corvallo**: Information brokers; they know things, and they sell that knowledge
- **(Seven others)**: Various specializations in shipping, insurance, luxury goods, and services the Crown prefers not to examine closely

#### The Common Population

Ethadian commoners enjoy practical freedoms unusual in the region. The kingdom needs skilled sailors, competent clerks, and reliable workers—oppressing them would be economically foolish. A capable commoner can accumulate wealth, own property, and live comfortably.

They cannot, however, accumulate power. Political voice remains firmly with Crown and Families. Commoners serve; they do not rule. This arrangement has persisted because prosperity makes it tolerable. How long that tolerance would survive economic hardship remains untested.

### Religion & Culture

#### The Religion of Getting Along

Ethadia observes standard Alarian religious practices without distinctive fervor. Temples to major deities operate in both cities. The population is conventionally pious in the way of people who have more pressing concerns.

One exception: mariners throughout the kingdom venerate **Pelagos**, a local spirit or minor deity associated with safe passage. Before every voyage, sailors pour fish blood into the harbor and toss coins into the sea. Whether Pelagos exists, whether these offerings accomplish anything, is a matter of faith. What matters is that the tradition continues.

#### The Ethadian Character

Ethadians are often described as slippery—not dishonest exactly, but resistant to commitment. They speak in qualifications. They hedge their positions. They maintain relationships with parties who despise each other. This is cultural adaptation, not individual failing. In a kingdom that survives by accommodating everyone, rigidity is death.

The older generation prizes discretion above all. What you know matters less than what you *admit* knowing. A successful Ethadian merchant can sit across the table from a Kuzagt bone-worker and a Free Isles abolitionist in the same day without expressing an opinion that would offend either.

The younger generation finds this exhausting. Some have begun speaking more directly—about Bonnetaz's camps, about Gorath's brutality, about Ethadia's profitable complicity. Their elders consider this dangerous idealism. The young consider it basic decency.

#### Generational Tension

The conflict between accommodation and resistance plays out in families across the kingdom. Parents who built careers on discretion watch their children openly question the moral cost of Ethadian prosperity. Children who grew up comfortable wonder why comfort required such compromise.

This tension has not yet produced organized resistance. It has produced awkward dinner conversations, strained relationships, and a growing sense that something must eventually change. What, when, and how violently remain open questions.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium (regionally significant)

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Transshipped goods (salt, timber, exotic materials), financial services, information

**Primary Imports**: Food (supplement), luxury goods, skilled labor

#### The Transshipment Trade

Ethadia produces little of unique value. Its wealth comes from moving other peoples' goods:

- Kuzagt salt becomes "Ethadian salt" when shipped under Ethadian flags to markets that won't deal with Bonnetaz
- Gorathi timber becomes "Gindrik lumber" through similar alchemy
- Goods of questionable origin pass through Ethadian warehouses and emerge with clean paperwork

The markup on this rebranding is substantial. Buyers pay premium for not knowing where their purchases originated.

#### Banking

The major Families offer financial services with legendary discretion. Deposits are secure. Loans are available. No one asks where the money came from. This policy has made Ethadia the preferred banking center for the Gindrik Sea region—including clients whose money the Families would rather not examine closely.

#### Information

Ethadian merchants travel everywhere, hear everything, and remember what they hear. This information has value, carefully commodified and sold to interested parties. House Corvallo has built an entire enterprise on knowing things others don't—and knowing who would pay to know them.

#### Neutral Ground

Enemies meet in Ethadian territory to negotiate truces, exchange prisoners, or conduct business impossible elsewhere. Ethadia charges for this service: hosting fees, security guarantees, witness services, and the implicit promise of discretion. Some of history's most significant treaties were negotiated in Regina's back rooms.

### Military

**Size**: Small (deliberately limited)

**Quality**: Moderate (naval focus)

**Enrollment**: Professional only

#### The Defensive Fleet

Ethadia maintains approximately forty naval vessels—enough to discourage casual piracy, insufficient to threaten any major power. The fleet's primary purpose is escort duty: protecting Ethadian merchants from the hazards of the Gindrik Sea.

This limitation is intentional. An armed Ethadia would frighten its neighbors and invite the very conflicts it seeks to avoid. The Families prefer spending money on commerce rather than conquest.

#### Land Forces

Minimal. A small professional guard maintains order in the cities. Beyond that, the population is expected to arm itself if circumstances require—though no one expects circumstances to require it. The Strymün Isles' geography makes invasion difficult, and Ethadia relies on diplomatic relationships rather than walls.

#### The Strategic Reality

Ethadia cannot defend itself against a determined major power. It survives because conquering it would damage trade networks that benefit everyone. If that calculation ever changed—if Gorath decided it wanted the islands, or the Kuzagt decided Ethadia had outlived its usefulness—the kingdom would fall.

The Families know this. They ensure it doesn't happen by remaining indispensable.

### Geography

**Location**: Strymün Isles, Gindrik Sea (Shacklands region)

**Biomes**: Temperate volcanic islands, temperate forest

**Named Regions**: Strymün (main island), the Strymün Isles (satellites)

**Adjacent Waters**: Gindrik Sea

The archipelago consists of one major island and numerous smaller satellites:

**Strymün** (main island): Approximately eighty miles long and forty wide, dominated by twin volcanic peaks—Mount Aldris (dormant) and Mount Brevani (venting steam and occasional ash). The Greenwood covers the mid-slopes; the coasts range from rocky cliffs to protected harbors.

**The Strymün Isles**: A dozen inhabited islets plus countless rocks and shoals, scattered northeast of the main island. Most support small fishing communities.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Regina (~50,000)

**Secondary City**: Sivirine (~15,000)

**Scattered Settlements**: Fishing villages, farming communities (~5,000 total)

#### Regina

The capital sprawls across Strymün's southern harbor—a natural bay so perfect for shipping that the city's founding was inevitable. Regina is commerce made physical: warehouses, counting houses, the Merchant's Exchange, and the Aldric Palace watching over it all. The twelve Family compounds form a ring around the palace, each a small fortress competing in architectural display.

#### Sivirine

The second city faces the open Gindrik from the northwest coast. Where Regina is cosmopolitan and commercial, Sivirine is working-class and resentful. The population traces its ancestry to the original founders; they speak older dialects, observe older customs, and maintain older grudges against the capital. The Fisher's Council holds real power here, not the Crown's appointed governor.

### Relations

**Bonnetaz**: Essential trading partner. House Valdren handles most Kuzagt trade, rebranding salt and bone-goods for markets that won't deal with slavers directly. Both parties profit. Neither discusses what makes the arrangement necessary.

**Gorath**: Careful distance. House Mircalis maintains connections for timber and other goods, but Ethadia avoids deeper entanglement. The Gorathi are too aggressive, too militaristic, and too likely to eventually want the Strymün Isles.

**Tamadrez/Yava**: Regular trade through the neutral port. Ethadian merchants are among Yava's most reliable customers.

**The Free Isles**: Complicated. Some Isles welcome Ethadian trade; others view them as collaborators with slavers. Ethadian merchants tread carefully, emphasizing their own non-participation in slavery while avoiding discussion of where their money comes from.

**Trømgar**: Distant but watching. The dwarven kingdom dislikes anyone who profits from Drasnian suffering. Ethadia has not yet crossed whatever line would provoke action—but the dwarves have long memories.

### History

Ethadia was founded approximately eight centuries ago by refugees from a mainland conflict whose details have been deliberately obscured. The founders established Regina as a trading post, built relationships with the Kuzagt (then a minor power), and gradually accumulated wealth sufficient to buy independence.

The kingdom's history since has been remarkably uneventful by regional standards. No great wars. No dramatic conquests. No existential crises. Just centuries of careful accommodation, profitable neutrality, and the steady accumulation of wealth.

This stability is Ethadia's greatest achievement and its greatest vulnerability. The kingdom has never been tested. When Queen Morvena dies—and she is sixty-seven—the succession may provide that test.

### Hooks & Tensions

**The Succession**: Queen Morvena's designated heir is young and untested. Several Families believe they could do better. When the queen dies, the careful balance she maintained may collapse into civil conflict—or worse, outside intervention by powers who prefer a weaker Ethadia.

**The Youth Movement**: Younger Ethadians increasingly reject their parents' accommodation. Some organize secretly, planning to disrupt the slave trade their kingdom enables. Others simply leave, seeking lives less morally compromised. Either trend, if it accelerates, could destabilize the kingdom.

**House Valdren's Secret**: The Family most involved with Bonnetaz trade has recently received unusual requests—and unusual payments. Something is happening in the Kuzagt council, and Valdren knows more than they're sharing.

**The Sivirine Question**: Resentment in the second city has simmered for generations. The current governor maintains peace, but he is a Thorne cousin, not a leader. If Regina's attention wavers during a succession crisis, Sivirine might finally push back.

**Pelagos**: The sailors' spirit has been silent lately. Offerings go unanswered. The superstitious worry something has changed in the Gindrik Sea—something that may require more than fish blood to address.

Inhabited by Kendrel. Member of the Kendrel Kingdoms.

## Free Colonies

Tags: dwarves, state

Started by various Drasnian survivors of the slave ships going from Gorath to Kyagos. Either by faking their deaths, or sinking the ship, they escaped their naval prison, and floated 100 miles south to this small island.

Only a few hundred people, even in the capital Ashk
