Inhabited by Kendrel. Member of the Kendrel Kingdoms.

## Glivornax

Tags: state, orc, westwilds

The most diplomatically sophisticated of the six orc states, Glivornax occupies the northwestern Wildwood between Hedroscobb and Uhionai. The Glivornaxi see themselves as guardians of the entire Tolkarsus watershed, and they fight Tarn for access to Plenjorn Swamp—where the Grand Tolkarsus river's waters possess miraculous healing properties.

### Overview

Glivornax is often called the "civilized" orc state—a term the Glivornaxi neither embrace nor reject. They have actual diplomats, written records (crude but functional), and something approaching consistent law. They're the only orc state that trades openly with the Vogenfeld dwarves, and the only one that has ever successfully negotiated with Tarkhon.

This relative sophistication comes from necessity. Glivornax fights for access to something other powers want: the healing waters of Plenjorn Swamp (controlled by Tarn). Defending their claim against Tarn's spirit warriors while also fighting Hedroscobb over the Yugurbas Dyos marshes requires more than brute strength.

### Government

**Type:** Council of Elders

The Glivornaxi are ruled by a council of the oldest and most scarred warriors—those who have survived decades of combat and proven their wisdom through endurance. Decisions require consensus, which means Glivornax moves slowly but with united purpose.

**Current Council:**
- **Elder Vorsha Swamp-Mother** — Eldest living Glivornaxi, over ninety years old. Her body is more scar tissue than skin. She counsels patience above all.
- **Elder Grukk River-Watcher** — Master of the swamp patrols, responsible for defending the healing waters.
- **Elder Thessik Spear-Broken** — Lost his arm to a Hedroscobbi axe in the Yugurbas Dyos; now leads diplomatic efforts and trade negotiations.

### Culture

**The Glivornaxi** have muddy green skin and dark, solid-colored eyes. They're primarily spear-fighters who've developed defensive combat styles optimized for swamp terrain—phalanx formations, fighting retreats, using the water and mud to negate enemy advantages.

**Core Values:** Defense of the sacred waters, patience, unity, survival through wisdom rather than aggression.

**The Healing Secret:** The Glivornaxi don't advertise the full extent of Plenjorn Swamp's power. Outsiders know the waters have some healing properties; few know that bathing in the swamp can cure even mortal wounds. If the truth spread widely, Glivornax would face invasion from every direction.

### Economy

**Size:** Small
**Tech:** Somewhat Developed (by orc standards)

**Exports:** Swamp herbs, preserved fish, leather goods, limited quantities of healing water (sold at enormous prices to trusted buyers)

**Imports:** Metal weapons and tools (primarily from Vogenfeld), grain, cloth

The Glivornaxi are primarily subsistence-focused—fishing, hunting, gathering swamp plants. But the healing water trade provides enough wealth to import the metal goods they can't produce themselves.

### Military

**Size:** Medium
**Quality:** High (defensive specialists)
**Enrollment:** Universal (all adults train)

The Glivornaxi military is built for defense. Their warriors train in phalanx formations using long spears, optimized for swamp terrain where they can control chokepoints and negate numerical disadvantage.

**Tactics:** The Glivornaxi never fight on open ground if they can avoid it. They retreat into the wetlands, where their knowledge of paths, depths, and dangers gives them enormous advantage. Pursuing armies find themselves mired in mud, picked off by spear-thrusts from concealment, or simply lost in the endless swamps.

**The Watershed Patrols:** Elite warriors who monitor the borders constantly, alert for Hedroscobbi algae-gatherers in Yugurbas Dyos or Tarni incursions into contested swampland.

### Geography

**Location:** Northwestern Wildwood, between Cykravikus Lake and the Yugurbas Dyos marshes

**Biomes:** Temperate forest transitioning to wetland; swamps and marshes along the eastern border

**Rivers & Lakes:** The upper Tolkarsus watershed; tributaries feed into Tarn's territory downstream

**Borders:**
- North: Uhionai territory (Ryunimir Forest)
- East: Tarn (hostile; the Water War over Plenjorn Swamp access)
- South: Hedroscobb (hostile; the War of the Marshes over Yugurbas Dyos)
- Southwest: Yugurbas Dyos (contested marshlands with Hedroscobb)

### Relations

**Hedroscobb:** Open warfare. The Hedroscobbi need swamp algae from Yugurbas Dyos to forge their corrosive armor. The Glivornaxi see themselves as guardians of all the Tolkarsus watershed. This War of the Marshes has continued for over a century—neither side can afford to lose.

**Tarn:** Open hostility. Tarn controls Plenjorn Swamp and its healing waters; Glivornax fights for access. The Tarni spirit weapons are terrifying—they attack souls directly—but the Glivornaxi are tenacious defenders. Neither side can win outright.

**Uhionai:** Respectful distance. The tree-orcs understand guardianship of sacred places. Occasional diplomatic contact; no conflicts.

**Grieb:** Minimal contact. The lakemen's hill territory is far from Glivornax's concerns, and fighting aquatic orcs in their home waters isn't worth it anyway.

**Shazuihn:** No direct contact. Tarn lies between Glivornax and the boar riders.

**Vogenfeld:** Trade partners (through difficult routes that skirt Hedroscobb). The dwarves provide metal; Glivornax provides information about orc movements. Both sides benefit, though neither fully trusts the other.

**The Orc Moot:** Glivornax participates but rarely leads. They prefer the status quo—endless low-level conflict that keeps other states too busy to threaten the swamp.

### Conflicts

**The War of the Marshes:** The southern conflict. Hedroscobb needs algae from Yugurbas Dyos to forge their signature armor. Glivornax considers all the watershed sacred. Armed expeditions clash every algae-harvesting season. Neither side can afford to lose—for Hedroscobb, it's their military advantage; for Glivornax, it's their identity as guardians.

**The Water War:** The eastern conflict. Tarn controls Plenjorn Swamp; Glivornax fights for access to the healing waters. Both states know that whoever controls the swamp controls the region. The Tarni fight with spirit weapons that damage souls; the Glivornaxi fight with terrain knowledge and defensive formations. Neither can win outright.

**Two-Front War:** Glivornax is the only orc state fighting on two fronts simultaneously. The strain shows. They can't commit fully to either conflict without leaving the other border exposed. This strategic weakness is balanced only by the fact that Hedroscobb and Tarn are also too busy with their own problems to exploit it.

### Player Hooks

- Seek healing in Plenjorn Swamp (requires getting through both Glivornaxi and Tarni territory)
- Negotiate between Glivornax and Tarn over swamp access
- Defend a Glivornaxi patrol against Hedroscobbi Gatherers in Yugurbas Dyos
- Escort a diplomatic mission to Vogenfeld (navigating around Hedroscobb)
- Investigate why the healing waters are said to be weakening

## Glorin

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Clan-based confederation

**Primary Inhabitants**: Glorindian (Gold Eyes Dwarves)

**Population**: 15,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**: Secrecy, worthiness, clan loyalty, the earth's gifts

- Glorin is governed by a loose confederation of mining clans, each controlling different sections of the vast underground workings. Clan elders meet to resolve disputes and coordinate when necessary, but mostly each clan manages its own affairs.
- The Glorindians are secretive by nature and by necessity. Their golden eyes can sense precious metals through solid stone—a gift that has made them targets of exploitation throughout history. They've learned to keep quiet about their wealth.
- They believe gold calls to those worthy of finding it, and that hoarding beyond one's needs brings misfortune. Wealth flows through the community rather than pooling in individual hands.

### Economy

**Size**: Large (secretly massive)

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Gold, precious metals (in controlled quantities)

**Primary Imports**: Food, textiles, surface goods

- Glorin sits atop one of the richest gold deposits in Alaria. The Glorindians have mined it for generations, carving vast underground chasms in pursuit of precious veins.
- The true scale of their wealth is unknown to outsiders. The Glorindians deliberately limit their visible exports to avoid attracting attention. What the world sees is a modest mining operation; what exists below is staggering.
- Trade with Kazül is steady but unremarkable. The Glorindians prefer it that way.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (clan obligation)

- Each clan maintains its own warriors, primarily for defending the mines. The Glorindians have no interest in conquest—they just want to be left alone.
- The mountains and tunnels are their best defense. Invaders who don't know the passages get lost, trapped, or worse.

### Geography

**Location**: Between Thespia (north) and Kazül (south), centered on the Whistle-Stone Mountains

**Biomes**: Mountain peaks, deep valleys, vast underground complexes

**Climate**: Temperate mountain—cool summers, cold winters

**Named Features**: Whistle-Stone Mountains (named for the eerie sound of wind through the peaks)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Gamgara (surface settlement above the main mine complex)

**Cities**: Gamgara

**Towns**: Matabono, Zubaydah, Taufig

### The Underground

The surface settlements are just the tip. Below lies a network of mine shafts, tunnels, and vast underground chasms where the Glorindians have extracted gold for centuries. Some chambers are so large they have their own weather. The deepest workings are clan secrets, known only to those who labor there.

The wealth down there is almost incomprehensible. The Glorindians have mined tons upon tons of gold—and they're still finding more. Their auric sight means they never dig blind; they always know where the veins run.

### The Whistle-Stone Mountains

The mountain range that defines Glorin. The peaks are riddled with natural caverns and tunnels that create an eerie whistling sound when wind passes through. Outsiders find it unsettling; the Glorindians consider it the voice of the mountains.

### Primary Conflicts

**Staying Hidden**

The Glorindians' greatest challenge is remaining unremarkable. If the outside world understood how much gold lies beneath these mountains, every kingdom within marching distance would find a reason to invade. So they export modest amounts, live modestly on the surface, and keep the vast underground wealth secret.

**Morgnor's Interest**

The dragon Morgnor, King of the Mountains to the northwest, knows about Glorin. He knows about the gold. He hasn't moved yet—perhaps he's patient, perhaps he's waiting for the right moment, perhaps he's dealing with other matters first. But the Glorindians are aware that a dragon's eye has turned their way.

When Morgnor decides to act, Glorin's secrecy won't save them. Their small military won't stop a dragon. They're quietly trying to figure out what to do about this—but options are limited when you can't ask for help without revealing what you're protecting.

### History

The Glorindians found these mountains generations ago, drawn by the call of gold that only they could hear. They dug deep, found riches beyond imagining, and learned to keep quiet about it. History taught them that their gift made them valuable—and being valuable to the powerful is dangerous.

So they built modest surface towns, traded modest amounts of gold, and let the world forget about them. It worked. Glorin became a footnote, a small mining state between more interesting neighbors.

Now Morgnor threatens that peace. The dragon has noticed what the world has not. The Glorindians face a choice they've spent centuries avoiding: reveal their wealth to seek allies, or face the dragon alone.

## Gondurak

Tags: state, fengruk, dragons-spine-coast, forges

A dwarven kingdom carved into the Bellowing Mountains at the far southern reach of the Dragon's Spine. Gondurak is home to the Fengruk—the Forgewarden dwarves who inherited the titan-built forges and have spent millennia mastering their use. The kingdom is cold, isolated, and defined by three things: fire, metal, and an ancient debt they believe they still owe.

Roughly forty thousand Fengruk live within Gondurak's borders, concentrated in three mountain cities and the fortress of Azanfrain. The population has never fully recovered from the collapse of the titan-era support system, and the Fengruk have adjusted their ambitions accordingly. They don't seek expansion or conquest. They seek perfection—in metalwork, in craft, in the endless refinement of techniques their ancestors learned from beings far greater than themselves.

### The Three Families

Gondurak's government descends from King Gondur, a Fengruk leader who united the scattered forge-clans approximately four thousand years ago. Upon his death, his three children—Sildraz, Morgrun, and Durkarn—each claimed one of the great forges, establishing the tripartite structure that persists today.

The families rule their respective cities with near-absolute authority, while a **Council of Embers** handles kingdom-wide matters. The Council meets in Sildraz (the capital by tradition and population), with each family sending three representatives. Decisions require consensus, which makes the Council slow but stable. Major disagreements are rare; the families learned long ago that civil conflict serves no one when existential threats lurk to the south.

The current family heads:
- **Theldris Sildraz** (female, 340 years) — Cautious, traditionalist, obsessed with maintaining the World Forge's reputation
- **Kornath Durkarn** (male, 285 years) — Ambitious, experimental, pushing dragon-bone techniques into new applications
- **Yeva Morgrun** (female, 310 years) — Secretive, scholarly, rumored to communicate with entities through Malstaric ash rituals

Succession within each family follows complex rules involving craft-testing, Council approval, and demonstrations of leadership during the winter watch rotations. Political marriages between families are common but don't merge the lines—children belong to their mother's family.

### Geography and Climate

The Bellowing Mountains occupy the southeastern Dragon's Spine, rising between the Hills of Sarrow to the north and the Pale Peaks to the south. The climate is polar—winter darkness lasts for months, summer barely reaches freezing at the lower elevations, and the upper peaks are permanently snow-covered.

The Fengruk live almost entirely underground, their cities warmed by forge-heat and geothermal vents. Surface structures are limited to watchtowers, gate-houses, and the fortress of Azanfrain. Visitors expecting impressive dwarven architecture on display find only ice-crusted rock and carefully hidden doorways; the real architecture is inside, where the heat allows it.

The mountains are volcanic in origin, providing both the heat that powers the forges and the exotic minerals that make Fengruk metalwork unique. The titans chose this location deliberately. The Fengruk have simply maintained what their predecessors built.

### The Titan Legacy

The Fengruk didn't build the great forges—they inherited them. During the Second Eon, titans walked these mountains and constructed facilities capable of working metal at scales no mortal race could achieve. The World Forge in Sildraz was designed to cast pieces the size of buildings. The titans used it to create weapons, armor, and devices whose purposes the Fengruk can only guess at.

When the titans fell—including Vorukar, who died fighting something from the Pale Peaks—the Fengruk were left with infrastructure they barely understood and a support population they couldn't feed. The famine that followed devastated the Hills of Sarrow and reduced the Fengruk themselves to a fraction of their former numbers.

The survivors made two decisions that define Gondurak to this day. First, they would master the forges, learning every technique the titans had used and developing new ones suited to dwarven scale. Second, they would remember what happened in the Hills of Sarrow—the people who died because the Fengruk couldn't save them.

This combination of inherited greatness and inherited guilt shapes Fengruk culture. They are master craftsmen who believe they're still learning. They are isolationists who maintain a frontier fortress because they remember what happens when threats from the south go unwatched.

### Economy

Gondurak exports metalwork—specifically, metalwork of qualities no other forge can match. The three cities specialize in different techniques, and the combination covers virtually any commission a buyer might imagine.

**Sildraz** produces large-scale work: ship anchors, fortress gates, siege engine components, architectural metalwork. The World Forge can cast pieces that would require assembly anywhere else, and the resulting structural integrity is unmatched.

**Durkarn** produces dragon-enhanced items: weapons that hold edges longer, armor that resists elemental damage, jewelry with subtle magical properties. The dragon graveyard beneath the city provides materials that have been absorbing draconic essence for millennia.

**Morgrundar** produces shadow-touched work: items with connections to Malstaris, blades that drink light, armor that muffles sound beyond physical explanation. The market for such items is smaller but willing to pay extraordinary prices.

Trade flows primarily through the eastern passes to the Dalizi lowlands, with a secondary route to Sweivyon in the Silver Slopes. Gondurak imports food (the mountains produce almost nothing edible), timber, textiles, and raw materials the local geology doesn't provide. The trade balance consistently favors Gondurak; their exports are worth more than their imports by a significant margin.

The kingdom maintains no currency of its own. Trade operates on barter for small transactions and letters of credit (often backed by Sweivyon banks) for larger ones. Fengruk craftsmen are notoriously difficult to hire for outside work—most refuse to leave the forges for any price.

### Military

Gondurak's military exists for one purpose: defending against threats from the Pale Peaks. The kingdom maintains no standing army in the conventional sense, but every adult Fengruk trains with weapons and serves rotating duty at Azanfrain. The total military-capable population is roughly eight thousand, though mobilizing all of them would halt the forges.

The **Frostwatch** is the professional core—approximately six hundred Fengruk who serve full-time at Azanfrain and patrol the southern approaches. They specialize in fighting frostwalkers and ice wyrms, using equipment developed specifically for these threats: vibration-dampened boots, thermal lances, harpoons designed to anchor in ice-wyrm hide.

In a full emergency—a major incursion from the Pale Peaks—the Frostwatch coordinates a general mobilization. Every city maintains armories with equipment for its population, and every Fengruk knows where to report and what to do. The system has been tested three times in recorded history. It worked each time, though the casualties were significant.

Gondurak has no navy, no cavalry, and no interest in projecting power beyond its borders. The military exists to hold the line. Everything else is someone else's problem.

### Culture

Fengruk culture centers on craft, memory, and the forge-philosophy that treats metalworking as a model for all of life.

**Craft** is identity. Every Fengruk learns a trade—usually related to metalwork, but not always—and advancement comes through demonstrated skill. Social status correlates with craft mastery rather than wealth or birth. Even the ruling families maintain their positions partly through proving they can work the forges their ancestors claimed.

**Memory** is obligation. The Fengruk maintain extensive records—stonescripts carved into the mountains themselves—documenting their history, their techniques, and their failures. The famine in the Hills of Sarrow features prominently; every Fengruk child learns what happened to those who depended on their ancestors. The lesson is that power carries responsibility, and failure to protect the vulnerable is unforgivable.

**Forge-philosophy** provides the conceptual framework. Just as raw metal must be heated, hammered, and tempered to become useful, so too must individuals be shaped by hardship and discipline. Comfort is suspect. Ease is weakness. The path to excellence runs through struggle, and those who avoid struggle remain forever untempered.

Religious practice is minimal. The Fengruk acknowledge the daemons but don't emphasize worship. Their closest thing to a patron is Azus, the primordial of law—not because they worship him, but because they respect what he represents: order, structure, and the principle that things should work the way they're supposed to work.

### Relations

**Sweivyon** is Gondurak's primary trading partner. The Silver Slopes provide raw metal that the Fengruk transform into finished goods, and the economic relationship benefits both parties. Cultural relations are cooler—the Fengruk consider Sweivyon's wealth-obsessed culture shallow, while Sweivyon considers Fengruk traditionalism stifling.

**The Dalizi Confederation** to the north provides food, timber, and access to broader trade networks. Relations are practical rather than warm; the Dalizi find the Fengruk grim, and the Fengruk find the Dalizi chaotic.

**Kanzekill** and the western Dragon's Spine are deliberately ignored. The Fengruk know the dragon exists; they don't interact with her territory, don't discuss Mountainveil, and don't offer opinions on the surface kingdoms' accommodations with their predicament. Gondurak's position is that Kanzekill is not their problem unless she makes herself their problem.

**The Pale Peaks** represent the eternal enemy—not a political rival but an existential threat. Whatever lives in the south has been probing north for as long as the Fengruk have records. The Frostwatch exists because the alternative is eventual annihilation.

### The Forge Cities

#### Sildraz

Population approximately 15,000. The capital, built around Sildraz's Crown—the central peak of the Bellowing Mountains. The World Forge occupies the mountain's heart, a titan-built facility capable of operations no other forge can match.

The city spirals inward and downward from multiple surface gates, with residential quarters in the outer rings and the forge complex at the center. Temperature increases as one descends; the deepest levels are uncomfortably warm even for Fengruk. The forge itself requires teams of dozens to operate, with specialized roles for every aspect of the process.

Sildraz hosts the Council of Embers and serves as the kingdom's diplomatic face. What few foreign visitors Gondurak receives usually see only Sildraz—and only its outer levels.

#### Durkarn

Population approximately 10,000. The northwestern city, built on an ancient dragon graveyard that predates Fengruk settlement. The bones have been absorbing trace magic for longer than anyone can calculate, and the forges here specialize in incorporating this material into metalwork.

Dragon-bone crafting is difficult and dangerous. The material resists normal working techniques; it must be coaxed rather than forced. Durkarn's smiths have developed specialized methods over centuries, and they guard these techniques jealously. A Durkarn-forged dragon-bone blade can fetch prices that would buy a small estate elsewhere.

The city's atmosphere is slightly morbid—bones are everywhere, from structural supports to decorative elements. Visitors either find it fascinating or deeply uncomfortable.

#### Morgrundar

Population approximately 8,000. The northeastern city, built on massive deposits of Malstaric ash—material that fell from the shadow plane during some ancient catastrophe and has been slowly contaminating the local geology ever since.

Working with Malstaric ash requires precautions. Prolonged exposure causes psychological effects; the forges maintain strict rotation schedules to limit individual contact time. The products are valuable precisely because they're dangerous to make—items with subtle connections to Malstaris, blades that seem to drink light, armor that muffles sound beyond physical explanation.

Morgrundar has a reputation for secrecy even by Fengruk standards. The Morgrun family controls access tightly, and rumors persist about experiments conducted in the deep levels. The Council of Embers officially monitors Morgrundar's activities; how effective this monitoring is remains unclear.

### Azanfrain

The fortress at the southern edge of Fengruk territory, built into a spur of rock overlooking the Shrapnel Strait. Azanfrain exists for one purpose: watching the Pale Peaks and responding when things cross north.

See the [Azanfrain](../../atlas_of_alaria/dragons_spine_coast/Azanfrain.md) entry for full details.

### Hooks

**The Deep Sounds**: Something in the sealed lower levels of the Bellowing Mountains has begun making noise—rhythmic pulses that don't match the forge patterns. The Council wants to know what it is without opening the seals. They're hiring experts who can investigate without breaking containment.

**The Morgrundar Question**: A Morgrundar smith has defected to Sildraz, claiming the family is communicating with entities through the Malstaric ash—not just using the material, but establishing actual contact. The Council is paralyzed by the political implications of investigating.

**The Ice Bridge**: An unusually cold winter has frozen the Shrapnel Strait earlier and more completely than anyone remembers. The Frostwatch reports unprecedented movement on the Pale Peaks slopes. Something is massing for a crossing.

**The Vorukar Expedition**: A scholar believes they've found evidence of the titan's tomb beneath the Shrapnel Strait—and evidence that he wasn't entirely killed, merely contained. Gondurak's official position is that some things should stay buried. Not everyone agrees.

**The Lost Technique**: A stonescript fragment suggests the titans taught the Fengruk something they've since forgotten—a technique that could revolutionize forge-work or, depending on interpretation, destroy it. Multiple factions want the fragment, and its current owner is playing them against each other.

## Gorath

Tags: state, empire, shacklands

The jungle empire. Six hundred miles of canopy, three great rivers, and a million souls bound by blood, glory, and the lash. Gorath is the largest state on Alaria by territory and the most aggressive by temperament—a conquest machine that has spent three centuries swallowing its neighbors and shows no sign of stopping. The Gorathi believe the gods placed them in the most hostile environment on the continent as a trial, and every acre wrested from the jungle proves their worthiness to rule.

To outsiders, Gorath is terrifying: a militaristic slave-state that treats war as sacrament and weakness as sin. To the Gorathi themselves, they are simply the only people brave enough to do what must be done. The jungle would consume civilization if left unchecked. Someone must tame it. Someone must rule.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Elected military dictatorship

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans, Shapers, Skaag

**Population**: 1,000,000+

**Stability**: Stable (internally); perpetually at war (externally)

**Core Values**: Glory, discipline, duty, conquest, strength, assimilation

#### The Empirial Council

Gorath's emperors are not born—they are chosen. The Empirial Council, a body of sixty patricians drawn from the wealthiest families and most decorated military veterans, convenes upon an emperor's death to select a successor. By law, candidates must be generals who have commanded at least one victorious campaign. In practice, the selection process involves weeks of political maneuvering, bribery, and occasionally assassination.

Emperors serve for life, but "life" in Gorath is often shorter than one might hope. Of the last twelve emperors, only three died of natural causes. The rest fell to war, poison, or "hunting accidents." This brutal turnover keeps the empire vital—stagnant rulers don't last long.

The current emperor, **Veramus the Unyielding**, has held power for seventeen years, the longest reign in a century. His survival is attributed to two factors: genuine military brilliance, and a network of Shaper spies so extensive that no conspiracy forms without his knowledge.

#### The Assimilation Doctrine

When Gorath conquers a people, it absorbs them. Defeated armies become auxiliary legions. Conquered nobles become provincial administrators (if they cooperate) or slaves (if they don't). Within two generations, the grandchildren of the conquered speak Gorathi, worship Gorathi gods, and fight in Gorathi legions. The empire grows not just in territory but in population and military strength with every victory.

This doctrine makes Gorath uniquely dangerous. Most empires weaken as they expand—supply lines stretch, garrisons thin, local resistance festers. Gorath gets *stronger* as it expands, feeding on the armies it defeats.

#### The Three Peoples

**Humans** form the majority and dominate the upper ranks of society. Gorathi humans tend toward darker complexions and stocky builds—generations of jungle living have bred a people resistant to disease and heat. They form the core of the legions and hold most political power.

**Shapers** occupy a strange position: simultaneously invaluable and deeply mistrusted. Their silver eyes mark them as shapeshifters capable of perfectly mimicking any humanoid form, making them peerless spies and infiltrators. The Imperial Intelligence operates almost entirely through Shaper agents. But no Shaper has ever been permitted to serve on the Empirial Council, and they are forbidden from owning land. The empire uses them ruthlessly while keeping them politically powerless.

**Skaag** dwell in the undercities—the vast tunnel networks beneath Gorathi cities. The rat-folk maintain their own parallel society below ground, emerging to trade, labor, and occasionally serve as scouts in the endless jungle campaigns. Most Gorathi barely acknowledge their existence, but the Skaag know secrets: forgotten tunnels, buried treasures, and which noble families have skeletons hidden in their cellars.

### Religion & Culture

#### The Eternal March

The Gorathi faith has no temples. It has monuments.

At the center of every Gorathi city stands a **Pillar of Conquest**—a towering obelisk covered in carved names. Every soldier who dies in victory has their name added. Every acre claimed from the jungle, every enemy city burned, every campaign concluded successfully earns new inscriptions. The pillars grow taller with each generation. Azantir's central pillar is over two hundred feet high, maintained by teams of stone-carvers who work year-round.

The gods, in Gorathi belief, do not want worship. They want *proof*. They placed humanity in a jungle that breeds disease, spawns monsters, and swallows the weak. To survive is to prove worth. To conquer is to prove favor. The Eternal March is not a metaphor—it is the literal, endless expansion of Gorathi civilization against everything that would destroy it.

This theology has convenient implications. Slavery is natural—the weak serve the strong, as the gods intended. Conquest is holy—spreading Gorathi civilization is divine work. Failure is sin—a general who loses a campaign has demonstrated that the gods have withdrawn their favor.

#### Blood and Names

Gorathi mark their achievements on their bodies. A soldier's first kill earns a small tattoo on the forearm. Promotions, victories, and honors accumulate as ink across skin until veteran centurions are covered in elaborate patterns telling their entire military history. To meet a heavily tattooed Gorathi is to meet someone who has seen much death—and survived.

Names follow a similar logic. A Gorathi is born with a family name, earns a personal name at majority, and may be granted additional names for great deeds. Emperor Veramus the Unyielding was born simply "Veramus Corvallo." He earned "the Unyielding" after holding the Esteves Pass against a giant assault for forty-three days without reinforcement.

#### Festival of Chains

Held at midsummer, the Festival of Chains commemorates Gorath's first great slave raid on Tamadrez three centuries ago. For three days, the empire celebrates with feasting, gladiatorial combat between slaves, and ceremonial auctions where wealthy families purchase the year's finest captives. The festival is deliberately grotesque to outside observers—the Gorathi see it as honest acknowledgment of what their prosperity costs.

### Economy

**Size**: Colossal—the largest on Alaria

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Exotic hardwoods, tropical dyes, rare alchemical ingredients, slaves

**Primary Imports**: Enslaved peoples (primarily Drasnian dwarves), metals, finished goods

#### The Jungle's Bounty

The Jungles of Titania contain resources found nowhere else: **bloodwood** that never rots and takes dye perfectly, **witchbark** used in high-end alchemy, **azure orchids** whose essence is worth its weight in gold to perfumers and poisoners alike. Gorathi merchants have made fortunes shipping these goods north through the Divinity Passage.

But the true engine of Gorathi wealth is labor. The jungle demands workers: loggers, farmers, miners, builders. Free citizens serve in the legions—they don't clear brush. That work falls to the enslaved.

#### The Tamadrez Pipeline

The Drasnian dwarves of Tamadrez have become Gorath's primary labor source. Three centuries ago, Gorath occupied the southern coast of Pesalolo by force, establishing the **Slaver's Coast**—a chain of ports for collecting and shipping human cargo. The northern settlements survived because they weren't worth conquering: too mountainous, too poor, and Gorath's military was already stretched thin. The empire's terms are simple: Gorath takes what it wants from the south; the north stays out of the way. Any interference—such as harboring escaped slaves—results in fire from the sky. One northern town learned this lesson permanently.

The slaves flow north through the **Divinity Passage**, Gorath's great maritime highway. At any given time, dozens of slave ships work this route. The passage's name is bitterly ironic to those in chains.

#### The Domestic Economy

River cities along the Lucenia, Droaz, and Torga handle most internal trade. The jungle is too dense for overland commerce at scale—everything moves by water. This makes river control politically crucial. The families that control the docks control the wealth.

### Military

**Size**: Colossal

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Mandatory service, volunteer career path

#### The Legions

Every Gorathi citizen serves two years in the legions starting at age sixteen. Most return to civilian life afterward; the dedicated remain as career soldiers. This system creates an enormous trained reserve while maintaining a professional core.

The legions are organized simply:

- **Legion** (5,000 soldiers): The primary strategic unit, each named and bearing distinct colors. Famous legions include the **Jade Serpents** (jungle specialists), the **Iron Tide** (siege warfare), and the **Pillar Guard** (capital defense).
- **Cohort** (500 soldiers): Tactical units within legions, each led by a **Tribune**.
- **Century** (50 soldiers): The basic fighting unit, led by a **Centurion**.

#### Auxiliaries

Conquered peoples serve in **auxiliary cohorts**—units attached to legions but not part of them. Auxiliaries receive lower pay, worse equipment, and more dangerous assignments. However, twenty years of auxiliary service earns full citizenship for a soldier and their descendants. This creates powerful loyalty among the conquered: serve Gorath well, and your grandchildren will be Gorathi.

#### The Path to Power

Military success is the only path to political power in Gorath. The progression runs: soldier → centurion → tribune → commander → general → candidate for emperor. A man born to nothing can become emperor if he wins enough battles. This meritocratic brutality keeps the military sharp—advancement requires proven competence, not family connections.

#### Current Campaigns

Gorath fights on four fronts simultaneously:

- **The Nashua Front** (northeast): A bloody stalemate against the Ix'Vasyla lizardfolk. The jungle-to-jungle warfare suits neither side, and the conflict has ground on for decades.
- **The Giant March** (northwest): A slow push into the Thunder Plains, where hill giants and their kin resist with frustrating effectiveness.
- **The Moon Wilds** (west): An attempted expansion into Gorath's most dangerous interior region. The Vexlings have made this campaign a nightmare.
- **Maintaining Tamadrez** (south): Constant patrol and enforcement along the Slaver's Coast.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Rimihuica, the Shacklands

**Biomes**: Tropical rainforest (dominant), hill country, river valleys

**Named Regions**: Jungles of Titania, Moon Wilds, Poison Hills, Purlin Hills, Bata Nujo Hills, Deserted Hills

**Rivers**: Lucenia River, Droaz River, Torga River, Myublin River

**Adjacent Waters**: Divinity Passage, Sea of Selegos

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Azantir

**Major Cities**: Esteves, Carvalhais, Pereiro, Ageito, Pombares, Velorasa, Bougado, Ranholas

**Regional Centers**: Fresta, Recarci (Poison Hills)

#### City Character

Gorathi cities rise from the jungle like stone fists. Architecture favors massive stepped pyramids, broad plazas, and walls thick enough to resist both siege and jungle alike. Every city maintains its Pillar of Conquest, military barracks, and slave markets. The wealthy live in upper levels; the poor crowd lower; the Skaag burrow beneath.

**Azantir**, the capital, sprawls where the Lucenia River empties into the Divinity Passage. It is the largest city in the Shacklands, a maze of pyramids, canals, and markets where anything can be bought. The Emperor's Palace crowns the city's highest pyramid, looking down on a million subjects.

**Esteves** guards the northern frontier, a fortress-city where the Lucenia emerges from the Myjornis Mountains. Soldiers outnumber civilians. The city exists to project power into the passes beyond.

**Carvalhais** dominates the upper Droaz, a logging and lumber center that strips the surrounding jungle for export. The city smells of sawdust and industry.

**Ageito** anchors the southeast, where the Torga River meets the sea. Newer than the western cities, Ageito grows rapidly as Gorath pushes toward Nashua.

### Relations

**Nashua**: Active war. The Ix'Vasyla lizardfolk have proven impossible to subjugate, and the conflict has become a bleeding wound for both sides.

**Tamadrez**: Occupation and extraction. Gorath controls the southern coast; the north survives by staying out of the way. This arrangement exists only as long as it benefits Gorath—and everyone knows it.

**Trømgar**: Cold war turning hot. The dwarven kingdom objects to Gorath's treatment of their distant Drasnian kin. Skirmishes increase yearly.

**The Giants**: Active war. The Thunder Plains tribes resist Gorathi expansion with brutal effectiveness.

### History

The Gorathi tell their history in conquests.

**The Founding (circa 700 years ago)**: Refugees from a forgotten northern war settled the Lucenia River valley. They found hostile jungle, hostile natives, and no mercy. Those who survived became the first Gorathi.

**The First March (circa 600 years ago)**: Under the legendary Emperor Talhas the Conqueror, Gorath unified the scattered river settlements and began expanding into the Jungles of Titania. This campaign established the Eternal March as official theology.

**The Tamadrez Contact (circa 300 years ago)**: Gorathi explorers discovered Pesalolo and the Drasnian dwarves. Within a generation, the slave trade had transformed Gorath's economy.

**The Present Day**: Emperor Veramus oversees an empire stretched across four fronts, bloated with conquest, and hungry for more. How long the Eternal March can continue is a question the Gorathi refuse to ask.

### Hooks & Tensions

**The Shaper Conspiracy**: Rumors suggest Shapers have infiltrated the Empirial Council despite the prohibition. If true, who are they, and what do they want?

**The Moon Wilds Mystery**: Something in the Moon Wilds is organizing the Vexlings. The creatures were once scattered and animalistic—now they fight with disturbing coordination.

**The Escape Networks**: Despite Slavewatch, slaves keep escaping north. Someone is running an underground railroad through the jungle. The Emperor wants them found.

**The Old Emperor**: Veramus is sixty-three. When he dies, the succession crisis will be spectacular. Smart players are already positioning themselves.

**The Skaag Secrets**: The rat-folk beneath the cities know things. Old tunnels lead to older places. Some Skaag whisper of ruins predating Gorath—ruins the empire has built over and forgotten.

## Grieb

Tags: state, orc, westwilds

The lakemen control the great lakes of the northeastern Wildwood—Cykravikus and the smaller lakes scattered through the surrounding hills. They're aquatic orcs who can breathe underwater and rarely speak above the surface, ruled by a hag queen who has dwelt in the deepest waters for centuries.

### Overview

Nobody attacks the Griebi because fighting them costs more than anyone is willing to pay.

The lakes in the hills are valuable—fresh water, fish, defensible terrain. Other orc states would happily take that land. But taking it means fighting aquatic orcs who can breathe underwater indefinitely, who hide in murky depths for days waiting to ambush, who have rows of needle-like teeth that tear flesh as easily as they catch fish.

Attack a Griebi settlement and they simply sink beneath the surface. Follow them and you're in their world—blind, gasping, flailing in cold dark water while shapes with too many teeth circle below. The Griebi don't need to win battles. They just need to make every battle so costly that invaders give up.

The other orc states tried, generations ago. The survivors decided it wasn't worth it.

### Government

**Type:** Absolute Monarchy (The Hag Queen)

The Griebi have no councils, no competing factions, no succession crises. There is the Queen, and there are her subjects. Her word is law. Her silence is also law. When she wants something done, it happens. When she is satisfied, the lakes are peaceful.

**The Hag Queen:**

No one knows her true name. She is simply the Queen, or the Deep Mother, or in whispered moments, She-Who-Knows. She dwells in the deepest part of Cykravikus Lake, in underwater caverns that no air-breather has ever seen. The Griebi commune with her through representatives who descend into the depths and return—sometimes days later, sometimes weeks—with her commands.

She is ancient. She is patient. She has outlived empires.

**The Silent Court:** A handful of Griebi elders who interpret the Queen's wishes and manage daily affairs. They meet in the shallows where air-breathers can survive, speaking in the minimal, gesture-heavy communication that the Griebi prefer above water. They do not explain the Queen's decisions. They implement them.

### Culture

**The Griebi** have blueish-gray skin that shimmers in certain lights. Their eyes are large and dark, adapted for murky depths. Most distinctively, they have multiple rows of long, needle-like teeth designed for catching fish underwater—a feature that makes encounters with them deeply unsettling for surface dwellers.

**Core Values:**
- **Silence Is Wisdom** — Words above water are wasted breath. Communication should be efficient, minimal, necessary.
- **The Deep Provides** — The lakes give everything the Griebi need. They want nothing from the surface world.
- **The Queen Knows Best** — Questioning her is not forbidden; it simply doesn't occur to anyone. She has been right for centuries.
- **What Happens Below Stays Below** — The depths are private. Surface dwellers do not need to understand.

**Society:** Griebi society is remarkably simple by orc standards. There are hunters and fishers, there are those who tend the underwater kelp gardens, there are the Queen's servants who communicate with her, and there are the rare individuals who interact with the surface world. No warrior caste, because no one attacks them. No smiths, because they don't use metal. No hierarchy beyond service to the Queen.

They are not hostile to visitors. They're simply... absent. A traveler passing through Griebi territory might see nothing but empty shores and still water. The Griebi are below, watching, and they will remain below unless they have reason to surface.

**Communication:** The Griebi speak rarely above water and use a complex language of hand signals, body positions, and facial expressions. Underwater, they produce a range of clicks and tones that travel perfectly through the lake. Surface dwellers describe Griebi verbal speech as halting, reluctant, and unsettling—each word seems to cost them something.

### Economy

**Size:** Small (possibly)
**Tech:** Unknown (underwater technology is hard to assess)

**Primary Exports:**
- Fish and lake products (traded minimally through intermediaries)
- Safe passage across the lakes (available to those who ask politely)
- Nothing else

**Primary Imports:**
- Nothing regular
- Occasionally, specific items—a particular type of glass, a rare metal, a book—requested through intermediaries with no explanation given

The Griebi economy is almost entirely internal. The lakes provide food. Underwater kelp provides material for tools and clothing. They need nothing from the surface world, and the surface world cannot easily take anything from them.

Trade happens, but it's strange. A Griebi representative will appear at a market town, purchase exactly what they came for, and leave without negotiating or socializing. They pay in freshwater pearls that they seem to have in abundance. They don't explain where they're going or why they need what they're buying.

### Military

**Size:** Small
**Quality:** Unknown (rarely tested)
**Enrollment:** As needed

The Griebi don't maintain a standing army because they've never needed one. When threats arise, the lake communities organize defense, but this hasn't happened in generations.

What's known about their fighting capability:
- They can breathe underwater, giving them escape routes and tactical options others lack
- Their needle-teeth make them dangerous in close quarters
- They know their lake terrain intimately
- They're patient—if threatened, they can simply wait in the water until attackers give up

**The Real Defense:** The lakes themselves. Any invasion force faces the same problem: the Griebi can retreat underwater and stay there indefinitely. Siege them out? They have the entire lake to fish. Storm the shores? They drag you into the shallows and tear you apart. The math never works for the attacker.

The Hag Queen presumably has power of her own, but no one's tested it in living memory.

### Geography

**Location:** Northeastern Wildwood, in the lake-dotted hills north of Tarn

**Biomes:** Freshwater lakes surrounded by forested hills; rocky shorelines

**Key Locations:**
- **Cykravikus Lake** — The great lake, deep and cold, where the Hag Queen makes her home. Multiple inlets extend into the surrounding hills.
- **The Shore Villages** — Griebi settlements along the lakeshores, built half on land, half extending into the shallows.
- **The Gathering Stone** — A flat rock near the lake's western edge where the Griebi occasionally meet with outsiders.
- **The Lesser Lakes** — Smaller lakes scattered through the hills, each with its own fishing communities.

**Borders:**
- South/Southwest: Tarn (distant, minimal contact)
- West: Uhionai (mutual wariness)
- North: Unclaimed wilderness
- East: The Steppe of Aziirn (no orcs there; just emptiness)

### Relations

**Every Other Orc State:** The same pattern. They leave the Griebi alone. The Griebi leave them alone. It's not friendship—it's practical. Why attack territory you can't hold against people you can't catch?

**Tarn:** The Tarni spirit-seers can sense the Hag Queen's power. They describe it as "old" and "deep" and "patient." They respect it the way one respects a sleeping dragon.

**Uhionai:** The Grove Council has memories that stretch back centuries—and those memories include reasons to avoid the lakes. First Root Yorunax has reportedly said, "Some bargains should not be broken." He does not elaborate.

**Shazuihn:** Even the boar riders learned. Two generations ago, a war-chief led fifty riders against the Griebi. They charged the shore villages. The Griebi retreated into the water. The raiders followed into the shallows. Three survivors made it back. They don't talk about what happened in that lake.

**Symonair the Dragon:** The dragon Symonair claims Symonair Lake to the south of Cykravikus. The two bodies of water are connected. The relationship between the Hag Queen and the dragon is unknown—but neither has attacked the other, and that alone is notable.

**The Orc Moot:** The Griebi send a representative. They listen. They rarely speak. When they do speak, everyone else listens.

### The Hag Queen

The Queen is ancient—older than the other orc states, possibly older than the orcs' arrival in the Wildwood. She rules the Griebi absolutely, and they serve her without question. What she wants, what she plans, what she thinks about the endless wars to the south—no outsider knows.

She doesn't need mysterious leverage to maintain peace with the other states. She just needs to be more trouble than she's worth, and she is.

### Conflicts

**None active.**

The Griebi have no ongoing conflicts because no one wants to start one. Their territory is valuable, but not valuable enough to justify the casualties. The other orc states have learned: pick fights you can win at acceptable cost. Fighting the Griebi isn't one of them.

### History

The Hag Queen was here before the orc states formed. Whether she created the Griebi, attracted them, or transformed ordinary orcs into aquatic forms is unknown. They serve her. They have always served her.

The other orc states tried attacking Grieb in the early days, before they understood what they were dealing with. Those attacks failed—not through magic or curses, but through the simple reality of fighting underwater enemies in their home territory. The lesson stuck.

### Player Hooks

- Seek an audience with the Hag Queen (rare, but she occasionally receives visitors)
- Trade with the Griebi for freshwater pearls or lake products
- Navigate Grieb territory to reach somewhere beyond
- Survive an accidental trespass into Griebi waters
- Investigate the relationship between the Hag Queen and Symonair the dragon
- Hire Griebi guides for underwater exploration elsewhere

## Griselia

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Confederation of city-states (loose)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Scalawag Goblins

**Population**: 80,000 (across all city-states)

**Stability**: Chaotic but functional

**Core Values**: Brood loyalty, performance, rivalry, dramatic flair

- Griselia is not a unified nation but a collection of eight independent city-states, each ruled by a brood-father lineage. They compete constantly but present a united (theatrical) front to outsiders.
- Scalawag goblins dominate—mischievous, impulsive, passionate. Known for performers, philosophers, and devoting horrifying amounts of time to absurd projects.
- Some non-Scalawag goblins and a very slim number of non-goblins have migrated here over the years.
- Goblin politics are loud, dramatic, and bewildering to outsiders. Alliances shift constantly between city-states. Feuds can last generations or be forgotten by next week.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Somewhat developed

**Primary Exports**: Crafts, performers, mercenaries, mountain goods, chaos

**Primary Imports**: Food, luxuries, anything shiny

- Trade with Erasnus is brisk despite (or because of) the constant theatrical threats of invasion.
- Each city-state has its own economic specialties and jealously guards them.
- Goblin craftsmanship is inconsistent—brilliant one day, incomprehensible the next.

### Military

**Size**: Large (on paper)

**Quality**: Variable

**Enrollment**: Brood obligation

- Every goblin is expected to defend the brood. This means Griselia can theoretically field enormous armies.
- In practice, coordinating eight city-states is like herding cats. Joint military exercises tend to devolve into arguments, competitions, or impromptu festivals.
- Individual city-states can be surprisingly effective when motivated. The problem is motivation.

### Geography

**Location**: The Crags of Geth, a shallow bowl-shaped mountain range west of Erasnus, east of Ta Minn, north of the Browthiel Hills

**Biomes**: Mountain valleys, rocky peaks, scattered forests in the bowl

**Climate**: Temperate, sheltered by the bowl shape

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None (each city-state is independent)

**City-States (east to west along southern rim)**: Pholiki, Dygon, Aster (closest to Erasnus)

**City-States (elsewhere in the bowl)**: Shinishini, Codunp, Gor, Grisel, Bu

### Primary Conflicts

**Inter-City Rivalry**

The eight city-states compete over everything—trade routes, prestige, whose brood-father is most impressive, whose philosophers are most profound, whose parties are most legendary. This rarely escalates to actual violence (goblins don't like killing half-siblings), but the drama is constant.

**The Erasnus Theater**

Griselia's relationship with Erasnus is a perpetual comic opera. Goblin delegations arrive in Traduci making grandiose threats of invasion. Erasnun diplomats nod politely. Everyone trades. The goblins go home claiming diplomatic victory. Repeat endlessly.

Both sides know the invasion will never happen. But the theater serves domestic purposes for the goblins (brood-fathers look strong) and the Erasnuns (the military looks necessary). It's a mutually beneficial farce.

**Something Underneath**

Most of the posturing is theatrical. But some city-states—particularly those further from Erasnus, away from the profitable trade—may harbor genuine ambitions. The bowl of the Crags of Geth hides things. Not every brood-father is content with performance.

**Anarak to the North**

The great basin of Anarak lies between the Crags of Geth and Argysis in the Kharvorn Mountains. Relations with Anarak are... complicated. The goblins don't threaten Anarak the way they threaten Erasnus. There may be reasons for that.

### History

The Scalawag goblins have inhabited the Crags of Geth for as long as anyone remembers. The bowl-shaped range provided natural shelter, and the eight major valleys became eight city-states, each dominated by a single brood lineage.

They've never unified. They've never needed to. The mountains protect them, trade sustains them, and the endless inter-city drama keeps everyone entertained. Outsiders have tried to conquer Griselia; the mountains and the chaos defeated them all.

The theatrical threats toward Erasnus started centuries ago—possibly as genuine ambition, possibly as a joke that stuck. Now it's tradition. No one remembers why it began, and no one wants to be the brood-father who stops.

## Grustos

Tags: state

A mountainous state in Central Aboyinzu, notable for the three non-human populations that share its territory with the human majority: the isolationist Kryaaji sun elves of Enuau Kemjallb, the Aciabro blood-mechanist goblins of the Sunwing Peaks, and the nomadic giant herders who pass through seasonally via Giants' Corridor.

### Government

Grustos is governed from its capital Cañas by a council system designed to accommodate its unusual demographics. The ruling council includes human merchant representatives, giant clan-speakers (when migration brings them through), and a perpetually empty chair reserved for the Kryaaji—who have never acknowledged the invitation.

The Aciabro goblins are not represented and have never requested to be. They govern themselves in their mountain workshops and have no interest in lowland politics.

In practice, humans run Grustos, with the understanding that their authority doesn't extend into sun elf territory, goblin workshops, or the Giants' Corridor during migration seasons. This limited sovereignty is accepted as the cost of coexistence.

### Populations

**Humans**: The majority, concentrated in the central lowlands and southern regions. They farm, trade, and serve as intermediaries between the other populations.

**Kryaaji Sun Elves**: Residing in the independent city-state of Enuau Kemjallb within Grustos's claimed borders. The sun elves maintain strict isolation, trading only through boundary markets. Grustos claims them as citizens; the Kryaaji consider this fiction and ignore it.

**Aciabro Goblins**: Blood-mechanist goblins who replace their flesh with machinery powered by vital essence. They inhabit workshops in the Sunwing Peaks, have no interest in outside contact, and trade only in blood-soaked equipment no sane person wants.

**Giant Herders**: Nomadic pastoral giants who pass through twice yearly with their enormous flocks. They're not citizens—they predate Grustos and will outlast it—but they trade at designated stops and generally avoid causing problems.

### Economy

Grustos is not wealthy. The mountains limit agriculture; the sun elves don't share their craftsmanship; the goblins produce nothing tradeable. What prosperity exists comes from:

- **Giant migration trade**: Twice yearly, towns along the corridor transform into massive markets for giant-produced wool, leather, and exotic meats.
- **Mining**: Iron and copper from the Torulius Mountains feed modest metalworking industries.
- **Kryaaji boundary trade**: Sun elf craftsmanship commands premium prices, even through indirect exchange.
- **Transit fees**: The main road between Chaal Nazzerox (for those foolish enough to go there) and Qintas passes through Grustos.

### Military

Grustos maintains a small professional army supplemented by militia levies—enough to patrol roads and handle bandits, not enough to fight a real war. The state's defense relies more on geography (mountains on all sides) and the assumption that any invader would have to contend with sun elves, blood-goblins, and giants.

This assumption may be optimistic. If Chaal Nazzerox's undead break west, Grustos will be the first to fall.

### Relations

**Enuau Kemjallb**: Technically within Grustos's borders, practically independent. The relationship is one of mutual non-interference and carefully maintained fiction.

**Qintas**: Friendly neighbor to the east. Trade flows freely; borders are nominal. The Amverela high elves consider Grustos slightly uncouth; Grustani consider Qintas slightly pretentious.

**Chaal Nazzerox**: The undead empire to the west. No diplomatic relations. Grustos watches the border nervously and hopes the Crystal Mountains hold.

### What Will Go Wrong

- **The undead will come**: Grustos is the buffer between Chaal Nazzerox and the civilized east. Buffers get ground down.
- **The sun elves know something**: The Kryaaji have lived on the Light leyline for millennia. Their isolation may be hiding more than religious purity.
- **Skyloft Castle**: Whatever destroyed it is still there. Eventually, it may wake up.
- **The giants are changing routes**: Climate shifts or something else is altering migration patterns. Towns built around giant trade may find themselves abandoned.

## Gymlstik

Tags: state

Once controlled by dwarves, these mountains have been taken over by goblins and Cirthikin, large, burrowing, praying mantis-like creatures. The tunnels form an enormous network that travel for hundreds of miles underground. The dwarves, driven deeper into the ground, have become pale monstrosities below, known as Surry dwarves. Mines, hidden lairs, and underground fortresses litter the mountains.

## Hedroscobb

Tags: state, orc, westwilds

The largest of the six orc states and the one that gives the Wildwood its name to outsiders. Hedroscobb controls the southern forest from Vogenfeld's mountain passes to the Yugurbas Dyos marshes, and its warriors in their terrible corrosive armor are what Tarkhon imagines when they think of "orc."

### Overview

Hedroscobb is a military state built around a single advantage: algae-iron. The Hedroscobbi have mastered the art of forging iron combined with specific swamp algae to create armor so heavy and corrosive that only their own naturally adapted skin can withstand wearing it. This armor—black, dripping, and caustic—provides extraordinary protection and destroys the weapons of anyone who strikes it.

Everything in Hedroscobb society serves the forges. The constant wars aren't about glory or territory; they're about securing the algae that keeps the armor coming. Without it, they're just orcs. With it, they're nearly unstoppable.

### Government

**Type:** Forge Council

The Hedroscobbi are ruled by a council of five Forge-Masters—the greatest smiths and war-leaders combined. Political power flows from the ability to produce armor; military command follows from the ability to wear it well.

**The Five Forges:**
- **Forge Vraketh** — The Black Anvil. Oldest and most productive forge, controls the main ironwood groves. Forge-Master Kethros has held his seat for forty years.
- **Forge Drul** — The Acid Gate. Controls the southern passes and first contact with Vogenfeld. Forge-Master Grusha is the primary voice for war with the dwarves.
- **Forge Ystok** — The River Forge. Controls the southeastern territory bordering Shazuihn; constantly fighting off raiders. Forge-Master Thokk has buried three children to boar-rider axes.
- **Forge Morul** — The Northern Reach. Controls the border with Yugurbas Dyos. Forge-Master Velka leads the algae expeditions.
- **Forge Sulth** — The Hidden Forge. Smallest, most secretive. Rumored to experiment with new algae combinations. Forge-Master unknown to outsiders.

Decisions require majority agreement. War requires unanimous consent. The council meets in the Slag Halls—a vast underground complex beneath the central Wildwood where the great forges never cool.

### Culture

**The Hedroscobbi** have dark, leathery skin that resists the corrosive properties of their own armor and weapons. They're larger than most orcs—the constant labor of the forges and the weight of the armor have bred them strong over generations. Acid scars are marks of honor; a smith who hasn't burned themselves hasn't worked hard enough.

**Core Values:**
- **The Forge Above All** — Personal glory means nothing. The forge-clan is everything.
- **Weight Is Strength** — The heavier the burden you can bear, the more respect you command.
- **Iron Remembers** — Every piece of armor carries the memory of its maker. Disgrace the armor, disgrace the smith.
- **The Algae Must Flow** — Without the swamp algae, there is no armor. Without armor, there is no Hedroscobb.

**Society:** Hedroscobb is rigidly hierarchical. Forge-clans control all production; warriors serve the forges; gatherers, hunters, and laborers support the warriors. The only social mobility is through exceptional smithing or exceptional killing—preferably both.

Children are tested at age ten. Those who can withstand the touch of algae-iron without screaming become smith-apprentices. Those who cannot become warriors. Those who flinch become laborers.

### Economy

**Size:** Medium
**Tech:** Developed (in metallurgy), Undeveloped (in everything else)

**Primary Exports:**
- Algae-iron weapons (extremely valuable, rarely sold)
- Ironwood timber
- War captives (to Shazuihn, ironically—the raiders trade for what they can't take)

**Primary Imports:**
- Swamp algae (the limiting factor—never enough)
- Food (Hedroscobb's focus on smithing means they don't farm well)
- Healing supplies (they can't make armor without getting hurt)

The economy is command-driven. The Forge Council controls all production and distribution. There is no currency; goods are allocated by status and need. Trade with outsiders is conducted through designated intermediaries and is heavily regulated.

### Military

**Size:** Large
**Quality:** Very High
**Enrollment:** Universal (all adults serve in some capacity)

The Hedroscobbi military is terrifying. Warriors in full algae-iron armor are nearly immune to conventional weapons—the armor provides extraordinary protection, and anyone who lands a hit finds their weapon corroding from the contact. A Hedroscobbi shield wall is a nightmare to face.

**The Black Tide:** The elite heavy infantry. Full algae-iron plate, tower shields, short stabbing swords. They advance slowly—the armor is enormously heavy—but nothing stops them.

**The Gatherers:** Lighter-armored raiders who secure algae from Yugurbas Dyos and defend against Shazuihni attacks. More mobile, less protected, but still dangerous.

**Weaknesses:**
- The armor is slow. Fast enemies who won't engage directly can bleed them.
- The armor is heavy. Long marches exhaust them. They can't pursue fleeing enemies.
- The armor requires maintenance. Without fresh algae, it loses its corrosive properties.
- They're terrible at naval warfare. The armor sinks.

### Geography

**Location:** Southern Wildwood, directly north of Vogenfeld's mountain passes

**Biomes:** Dense temperate forest with ironwood groves; rocky ravines and hidden valleys

**Key Locations:**
- **The Slag Halls** — Underground forge complex beneath central Hedroscobb; seat of the Forge Council
- **Ironwood Grove** — Ancient forest of iron-hard trees; essential for forge-fuel and weapon handles
- **The Acid Gate** — Fortified position overlooking the passes to Vogenfeld
- **Gatherer's Camp** — Seasonal encampment in Yugurbas Dyos during algae-harvesting season

**Borders:**
- South: Vogenfeld (the dwarves watch, but rarely cross)
- North: Glivornax (hostile; the War of the Marshes)
- Northeast: Tarn (neutral; little contact)
- East: Shazuihn (hostile; constant raiding)
- West: Plains of Oblivion (no one goes there)

### Relations

**Glivornax:** Open warfare. The Glivornaxi claim the Yugurbas Dyos marshes and try to prevent algae harvesting. Hedroscobb sends armed expeditions every season. People die.

**Shazuihn:** Constant low-level conflict. The boar riders attack from the east without warning, take what they want, and vanish. Hedroscobb has never been able to stop the raids completely—the Shazuihni are too fast, and the armor is too slow.

**Tarn:** Minimal contact. The Tarni control river territory; Hedroscobb doesn't care about rivers. Occasional trade through intermediaries.

**Uhionai:** Almost no contact. The tree-orcs are far to the northwest; Hedroscobb has no reason to go there.

**Grieb:** No contact. The lakemen are too far north, and nobody wants to fight aquatic orcs in their home waters anyway.

**Vogenfeld:** Cold hostility. The dwarves watch the passes; Hedroscobb watches back. Neither side has attacked the other directly in generations, but both know war could come. The dwarves trade metal to Glivornax—Hedroscobb considers this a provocation.

**The Orc Moot:** Hedroscobb sends representatives but rarely cooperates. They don't need allies; they have armor.

### Conflicts

**The War of the Marshes:** The defining conflict. The Yugurbas Dyos contains the richest algae beds outside Glivornax proper. Hedroscobb needs that algae to survive; Glivornax says the marshes are sacred. Neither side can afford to lose.

**The Eternal Raid:** Shazuihni attacks drain resources and kill people constantly. Hedroscobb can't stop them and can't ignore them.

**The Dwarven Question:** Vogenfeld controls the only practical passes into Tarkhon. If Hedroscobb ever decided to expand south, the dwarves would be first. Some Forge-Masters whisper that the time is coming.

### History

The Hedroscobbi were once just another orc tribe—larger than most, but unremarkable. Everything changed when a smith named Vraketh discovered that certain swamp algae, when properly treated and combined with iron, created metal with extraordinary properties.

Within a generation, the Hedroscobbi conquered their neighbors and established the Forge Council. Within three generations, they controlled the entire southern Wildwood. They might have conquered more, but the algae limits them. Without access to the marshes, they can't produce enough armor to expand.

The War of the Marshes has been ongoing for over a century. Both sides have bled, but neither has broken.

### Player Hooks

- Infiltrate the Slag Halls to learn the secret of algae-iron smithing
- Negotiate a ceasefire between Hedroscobb and Glivornax (good luck)
- Survive the Acid Gate as a Vogenfeld diplomat
- Join a Gatherer expedition into Yugurbas Dyos during algae season
- Investigate why Forge Sulth is so secretive—what new weapons are they developing?
- Convince the Forge Council that the Shazuihni raids could be stopped with better tactics

## Illron

Tags: state, elves, confederation, fae

The southernmost of the three elven kingdoms, and the most traditional. Illron follows the old ways—not as empty phrase, but as literal practice. Where Lenora has cleared forests and built roads, Illron has kept faith with agreements older than the confederation itself. The kingdom's heart is a forest no elf will settle, surrounding a ruined palace no elf will enter.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Hereditary monarchy with council of elders

**Primary Inhabitants**: Elves

**Population**: ~100,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**:
- Honor to ancestors and ancient pacts
- Preservation of the old ways
- Respect for the forest and its inhabitants
- Patient endurance over aggressive expansion

Illron is ruled by the **Thornwood Crown**—a hereditary monarchy that has passed through the same bloodline for over 4,000 years. The current monarch, traditionally styled the Keeper of the Old Ways, governs from Ishla'Anore with the counsel of an assembly of elders drawn from the kingdom's settlements.

The monarchy's power is largely ceremonial in peacetime. Day-to-day governance happens at the local level. But the Crown holds absolute authority in matters of tradition, treaty interpretation, and relations with non-elven powers—including the fae.

### The Iyaklomori Grera

The great forest at Illron's heart is not elven territory. It belongs to the fae.

Three rivers run through Iyaklomori Grera—the Kilgre Venora in the north, Ver Ta'Eres in the center, and Ver Suterela in the south. Any of them could support thriving settlements. None do. The forest is empty of elven habitation except for one ruin: Kilren's Palace, which stands as monument to the last time the elves forgot this fact.

The Illronese have a treaty with the fae court. The terms:
1. Elves may travel on two designated routes: the river-road along Kilgre Venora, and the diagonal trail from Hassera to E'lethelas
2. Elves may not settle, build, or clear any part of the forest
3. Elves may not hunt, harvest, or take anything that grows within the bounds
4. In exchange, the fae leave the coastal settlements and eastern edge alone

Breaking these terms is not punished by elven law. The fae handle enforcement themselves.

Travelers on the legal routes report being watched constantly. Movement in peripheral vision. Laughter at night. The sensation of attention. Nothing directly hostile—but nothing welcoming either. The 180-mile diagonal trail through the forest's heart takes 8-10 days. Most elves who've walked it once refuse to do it again.

Those who stray from the paths, or who take something, or who camp too long in one place—most are never seen again. The few who return are changed. Silent. Hollow. Wrong in ways that are hard to describe. They live out their remaining years but never speak of what happened.

### Kilren's Palace

Three thousand years ago, King Kilren decided to break the treaty.

He was a young king, ambitious, frustrated by the restrictions his ancestors had accepted. He believed the fae were not as powerful as the stories claimed. He marched 10,000 soldiers into the forest to establish a permanent military presence and prove that elves could take what was theirs.

The forest let them enter. Let them build. Kilren's Palace rose in a clearing on the Ver Ta'Eres—a proper fortress, stone walls, garrison quarters, armories. The elves brought their best weapons: blades forged from titan bone, armor worked with titan blood, the finest craftsmanship of the age. They garrisoned the palace for a full year. Nothing happened.

Then, one night, everyone inside vanished.

Not killed—gone. The fortress stood empty. The weapons remained in the armory. The food sat uneaten. Ten thousand soldiers simply ceased to be present.

King Kilren was found a week later, walking out of the forest toward Ishla'Anore. He couldn't speak. He lived another forty years, sitting in his palace, staring at walls. He never wrote. Never gestured meaningfully. Never communicated in any way. When he died, he was buried without ceremony—his name is not among those the Illronese remember.

The fortress still stands. The armory is still stocked with the finest titan-bone weapons ever forged—an arsenal worth more than most kingdoms' treasuries. The elves refuse to touch it. The few outsiders who learn of the treasure and attempt to claim it almost never return. Those who do return are like Kilren, or worse.

The fae don't seem to use the palace. They don't build or live in structures. It simply sits there, preserved, waiting. A monument and a warning.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Coastal fish, crafted goods, traditional textiles

**Primary Imports**: Grain (from Lenora), metals, titan-bone ritual objects (from Deo Esari)

Illron's economy is modest by necessity. They fish the coastal waters, craft goods in their settlements, and trade for what they cannot produce. They have no agricultural heartland—the forest takes up most of their territory, and they cannot use it.

This dependence on Lenora's grain is a source of quiet resentment. Every shipment is a reminder of what Illron gave up to honor the old pacts.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Traditional service obligations

Illron's military is small but exceptionally trained. They don't need large numbers—no one invades through fae territory, and the coastal approach is easily defensible. What soldiers they have are drilled in traditional combat forms passed down for millennia.

The Illronese do not make war. They haven't fielded an army beyond their borders since before the confederation. Their military exists to defend, not to project power.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Farlands, bordered by Ocean of Elorinia (west), Amholia Greras (north), and Wycendeula (south)

**Biomes**: Temperate forest, coastal scrubland

**Major Geographic Features**:
- **Iyaklomori Grera**: The sacred forest. Fae territory. See above.
- **Poscuni Gretanu**: Grasslands on the northeastern edge, transitioning toward Amholia Greras

**Rivers**:
- **Kilgre Venora**: Northern river, one of two legal travel routes
- **Ver Ta'Eres**: Central river, site of Kilren's Palace
- **Ver Suterela**: Southern river

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Ishla'Anore (eastern edge, outside the forest)

**Cities**:
- **E'lethelas**: Secondary city, south of Ishla'Anore, connected by road

**Coastal Settlements** (connected by coastal road):
- **A'valenor**: Northern coast, sits on the Air astral current
- **Olymethemar**: Central coast
- **Hassera**: Southern tip

**Cross-Forest Routes**:
- **Northern road**: Along Kilgre Venora, from Ishla'Anore to A'valenor
- **Diagonal trail**: 180 miles from Hassera to E'lethelas through the forest interior (rarely used)

The kingdom's settlements are arranged around the edges of Iyaklomori Grera, never within it. The eastern cities handle administration and trade with the other kingdoms. The coastal cities handle fishing and what little foreign commerce Illron engages in.

### Relations

**Lenora**: Tense. The confederation's voting structure gives Illron equal voice with Lenora despite having a quarter of the population. Lenora resents this. Illron insists the old agreements are binding and that population is irrelevant to a kingdom's worth. Both sides are correct according to their own values.

**Deo Esari**: Respectful. Both kingdoms prioritize tradition over growth. Deo Esari's recent disaster has only deepened their conservatism, which Illron quietly approves of. They are natural allies in confederation disputes.

**The Fae**: The Illronese do not consider their relationship with the fae to be diplomatic in the normal sense. The fae are not negotiating partners—they are powers to be acknowledged. The treaty is honored because the alternative has been demonstrated. Illron's monarchy derives some of its legitimacy from being the designated keeper of this relationship.

### Primary Conflicts

- **Confederation Voting**: Lenora wants representation proportional to population. Illron refuses. The treaty signed at Laeroth Esori specifies one kingdom, one voice. The ancestors are watching.
- **Economic Dependence**: Relying on Lenora for food is humiliating but unavoidable. Some younger Illronese wonder if there's a way to reduce this dependence without breaking the fae treaty.
- **The Palace Question**: The treasure in Kilren's Palace is immense. Every few generations, someone suggests mounting an expedition. The Crown always refuses. But the temptation doesn't go away.

### History

The elves who became the Illronese were the first of the eastern migrants to reach the Farlands. They found the forest already occupied. Their initial attempts to settle it ended badly. After enough failures, they negotiated.

The treaty with the fae predates the Three Kingdoms Treaty by at least a millennium. When the confederation was formed, Illron's representatives insisted that their prior obligations be acknowledged—the forest would remain fae territory, and no confederation decision could overrule that. Lenora and Deo Esari agreed. They had no reason not to; the forest wasn't theirs anyway.

King Kilren's invasion, roughly 3,000 years ago, was the last serious attempt to change the arrangement. The lesson it taught has not been forgotten.

## Ios

Tags: state, decline, constructs

A civilization running out of time. Ios built its society around the stone dolls — animated servants of stone and porcelain that handled all manual labor, freeing the human population for leisure, art, and governance. For generations, this arrangement worked beautifully. The dolls farmed the fields, hauled the goods, built the buildings, and asked nothing in return.

Then the dolls started breaking. And no one remembered how to make new ones.

The art of creating stone dolls was lost generations ago — how, exactly, is a matter of bitter debate among Ios scholars.
