Some say the last craftmasters died in a plague without passing on their knowledge. Others claim the secrets were deliberately destroyed to prevent the dolls from being weaponized. A few whisper that the dolls themselves somehow arranged for the knowledge to be lost. Whatever the cause, the result is the same: Ios has hundreds of stone dolls, they break down at a steady rate, and there will never be more.

### The Stone Dolls

The dolls are humanoid figures of stone and porcelain, ranging from three to seven feet tall depending on their function. They move smoothly, respond to simple commands, and perform physical labor with tireless precision. They don't speak, don't eat, don't sleep. They simply work until they break.

No one knows exactly how they function. The craftmasters who created them understood the animating principles; modern Ios citizens understand only that the dolls exist and must be maintained. Repairs are possible — broken limbs can be reattached, cracked surfaces can be sealed — but fundamental failures cannot be fixed. When a doll's animating core fails, it becomes a statue. Nothing anyone has tried can restore it.

The dolls are treated with a strange mixture of casual use and anxious care. They're tools, worked hard because the work must be done. But they're also irreplaceable, and every citizen knows that each broken doll brings Ios closer to a future where humans must do the labor themselves.

### The Crisis

The mathematics are simple and merciless. Ios has perhaps three hundred functioning stone dolls. They break down at an average rate of five to ten per year. In a generation, there will be none left. In two generations, Ios will be indistinguishable from any other state where humans do their own work.

The ruling families understand this. They've spent fortunes trying to rediscover the lost art — hiring scholars, sponsoring expeditions to find lost texts, offering rewards for anyone who can repair a failed doll. Nothing has worked. The closest anyone has come was a scholar who identified the rare stone from which the dolls were carved; the quarries at Petras were supposedly exhausted long ago, and no new deposits have been found.

The population is slowly adapting. Young people learn trades their grandparents never practiced. Fields that were maintained by dolls are being abandoned or converted to less labor-intensive uses. The terraced city of Todakres is contracting downward as the upper levels become impractical without doll labor. It's not catastrophic — Ios will survive — but the graceful society of generations past is already gone.

### Government

Ios is ruled by a council of noble families — the descendants of those who first established the stone doll tradition. Their authority was once based on control of the dolls; now it's based on tradition, wealth, and the absence of any alternative.

The council meets in Todakres, in halls that were built to showcase the dolls' capabilities. The architecture is impressive and increasingly impractical — grand staircases that strain human bearers, high ceilings that require scaffolding to maintain, elaborate mechanisms that only doll strength can operate. The noble families maintain these spaces out of pride, even as practical governance moves to more accessible locations.

Decision-making is slow and conservative. The families are paralyzed by their situation, unable to imagine a future without the dolls but equally unable to prevent it. Bold proposals are rejected because they're too risky; cautious proposals are rejected because they're too slow. The council debates while the dolls continue to break.

### Economy

Ios was once wealthy — the dolls enabled production that would be impossible with human labor alone. Now that wealth is eroding. Agricultural output has fallen as fields are abandoned. Manufacturing has declined as doll-powered workshops stand idle. Trade has shrunk as Ios has less to offer and more difficulty getting goods to market.

The shallow harbor at Methons compounds the problem. Ios can't easily import the labor-intensive goods it once produced domestically, and it can't easily export what it still makes. The state is slowly becoming self-sufficient by necessity, retreating from the broader economy as its capabilities diminish.

What remains valuable: the dolls themselves. Other states have noticed Ios's predicament and offered to purchase functioning dolls. The council has refused — selling the dolls would accelerate the decline — but as the situation worsens, the temptation grows. Some have been stolen. Some may have been sold quietly. The council doesn't discuss it.

### Culture

Ios culture was shaped by abundance — generations of humans who never performed manual labor, who grew up watching dolls handle every physical task, who developed arts and philosophies suited to lives of leisure. This culture persists even as its foundations crumble.

The noble families still maintain elaborate households, still observe complex social rituals, still patronize artists and scholars. But the rituals feel hollow now, the patronage is constrained by shrinking resources, and everyone knows that the next generation will live differently. Some families have adapted, training their children in practical skills. Others refuse, insisting that their way of life will somehow continue.

The common people are more practical. They've already begun the transition to working society, taking up trades and agriculture that their ancestors never practiced. There's resentment toward the nobles who delayed this adaptation, but also a surprising resilience. Ios citizens have survived harder things than learning to carry their own water.

### Relations

**With Moigos**: Minimal. The mountain passes are difficult, and neither state has much to offer the other. The Kappa have shown no interest in Ios — a declining state with no significant trade connections is not worth infiltrating.

**With Rilyn**: Occasional contact through the eastern passes. The Splinkreen find Ios fascinating — a culture built around artificial servants, now learning to serve itself. Some Splinkreen scholars have visited Todakres to study the dolls. Whether they learned anything useful, they haven't shared.

**With everyone else**: Ios is increasingly isolated. The state that once traded throughout the Seacleft Coast now struggles to get goods to market. Foreign merchants still visit, but their numbers decline each year as Ios has less to buy and sell.

### Notable Locations

- **Todakres**: Mountain capital, terraced city slowly contracting downward
- **Methons**: Coastal city with shallow harbor, limited maritime access
- **Petras**: Quarry town, source of the stone used for dolls
- **Rhodanos**: Agricultural center, fields shrinking as doll labor fails
- **Tolosa**: Market town, adapting better than most
- **Capsa**: River confluence town with defensive walls
- **Koilos**: Southern forest, largely unexplored, rumored ruins

### Player Hooks

- **The Last Craftmaster**: Rumors persist of a surviving craftmaster — or their notes, or their apprentice, or something that could restore the art of doll creation. The noble families will pay handsomely for any lead. So will foreign powers who want the secret for themselves.

- **Doll Theft**: Functioning dolls are being stolen from Ios estates, appearing later in foreign markets. Someone is running an operation, and the council wants it stopped — but some council members might be involved.

- **The Petras Deposits**: A prospector claims to have found new deposits of the specialized stone used for doll construction. If true, it could save Ios — or spark a war for control of the resource. If false, it's a scam. Either way, someone needs to investigate.

- **Koilos Expedition**: The ruins in the Koilos forest might hold secrets from before the stone doll tradition — including, possibly, alternative forms of magical labor. A noble family is funding an expedition. The forest has killed previous explorers, but desperation makes people brave.

- **The Awakening**: A stone doll has started behaving strangely — responding to commands differently, moving when not instructed, perhaps even attempting to communicate. Is it malfunctioning, or is something else happening? And what does it mean for the other dolls?

## Iqes

Tags: state, trade, privateering

A glittering maritime power built on coral and colonization. Iqes's coastal cities are architectural marvels — towers of cultivated pink coral, walls shimmering with mica, plazas where wealth is displayed without shame. The Qindo people who built these cities, and whose labor still sustains them, have been relegated to the bottom of a society ruled by foreign-descended merchant families.

Two centuries ago, Iqes faced absorption by a larger power. The foreign merchants who'd slowly taken economic control formalized their grip through the voting ring system, presenting a unified front that preserved independence while cementing their dominance. The native Qindo were permitted to participate — in theory. In practice, they're sailors on other people's ships, servants in other people's houses, workers whose labor builds wealth they'll never share.

### The Qindo People

The indigenous people of Iqes are distinctive: dark-skinned humans and halflings with piercing blue eyes that seem to catch the light. They share a common culture, a common language (still spoken in the interior, suppressed in the cities), and a common history of dispossession.

**Before the merchants came**, humans farmed and fished the lowlands while halflings lived in elaborate mountain cave-communities. The two peoples traded, intermarried occasionally, and shared spiritual traditions centered on ancestor veneration. The caves held generations of accumulated craft knowledge; the lowland villages held generations of agricultural wisdom. It was not wealthy by foreign standards, but it was theirs.

**The merchant takeover** happened through economics, not armies. Foreign traders arrived with ships, capital, and ambition. Trade deals became debt obligations became ownership of land and resources. The humans were economically subjugated over generations; the halflings faced something worse.

**The cave destruction** remains the defining trauma of Qindo identity. When halflings resisted economic integration — when they refused to abandon their mountain homes for wage labor in the coastal cities — the foreign merchants responded with violence. Cave entrances were collapsed, water sources poisoned, access routes destroyed. The halflings' homes became their tombs.

This wasn't just displacement; it was cultural annihilation. The caves held ancestral shrines, craft traditions, the physical record of halfling history. All gone. The survivors were "incorporated into civilized life" — forced into the coastal economy as a permanent underclass. Elders who remember the caves are still alive. The grief is fresh.

**Modern Qindo** occupy every level of society except the top. Most work as laborers, sailors, servants, craftspeople — essential workers without whom Iqes would collapse. A small middle class exists in Simphiwe, where traditional culture retained more influence. Some Qindo have accumulated enough wealth to purchase voting rings, but they're vastly outnumbered.

The halfling population is particularly marginalized. The cave-destruction trauma is recent enough that survivors still live; their children carry the grief forward. Halfling communities maintain traditions of remembrance, making pilgrimages to the ruined cave-sites, holding ceremonies that preserve what can be preserved.

### The Voting Ring System

Iqes is governed by a council whose members' votes are determined by physical tokens — heavy golden rings worn on the fingers, each one representing a vote. The more rings you hold, the more votes you cast. There is no maximum; the wealthiest ring-holders possess dozens.

**How rings are acquired:**
- **Inheritance** — the most common path; ring-holding families maintain their status across generations
- **Purchase** — possible but deliberately expensive; existing holders keep prices high
- **Debt collection** — fail to repay a ring-holder, lose your ring
- **Council grant** — for "exceptional service," i.e., corruption and political favors
- **Force** — rare and risky; taking rings by violence requires covering your tracks

The system concentrates power among those who already have it. New wealth can buy in, but old wealth controls the game. Qindo who acquire rings are tolerated but watched — their presence in the council chambers makes the foreign elite uncomfortable, reminds them that their rule requires constant maintenance.

**The Ring Hall** in Mjiqa is where the council meets. Physically, it's impressive — coral columns, mica mosaics, galleries for observers. Politically, it's a theater where decisions made in private rooms receive public endorsement. The real negotiations happen elsewhere; the Ring Hall is for announcements.

### Current Power

Power in Iqes currently concentrates around two figures:

**Sir Calroy Garoldson** represents the traditional merchant elite — foreign-descended, old money, the families who've held power since the consolidation. The "Sir" is a purchased title from somewhere distant, meaningless in Iqes but impressive to outsiders unfamiliar with the island's politics. Garoldson is pragmatic rather than cruel; he maintains the system because it benefits him, not from particular animus toward the Qindo.

**Sorvos** is more complicated. Mixed heritage, possibly partly Qindo, risen through the system rather than born into it. Whether he's a genuine reformer working within constraints, a sellout who's abandoned his people, or playing a longer game than anyone realizes — the truth depends on who you ask and when you ask them. The Qindo themselves are divided on him.

The two reportedly balance each other — Garoldson representing stability and tradition, Sorvos representing the possibility of change. Whether this balance is genuine or performed is unclear.

### Privateering

Iqes privateers operate with official sanction — letters of marque authorizing raids on foreign shipping. In theory, they target only enemy nations. In practice, definitions are flexible, and "enemy" is defined by whoever holds the most rings this season.

**How privateering works:**
- Ring-holders sponsor captains, providing ships and letters of marque
- Privateers raid foreign shipping, keeping a percentage and paying the rest to sponsors
- The council maintains plausible deniability
- Neighboring nations complain; nothing changes
- Everyone who matters profits

**The privateer class** represents one of the few paths to wealth open to Qindo. Ship captains are often natives — skill matters more than bloodline when you're hunting prizes. Successful captains can accumulate enough to buy voting rings, creating tension with establishment ring-holders who resent these upstarts.

The **Privateers' Berths** in Mjiqa harbor represent a power center somewhat independent of the old elite. Privateer captains have money, armed followers, and connections across the Seacleft Coast. They're loyal to the system that enriches them, but their loyalty is to their own interests rather than to particular ring-holders.

### Economy

Iqes's wealth comes from three sources: trade, privateering, and the labor of its people.

**Trade** flows through Mjiqa — goods from across the Seacleft Coast, up the Shulka River from the interior, across from Gyosha and beyond. The coral cities exist because Iqes sits at a crossroads; merchants from a dozen nations do business here. The ring-holders take their cut of every transaction.

**Privateering** supplements legitimate trade with stolen goods and ransomed cargoes. The line between merchant and pirate blurs in Iqes; many ring-holders have investments in both. Foreign nations complain; Iqes expresses regret and does nothing.

**Labor** — Qindo labor — makes everything possible. The dockworkers who unload ships, the servants who maintain estates, the sailors who crew vessels both merchant and privateer, the craftspeople who build the coral towers. The economy would collapse without them. They see little of the wealth they create.

### Culture

Iqes culture is layered — foreign elite aesthetics laid over Qindo foundations that never entirely disappeared.

The **coastal cities** embrace foreign fashions, imported luxuries, cosmopolitan attitudes. Ring-holders compete through displays of wealth; coral architecture grows more elaborate each generation. The language of commerce is whatever the traders speak; the language of power is the foreign tongue of the original merchants.

**Ntando and the interior** preserve Qindo traditions — ancestor veneration, communal decision-making, crafts and practices that predate the merchant takeover. Village elders hold authority here; voting rings are irrelevant. The old language survives. The old ways persist.

Between these extremes, most Qindo navigate a mixed existence — working in the foreign-dominated economy, maintaining what traditions they can, speaking one language at work and another at home.

### Relations

**With Mirko**: Complicated entanglement. Mirko's criminal networks handle goods that Iqes privateers acquire and legitimate merchants prefer not to touch directly. The relationship is profitable for both sides. Zukisani serves as the primary interface, its shipyards building vessels for buyers who ask few questions.

**With Enavadi**: Minimal contact. Enavadi's insular fishing culture has little interest in Iqes's commerce, and Iqes has nothing Enavadi wants. The two ignore each other politely.

**With Linas**: Trade relationship. Linas dye reaches markets through Iqes merchants; Iqes goods reach Linas through the same channels. Privateers mostly leave Linas shipping alone — the dye trade is too profitable to disrupt.

**With neighboring powers**: A mix of trade, raiding, and diplomatic maneuvering. Iqes maintains relations with everyone, preys on everyone, and allies with whoever offers the best terms this season.

### Notable Locations

- **Mjiqa**: Capital city at the Shulka River mouth; Ring Hall, Coral Docks, Privateers' Berths, Qindo Quarter
- **Simphiwe**: Western city, gateway to Ntando; largest halfling population, most Qindo cultural influence
- **Zukisani**: Northern port on Myusha River; shipyards, fishing fleet, trade connection to Gyosha
- **Zuko**: Interior garrison city; Tax Houses, military control, resistance cells
- **Ntando**: Traditional Qindo interior; forest villages, elder governance, halfling pilgrimage sites

### Player Hooks

- **The Ring Game**: A Qindo captain has accumulated enough wealth to buy voting rings. Established ring-holders want to prevent this without creating a martyr. Players might be hired by either side — or play both against the middle.

- **Cave Secrets**: The destroyed halfling caves weren't just homes. They contained something the merchants wanted — or wanted buried. A halfling elder knows the location of a cave that wasn't completely destroyed, and what lies within.

- **Murder Point**: Someone important was sent to the point who shouldn't have been — a ring-holder who knew too much, or a resistance leader whose death will spark violence. The body needs to be recovered before the currents scatter it, and the truth needs to come out. Or be buried deeper.

- **Sorvos's Game**: The enigmatic ring-holder is making moves that could reshape Iqes politics — but toward what end? Players might be recruited as agents, obstacles, or pawns in a game whose rules they don't fully understand.

- **Privateer Complications**: A privateer captain with connections to the players has taken a prize that someone powerful wants back — or wants destroyed. The cargo might be valuable, dangerous, or both.

## Iques

Tags: state

Land of coral and black gold (micah). Gorgeous, towering cities, built atop beautiful aquaducts. City has plumbing. Big trading ports.

Currently dealing with trading issues due to pirates believed to be harboring in Blackport. Stifling trade.

## Istokos

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Bloodline aristocracy (noble houses ranked by magical potency)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Chargon (eel-descended humanoids)

**Population**: ~28,000 (Kargak ~12,000; island communities ~16,000)

**Stability**: Stable but rigid (the hierarchy is absolute)

**Core Values**: Bloodline purity, land-dwelling ascension, the rejection of the ancestral sea

The Chargon are an eel race—elongated humanoids with smooth, glistening skin that ranges from deep black to pale silver. Their bodies retain the flexibility of their aquatic ancestors; they can contort through spaces that would stop a human. Their eyes are large and dark, adapted for seeing in murky depths they now refuse to enter.

What makes Istokos unique isn't the Chargon themselves—it's what they've done to themselves.

Centuries ago, the Chargon were fully aquatic. They lived in the sea, breathed water, and knew nothing of the surface world. Then their shamans discovered bloodline magic—the ability to encode powers into their very lineage. Through generations of selective breeding and magical enhancement, they evolved themselves to live on land.

The transformation came with a price. Seawater now *weakens* the bloodline magic. A Chargon who immerses themselves in the ocean feels their inherited powers fade, their connection to their ancestors drain away. Extended exposure can strip a bloodline entirely, leaving an ordinary Chargon with no magic at all.

So the Chargon rejected the sea. They walk on land. They sail in ships designed to keep water away from their skin. They consider the ocean their enemy—the element they conquered and can never return to.

### The Bloodlines

Every noble Chargon family carries bloodline magic—powers encoded in their ancestry and passed down through generations. Different families carry different gifts, each traced to one of the **Primordial Eels**: mythological ancestors who first received magic from the deep gods.

**Major Bloodlines:**

**House Murk** (Sight Beyond) — Descended from the Eel of Depths. Their bloodline grants vision through darkness, illusion, and time. The most politically powerful house; their seers advise the throne.

**House Coil** (Body Mastery) — Descended from the Eel of Currents. Their bloodline grants extreme flexibility, speed, and the ability to squeeze through impossibly small spaces. Assassins and scouts.

**House Spark** (Lightning Blood) — Descended from the Electric Eel. Their bloodline lets them generate and control electrical discharges. Rare and feared; their touch can kill.

**House Slick** (Surface Walking) — Descended from the Eel of Shores. Their bloodline grants perfect balance, the ability to climb any surface, and resistance to being grasped. The first family to master land-dwelling.

**House Depths** (Pressure Sense) — Descended from the Abyssal Eel. Their bloodline lets them sense weight, density, and structural weaknesses. Engineers and builders.

**Lesser houses** carry diluted versions of these bloodlines, or combinations that produce weaker, stranger effects. Commoner Chargon have no bloodline magic at all—their ancestors either never received it or lost it to seawater exposure.

### The Hierarchy

Istokos society is organized entirely around bloodline potency. The stronger your ancestral magic, the higher your rank. This is determined through formal testing—**The Tasting**—conducted on every Chargon at adolescence.

The Tasting involves controlled seawater exposure. A Chargon's bloodline magic resists the dilution; the longer they can maintain their powers while partially immersed, the stronger their bloodline. Results are recorded, published, and determine that individual's place in society forever.

**King Thesslan IV** rules from Kargak—not because his family conquered or inherited, but because House Murk consistently produces the strongest bloodlines. The throne passes to whoever can prove the most potent Sight Beyond, regardless of direct descent.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Standard, advanced in certain magical applications

**Primary Exports**: Bloodline services (seers, assassins, engineers), specialized magical goods, fish (processed by commoners)

**Primary Imports**: Everything that requires water contact in production, mainland goods, laborers

Istokos runs on magical aristocracy. Noble houses hire out their bloodline abilities—Murk seers advise foreign kings, Coil assassins take contracts across the Western Isles, Spark warriors serve as elite mercenaries.

Commoner Chargon handle everything that requires water contact: fishing, sailing, cargo handling. They do the work nobles can't without risking their bloodlines. This creates a rigid class divide—nobles literally cannot do commoner jobs.

Trade flows through Kargak, the only major port. Foreign ships dock; Chargon merchants negotiate from the pier without boarding. Goods are transferred through human intermediaries who don't understand why the eel-folk won't step onto wet decks.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Elite specialists, weak conventional forces

**Enrollment**: Noble retinues (magic users), commoner conscripts (sailors and laborers)

Istokos doesn't field armies. They field bloodline specialists.

A single Spark noble can kill a dozen soldiers before they close the distance. A Coil assassin can infiltrate anywhere. A Murk seer can predict enemy movements with unsettling accuracy. But there aren't many of them, and they can't operate near significant water.

Naval power is handled by commoner crews on ships specifically designed to keep nobles dry. These vessels have covered decks, sealed compartments, and elaborate drainage systems. Nobles who must travel by sea stay in waterproof cabins, never touching the hull.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Western Isles, south of the Shattered Sea

**Biomes**: Tropical islands, warm waters

**Climate**: Warm and humid year-round

**Named Features**: Kargak (capital), various island territories

Istokos occupies a cluster of islands south of the main Shattered Sea. The waters here are warm, calm, and full of fish that the Chargon once hunted from below. Now they hunt from above—or hire others to hunt for them.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Kargak (~12,000)

**Major Islands**: Controlled by noble houses, each island essentially a house's domain

### Kargak

The capital rises from the largest island in Istokos. Stone buildings with elaborate drainage keep rainwater away from noble quarters. The streets are covered during monsoon season. Everything is designed to let the aristocracy move through their city without risking accidental water exposure.

The **Dry Palace** dominates the city center—a massive complex where the king holds court, nobles negotiate, and the Tasting ceremonies occur. The palace has never allowed seawater within its walls in four hundred years.

**King Thesslan IV** is seventy-three and his Sight Beyond remains formidable. He can see up to a week into possible futures—not certainty, but probability. This makes him nearly impossible to surprise and extremely difficult to plot against.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Dilution Crisis**

Bloodlines are weakening. Each generation, the Tasting results show slightly lower averages. Noble families quietly panic—is the magic fading? Are marriages diluting the lines? Some houses have begun extreme measures: refusing to let children touch any water, not even rain.

**Commoner Resentment**

The Chargon who do all the water-work receive none of the power. Commoner movements argue that the bloodline system is oppressive—that the nobles' fear of water has created an artificial hierarchy. These movements are ruthlessly suppressed, but they keep returning.

**The Sea's Call**

Some young Chargon feel drawn to the water despite everything. They slip away to swim, to dive, to remember what their ancestors were. Most return diminished—their bloodline damaged. Some don't return at all. The elders call this madness. The rebels call it freedom.

**External Pressure**

Other Western Isles powers would love to understand—and exploit—the bloodline system. Istokos maintains strict secrecy about how the magic works, but secrets leak. Someone, someday, will figure out how to weaponize seawater against the Chargon nobility.

### Notable Figures

**King Thesslan IV** — Ruler of Istokos, head of House Murk. His Sight Beyond is legendary; he's reportedly never been surprised by anything in sixty years. Ancient, patient, and quietly terrified that his visions show the bloodlines fading.

**Lady Seris of House Spark** — Most powerful living Spark noble. Her electrical discharges can kill at thirty paces. She's also the last Spark with full bloodline potency; her children all tested weaker.

**Venn the Drowned** — Commoner revolutionary. She deliberately immersed herself in the sea, emerged with no bloodline magic, and now preaches that the Chargon should abandon their self-imposed exile and return to the water. The nobility wants her dead.

**Prince Malkon** — King Thesslan's grandson and likely heir. His Sight Beyond tested abnormally weak. The court whispers about what this means for House Murk's future.

### History

**The Sea Time**: The Chargon lived fully aquatic for millennia. Their shamans discovered bloodline magic and began encoding powers into family lines.

**The Ascension** (~800 years ago): Over generations, Chargon shamans evolved their people to breathe air and walk on land. The cost: seawater now weakened the bloodline magic.

**The Rejection**: The first land-dwelling Chargon formally rejected the sea. They built Kargak, established the Tasting system, and created the rigid hierarchy that persists today.

**The Golden Age**: Istokos at its height—powerful bloodlines, wealthy from magical services, feared across the Western Isles.

**The Present**: Bloodlines weakening, commoners restless, young Chargon drawn to the forbidden sea. The structure that made Istokos powerful may be crumbling from within.

## Istora

Tags: state, winter-elf, istori, crimson-coast

The eastern successor to the unified Winter Elf kingdom, Istora is ruled by Queen Lamenrae—daughter of the murdered Istor XXVI and the first woman to claim the elven throne. What was once the summer half of a realm that stretched across the Crimson Coast is now a kingdom defined by grief, rage, and the slow grind of civil war.

### Overview

Istora follows the Sova Chyda river from its sources in the Suftos Hills to the Void. The sacred Winterwood anchors its spiritual life; the contested hills define its western border. The Istori were once renowned for regal calm, dignified restraint, and the patient wisdom of the long-lived. The succession crisis has burned much of that away.

Capital: **Tuur** (~3,000 population)

### Government

**Queen Lamenrae** rules—the first woman to hold the Winter Elf throne. Her claim rests on blood (daughter of the murdered king), necessity (no male heirs existed), and force of will. Traditionalists in both kingdoms consider her reign illegitimate; her supporters argue that tradition must evolve or the elves will die clinging to it.

Her court is divided between progressives (who see the crisis as opportunity for reform), revanchists (who want Klevnaf conquered and reunified), and pragmatists (who just want the kingdom to survive). Lamenrae herself is revanchist at heart—she wants her uncle dead and her kingdom restored—but she's learned to speak the progressives' language when it serves her purposes.

**The Council of Seasons** traditionally advised the monarch, with members representing different aspects of elven society. Lamenrae has gutted it, replacing deliberation with command. The remaining councilors are loyal but increasingly concerned about what their queen is becoming.

### Culture

The Istori are Winter Elves—the Istori subrace, to be precise, though they'd bristle at being called a "subrace" of anything. They live for centuries, mature slowly, and have traditionally valued patience, ceremony, and the long view. Foxes and snowy owls serve as companions and messengers, a tradition dating back to the first settlements.

The war has changed them. Outsiders describe the Istori as having "become much nastier"—centuries of regal calm replaced by siege mentality. Strangers are suspected; questions are threats; mercy is weakness. They still perform the old ceremonies, but the grace has gone out of them. What remains is going through motions, clinging to forms while their substance erodes.

**Theological innovation** has emerged from necessity. Traditional elven religion demands a male monarch leading the rites; Lamenrae's coronation required new interpretations. Istora's priests have developed justifications—arguments that the gods care about spirit rather than form, that tradition must serve the people rather than rule them. Klevnaf considers this heresy; Istora considers it survival.

The Winterwood remains central to Istori spirituality. Unlike Klevnaf's absolute prohibition on logging, Istora permits sustainable harvesting from the sacred forest's edges—another theological innovation that horrifies traditionalists. The elves say the ancestors understand the needs of the living. Whether they actually do is a matter of faith.

### Economy

Istora's economy is strained but functional. The Sova Chyda provides irrigation for limited agriculture; the Winterwood offers sustainable timber; eastern trade routes to the Dragon's Spine Coast remain open. The kingdom exports exquisitely carved woodwork—Istori craftsmen are renowned across Aboyinzu—and imports food, weapons, and metal goods.

Fishing supplements agriculture. The Istori have developed preservation techniques that allow catches to last through long winters. It's not enough; the kingdom runs a food deficit and depends on imports that the war makes expensive and unreliable.

### Military

Istora fields roughly 1,000 trained soldiers—more than Klevnaf due to larger population and better supply. Training occurs at Bejakørn, far from the front lines. Young elves arrive as civilians and leave as warriors, ready for deployment to the Suftos line.

Their equipment is superior—eastern trade keeps weapons and armor flowing—but terrain favors defense. Advancing across the Suftos Hills means attacking uphill into prepared positions. The war has stalemated because neither side can project force effectively.

**Special forces**: Istora has developed units specialized in shadow warfare—assassination, sabotage, intelligence gathering. Lamenrae denies their existence while deploying them aggressively. Several Klevnaf officials have died under mysterious circumstances.

### Relations

**With Klevnaf**: Active war. Border skirmishes are constant; major offensives occur when one side thinks it sees an opening. Both kingdoms are exhausting themselves without achieving decisive results.

**With Tangiern**: Complicated. Lamenrae has approached King Halvard about alliance, emphasizing that a progressive elven kingdom would be a better neighbor than traditionalist alternatives. Halvard has listened but not committed. Neither trusts the other, and both suspect any deal would leave them worse off.

**With the Dragon's Spine Coast**: Limited trade continues through Bejakørn. The eastern powers consider the Crimson Coast a backwater and its civil war a local problem.

### History

The Winter Elves arrived on the Crimson Coast millennia ago, displacing or absorbing earlier inhabitants. They built a civilization spanning the region—elegant in summer, enduring in winter—that lasted until the murder of Istor XXVI.

The succession crisis began when the old king (Istor XXV) died and his eldest son inherited. Within months, Istor XXVI was dead—poisoned, his daughter claimed, by his own brother Taoinor who wanted the throne. Taoinor denied it; the evidence was circumstantial; the kingdom split along existing fault lines. Those who valued tradition over evidence followed Taoinor; those who believed Lamenrae (or simply wanted change) followed her.

The split wasn't clean. Families divided; villages were purged; the Suftos Hills became a killing ground. The initial chaos has settled into attritional warfare that neither side can win or end.

### What Outsiders Notice

- The cold courtesy that feels like it might turn hostile at any moment
- Foxes and owls that watch strangers with uncanny intelligence
- Architecture that combines elegance with recent fortification
- The way conversations stop when outsiders approach
- References to "the usurper" delivered with genuine hatred

### Adventure Hooks

- Lamenrae wants deniable agents to infiltrate Klevnaf—information, assassination, or sabotage
- Something in the Winterwood is behaving strangely; the priests won't discuss it but seem frightened
- A moderate faction in both kingdoms wants to negotiate peace; they need neutral intermediaries
- The Shrine of Reflections has begun producing prophecies that terrify those who hear them

## Iylovia

Tags: state

Links: States

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Council of Silence (nominal)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Gyv

**Population**: ~20,000

**Stability**: Unstable

**Core Values**: Self-preservation, cunning, distrust

The Gyv cannot trust each other. Their Silver Tongue ability—subtle mind manipulation through conversation—means every interaction could be an attempt at control. So they built systems to govern without trust.

The **Council of Silence** meets in Theochori, in a chamber where speaking is forbidden. Proposals are submitted in writing. Votes cast by sealed ballot. Debates happen through intermediate written exchanges over days or weeks. It's agonizingly slow, but it prevents any single Gyv from manipulating the council through speech.

In peacetime, this worked. Representatives from across Iylovia would gather, deliberate through written protocol, and reach decisions. The process was glacial but fair—or at least, evenly manipulated.

In war, it's failing. Decisions take weeks while people die. The western settlements have started ignoring Theochori, making their own calls. The eastern settlements barely participate at all, too busy securing their own escape routes. The council still meets, still debates in silence, but its authority erodes with every passing season.


### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

**Primary Exports**: Timber, woven goods, forest medicines, rare plants

**Primary Imports**: Metal, grain, weapons

Iylovia's forests provide abundant resources: timber, plant fibers for cloth and rope, medicinal herbs, honey, rare dyes. The Gyv are skilled weavers and their textiles are prized across the region.

The problem is trade. The western regions are war zones—no merchants venture there. The center is isolated. Only the eastern coast has reliable trade access, through ports that connect to the Gray Isles and beyond.

This creates economic imbalance. The east grows wealthy while the west starves for supplies. Eastern merchants charge western settlements premium prices for metal and weapons. The west accuses the east of profiteering from their suffering. The east points out that they're the only ones keeping any trade flowing at all.

Both sides are probably right. Both sides are probably manipulating the situation for advantage.


### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Mandatory (in western regions), Voluntary (elsewhere)

The Gyv have no conventional army. They never needed one—their manipulation worked better than swords. When the Silzar invaded, the Gyv discovered that mind control doesn't stop a charging mammoth.

Now they fight through **ambush and guerrilla tactics**. Small groups of fighters lurk in the forests, waiting for Silzar raiding parties to push too deep. When targets are isolated, the Gyv emerge, get close, and start talking. A minute of conversation can turn enemies against each other. The forests become killing grounds—not through superior force, but through confusion, betrayal, and manipulated rage.

The western settlements have developed the most effective fighters. They've learned to coordinate—somewhat—despite their cultural inability to trust. They use written signals, prearranged plans, and strict protocols that minimize conversation between allies. It's crude compared to how other armies operate, but it's better than nothing.

The east has almost no military presence. They're counting on the west to hold. Or they're counting on negotiating separately if the west falls.


### Geography

**Location**: Forested region east of Silwyn, with eastern coastal access

**Biomes**: Temperate forest, swampland, hills, coastal lowlands

**Named Entities**: Gwyrdyr Forest, Leering Wood, Astrella Forest, Bittermarsh, Spinefir Forest, Terror Grove, Hagwood, Howlwood, Graymist Forest

**Rivers & Lakes**: River Sorrow, Gwyrdyr River, Skleidyon River, Odrigi River, Savarin River, Torpastros River, Graymist Lake

**Adjacent Waters**: Eastern coastal waters (access to Gray Isles and beyond)

Iylovia is defined by its forests. Dense woodland covers most of the nation, broken by rivers, marshland, and scattered settlements. The forests provide the Gyv's only military advantage—close quarters where their manipulation works, cover for ambushes, terrain that mammoths cannot navigate.

The western border with Silwyn runs through Gwyrdyr Forest, the most contested territory in the region. The Skleidyon River cuts through the center, passing through Theochori. The eastern regions slope down toward the coast, where the forests thin and trade ports connect to the wider world.


### Political Geography

**Capital**: Theochori

**Cities**: Theochori (capital), Krania (northern fortress), Balastafika (central)

**Major Towns**: Malesi, Ambelokipol, Mega Kalitsi, Ayia Paraskevi (western front); Skala Tuzlas, Varvares, Andigonos (eastern coast)

**Notable Sites**: Cheykiov (fortress ruins)

Settlement patterns reflect the war:

**The Western Front** (Gwyrdyr Forest region): Dense clusters of towns along the River Sorrow and Gwyrdyr River. Krania anchors the north—a fortress-city that has held against repeated Silzar pressure. Settlements like Malesi, Ambelokipol, and Ayia Paraskevi form a defensive network, supporting each other through the forests. These are the stubborn Gyv, the ones who refused to flee when the plains fell.

**The Center** (around Theochori): The capital sits on the Skleidyon River, protected by Spinefir Forest to the north and Terror Grove to the south. Balastafika serves as a secondary administrative center. This region is relatively safe—far from the front, connected by river trade.

**The Eastern Coast** (Odrigi and Savarin rivers): Trading settlements with port access. Skala Tuzlas, Varvares, and Andigonos handle most of Iylovia's external commerce. These communities are wealthier than the west, more connected to the outside world, and increasingly willing to consider their own survival over national unity.

**Cheykiov**: Fortress ruins in the hills southwest of Spinefir Forest. This defensive position fell early in the war—not to Silzar assault, but to betrayal. Someone manipulated the defenders. Someone opened the gates. The details are disputed; everyone blames everyone else. Cheykiov stands as a monument to Gyv dysfunction: they couldn't hold even a fortified position because they couldn't trust their own garrison.


### Primary Conflicts

**The War with Silwyn**

For forty-odd years, the Silzar have been pushing the Gyv back. What was once a Gyv domain stretching to the southern desert is now Silwyn—plains controlled by mammoth-riding ogres who've learned to counter Gyv manipulation with ear protection and overwhelming noise.

The Gyv hold the forests. The Silzar raid the edges. Neither side can break the stalemate, but the Gyv are losing slowly. Every raid costs them people, supplies, morale. The Silzar can afford attrition; the Gyv cannot.

**East vs. West**

The western Gyv are fighting and dying. The eastern Gyv are trading and preparing escape routes. Neither trusts the other.

The west accuses the east of abandonment, profiteering, perhaps even secret deals with the Silzar. The east accuses the west of strategic incompetence, wasted resources, perhaps even provoking the Silzar unnecessarily.

Both accusations probably contain truth. Both sides are certainly manipulating the narrative. The Council of Silence is supposed to mediate, but the council is too slow, too compromised, too far from either faction to matter.

**Internal Betrayal**

Someone is selling information to the Silzar. Probably multiple someones. Raid patterns suggest the Silzar know things they shouldn't—patrol schedules, supply routes, which settlements have weak defenses.

Everyone suspects everyone. Accusations fly constantly. Some are genuine attempts to find traitors; others are schemes to eliminate rivals by branding them collaborators. The paranoia feeds itself: the more accusations, the more distrust, the more betrayal seems inevitable, the more people consider betraying before they're betrayed.

**The Reform Movement**

Some Gyv argue that manipulation itself is the problem. They point to the Marked—Gyv who've had their Silver Tongue suppressed—as potential leaders. "Let the trustworthy lead us. Let us learn to speak honestly, at least among ourselves."

The traditionalists call this cultural suicide. The reformers call it survival. The argument has no resolution because neither side trusts the other's motives.


### History

**The Dominion**

The Gyv once ruled far beyond the forests. The plains that are now Silwyn? Gyv territory. The Silzar who lived there were scattered, weak, hunted for sport or enslaved for labor. The Gyv built settlements across the grasslands, managed the land, took what they wanted from the primitive ogres who couldn't resist their manipulation.

This lasted for generations. The Gyv assumed it would last forever.

**The Covenant**

Then the Silzar formed their alliance with the mammoth herds, and everything changed.

The Gyv didn't understand the threat at first. Ogres riding beasts? Amusing. Let them play at cavalry. A few words in the right ears would send them fighting each other.

But the mammoths trumpeted. The sound drowned out conversation. The Silzar wore coverings over their ears. And the ballistae struck from distances where no Gyv voice could reach.

The first Gyv settlements on the plains fell quickly. Then more. Then the retreat became a rout.

**The Retreat**

The Gyv fell back to the forests, where their abilities still functioned. Dense trees provided cover for ambush. Close quarters let them get within speaking range. The forest itself became their weapon—not magically, but tactically.

But they'd lost the plains. Lost settlements their families had held for generations. Lost slaves, livestock, resources, everything that wasn't portable. The Gyv who escaped to the forests arrived as refugees, crowding into communities that barely tolerated each other in good times.

**The Stalemate**

For decades now, the war has ground on. The Silzar control the plains. The Gyv hold the forests. The border is written in blood—raids, ambushes, atrocities on both sides.

The Gyv are losing. Slowly. Inexorably. They can't replace losses as fast as the Silzar inflict them. They can't coordinate defense because they can't trust each other. They're bleeding out, and everyone knows it, and no one can agree on what to do about it.


### Relations

**Silwyn**: War. The Silzar raid constantly; the Gyv ambush what they can. Neither side negotiates—the Silzar don't trust Gyv words, and the Gyv can't agree on what to offer anyway.

**Eros**: Avoidance. The haunted forest lies north of Iylovia, but the Gyv ignore it. The Nemo are terrifying, and unlike the Silzar, they cannot be manipulated. Some desperate voices suggest reaching out to Eros, pointing the Nemo at the Silzar somehow. Most consider this insane.

**Gray Isles and Beyond**: Trade. The eastern ports connect Iylovia to the outside world. Merchants come for timber and textiles; they bring metal and news. These relationships are Iylovia's lifeline—and the east's leverage over the rest of the nation.

**Everyone Else**: Suspicion. The Gyv reputation precedes them. Everyone knows they're manipulators. Trust is impossible. Trade happens anyway, because money talks louder than reputation, but no one deals with a Gyv without counting their fingers afterward.


### Player Hooks

**Nothing They Say Is True**: Players entering Iylovia will be manipulated. Everyone has an angle. The question isn't whether to trust the Gyv—you can't—but whose manipulation serves your interests.

**The Informant Hunt**: Someone is selling secrets to the Silzar. Multiple factions want the traitor found—some to punish them, others to silence them, others to take over the operation. Players could be hired by any faction, set against each other, or caught in the crossfire.

**East vs. West**: The eastern Gyv might hire players to sabotage western defenses, hastening collapse so they can negotiate separately. The western Gyv might hire players to prevent eastern betrayal. Both sides are lying about their true goals.

**Cheykiov**: The fortress ruins hold secrets. What really happened there? Who opened the gates? Some say treasure lies buried in the collapse. Others say something worse than treasure was sealed away.

**The Reform Movement**: The Marked—Gyv without manipulation powers—are trying to build a different kind of society. They might seek outside help: protectors, witnesses, people who can verify that they're actually trustworthy. Or their whole movement might be an elaborate scheme by Speakers pretending to be reformed.

**The Nemo Option**: Some desperate Gyv are seriously considering contact with Eros. They need someone willing to enter the haunted forest and speak to things that should not be spoken to. They're offering everything they have. They're probably also lying about what "everything" means.

## Jeh Bli

Tags: state, citystate, orc, farlands

The only city in the northern Farlands—an orc settlement on the shores of Lake Kro Nymos, five hundred miles from the nearest civilization. The Xi'ivrach who live here are among the strangest orcs in the known world: stone-weighted, scar-covered, and utterly devoted to gods that might actually answer.

### Overview

Jeh Bli is less a city than a sprawling religious complex that happens to house twelve thousand people. Stone towers rise from the lakeshore, their bases submerged, their tops hung with offerings. Bridges of carved basalt connect platform neighborhoods built on artificial islands. Everything is heavy, deliberate, built to endure—and built to sink, eventually, into the sacred waters below.

The Xi'ivrach came here centuries ago, following visions that promised a place where the gods would be close. They found Kro Nymos and its central island, *Ghural-Kro*, where something answered their prayers. What exactly it is, the Xi'ivrach don't say—perhaps they don't know. But their priests speak in Deoric, the language of truth, and their prayers produce results. That's enough for them.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Theocratic oligarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Xi'ivrach orcs

**Population**: ~12,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

The city is ruled by the **Keth-Vorun**, the "Bearers of Weight"—seven high priests who have survived the trials of ascending to the highest ranks of their faith. Each wears ceremonial stone armor weighing hundreds of pounds, which they never remove. They sleep in it, eat in it, conduct all business in it. The weight is their proof of devotion.

Decisions are made by consensus among the Keth-Vorun, with the eldest serving as tiebreaker. In practice, most governance is handled by lower-ranking priests; the Keth-Vorun concern themselves primarily with religious matters and interpreting the will of the gods.

**Core Values**:

- Devotion expressed through burden—the more you carry, the closer you are to the divine
- Scars as sacred text, earned through trials of pain and endurance
- The lake provides; the lake is owed

### The Faith

The Xi'ivrach worship what they call *Keth-Ghural*, "the Weight Beneath"—a divine presence they believe resides in or beneath Lake Kro Nymos. They don't personify it with faces or stories. It simply is: vast, patient, hungry for devotion.

Worship takes the form of burden. Children begin wearing stone beads at age three. By adulthood, a devout Xi'ivrach carries fifty pounds of sacred stone at all times—jewelry, clothing, woven stone cord in their hair. The priests carry more. The Keth-Vorun carry weights that would crush an ordinary orc.

The scarification is equally important. Every significant moment in a Xi'ivrach's life is marked on their skin—births, deaths, victories, failures, visions, answered prayers. The scars are raised with ash rubbed into the wounds, creating ridged patterns that cover most of their bodies by middle age. Reading someone's scars is an intimate act; lying about what your scars mean is the closest thing the Xi'ivrach have to blasphemy.

### Deoric Magic

The high priests of Jeh Bli have mastered fragments of Deoric, the language of truth that underlies reality itself. Speaking Deoric is enormously difficult—most mortals cannot form the sounds at all—but the Xi'ivrach have spent generations training their mouths and throats to approximate it.

When a priest speaks a Deoric word, reality pays attention. The language doesn't grant wishes; it describes what is true, and the universe adjusts. A priest who can say "healed" in Deoric doesn't cast a healing spell—they state a fact that wasn't true until they said it.

This power is limited. No one in Jeh Bli speaks more than a handful of Deoric words, and each one requires years of training. But even a few words of truth in a world of lies gives the Xi'ivrach an advantage that's kept them safe in their isolation for centuries.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

**Primary Exports**:
- Carved stone (religious objects, weapons, architectural elements)
- Deoric blessings (extremely expensive, rarely offered to outsiders)
- Ridgeback hides (from mountain hunts)
- Preserved fish (*kro-zheth* oil is valuable as fuel and waterproofing)

**Primary Imports**:
- Metal (they have almost none locally)
- Textiles (stone doesn't replace all clothing)
- Medicines (the lake provides food, not pharmacology)
- Occasional luxury goods for the temples

The Xi'ivrach are largely self-sufficient. The lake provides fish, the Butterfly Forest provides game and timber (during careful daytime expeditions), and the mountain streams provide water. What they can't produce, they trade for—primarily with merchants brave or foolish enough to make the five-hundred-mile journey from Lenora.

Trade caravans arrive perhaps twice a year. The Xi'ivrach pay in carved stone, which is valued by elven collectors for its alien aesthetics, and occasionally in blessings—a Deoric word spoken over a newborn, a sacred scar cut into a merchant's flesh to ward off misfortune. These services are expensive. They're also genuine.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (but all adults are trained)

Every Xi'ivrach learns to fight. The weight training that begins in childhood produces warriors of tremendous strength and endurance. A Xi'ivrach soldier can march in stone armor that would exhaust a human in minutes. Their weapons are stone-headed—axes, hammers, maces—and they hit harder than metal in Xi'ivrach hands.

The city maintains no standing army; it doesn't need one. No enemy has ever reached Jeh Bli. The wilderness filters out anyone who isn't determined enough to cross it, and anyone that determined is either trading or dying in the Butterfly Forest overnight.

The priests are the elite. Those who've mastered even one Deoric word are walking weapons—capable of stating "broken" or "stopped" or "dead" and having it be true. The Keth-Vorun could end most battles before they begin. They've never had to.

### Geography

**Location**: Northern Farlands, shore of Lake Kro Nymos

**Biomes**: Mountain valley, lake shore, forest edge

**Named Entities**:
- *Ghural-Kro* (the island) — sacred, forbidden
- *Kro Gheth* (the north shore) — sacred ground for shamanic rites
- The Sunset Mountains — western boundary, hunting grounds
- The Butterfly Forest — eastern boundary, dangerous

**Rivers & Lakes**: Lake Kro Nymos (the foundation of everything)

**Adjacent Waters**: Troikan Strait (distant, irrelevant)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Jeh Bli (the only settlement)

**Cities**: None besides the capital

**Towns**: None

**Villages**: Seasonal fishing camps on the lakeshore, abandoned in winter

### Neighbors and Relations

The Xi'ivrach have no neighbors in any meaningful sense. The elven kingdoms are five hundred miles south; they've never had a conflict because they've never had contact. Ios lies across the Troikan Strait, but the Xi'ivrach have no interest in the stone-doll builders and their failing civilization.

Occasional traders come from Lenora. The Xi'ivrach treat them with formal hospitality, trade fairly, and make clear they should leave before sunset. The traders obey.

The orcs are aware of the wider world. They're not curious about it. They found what they were looking for when they found Kro Nymos. Everything else is distraction.

### Primary Conflicts

- **The Island Stirs**: Something on *Ghural-Kro* has become more active lately. The offerings sink faster. The shamans return from *Kro Gheth* with new scars they didn't cut themselves. The Keth-Vorun are praying more and eating less.

- **The Youth Question**: Some younger Xi'ivrach chafe at the isolation. They want to know what's beyond the forest, across the strait, south of the mountains. The elders say there's nothing worth knowing. The tension is growing.

- **The Forgetting**: An old shaman died last year, and with her went knowledge of a Deoric word no one else could speak. It's happened before, will happen again. The language of truth is slipping away, one death at a time.

### History

The Xi'ivrach arrived at Kro Nymos approximately four centuries ago, part of a larger orc migration whose origins are lost to oral history. They say they were called—that shamans dreamed of a still lake where the gods waited. They followed the dreams across unmapped wilderness until they found it.

What happened when they arrived is unclear. The Xi'ivrach don't record history the way other peoples do; they record it in scars, and the first generation's scars died with them. What's known is that they made contact with something in or beneath the lake, and that something made a bargain with them.

The terms of the bargain, if there are terms, aren't public. The Xi'ivrach are allowed to live here. The lake provides. The island is forbidden. Offerings are made. The priests learn Deoric. That's all outsiders know.

Four centuries later, nothing has changed. The Xi'ivrach aren't expanding, aren't building empire, aren't interested in the affairs of the wider world. They're waiting for something. They might not know what.

### Player Hooks

- **The Deoric Trade**: A dying merchant offers the party a fortune to escort him to Jeh Bli. He's heard the priests can speak health into existence. The journey is five hundred miles through monster territory. The priests might refuse him. He doesn't care.

- **The Exile**: A young Xi'ivrach has fled the city, her sacred stones abandoned, her scarifications defaced with self-inflicted burns. She's trying to reach Lenora. She says something is wrong at Kro Nymos—that the island is waking up, that the Keth-Vorun are preparing for something terrible. She might be telling the truth. She might be insane from guilt. She might be both.

- **The Empty Word**: A Deoric fragment has surfaced in Lenora—a stone tablet inscribed with a word no one can read. The elves want to sell it. The Xi'ivrach want it back. Various other parties want it for their own reasons. It's worth killing over, if it's genuine.

- **The Trade Caravan**: A merchant house is organizing its annual expedition to Jeh Bli and hiring guards. Good pay, dangerous work. The merchants know the route, know the risks, know exactly how not to anger the orcs. All the party has to do is keep them alive.

## Joswik

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans, mixed

**Population**: 250,000

**Stability**: Unstable

**Core Values**: Tradition, community, resilience

- Ruled by Prince Edric, the youngest of the three siblings who inherited Ofrenia. He's kind, well-meaning, and completely out of his depth.
- Edric inherited the southern kingdom—rich in minerals and farmland, but lacking Tornia's trade networks or Sylke's ruthless efficiency. He's tried to rule fairly and has mostly succeeded in pleasing no one.
- The population is fiercely independent, descended from miners and farmers who carved their living from difficult land. They don't love Edric, but they don't hate him either.
- With Alisandra now controlling Tornia, Joswik is the last independent kingdom on Ofrenia. Everyone knows what's coming.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Iron, copper, stone, grain, livestock

**Primary Imports**: Manufactured goods, timber, luxury items

- The Gnomestone Mountains yield iron and copper that once supplied all of Ofrenia. Now those minerals flow to whoever will buy them—and increasingly, Alisandra's agents are the only buyers offering fair prices.
- The Oshkensi Plateau supports sheep and cattle in numbers that feed both Joswik and its neighbors. The herders there answer to no one but themselves.
- Trade has suffered since the war. Merchants fear traveling through occupied Tornia, and the route through Myastor adds weeks and costs.
- Joswik could be prosperous, but prosperity requires stability that Edric can't provide and peace that Alisandra won't allow.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Low

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- Joswik has never maintained a large standing army. The population is spread thin across mining towns and farming villages, and pulling workers away disrupts everything.
- What military exists is poorly equipped, poorly trained, and knows it can't stop Sylke if Alisandra decides to invade.
- The one bright spot: the terrain. The Gnomestone Mountains and the interior hills favor defense. An invader would need to fight through passes and valleys where numbers matter less.
- Some nobles have begun hiring mercenaries, but there aren't enough gold and there aren't enough mercenaries to make a real difference.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Ofrenia, the island at the heart of the Middle Sea

**Biomes**: Mountain ranges, rolling hills, fertile valleys, coastal lowlands, plateau grasslands

**Named Entities**: Gnomestone Mountains, Oshkensi Plateau, Gulf of Joswik, Meadow of Laughing

**Rivers & Lakes**: Multiple rivers flowing from the Gnomestone Mountains to the coasts

**Adjacent Waters**: The Middle Sea, Gulf of Joswik, the Passage (north)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Padena

**Cities**: Padena, Valeria

**Towns**: Trenton, Greymill, Sculpton, Shardak, Canfaldo, Banksted, Clifton, Ellismare, Osperton, Dagmanton, Welldale, Pasterton

**Villages**: Mining villages in the mountains, farming villages in the valleys, herding communities on the plateau

### Primary Conflicts

**The Coming Storm**

Everyone in Joswik knows Alisandra is coming. The question is when, not if. Edric has tried diplomacy—his sister won't negotiate. He's tried building alliances—no one wants to challenge Sylke. He's tried preparing defenses—there isn't enough money or time. The kingdom waits for the axe to fall.

**The Refugee Crisis**

Tornian refugees have been flooding into Joswik since the occupation—nobles who fled with their wealth, soldiers who refused to serve Alisandra, common folk who saw which way the wind was blowing. They bring skills and gold, but also mouths to feed and resentments to manage. Some want Edric to lead a liberation; others just want safety.

**The Mountain Lords**

The mining towns of the Gnomestone Mountains have always been semi-independent, paying taxes but governing themselves. With Joswik's central authority weakening, some are asking why they should pay taxes at all. Edric needs their minerals for any hope of defense, but he can't afford to antagonize them.

**The Oshkensi Question**

The herders of Oshkensi Plateau care nothing for lowland politics. They've weathered regime changes before and expect to weather this one. If Alisandra conquers Joswik, the plateau folk will simply retreat into their grasslands and wait. This attitude infuriates Edric's advisors but might actually be the wisest response.

### History

Joswik was the old king's favorite project—the troubled southern province he'd spent decades developing. When he divided Ofrenia among his children, he gave Joswik to Edric, his youngest, believing the gentlest son needed the most guidance and the kingdom with the most potential.

He was half right. Edric is gentle, thoughtful, and genuinely cares about his people. He's also indecisive, easily manipulated, and lacks the ruthlessness that ruling requires. His father's advisors have mostly died or retired; the replacements serve their own interests first.

For the first years after the division, Joswik muddled along. The mines produced, the farms yielded, and Edric presided over a kingdom that functioned despite rather than because of his rule. He maintained cordial relations with both siblings, hoping the family could eventually reconcile.

That hope died when Alisandra invaded Tornia. Now Edric rules the last free kingdom on Ofrenia, surrounded by his sister's territory, watching refugees pour across his borders, and knowing he lacks the strength to stop what's coming.

His one advantage: he hasn't given Alisandra a reason to invade. No insults, no provocations, no alliances with her enemies. If she moves against Joswik, she'll be the naked aggressor—and even her cowed subjects might balk at conquering a brother who offered nothing but peace. Edric is betting that shame, at least, might slow her down.

He's probably wrong. But he doesn't know what else to do.

## Jüt

Tags: state, port, westwilds

A trading city of roughly twenty thousand people positioned on Kettle Bay, where the Westwilds coast meets the sea. Jüt is the region's window to the outside world—the only significant port on this stretch of coastline.

### Overview

Jüt survives by being useful to everyone. The city doesn't produce much itself; instead, it serves as a crossroads where the Westwilds' strange goods meet the broader markets. Seyiki gnomes bring crystal fragments and dragonfly chitin from the desert pillars. Melodians arrive with resonant wood and rare botanicals. Lacirean caravans carry spring water and Grayl-amber. Ships from the Western Isles bring metal, textiles, and everything the land-locked interior can't produce.

The city is built into the base of the Cliffs of Shondromos—dramatic pale limestone riddled with caves. The harbor is carved from a natural inlet where the cliffs recede, providing shelter from storms and a natural barrier against landward attack.

### Government

The Jüt Council consists of representatives from the twelve most powerful trading houses, rotating through leadership positions annually. Decisions require majority agreement; controversial matters need supermajority.

The system is corrupt, factional, and inefficient—but stable. The trading houses have too much to lose from instability to let disputes escalate beyond economic warfare. Deadlocks resolve through negotiation, bribery, or occasionally strategic assassination (considered gauche but not unprecedented).

Citizenship ties to property ownership or guild membership. Non-citizens can live and work freely but have no political voice—which suits most traders, since they're not staying anyway.

### Culture

**Pragmatism:** Jüt's defining virtue. The city exists to move goods between people who'd otherwise never interact. That requires tolerance, flexibility, and a willingness to overlook inconvenient details.

**Polyglot:** Half a dozen languages echo through the streets. Gnomes bargain with orcs bargain with human sailors bargain with elven merchants. Everyone learns at least two languages; many speak five or six badly.

**Rough Edges:** Jüt isn't beautiful or cultured. The architecture is functional—stone and timber built to withstand salt air, not to impress. The wealthy live in fortified compounds; everyone else makes do. Crime is managed, not eliminated; the Council considers it a cost of doing business.

### Economy

Jüt is pure trade:

**From Seyiki:** Twyl crystal fragments, dragonfly chitin, locust products, occasional Postronamas artifacts

**From Melodia:** Pipe-willow wood, unique botanicals, Melodian crafts

**From Lacire:** Waters of Vyowehr, Grayl-amber, Walking Forest herbs

**From the Western Isles:** Metal (especially iron and steel), textiles, spices, luxury goods, weapons

**From the Orc States:** Furs, worked leather, gemstones (sometimes cursed), mercenary contracts

The fishing fleet is substantial; salted fish is one of the few things Jüt actually produces rather than just handles.

### Military

Being the only port makes Jüt everyone's target.

**The Militia:** Well-funded, well-equipped, experienced. Service is prestigious—one of the few paths to citizenship for outsiders.

**The Walls:** Solid if not massive. The cliffs at the city's back limit approach angles.

**Protection Payments:** Jüt pays tribute in multiple directions—to orc states that could overwhelm them if united, to Western Isles captains who guarantee safe passage, to whoever else needs paying this year. Expensive, but cheaper than war.

**The Fleet:** Small but capable. Enough to discourage casual piracy and escort valuable shipments.

### Relations

**The Seyiki:** Primary trading partners from inland. Relations are commercial and cordial; neither side has reason to make trouble.

**The Orc States:** Complicated. Some trade; some raid; some do both depending on the season. The protection payments help, but tensions are never entirely absent.

**Western Isles:** Vital maritime connection. Pirates are a constant problem; legitimate traders are essential.

**Lacire:** Valuable but distant. The caravans are irregular, and the Lacireans are strange, but their goods command premium prices.

### The Cliffs

The Cliffs of Shondromos are riddled with caves at waterline—thousands of them, forming a honeycomb that smugglers have used for generations. The militia plays endless cat-and-mouse with operators who know the cave systems better than any guard. Officially, the caves belong to the city. Unofficially, they're ungovernable.

### Notable Locations

**The Harbor:** The city's heart—always busy, always noisy, always dangerous after dark.

**The Cliff Warrens:** Where the poorest citizens live, in structures built against or into the limestone.

**The Trading Houses:** Fortified compounds where the wealthy conduct business behind walls.

**Kettle Bay:** The broad, sheltered inlet—excellent anchorage surrounded by smuggler-riddled caves.

### Player Hooks

- Seek passage to the Western Isles
- Find buyers for exotic Westwilds goods
- Navigate the city's factional politics for profit or justice
- Hunt smugglers in the Shondromos caves
- Defend against a pirate raid or orc warband
- Investigate what's living in the deepest caves—something that's making the usual smugglers nervous

## Kadroka

Tags: state, nation, dragon-ruled

Kadroka is a savanna state in southwestern Central Aboyinzu, ruled by the paralyzed dragon Melekas from the capital city of Ponoigari. It is a land of contradictions: prosperous but besieged, well-governed but under the thumb of a dragon, connected to major trade routes but functionally landlocked by hostile orcs.

### Geography

Kadroka occupies the transitional zone between the dense forests of the northwest (Penor Syalki, Sokorgül) and the Ramakemizi Mountains to the east. The terrain is largely open savanna and grassland, punctuated by rivers draining toward the southwestern coast.

**Borders:**
- **North**: Penor Syalki forest and Phaerhom (fairy territory)
- **East**: Ramakemizi Mountains (beyond which lies the Dalizi Confederation)
- **South/Southwest**: Tythikerys (blood orc territory) and the Agony Stones
- **West**: Sokorgül forest and Bluesong Bay (coastal access blocked by Tythikerys)

Despite proximity to the coast, Kadroka has no functional maritime access. The Tytheri blood orcs control the entire coastline of Bluesong Bay, effectively landlocking the state.

### The Dragon-King

Kadroka is ruled by Melekas the Stillbound, an ancient dragon who has been paralyzed and fused to the bedrock beneath Ponoigari for nearly eight hundred years. The dragon was crippled by contact with the Agony Stones and now rules through cunning, information networks, and the cultivation of loyal agents rather than physical might.

This arrangement is unusual among dragon-ruled territories. Most dragons dominate through the threat of aerial assault and mobility. Melekas can do neither—but the dragon has had eight centuries to perfect the art of ruling without them.

### Population

Kadroka is home to a mixed population of humans and naga, with smaller numbers of other peoples drawn by trade or service to the dragon.

**Naga** hold significant influence, serving in Melekas's inner circle as advisors, administrators, and interpreters of draconic will. Dragons and naga share ancient connections, and many naga families have served the Stillbound for generations.

**Humans** form the majority of the common population—farmers, merchants, soldiers, and craftspeople. Some resent naga prominence; others have prospered under the stability that draconic rule provides.

### Major Settlements

**Ponoigari** (Capital): Built around and upon the immobilized body of Melekas. The palace complex incorporates the dragon's petrified form, and the living head serves as the center of government. Population concentrated here for protection and proximity to power.

**Sukinada** (City): Eastern city near the Ramakemizi Mountains, serving as the primary gateway for trade with the Dalizi Confederation. Mountain passes connect through to Chechol and the eastern territories.

**Ibasaka** (City): Northern city on the Noara River, positioned between the capital and the forest territories to the north.

**Dranabaru** (City): Southern city near the Bloodswamp, uncomfortably close to Tytheri raiding routes but strategically important for monitoring orc movements.

**Arigaska** (City): Southernmost major settlement, essentially a fortified frontier town that bears the brunt of Tytheri raids.

### Economy

Kadroka's economy is shaped by its geographic constraints:

**Agriculture**: The savanna supports extensive cattle herding and grain cultivation. Kadrokan beef and leather are traded eastward over the mountains.

**Mountain Trade**: With coastal access denied, virtually all external trade flows through the Ramakemizi passes to Dalizi and Chechol. This makes the eastern cities disproportionately wealthy and important.

**Dragon Taxes**: Melekas demands substantial tribute, siphoning significant wealth to maintain the apparatus of draconic rule. The dragon claims this is protection money, and given the alternative, few argue.

### Military

Kadrokan military forces are primarily defensive, organized to repel Tytheri raids rather than project power beyond the borders.

**The Stillwatch**: Elite soldiers who guard Ponoigari and the dragon directly. Fanatically loyal, well-equipped, and trained to coordinate with the dragon's fire in urban defense.

**Border Patrols**: Light cavalry and infantry that screen the southwestern frontier. They cannot prevent all raids but provide early warning and pursue retreating orc bands.

**Naga Units**: Specialized forces leveraging naga abilities. Particularly effective in the Bloodswamp region, where their affinity for water and swamp terrain provides advantages.

### The Tytheri Threat

Kadroka exists in a state of constant low-intensity warfare with Tythikerys. The blood orcs raid regularly—taking slaves, cattle, and supplies back to their territory. Kadrokan forces can defend major settlements and pursue raiders, but cannot eliminate the threat at its source.

The dynamic is simple: Melekas cannot fly, so Melekas cannot burn the orcs from the sky. Kadrokan ground forces struggle in the rough terrain of Tythikerys and dare not approach the Agony Stones that sustain the orc's Ichor addiction. The orcs know this and grow bolder each generation.

Melekas schemes constantly for a way to break this impasse. The dragon would pay almost any price for a solution—and adventurers willing to investigate the Agony Stones, disrupt Tytheri Ichor harvesting, or find some other lever against the orcs would find a powerful patron in the Stillbound.

### External Relations

**Dalizi Confederation** (East): Kadroka's primary trading partner and diplomatic contact. Dalizi merchants are common in Sukinada, and the confederation provides an economic lifeline that prevents Kadrokan isolation.

**Chechol** (Northeast): Closer than Dalizi proper, Chechol serves as an intermediary for some trade and communication. Relations are cordial but not close.

**Phaerhom** (North): The fairy enclave in the Morygun Mountains is officially neutral toward Kadroka. Unofficially, the fae find the situation amusing—a dragon trapped in stone, harassed by orcs—and have shown no interest in helping.

**Tythikerys** (Southwest): Open hostility. No diplomatic contact. The orcs raid; Kadroka defends; the cycle continues.

### Adventure Hooks

- **The Dragon's Offer**: Melekas seeks agents willing to investigate the Agony Stones—specifically, to find a cure for the stillbinding. The reward would be substantial. The risk is becoming part of the Dying One.

- **Ichor Disruption**: Can the Tytheri's addiction be weaponized against them? Someone who could poison or redirect the Ichor supply might break the orc threat permanently. Or create something worse.

- **Mountain Politics**: The eastern cities grow wealthy while the frontier bleeds. Some in Sukinada whisper about independence, or at least reduced obligations to a capital that cannot protect them.

- **Naga Intrigue**: Human resentment of naga influence creates opportunities for those willing to play factional politics under the dragon's watchful eye.

## Kazül

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Absolute monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Oznak, Glorindian (Humans)

**Population**: 100,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Tradition, religion, wealth, night/day

- Kazül is ruled by **King Salomor**, who wears a legendary ring granting him the power to summon extremely powerful creatures to do his bidding. The ring is well known—it's why no one challenges him.
- Society is divided into three castes: laborers, merchants, and nobility. Only nobility can own land. Only merchants and nobility can make or procure loans.
