- When you marry, your children inherit the **lower** caste of their two parents. This ensures downward mobility and keeps the lower castes growing while the nobility remains exclusive.

### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Glass, stained glass, various minerals

**Primary Imports**: Metal, textiles

- Kazül is the **largest producer of glass in Alaria**. The glass artists of Kazül are world-renowned, and Kazüli stained glass decorates temples and palaces across the continent.
- The desert sands provide perfect raw materials for glassmaking. The secret techniques are jealously guarded by guild families.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: Very Low

**Enrollment**: Mandatory (lower castes)

- Kazül lacks violent neighbors, and the desert is not an appetizing prize for would-be attackers.
- Lower castes are required to serve two years in the military. Merchant castes may volunteer and enter as officers. Nobility don't serve.
- The real military power is Salomor's ring—why maintain an army when the king can summon monsters?

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Middle Sea coast, south of Glorin, south/southwest of Zintacas, bordering the Jahazai Desert

**Biomes**: Coastal settlements, river valleys, vast interior desert

**Climate**: Arid—hot days, cool nights, rain only along the coast and river

**Named Features**: Jahazai Desert (interior), Gulf of Kazül (west coast), Peecked Hills (west), Okesha's Passage (southeastern waterway)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Urzahd (northwest, on the river)

**Cities**: Urzahd, Sigiel, Duhkmayas

**Towns**: Shohas, Malki, Bhutag, Boxwatch, Dygalva, Seneka

### The River Settlements

A river flows from Glorin in the north down through Kazül, and most settlements cluster along it. The river is life—without it, the interior would be uninhabitable. Kazüli settlements stretch up the river almost to Glorin's border.

### The Jahazai Desert

The vast interior desert that makes up most of Kazül's territory. Harsh, beautiful, and full of buried secrets. Ancient ruins lie beneath the sands—remnants of civilizations that flourished before the desert claimed the land. Treasure hunters and archaeologists venture into the Jahazai, though many never return.

### The Towers of Ederhi

Deep in the Jahazai Desert stand the Towers of Ederhi—ancient spires of fused glass rising from the sand. No one knows who built them or why. The glass is unlike anything Kazüli artisans can produce—harder, clearer, and faintly luminescent at night.

Some believe the towers are tombs. Others think they're beacons, waiting for someone to activate them. Expeditions have explored the exterior but found no entrance. The towers remain a mystery—and a reminder that the desert holds secrets older than Kazül itself.

### Bhutag

A town in the eastern desert, near the coast. Bhutag sits atop a vast underground complex—catacombs that predate the current settlement by millennia. The locals avoid the deeper tunnels, which are said to be haunted. Occasionally, artifacts emerge from the depths, sold quietly to collectors who don't ask questions.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Caste System**

The brutal downward-mobility rule ensures resentment festers in the lower castes. Laborers have no path upward, and mixed-caste marriages doom children to the bottom. Unrest simmers, though Salomor's ring keeps open rebellion unthinkable.

**Desert Secrets**

The Jahazai holds treasures and dangers. Expeditions into the deep desert sometimes disturb things better left buried. Not every ruin is empty.

### History

Kazül grew along the river, sustained by water in a land of sand. The desert was always there, full of ruins from older times—civilizations swallowed by the advancing sands.

King Salomor's dynasty has ruled for generations, their power secured by the summoning ring. Where the ring came from is a matter of legend—some say it was found in the Jahazai ruins, others that it was a gift from a dying god. Salomor isn't telling.

The glass trade made Kazül wealthy. The caste system keeps that wealth concentrated. The desert keeps enemies away. It's a stable arrangement—for those at the top.

## Keletus

Tags: state, alrock ocean, democracy, minotaur

An island democracy of Lorean minotaurs renowned for producing the finest naval officers in the world. Keletus occupies Apketrii, a mountainous island in the Alrock Ocean, where sailing isn't just livelihood—it's identity. The nation's fortunes swing wildly with each decade's elected king, some bringing unprecedented prosperity, others steering toward disaster.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Elective Monarchy (Democratic)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Lorean minotaur

**Population**: 60,000

**Stability**: Somewhat stable

**Core Values**: Might, cunning, sailing skill

**The Kingship**: Every decade, Keletus elects a new king by popular vote. There are no formal restrictions on candidacy—any adult minotaur may declare. The winner rules for exactly ten years, no extensions, no exceptions. This apparent egalitarianism masks a deeper truth: only those who've proven themselves at sea have any real chance of winning.

**The Captains' Consensus**: Before any election, senior ship captains meet informally to discuss candidates. There's no official vote, no binding outcome. But when the Consensus settles on someone, that candidate suddenly finds their campaign fully funded, their rallies packed, their name on every sailor's lips. Defying the Consensus isn't illegal—it's just politically suicidal.

**The Countinghouses**: Five great merchant-banking families—the Thornwakes, the Bronzehorns, the Saltmanes, the Deepkeel, and the Gallowglass—control who gets to compete. Campaigning is ruinously expensive. Without Countinghouse backing, you can't hire criers, can't throw feasts, can't outfit the ceremonial ships that prove you're serious. The Countinghouses don't pick kings; they pick which candidates get to run.

**The Old Bulls**: Former kings don't disappear after their terms. They become the Old Bulls—an informal council with no official power but enormous influence. They advise current kings, broker deals between factions, and occasionally arrange things when a king becomes dangerous. Three kings in Keletan history have drowned in suspicious sailing accidents. All three had threatened Countinghouse interests.

**Why Kings Fail**: The wild swings in prosperity follow a pattern. *Captains' Kings*—those with real sailing records—succeed. They understand the sea, the trade routes, the navy. They negotiate well because they've met foreign powers at sea. *Countinghouse Kings*—candidates with money and charisma but no nautical credentials—fail catastrophically. The Countinghouses occasionally install one when they want someone controllable, accepting the economic damage as the cost of maintaining influence.

### The Lorean Difference

Lorean minotaurs aren't like their landlocked cousins. Generations on Apketrii rewired something fundamental: the labyrinthine instinct turned outward. Where other minotaurs feel the pull of enclosed spaces and winding corridors, Loreans feel the sea's currents like a three-dimensional maze. They sense pressure changes, can dead-reckon through fog, and experience genuine discomfort on land for too long—a low-grade wrongness, like clothes that don't fit.

This makes them uncanny navigators. A Keletan helmsman doesn't read the water—they *feel* it, the way you feel which direction is down. Other nations pay fortunes to poach retired Keletan captains.

Young minotaurs who can't develop this sea-sense are pitied. Some leave for the mainland. Some stay and become farmers, craftsmen, merchants—respected, but never leaders. A minotaur who hasn't proven themselves at sea will never be king.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Farseeing lenses, wine, sea products, naval expertise

**Primary Imports**: Wood, ships, grain

**The Shapers' Guild**: The Diamondback Mountains produce seaglass quartz—a crystalline mineral essential for crafting farseeing lenses, the enchanted spyglasses that give Keletan ships their legendary ability to spot threats at impossible distances. The Shapers' Guild controls this trade absolutely. They're older than the kingship itself, a closed guild guarding both the mining sites and lens-crafting techniques. Kings come and go, but the Shapers endure. A good king keeps them happy. A bad king tries to break their monopoly and ends up blind—sometimes literally, when their personal farseeing lens mysteriously cracks.

**Keletan Wine**: Blossom Valley produces wine famous across the Middle Sea—not for subtlety, but for strength. It travels well, keeps sailors healthy, and gets you drunk enough to forget you're six weeks from home.

**The Naval Trade**: Keletus doesn't just produce officers—it rents them. Foreign navies pay handsomely for Keletan navigators, harbor pilots, and tactical advisors. This "officer trade" is controversial; some see it as selling Keletan blood for foreign gold, others as spreading Keletan influence across every fleet that matters.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Exceptional

**Enrollment**: Mandatory service, selective advancement

Keletus maintains a small standing army of moderate skill, but their navy is legendary. Every minotaur serves two years at sea during youth; the talented are recruited into the officer corps. Keletan naval officers are considered the finest in the world—not because of superior ships or numbers, but because they *understand* the sea in ways other races cannot match.

**The Tidemasters**: Elite navigators who've proven they can sail through anything. During the worst storms, when other ships make for harbor, Tidemasters take their vessels into the teeth of it—and come out the other side. They're part navigator, part weather-reader, part sea-mystic. Foreign admirals have standing offers for any Tidemaster willing to defect. None have.

### Geography

**Location**: Apketrii Island, Alrock Ocean

**Biomes**: Mountainous interior, coastal lowlands, fertile southern valley

**Named Entities**: Diamondback Mountains (central spine), Hallenfell (northwestern mountains), Blossom Valley (southern agricultural region), the Long Reach (southeastern peninsula)

**Adjacent Waters**: Alrock Ocean

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Zenbryos

**Cities**: Lelakai (eastern port), Theolla (northwestern harbor)

**Towns**: Euphemia, Kallias, Menelos

**Villages**: Mendas, Isokaros, Niklandrus, Lagdike

**Zenbryos**: The capital wraps around a natural deepwater harbor shaped like a curled horn. The Kingstone stands at the harbor's mouth—a massive natural pillar where new kings are crowned at dawn, with the whole fleet anchored in witness. The Tidemoot Hall hosts elections in a roofless amphitheater, sea wind always present. The Countinghouses maintain their headquarters here, funding campaigns and quietly deciding which candidates get resources to compete.

**Blossom Valley**: The agricultural south, below the Diamondbacks. Lorean minotaurs here have weaker sea-pull; many have never sailed beyond sight of land. They vote—their wine money matters—but a Valley candidate for king is a joke. This has bred quiet separatist sentiment. The Valley feeds the island and funds the fleets, yet will never rule.

**The Long Reach**: The southeastern peninsula stretching toward Ve—Kallias, Menelos, Niklandrus, and Lagdike at the tip. These are watcher-towns: lighthouse chains, signal towers, the Lamplighter Corps. The people here are isolated, superstitious, more aware of what moves in deep water. Lagdike overlooks sea lanes and serves as a fortress-town, though no one's attacked in centuries.

**Hallenfell**: The northwestern mountains hold something older than the minotaurs. Black stone structures emerge from cliff faces—halls carved into the mountains, then partially collapsed when sea levels rose millennia ago. The builders, called the Kelemyr ("those before"), left artifacts of impossible clockwork and tablets in unknown languages. Some halls flood at high tide. What comes out during storm season isn't alive, and nearby villages maintain ancient wards. The Countinghouses would pay enormously for Kelemyr artifacts, but expeditions rarely return.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Courtship Problem**: Both Vetral (triton theocracy) and Bfaspeik (Bfaspeen broods) would benefit from Keletan naval support in their ongoing war. Vetral wants Keletan ships to enforce their "Old Lanes" system. The Bfaspeen brood mothers, cunning enough to negotiate, offer to leave Keletan-flagged ships alone in exchange for tacit permission to raid rivals. King Varrosh refuses both, determined to maintain independence—but neutrality has costs.

**The Sovereignty Insult**: Vetral claims ancient sovereignty over the entire Alrock Ocean. Recently they've expanded enforcement of this claim, sending formal notices to distant nations demanding vessel registration and adherence to approved routes. Varrosh sent their messenger back with a pointed refusal. It's not war, not even close—but the tritons won't forget who refused them when they eventually resolve their conflict with Bfaspeik.

**The Eastern Routes**: Trade with Ve (Sestros, Avalon) is Keletus's growth market, but those routes pass closer to the Vetral-Bfaspeik conflict zone. Ships have been lost. Insurance rates climb. The Countinghouses are split: some want to ally with Vetral for protection, others want to maintain profitable neutrality.

**Valley Separatism**: Blossom Valley lords have started quietly meeting with Vetral's surface agents, exploring whether they could cut a separate trade deal. If Varrosh discovered this, it would be treason. But Varrosh doesn't spend much time thinking about farmers.

**The Current King**: Varrosh Gallowglass was a Captains' King—legendary navigator, veteran of forty years at sea, winner of the Twice-Around race. He should have been a great king. Instead, his stubborn refusal to bend on foreign policy is costing Keletus money and making enemies. The Countinghouses grow restless. The Old Bulls whisper. Seven years remain in his term.

### History

The Lorean minotaurs arrived on Apketrii in an age before reliable records, fleeing something on the mainland they no longer remember. The island shaped them over generations—the sea-sense developing slowly, the labyrinthine instinct turning outward. By the time other nations noticed Keletus, the transformation was complete.

The democratic system emerged from necessity: early kings who ruled for life produced dynasties that weakened with each generation. The ten-year limit was a compromise between those who wanted perpetual elections and those who wanted stability. The compromise has held for three centuries.

The Shapers' Guild predates the current government. Some scholars believe they were here before the minotaurs—perhaps connected to the Kelemyr, perhaps not. The Guild neither confirms nor denies.

### Player Hooks

- **The Hallenfell Expedition**: A Countinghouse is funding another delve into the drowned halls. They need expendable outsiders. The pay is extraordinary. The survival rate is not.

- **The Varrosh Problem**: Various factions want the current king removed, replaced, or supported. The Old Bulls are watching. The Countinghouses are scheming. Someone's going to make a move before his term ends.

- **The Valley Question**: Separatist meetings are happening. Vetral agents are involved. This could be treason or diplomacy depending on who wins. Players could expose it, join it, or play both sides.

- **The Officer Trade**: A foreign power wants a Tidemaster. They'll pay anything. Finding one willing to defect—or kidnapping one who isn't—could make players rich or hunted.

- **Bfaspeik Negotiations**: A brood mother wants to talk. She needs surface intermediaries. The pay is in centuries of sunken treasure. The catch is surviving the meeting.

## Kendrel Kingdoms

Tags: state

Multiple, semi-united Kendrel monarchies.

- Flurin
- Glador
- Pelaria

## Kerwin

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Immortal monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Halflings

**Population**: 45,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Contentment, tradition, pastoral simplicity, quiet endurance

- Kerwin is ruled by **Ulyas the Immortal**, a halfling who has sat the throne for 898 years. He is, by any reasonable definition, a lich—though he maintains a living appearance and his subjects prefer not to think too hard about what sustains him.
- The halflings of Kerwin are agrarian and pastoral. They farm, they herd, they craft modest goods. They have no great ambitions. They want to live peaceful lives, raise families, and die in the same villages where they were born.
- Ulyas is, by all accounts, a good king. He settles disputes fairly, keeps taxes low, maintains roads, and asks little of his people. In return, they don't ask questions about his yearly journey to the Fireknife Mountains.
- Kerwin is nominally part of the Tarkhon Empire, but this is a polite fiction. Ulyas agreed to fealty five centuries ago when Tarkhon expanded, but he has outlived every emperor since. He pays modest tribute, allows Tarkhon merchants to operate freely, and in exchange, Tarkhon pretends they conquered him.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Simple

**Primary Exports**: Grain, wool, copper, modest crafts

**Primary Imports**: Metal goods, luxury items, wine

- Kerwin's economy is subsistence-plus. The halflings grow more than they need, so they trade the surplus for things they can't make themselves.
- The **Copper Hills** provide modest mining income—enough to trade for iron and steel, which Kerwin cannot produce.
- Kerwin merchants are pleasant but unambitious. They want fair deals, not fortunes.
- Tarkhon traders pass through regularly, taking advantage of Kerwin's position on overland routes, but the halflings themselves rarely travel far.

### Military

**Size**: Tiny

**Quality**: Low

**Enrollment**: Volunteer militia

- Kerwin has no standing army. Villages maintain small militias for bandit problems and wild animal attacks.
- Ulyas himself is the kingdom's true defense. Whatever dark power sustains him has made him extraordinarily dangerous when roused. In 898 years, three armies have invaded Kerwin. None succeeded.
- The halflings don't like to talk about what happened to those armies.

### Geography

**Location**: Northeastern Tarkhon, north of Nektuna and east of Enymu

**Biomes**: Rolling farmland, arid grassland, copper-rich hills, volcanic mountains

**Climate**: Semi-arid continental—hot summers, cold winters, modest rainfall

**Named Features**: Fireknife Mountains (northwest border), Copper Hills (west-central), Da Sharma (southern region)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Tarkha

**Cities**: None

**Towns**: Sharmatenu, Hebbythus, Nehemiter, Hebmu, Clynisi, Ankbalen, Dlesy, Cuntzam, Kastorbekt, Akhzubdet, Chykum

### Tarkha

The capital of Kerwin—an ancient city that predates Tarkhon itself.

Tarkha is a quiet city now, its grand avenues too wide for its current population, its ceremonial plazas hosting weekly markets instead of the festivals they were built for. The old palace still stands, and Ulyas rules from it, but much of the building is closed off, dusty halls echoing with centuries of silence.

The halflings built Tarkha for their own purposes: wide avenues for their festivals, great plazas for their markets, a palace for a king who expected to rule forever. The scale sometimes surprises visitors who expect halflings to build small. Tarkha is comfortable, if oversized.

Visitors from Nektuna find the scale disorienting—everything built for importance, inhabited by people who don't care about importance at all. The halflings offer them tea and wonder what the fuss is about.

### The Fireknife Mountains

The volcanic range along Kerwin's northwestern border. Unstable, dangerous, and largely avoided. Smoke rises from vents, hot springs bubble in unexpected places, and the peaks occasionally glow red at night.

Every year, King Ulyas travels into the Fireknife Mountains. He takes a small retinue and a larger group of "volunteers"—criminals, prisoners, and occasionally people who simply weren't missed. He returns alone.

The halflings don't ask what happens in those mountains. They've learned not to.

### The Copper Hills

Low, scrubby hills west of Tarkha, rich with copper ore. The town of **Chykum** serves as the mining center—halflings and a smattering of dwarven advisors extract enough copper to meet Kerwin's modest needs and trade the surplus.

The hills are dotted with ancient mine shafts, some predating halfling settlement. What the original miners were looking for—and whether they found it—is unclear.

### Da Sharma

The southern region of Kerwin, where the land grows drier and the farms give way to sheep pastures. Da Sharma halflings are considered rustic even by Kerwin standards—insular, traditional, and deeply superstitious about the old ways.

They maintain shrines to spirits that predate Tarkhon, Kerwin, and perhaps the halflings themselves.

### Ulyas the Immortal

Ulyas has ruled Kerwin for 898 years. He appears as an elderly halfling, weathered but hale, with bright eyes and a gentle manner. He settles disputes, blesses weddings, and remembers every citizen's grandparents.

He is almost certainly a lich.

Ulyas does not rot, does not wither, does not age. Whatever sustains him requires yearly renewal—a journey to a forgotten shrine in the Fireknife Mountains, where something ancient and hungry waits. The price is blood. Ulyas has paid it for nearly nine centuries.

He tells himself it's justified. The people he sacrifices are criminals, enemies, the forgotten. He's a good king. His people are happy. Surely that's worth a few dozen lives a year?

The math has grown uncomfortable over the centuries. Ulyas tries not to add it up.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Weight of Centuries**

Ulyas is tired. Nine hundred years is a long time to rule, a long time to make the same compromises, a long time to watch everyone he loves die while he continues. He's considered stopping—letting the yearly sacrifice lapse, letting himself finally rest. But who would rule Kerwin then? His people need him. Don't they?

**Tarkhon's Shadow**

Kerwin's nominal allegiance to Tarkhon is stable but hollow. Ulyas has outlived every attempt to integrate Kerwin more fully into the empire. But King Selron II is ambitious, and some in Nektuna whisper that it's time to remind Kerwin who's really in charge. Ulyas has heard these whispers before. He's buried everyone who acted on them.

**The Neferati Memory**

Ulyas remembers the Neferati founders—the fire-blooded conquerors who built Tarkhetan and expanded their empire to swallow Kerwin. He remembers their emperors, their court intrigues, their slow dilution into humanity. He watched the Severance from a careful distance, keeping Kerwin neutral while both sides burned. Now humans hold the throne, and they've forgotten most of what Ulyas remembers. He hasn't told them. He hasn't decided if he ever will.

### History

Kerwin was settled by halflings long before Tarkhon existed as an empire. They farmed, they traded, they lived modest lives. Ulyas became king through means he no longer discusses, and he has ruled ever since—nearly nine centuries now.

When the Neferati came north and built Tarkhetan, Ulyas watched. When they expanded their empire five centuries ago, he negotiated rather than fought. He recognized the empire's strength but also its limits—Tarkhon wanted the strait, not the hinterlands. Ulyas offered fealty, tribute, and open roads. In exchange, Tarkhon left Kerwin alone.

The arrangement has held through the Neferati dynasty, the Severance, and three centuries of human rule. Ulyas has outlived every emperor and every king. He's seen the empire's character change but its demands stay the same. He pays his tribute, tends his people, and makes his yearly journey to the mountains.

Kerwin endures. Ulyas endures. The shrine in the mountains waits for its yearly offering. Nothing changes, and perhaps nothing ever will.

## Kingdoms of Fire

Tags: alliance, state

### Overview

The Kingdoms of Fire are four Neferati monarchies—Yaif, Kabir, Gissemari, and Wadiyah—that occupy a peninsula in southwestern Tarkhon. Though each kingdom is independent with its own ruler, they act as a political bloc, coordinating foreign policy and presenting a united front to the wider empire.

This alliance isn't merely convenient—it's existential. The Neferati founded Tarkhon six centuries ago. They lit the Evertorch. They established the institutions that made the empire possible. And three centuries ago, they were pushed aside when the human faction won the Severance.

The Kingdoms of Fire remember. They consider themselves founders in exile, not tributaries. The Evertorch burns with Neferati fire. Tarkhon was built on Neferati foundations. The humans may have forgotten this, but the Neferati have not.

### The Four Eternal Flames

Each kingdom is built around an **Eternal Flame**—a fire that has burned since the world's creation, sustained by magic older than civilization. These flames are the heart of Neferati spirituality, the source of their fire magic, and the model for the Evertorch that burns in Tarkhetan.

- **The Flame of Yaif**: The oldest and largest, burning in the Temple of First Fire. Said to be the flame from which all others were kindled.
- **The Flame of Kabir**: Burns in a lighthouse that has guided ships since before human memory. Its light never wavers.
- **The Flame of Gissemari**: Burns in a great forge where master craftsmen create works of legendary beauty.
- **The Flame of Wadiyah**: Burns in a pit so deep that none have seen its bottom. The oldest priests say it connects to the world's molten heart.

The Evertorch in Tarkhetan was lit from these flames—specifically from the Flame of Yaif, carried north by the Neferati conquerors who founded the empire. This is why the Kingdoms of Fire consider the Evertorch theirs by right of heritage.

### The Neferati People

The Neferati are children of fire—born when primordial flame danced with divine essence. Their distinctive red skin seems to flicker with inner fire in certain lights. Curling ram horns crown their heads, their eyes are dark as coals, and their hair is black as charcoal.

Fire is not merely an element to the Neferati—it is life itself. They gather regularly for sacred fire ceremonies, basking in heat that would kill other races. Fire dancing blurs the boundary between dancer and flame. They approach everything with burning intensity: loving fiercely, exploring fearlessly, experimenting boldly.

The Neferati don't sleep as other races do. Instead, they must spend four hours each night near fire—at least a campfire's worth. This requirement shapes their architecture, their culture, and their sense of community. Every Neferati home has a hearth. Every Neferati gathering has flames.

### The Council of Four Flames

The four monarchs meet regularly at the **Council of Four Flames**, rotating between kingdoms. Here they coordinate policy, resolve disputes, and decide how to present their collective interests to Tarkhon.

The council has no formal leader—decisions require consensus among all four monarchs. This makes the bloc slow to act but nearly impossible to divide. Nektuna has tried to buy off individual kingdoms; all attempts have failed. The Neferati remember what happens when they're not united.

Current monarchs:
- **Queen Seraphel of Yaif**: The eldest, most respected, and most patient. She remembers the old ways.
- **King Rashaan of Kabir**: The wealthiest, most pragmatic, and most willing to deal with Tarkhon. Some call him accommodating; he calls it strategic.
- **Prince-Consort Vhelan of Gissemari**: Rules as regent for his young daughter. An artist-king who values beauty over politics.
- **Queen Khalira of Wadiyah**: The youngest monarch, the most radical, and the most eager to challenge Tarkhon. She believes the time for patience is ending.

### The Founding Legacy

Six centuries ago, Neferati warlords from Yaif recognized that the Tarkhon Strait could be controlled—and that whoever controlled it would control trade across half the world.

They brought fire north. They lit the Evertorch. They built Tarkhetan and established the empire. For nearly three centuries, Neferati blood sat on the throne. The Firemage Corps was created by Neferati founders. The traditions that still govern Tarkhon were written in Neferati hands.

Then came the dilution—generations of marriage to local humans, the slow fading of fire in the imperial bloodline. By the end, the "Neferati" emperors were mostly human.

The **Severance** came when the last fire-blooded emperor died without legitimate heir. His children by his Neferati mistress were passed over for his fully human nephew. The Neferati faction resisted. They lost.

The pure Neferati withdrew to the Kingdoms of Fire. The Evertorch stayed in Tarkhetan—too sacred to move, too dangerous to extinguish. The humans took the throne and slowly forgot who had built it for them.

### Why They Stay

The Kingdoms of Fire could leave Tarkhon. They have the military strength to resist reconquest—four kingdoms of firemages in defensible terrain would be nearly impossible to subdue. So why do they remain?

**Trade**: The strait makes everyone rich, including the Neferati. Leaving would mean losing preferential access to the greatest trade route in the world.

**Heritage**: This is the harder truth. The Neferati don't want to leave Tarkhon—they want to *reclaim* it. Walking away would mean admitting that the humans won, that the Severance was final, that the Evertorch belongs to Tarkhetan now.

**Patience**: The Neferati live fast but think long. Three centuries seems like a long time to humans. To a culture built around eternal flames, it's a temporary setback. They're waiting for the right moment.

**Division**: Not all Neferati agree on strategy. King Rashaan thinks accommodation is wise. Queen Khalira thinks it's cowardice. Until they reach consensus, dramatic action is impossible.

### Why They Might Leave

Several scenarios could push the Kingdoms of Fire out of Tarkhon:

**The Evertorch Claim**: If the Neferati formally asserted their right to the Evertorch—demanding its return or acknowledgment of their heritage—and Tarkhon refused, it could trigger a break.

**Imperial Overreach**: If Nektuna tried to impose direct control, extract excessive tribute, or station troops in the kingdoms, the bloc would resist.

**A Restoration Opportunity**: If a succession crisis, military defeat, or other disaster weakened Tarkhon enough, the Neferati might see their chance to restore fire-blooded rule.

**A Better Offer**: If someone offered the kingdoms a superior arrangement—perhaps a rival trade route, or alliance against Tarkhon—they might take it.

### Relations with Tarkhon

The relationship is stable but not warm. The Kingdoms of Fire pay their tribute, maintain trade agreements, and participate in imperial councils. In return, they receive preferential trade terms and effective autonomy.

But there's always an edge. The Neferati ambassadors in Tarkhetan are too numerous, too well-informed, too interested in the Evertorch's flame. High Priestess Salenne has noticed them studying the torch, measuring its light, taking notes.

King Selron II knows the Kingdoms of Fire are dangerous. He treats them carefully, grants them respect, and never pushes too hard. His greatest fear is that they'll remember their claim—and act on it.

### The Individual Kingdoms

**Yaif** — The westernmost and most traditional. Home to the Flame of First Fire and the oldest Neferati temples. Queen Seraphel rules with ancient wisdom.

**Kabir** — Northeastern, facing the Sea of Merchants. The wealthiest kingdom, built on trade. King Rashaan balances profit and pride.

**Gissemari** — South of Kabir. The artistic heart of Neferati culture. Prince-Consort Vhelan rules as regent for his young daughter, Queen Ishari.

**Wadiyah** — The southernmost and most isolated. Home to the deepest flame and the most radical politics. Queen Khalira believes the time for patience has ended.

### What Will Happen

The Kingdoms of Fire are waiting. They've been waiting for three centuries. But the current generation of rulers is more divided than any before:

- Seraphel councils patience.
- Rashaan wants to maintain profitable stability.
- Vhelan doesn't care about politics—he wants to make beautiful things.
- Khalira wants to burn Tarkhetan and take back what's theirs.

The consensus that has held the bloc together is fraying. If the four monarchs can't agree on a path forward, individual kingdoms might start acting alone.

And if one kingdom breaks from the bloc—either toward accommodation or toward confrontation—the careful balance that has kept the Kingdoms of Fire stable for three centuries will shatter.

Something is going to change. The only questions are when, and how much fire will be involved.

## Klevnaf

Tags: state, winter-elf, istori, crimson-coast

The western remnant of the unified Winter Elf kingdom, Klevnaf claims legitimacy through Prince Taoinor—uncle to the murdered King Istor XXVI. They control Murder Creek and the sacred Whitewood, drawing authority from possession of the ancestral heartland. Their enemies call them usurpers and murderers; they call themselves the true continuation of the elven realm.

### Overview

Klevnaf occupies the central coastal strip of the Crimson Coast, wedged between Tangiern to the west and the contested Suftos Hills to the east. The realm follows Murder Creek from its highland sources to the sea—cursed water defining cursed kingdom. The sacred Whitewood provides spiritual legitimacy; the curse provides... something else.

Capital: **Svedlind** (~2,500 population)

### Government

**Prince Taoinor** rules Klevnaf—though "prince" is a pointed choice, not "king." He has never claimed the royal title, insisting that he rules as regent for a unified kingdom that will someday be restored. His supporters call this humility; his opponents call it cowardice; the practical truth is that claiming kingship would close the door to reconciliation.

Taoinor is old even by elven standards—ancient, weary, and possibly relieved that the succession crisis gives him something to do besides wait for death. His court is divided between hawks (push east, crush Istora, reunify by force), doves (negotiate settlement, accept partition), and traditionalists (focus on maintaining proper customs regardless of outcome).

Taoinor vacillates between factions, making decisions slowly and often reversing them. This frustrates everyone but may be intentional—a leader who commits to nothing can't be blamed for failure, and delay benefits the side that can't win quickly.

### Culture

Klevnaf represents traditional Winter Elf society—or claims to. They venerate the old ways: sacred forests where ancestors speak, ceremonies performed exactly as they've always been performed, rigid hierarchies that have governed elven life for millennia. The Whitewood is the physical manifestation of these values.

The curse of Murder Creek has been absorbed into this traditionalism rather than rejected. The visions are painful—those who drink from the creek experience King Ghy's ancient assassination as if they were present—but pain is reframed as sacred connection. Some warriors deliberately drink before battle, seeking the dead king's guidance through hallucination. This practice horrifies outsiders and increasingly concerns Klevnaf's own healers.

Foxes and snowy owls serve as companions, a tradition shared with Istora. The animals don't understand the civil war and will still carry messages to elves on either side if their masters aren't watching carefully. This has led to unofficial communications that both courts pretend don't exist.

**Theological conservatism** defines Klevnaf's religious life. They reject Istora's innovations—a queen leading rites, sustainable logging in sacred forests, the idea that tradition can be adapted. These aren't merely disagreements; they're fundamental challenges to cosmic order. The gods established proper forms; deviating from them invites disaster.

### Economy

Klevnaf's economy is strangled. The kingdom traditionally relied on trade with what's now Istora, tribute from Tangiern (now suspended), and prestige goods that require peace to produce. What remains is subsistence fishing, limited agriculture, and whatever can be salvaged from border skirmishes.

The Whitewood could solve everything. Its silver-barked trees are valuable beyond measure—lumber, furniture, magical components. But Taoinor has forbidden any logging in the sacred forest. Soldiers whose equipment is wearing out, families who can't afford food—they watch priceless trees stand untouched while the kingdom slowly starves. Resentment builds.

### Military

Klevnaf fields perhaps 800 trained soldiers—fewer than Istora, with worse equipment and shakier supply. Their strength lies in terrain: Murder Creek provides natural defense, the Whitewood offers endless ambush opportunities, and the curse itself might deter invaders who fear drinking the wrong water.

Their weakness is everything else. Equipment wears out and can't be replaced. Young elves who should be training are farming or fishing instead. The hawks want offensive action, but attacking across the Suftos Hills would be suicide with current resources.

**The Oath-Bound** are elite warriors who have drunk from Murder Creek deliberately, binding themselves to King Ghy's memory through shared vision. They're fearsome—the visions seem to grant insights in combat—but also increasingly unstable. Prolonged exposure to the curse damages minds in ways that are becoming impossible to ignore.

### Relations

**With Istora**: War. Skirmishes along the Suftos line are constant; assassination attempts flow in both directions; neither side can achieve decisive advantage. The conflict has become self-sustaining—too many dead for either side to accept peace without victory.

**With Tangiern**: Careful outreach. Taoinor has approached King Halvard about alliance—Tangier support for Klevnaf in exchange for reduced tribute when victory comes. Halvard hasn't committed. He probably never will; Tangier benefits from elven division and has no reason to end it.

**With other powers**: Minimal. The Crimson Coast is isolated behind the Dragon's Spine, and nobody outside cares enough about the civil war to intervene.

### History

Murder Creek's name dates back over a thousand years, to the assassination of King Ghy the Elder—the last monarch of a previous unified realm. The circumstances of Ghy's death are lost to time, but the curse that resulted is very much present. The creek remembers.

When Istor XXVI died under similar circumstances, the parallels were impossible to ignore. Lamenrae accused Taoinor; Taoinor denied it; the kingdom split along lines that had existed since Ghy's time. Western territories that had always chafed under eastern dominance chose Taoinor; eastern territories rallied to Lamenrae. The division was geographic, political, and theological simultaneously.

**Did Taoinor kill his nephew?** The evidence is circumstantial. Servants saw him near the king's chambers; a cupbearer noticed something strange about the wine; Taoinor benefited from the death. But witnesses can be bought, memories can be mistaken, and benefiting from a death doesn't prove causing it. Taoinor insists he's innocent. Maybe he is. Maybe Lamenrae accused him to seize power. Maybe someone else killed Istor XXVI and let the obvious suspect take the blame. The truth may never be established.

### What Outsiders Notice

- The dark water of Murder Creek, visible from everywhere in Svedlind
- The careful way locals avoid drinking from obvious sources
- Architecture that's elegant but poorly maintained—repairs deferred, decorations faded
- The silver gleam of Whitewood visible on the southern horizon
- References to "the pretender queen" delivered with weary contempt

### Adventure Hooks

- Taoinor wants to know the truth about Istor XXVI's death—someone to investigate without political agenda
- The Oath-Bound are becoming dangerous; find out what the curse is doing to them and whether it can be stopped
- A faction wants to log the Whitewood to fund the war; they need outsiders to do it without Taoinor's knowledge
- Something in the depths of Murder Creek has begun calling to those who drink its water

## Knova

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Institutional meritocracy (the Academy IS the state)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Mixed (students and faculty from across Alaria), Triton (defensive forces)

**Population**: ~6,000 permanent (fluctuates to ~10,000 during peak enrollment)

**Stability**: Extremely stable (clear hierarchy, absolute authority)

**Core Values**: Martial excellence, earned rank, the purity of combat

Knova isn't a nation that happens to contain a famous academy—it's an academy that happens to be a nation. The Iron Academy governs absolutely. The Commandant speaks with the authority of a king. Students and faculty form the population. There are no citizens, only practitioners.

The hierarchy is brutally simple: your rank depends on your combat record. Win fights, rise. Lose, fall. The Commandant holds their position because no one has defeated them—and many have tried. The current Commandant, **Irontide Vesk**, earned his title by killing a deep-sea serpent in single combat. The beast's skull still adorns the central courtyard.

Triton warriors patrol the surrounding waters under an ancient compact. They defend Knova's shores; in exchange, their warriors receive free training at any of the three schools. This has made Knova's triton alumni some of the most dangerous fighters in the undersea kingdoms—and ensures no naval force can approach the islands without a fight.

### Economy

**Size**: Small (but wealthy)

**Tech**: Advanced metallurgy, standard otherwise

**Primary Exports**: Trained warriors, tactical consultation, refined metals, weapons

**Primary Imports**: Food (almost everything), luxury goods, raw materials, students

Knova's economy runs on tuition and iron.

The islands are phenomenally metal-rich—iron, copper, tin, and traces of rarer ores. The forges run constantly, producing weapons and armor of exceptional quality. But the real product is people. A graduate of the Iron Academy commands premium wages anywhere in Alaria. A recommendation from Blackwater can make a general's career. Seatower alumni lead mercenary companies across the continent.

Tuition is steep. Noble families pay fortunes to train their heirs. Mercenary companies sponsor promising recruits as investments. Some students earn their way through arena victories—Knova's training matches draw betting crowds from across the Western Isles.

Food is the weakness. The rocky islands grow almost nothing. Everything must be imported. The triton help with fishing, but grain, vegetables, and livestock all arrive by ship. Knova maintains strategic reserves, but a determined blockade could starve them out—if anyone could get past the triton.

### Military

**Size**: Small but elite

**Quality**: Best individual fighters in Alaria

**Enrollment**: Students and faculty (everyone trains)

Every resident of Knova is a warrior—or training to become one. There's no separate military because the entire population IS the military.

The three schools each produce different specialists:

**Iron Academy** (main island): Personal combat in all forms. Sword, spear, axe, bare hands—students master multiple disciplines before specializing. Graduates are among the deadliest individual fighters alive.

**Seatower**: Tactics, command, and wargames. Students learn to lead units, plan campaigns, and think three moves ahead. The fortress hosts constant war simulations; commanding officers from across Alaria pay to observe.

**Blackwater**: History, strategy, and logistics. The unglamorous school, and the smallest—but arguably the most valuable. Wars are won by supply lines. Blackwater graduates keep armies fed, equipped, and moving.

The triton legion numbers around 500 warriors at any time, rotating through training cycles. They fight from the water—ambushing approaching ships, dragging sailors overboard, turning the sea itself into a killing ground.

### Geography

**Location**: Northern Western Isles, south of the Iron Sea

**Biomes**: Rocky islands, mineral-rich terrain, deep surrounding waters

**Climate**: Temperate maritime, frequent storms

**Named Features**: Iron Academy (main fortress), Seatower (western fortress), Blackwater (northern fortress)

The Knova archipelago consists of a dozen significant islands and scores of rocky outcroppings. The three largest islands host the three schools; the smaller ones contain mines, training grounds, and support facilities.

The main island rises steeply from the water, its central fortress built directly into the rock. Iron reinforcement makes the walls nearly impervious—hence the name. The harbor is a narrow channel, easily defended, with triton patrols making underwater approach suicidal.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Iron Academy fortress (no other name—the fortress IS Knova)

**Other Settlements**: Seatower (western island), Blackwater (northern island)

### The Three Schools

**Iron Academy** (~4,000 residents)

The heart of Knova. A towering fortress of iron-reinforced stone rising from the central island. Training yards, sparring halls, armories, dormitories—all built for one purpose: creating fighters.

Students train for years, progressing through ranked tiers. Combat matches determine advancement. The annual Iron Tournament draws observers from across Alaria; victory guarantees employment anywhere.

**Commandant Irontide Vesk** rules from the Iron Throne—literally a chair forged from weapons taken from defeated challengers. He's held the position for twenty-three years. Before that, he held it for twelve years, lost it, spent eight years rebuilding his skills, then reclaimed it by defeating his replacement in single combat.

**Seatower** (~1,500 residents)

The western fortress, built on a rocky spire overlooking the shipping lanes. Seatower produces commanders, not fighters. Students study tactics through endless wargames—mock battles using ship models, sand tables, and (weather permitting) actual vessels.

**Battlemaster Kenna Stormwright** leads Seatower. She's never lost a wargame. Her actual combat experience is limited, which some Iron Academy purists consider disqualifying—but her tactical record is flawless.

**Blackwater** (~800 residents)

The northern fortress, named for the dark waters of its harbor. The smallest school, focused on strategy and logistics. Students learn supply calculation, terrain analysis, historical campaigns, and the unglamorous work that makes wars possible.

**Archon Drell Marshkeeper** runs Blackwater. He's seventy years old, has never won a fight in his life, and has helped plan three successful invasions. The Iron Academy sneers at him. Former employers worship him.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Meritocracy Problem**

Knova's system works—until it doesn't. When the Commandant is strong, everyone respects the hierarchy. When the Commandant weakens, challenges multiply. Irontide Vesk is still formidable, but he's aging. The next few years will see increasing challenges, political maneuvering, and potentially a bloody succession.

**Triton Tensions**

The triton compact has held for centuries, but some younger triton question why they should defend surface-dwellers. The training is valuable, but triton society has its own needs. A faction argues for renegotiation—or withdrawal.

**The Food Question**

Every year, Knova's population grows slightly. Every year, food imports must increase. The academy depends on peaceful trade routes—which means staying neutral in regional conflicts. But neutrality limits influence, and influence is how academies attract students.

### Relations

**Allies**: None formal (Knova maintains studied neutrality)

**Trade Partners**: Sheîr (closest port), Tarkhon (wealthy students), various merchant fleets

**Enemies**: None declared (but many grudges from training accidents and tournament injuries)

Knova's neutrality is its greatest asset and its greatest vulnerability. They train anyone who pays—noble heirs, mercenary recruits, even occasional pirates. This makes them universally useful and universally distrusted.

The triton connection provides some diplomatic weight. Offending Knova risks offending the undersea kingdoms. But this cuts both ways; triton politics occasionally drag Knova into conflicts they'd rather avoid.

### Notable Figures

**Commandant Irontide Vesk** — Current leader of Knova and the Iron Academy. A massive man scarred from decades of combat, famous for killing a deep-sea serpent solo. Aging but still deadly. His succession is the unspoken question hanging over everything.

**Battlemaster Kenna Stormwright** — Leader of Seatower. Brilliant tactician, mediocre fighter. Her wargame record is undefeated; her actual command experience is theoretical. Some respect her; others consider her an academic playing soldier.

**Archon Drell Marshkeeper** — Leader of Blackwater. Ancient, physically unimpressive, strategically invaluable. He's planned supply lines for half the successful campaigns in the last fifty years.

**Tide-Captain Muravel** — Senior triton commander. Liaison between Knova and the undersea kingdoms. She's trained three generations of triton warriors here and has strong opinions about the compact's future.

### History

**The Founding** (~400 years ago): A legendary warrior named Knovus established a training camp on the iron-rich islands after being exiled from his homeland (accounts vary on which). Students came. A school formed. The school became a fortress. The fortress became a nation.

**The Triton Compact** (~350 years ago): Early Knova faced constant pirate raids. A triton warrior named Deepcurrent offered a deal: his people would defend the waters if their warriors could train at the academy. The compact has held ever since.

**The Three Schools**: Originally there was only the Iron Academy. Seatower split off when tactical training outgrew its space. Blackwater formed later, when graduates demanded strategic education to match their combat skills.

**The Present**: Knova at its peak—famous, wealthy, influential. But an aging Commandant and growing triton restlessness suggest changes are coming.

## Kura

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Aristocracy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Nuun, Yngli

**Population**: 200,000

**Stability**: Very stable

**Core Values**: Divine approval, justice, eye for an eye

- Kura must always be controlled jointly between one blue-eye, and one golden-born. Together, they balance each other out, and provide equilibrium to the country. Each has a paragon level in fire or water.
- The Nuunish blue-king wears a crown granting him the ability to produce drinkable water with his attunement.
- The Nuunish gold-king wears a crown granting him the ability to moderate the heat in a 100 mile radius.


### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Glass, information

**Primary Imports**: Animal products, wood

- The glass trade is Kura's lifeblood. The Ley Line of Fire provides the intense, consistent heat needed for exceptional glasswork—Kuran glass is prized across Alaria for its clarity and the unique colors achieved through fire-attunement techniques.
- "Information" as an export refers to Kura's reputation as a neutral ground where secrets are traded. The dual monarchy's isolation and stability make it a safe place for spies, merchants, and diplomats to meet.


### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Low

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- Kura relies heavily on its extreme climate to deter invaders. This has been successful in the past.


### Geography

**Location**: Western Westrim, straddling the western edge of the Dunes of Kunagi and the southeastern foothills of the Eronia Range

**Biomes**: Desert (dominant), rocky foothills, scattered oases

**Named Entities**: Ley Line of Fire (bisects nation N-S), Shad Usk (great dune), Dunes of Kunagi (eastern desert)

**Rivers & Lakes**: None permanent; underground aquifers sustain settlements; the Nuun water-crown produces the only reliable surface water in the capital

**Adjacent Waters**: None direct; trade routes lead to the Agreben Sea via coastal settlements to the southwest

**Borders**: Righteous Peaks (north, Tarkhon border), Atriik (south), Wyrm Teeth hills (east), Eronia Range foothills and dwarven territories (northwest)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Bani Bari (~45,000) — the Twin-Throne city straddling the Ley Line of Fire

**Cities**: Labakh (~12,000) — northern trade city near dwarven territories

**Towns**: Fata M'darah — eastern waystation and trading post

**Villages**: Shalif Mulak (~2,000) — agricultural village near capital; numerous small oasis settlements 


### Primary Conflicts

- Honor is a dying virtue in Kura.


### History

The dual monarchy of Kura was established centuries ago when Nuun and Yngli settlers discovered they could only survive the harsh desert by combining their abilities. The Nuun could conjure water, essential for life; the Yngli could moderate the killing heat that made the desert impassable to others. Neither could thrive alone, but together they claimed territory no one else wanted.

The discovery of the Ley Line of Fire transformed Kura from a marginal survivor-state into a prosperous kingdom. The ley line's power enabled the glass industry that now sustains the economy, and the midnight fire academy at Tul Azreitnok has made Kura a destination for mages seeking dangerous knowledge.

Kura has never been successfully invaded. The desert itself serves as the nation's primary defense—armies die of thirst and heat exhaustion long before they reach the settlements. The few attempts have ended in disaster for the invaders, their bones still occasionally uncovered by shifting sands.

## Kyagos

Tags: state

### Overview

A mountainous island-nation in the southern seas, Kyagos sits between the Sea of Seven Snakes and the Alrock Ocean. The Aktarvkus Mountains dominate the interior, dividing the island into isolated coastal territories that never unified under a single government. Instead, four city-states emerged: Syvlius, Vassum, Tekos, and Sy—each controlling its own slice of coast, its own mountain approaches, and its own slave-worked mines.

The Sylthik who rule these city-states built a society around visible hierarchy. Scars mark rank: one cheek-scar for minor distinction, five for the highest lords. Outsiders wear handkerchiefs around their necks marking them as rightless visitors. Non-Sylthik races are considered sub-sentient, undeserving of even basic decency.

This arrangement held for centuries. Then Syvlius burned.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Confederacy of city-states (fractured)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Sylthik, Drasnian slaves, Dead Moon Tribe (humans)

**Population**: ~100,000 (pre-revolution); current numbers uncertain

**Stability**: Crisis

**Core Values**: Discipline, influence, societal rank, power

The scar-ranking system that once defined Kyagosi society is now openly questioned. In Syvlius, it's meaningless—the city is controlled by freed slaves and gangs who never participated in Sylthik customs. In Vassum, refugees have overwhelmed the social order. Only Tekos still enforces the old ways, and their doubled-down brutality suggests desperation rather than confidence.

Non-Sylthik have always been considered property or worse. The revolution hasn't changed that view among the remaining Sylthik—if anything, it's hardened their contempt. But the Drasnians who now control Syvlius's mines have demonstrated that contempt doesn't stop rebellion.

### Economy

**Size**: Large (diminished)

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Gems, jewels, metals

**Primary Imports**: Drasnian slaves from Tamadrez (disrupted)

Kyagos's economy has always rested on mining. The Aktarvkus Mountains contain legendary deposits of silver, copper, iron, and gemstones. Drasnian dwarves—shipped from Gorath's Slaver's Coast—extracted this wealth under brutal conditions. The most lucrative mines clustered around Syvlius, feeding an export economy that made the island wealthy despite its isolation.

The revolution shattered this system. Syvlius's trade tower—the infrastructure connecting the island to the astral current that passes 35 miles east of the mountains—was toppled during the uprising. Sky trade has collapsed. The mines still operate, but under chaotic conditions: Drasnian factions fight over claims, production is erratic, and there's no functioning export system.

Tekos sits roughly 35 miles from the same astral current, on the opposite side of the mountains. Before the revolution, this was a disadvantage—they routed exports through Syvlius. Now it's an opportunity, if they can build their own trade tower. The investment would be staggering, but Tekos may have no choice.

Vassum controlled sea trade and overland routes to Syvlius. With that hub destroyed, they're scrambling to find new arrangements. Some merchants are already negotiating directly with Drasnian gang leaders in the ruins.

Sy's silver-focused economy may actually benefit from the chaos—silver is valuable everywhere, and their extraction doesn't depend on Syvlius's infrastructure.

### Military

**Size**: Very small

**Quality**: Very low (except Sy)

**Enrollment**: Mandatory

Military service in Kyagos has traditionally been a mark of failure. If you weren't talented enough for anything else, you were enlisted. The pay was poor, the respect nonexistent. This left all four city-states with paper armies incapable of serious warfare.

The exception is Sy, where constant werewolf pressure forced actual military investment. Sy's border patrols are competent, experienced, and respected—a stark contrast to the ceremonial forces elsewhere.

The revolution exposed this weakness. Syvlius's garrison crumbled when faced with thousands of desperate slaves backed by Dead Moon warriors. The other city-states watched and realized they couldn't have done better.

Tekos is now attempting military reforms, but building a real army takes time they may not have. Vassum has largely given up on military solutions, focusing instead on negotiation and economic adaptation.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern seas, between Sea of Seven Snakes and Alrock Ocean

**Biomes**: Mountains, temperate forest, tropical jungle, swamp

**Named Regions**: Aktarvkus Mountains, Moonwood, Jungula Serpentine, Traevluong, Mythlias, Killing Hills, Avakus Forest

**Rivers & Lakes**: Lake Shikaya, Lake Teklan, Kessus Lake, Deadman's River, Merthcant River, Sygum River, Tyatach River, Mysik River

**Adjacent Waters**: Sea of Seven Snakes (west), Alrock Ocean (east), Sea of Sighs (southwest), Dawn's Passage (south)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None (confederacy)

**City-States**:
- Syvlius (destroyed/chaotic)
- Vassum (strained but functional)
- Tekos (fortress mentality)
- Sy (pragmatic survival)

**Contested Territories**: Avakus Forest, Valley of Whispers, mountain passes between city-states

### Current Conflicts

**The Syvlius Aftermath**
The revolution succeeded but left chaos. Drasnian factions control different districts of the ruined city, fighting each other over mine claims and resources. The Dead Moon Tribe helped destroy Syvlius but hasn't occupied it—they've retreated to Moonwood, leaving the Drasnians to sort themselves out. No unified government has emerged.

**Slave Unrest**
Every remaining slave in Kyagos has heard what happened in Syvlius. Tekos responds with increased brutality; Vassum tries to maintain order through economic incentives; Sy has quietly reduced the harshest treatment. None of these approaches is working particularly well.

**The Dead Moon Question**
For decades, Kyagos was "at war" with the Dead Moon Tribe in a conflict where neither side inflicted serious damage. The revolution revealed this as deliberate restraint—the tribe was waiting for their moment. Now they've demonstrated real military capability, and no one knows what they want next. Their shamans meet with Drasnian leaders in Syvlius. Their warriors patrol further south than they have in generations. Something is being negotiated.

**Economic Collapse**
The trade tower's destruction cut Kyagos off from the astral current that made sky trade possible. Sea routes remain, but they're slower, more dangerous, and lower capacity. The island's export economy is crippled until someone builds new infrastructure or finds alternative arrangements.

**Werewolf Pressure**
The Ulvsjael of Traevluong don't care about Kyagos's political crisis. They continue probing Sy's borders, picking off isolated patrols, enforcing their territorial claims. Sy can't afford to weaken their eastern defenses regardless of what's happening elsewhere.

**Kuru Opportunism**
The beast-warriors of the Killing Hills and Mythlias have noticed Kyagos's weakness. Raids on Tekos's agricultural settlements have increased. The Kuru aren't numerous enough to threaten the city directly, but they're making life harder for a city-state already under pressure.

### History

The Sylthik arrived on Kyagos centuries ago, displacing the human population that had lived there previously. These humans—the Dead Moon Tribe—retreated into Moonwood rather than submit to Sylthik rule. The new arrivals established city-states around the coastal regions they could defend, discovered the mountain wealth, and built an economy on mining.

The slave trade began when Kyagos's own population proved insufficient for the labor demands of the mines. Connections with Gorath's Slaver's Coast provided a solution: Drasnian dwarves, shipped across the sea in chains, extracted the wealth that made Kyagos prosperous. For three centuries, this arrangement held.

### The Syvlius Revolution

The revolution came suddenly. A band of outsiders—adventurers whose names are now legendary among the Drasnians—saw opportunity in Syvlius's massive slave population and Kyagos's weak military. They made contact with the Dead Moon Tribe, who had been waiting generations for exactly this kind of chance.

When the uprising began, it was coordinated. Slaves rose throughout the city while Dead Moon warriors swept down from Moonwood. The Sylthik garrison, trained for ceremony rather than combat, collapsed. Within days, Syvlius was burning.

The rebels didn't just overthrow the government—they demolished the city. The great trade tower, symbol of Syvlius's economic dominance, was toppled in a final act of destruction. Whether this was strategic (denying the other city-states easy reconquest) or emotional (destroying everything associated with Sylthik rule) depends on who you ask.

The heroes who led the revolution—Xe, Alice, Bryce, Woof, Bordrick, and Snuffles—departed after their victory. They lit the fire and left others to deal with the aftermath. The Drasnians they freed now struggle to build something from the ashes, while the Dead Moon Tribe has retreated to Moonwood with unclear intentions.

### Relations

**Gorath**: Primary source of Drasnian slaves. The revolution disrupts a profitable trade relationship—Gorath may intervene to protect their investment, or may simply redirect slaves elsewhere.

**Tamadrez**: Origin point for Drasnian slaves. News of the Syvlius revolution will spread to enslaved populations throughout the Shacklands.

**Trømgar**: The battle dwarves have long protested Drasnian enslavement. Syvlius's fall may embolden their underground resistance networks.

**Free Colonies**: Founded by Drasnian escapees from the Gorath-Kyagos slave route. They may reach out to the liberated Syvlius—or may stay cautiously distant.

**Phirexes**: Northern neighbor, separated by the Sea of Seven Snakes. Minimal contact; the gorgon queen has her own problems.

**Shykasas**: Insular alligator-folk kingdom to the southwest. No meaningful relationship.

### Player Hooks

**Chaos in the Ruins**: Syvlius offers opportunities for those willing to navigate gang politics. Someone needs to broker peace between Drasnian factions, establish trade relationships, or simply loot the wealthy estates before someone else does.

**The Dead Moon Mystery**: What do the tribal shamans want? Their meetings with Drasnian leaders suggest plans beyond simple revenge. An outsider might learn things neither side would share with Kyagosi.

**Werewolf Diplomacy**: Sy has begun cautious outreach to the Ulvsjael. An envoy who could actually communicate with the werewolves—or survive long enough to try—would be invaluable.

**The Trade Tower Race**: Both Tekos and Vassum want to build replacement infrastructure for the astral trade. The engineering challenges are immense, the financial stakes enormous, and sabotage from rivals is guaranteed.

**The Underground Railroad**: Escaped slaves from Tekos need routes to safety. The traditional paths through Syvlius are now chaotic rather than hostile—is that better or worse?

**Skyreach Secrets**: The ancient fortress in the mountains predates Sylthik settlement. With Kyagos destabilized, someone might finally investigate what it was built for—and whether anything useful remains.

## Kyorda

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Constitutional Monarchy with elected councils

**Primary Inhabitants**: Tikhaya (humans), Wytrolape (giant apes)

**Population**: ~45,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**:

- Philosophical inquiry and wisdom
- Coexistence between different peoples
- Discipline and self-mastery
- Preparation for the moon-curse

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

**Primary Exports**: Furs, timber, philosophical texts, crafted goods

**Primary Imports**: Metals, weapons, luxury goods from the south

- The Wytrolape serve as laborers and craftsmen, their great strength making them invaluable for forestry and construction
- Tikhaya scholars produce sought-after philosophical and mathematical treatises
- Trade relationships with Kyorda are valuable precisely because the nation is stable in an unstable region

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- The Kyordan military emphasizes discipline and coordination over raw numbers
- Tikhaya martial philosophers combine combat training with strategic thinking
- Wytrolape warriors are devastating in combat but must be carefully managed around Hollownight
- The nation maintains neutrality in the Enti-Danaki conflict, though border incidents sometimes occur

### Geography

**Location**: Western Frostmarch Peninsula

**Biomes**: Temperate forest, taiga

**Named Entities**: Buleeri T'han (forest), Gysheyani (forest), Bank of Infindior

**Rivers & Lakes**: Multiple unnamed rivers flowing to Wavecrash Sound

**Adjacent Waters**: Wavecrash Sound, Denrik Sea approaches

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Xandur—a city of stone and timber built around a natural harbor on Wavecrash Sound

**Cities**: Xandur

**Towns**: Phelmuz, Petvok, Rylvok, Skelgrod

**Villages**: Numerous scattered through the forests

### Primary Conflicts

- Managing the Wytrolape moon-curse during Hollownight—every month brings the risk of rage-induced violence
- Protecting the Bank of Infindior, a planar institution of immense importance
- Maritime threats from the Denrik Sea, including whatever dwells in the Darkstone Caves beneath the waters
- Isolation—Kyorda is far from other civilized lands, making supply chains precarious and outside aid slow to arrive

### History

Kyorda was founded centuries ago by Tikhaya settlers who recognized that the region could support civilization if properly managed. The integration of the Wytrolape came later—the giant apes were already present, and rather than conflict, the Tikhaya chose coexistence.

The moon-curse has shaped Kyordan society profoundly. Every aspect of life accounts for the periodic madness that affects the Wytrolape population. Buildings are reinforced, contingency plans exist for Hollownight, and both peoples have developed rituals and practices to minimize casualties during the dangerous days.

### The Bank of Infindior

East of Xandur, where void and time leylines cross, stands the Bank of Infindior—a planar institution where time does not pass. Depositors can store possessions here with perfect security; items remain exactly as they were when deposited, regardless of how long they remain. The bank serves clients from across Alaria, and protecting it is one of Kyorda's most important duties.

## L'coth D'hari

Tags: state, elves, gamori

The northern peninsula of the Elder Wilds—a multi-lobed landmass of dense jungle surrounded by the South Sea. L'Coth D'hari is the territory of the **Gamori**, an elven people who have developed a culture centered on death, transformation, and the sacred hunt.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Republic

**Primary Inhabitants**: Gamori (elves)

**Population**: Unknown (the Gamori do not census themselves for outsiders)

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**:
- **Death and rebirth cycles** — The Gamori view death not as ending but as transformation. Funerals are massive, elaborate affairs celebrating the transition.
- **Body modification** — Scarification, bone-shaping, implants of natural materials, rings, piercings, paint, and extensive tattoos. Modification marks status, achievement, and spiritual growth.
- **Sacred hunting** — Rituals involving the pursuit and consumption of specific prey. You drink the blood of what you kill to gain its strength.
- **Territorial isolation** — Outsiders are rarely welcome. The Gamori consider the wider Elder Wilds their hunting grounds and have been known to dismantle settlement attempts beyond their borders.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Undeveloped (by choice—they have access to trade goods but prefer traditional methods)

**Primary Exports**: Rare jungle herbs, bloodwood timber (limited), ritual components

**Primary Imports**: Metal goods, worked glass, alchemical compounds

The Gamori trade reluctantly and only when necessary. Most contact occurs at the edges of their territory or through intermediaries in Urzula.

### Military

**Size**: Medium (relative to population)

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Cultural (all Gamori participate in hunts, which serve as military training)

Every Gamori is a hunter. The distinction between "civilian" and "warrior" doesn't exist in their culture. Their knowledge of the jungle terrain, combined with body modifications that often include enhanced senses or natural weapons, makes them deadly defenders of their territory.

They do not field armies in the conventional sense. Instead, the jungle itself becomes the weapon—ambushes, traps, poison, and psychological warfare.

### Geography

**Location**: Northern peninsula of the Elder Wilds, eastern Aboyinzu

**Biomes**: Dense tropical rainforest

**Named Entities**:
- **Breathless Bay** — Western inlet, fog-shrouded and unnaturally still
- **Paradise Inlet** — Central waterway dividing the main peninsula lobes
- **Farthing Inlet** — Southern boundary separating L'Coth D'hari from the Apelands

**Adjacent Waters**: South Sea (north and west)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Eriyen (ceremonial gathering place rather than permanent city)

**Cities**: None in the conventional sense

**Towns**: Multiple scattered communities throughout the peninsula

**Villages**: Numerous, hidden, deliberately difficult to find

### Internal Complications

**Thorne**: In the interior of the peninsula dwells an ancient druid who predates the Gamori arrival—possibly by millennia. The Gamori have an understanding with Thorne, the terms of which they do not discuss. The grove called Thorne is a place where the forest has become alien, and even the death-obsessed Gamori give it wide berth.

### Primary Conflicts

- Occasional friction with Urzula over hunting grounds in Seyiy
- Rare conflicts with ape incursions from the south
- Internal disputes between communities over territory and prey

### History

The Gamori arrived in L'Coth D'hari at an unknown point in history—likely during the early Third Eon. They found the peninsula already inhabited by the druid Thorne and reached an accommodation that persists to this day. The nature of this agreement shapes Gamori culture in ways they do not explain to outsiders.

They have no written history. Their past is preserved through ritual scarification, where major events are carved into the bodies of those who experienced them and passed down through generations of modification.

See: [Elder Wilds - L'Coth D'hari](../../atlas_of_alaria/elder_wilds/_intro.md#lcoth-dhari)

## Lenora

Tags: state, elves, confederation

The agricultural heartland of the elven confederation. Lenora occupies the fertile lowlands of the southern Farlands, bounded by the Ocean of Elorinia to the west, the Kelder Mountains to the east, and the Amholia Greras forest to the south. Of the three elven kingdoms, Lenora is the most populated, the most productive, and—its neighbors would say—the most ambitious.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Landsmeet (council of landholders)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Elves

**Population**: ~400,000 (largest of the three kingdoms)

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**:
- Productivity and stewardship of the land
- Respect for ancestral holdings
- Confederation loyalty (contested)
- Ritual and seasonal observance

The **Landsmeet** governs Lenora—a council of every elf who holds productive land. In theory, this is democratic. In practice, the ancient families who settled the river valleys generations ago control most of the good territory. The Landsmeet convenes in Jien Asari four times yearly, aligned with the solstices and equinoxes.

#### The Succession Question

The Landsmeet has always been led by a **High Steward**—a position that rotates among the twelve founding families according to a schedule set nearly 3,000 years ago. The rotation made sense then: twelve families, twelve positions in the cycle, each ruling for a century before passing the mantle.

But time has complicated matters. Three of the original families have died out entirely. Two others merged through marriage five centuries ago. Meanwhile, families that were minor landholders at the founding now control vast territories through shrewd acquisition and favorable harvests.

The current schedule no longer reflects reality. Families that no longer exist still have their turn "reserved." Living families argue over who inherits the empty slots. The families enriched by recent centuries demand inclusion in the rotation. Those who benefit from the old schedule refuse to renegotiate.

This is not a class conflict—everyone involved is traditional, respects the ancestors, and believes in the old ways. They simply disagree about which old ways apply. The dispute has simmered for two centuries without resolution. Every Landsmeet spends at least a day arguing about it before moving to other business.

### Economy

**Size**: Very Large

**Tech**: Very Developed

**Primary Exports**: Grain, preserved foods, timber, Elorshianod hardwoods, river fish, cultural goods

**Primary Imports**: Titan-bone ritual objects (from Deo Esari), mountain herbs, metals

Lenora feeds the confederation. The kingdom's three great rivers—Kilgre Kevi, Kilgre Apnuk, and Kilgre Thovrys—water vast agricultural lands. The eastern plains between Elorshianod forest and the Kelder foothills are among the most productive farmland on the continent.

Grain barges travel constantly between the interior farms and the coastal ports. Eilren Thalas and Jien Asari handle most of the trade; Imyena Edhil operates independently (see below).

The Lenorans also harvest Elorshianod's hardwoods—carefully, ritually, with appropriate offerings. The lumber is prized throughout the known world, though the elves sell relatively little of it. Most goes to confederation shipyards or internal construction.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Traditional levy system

Lenora maintains a small professional guard for the major cities and relies on levy obligations for larger conflicts. Every landholder must provide soldiers proportional to their holdings—another ancient rule that causes friction when new-wealth families are assessed at rates set for their ancestors' tiny plots.

The Lenoran military has not fought a serious external war in over a millennium. Their most recent conflicts have been minor border disputes with Rakite herders and occasional bandit suppression. The soldiers are well-trained but untested.

### Geography

**Location**: Western Farlands, between Ocean of Elorinia and Kelder Mountains

**Biomes**: Temperate forest, fertile plains, river valleys

**Major Geographic Features**:
- **Elorshianod**: The great coastal forest, running north-south along the western shore. Unlike Illron's sacred forest, Elorshianod is settled and worked—river towns dot both banks of the Kilgre Kevi and Kilgre Apnuk.
- **Starluck Hills**: Southern hill country separating Lenora proper from the Amholia Greras. Named for the phosphorescent fungi that bloom on autumn nights.
- **Vokas Pendl**: The northern mountain spur where the Kilgre Kevi originates.

**Rivers & Lakes**:
- **Kilgre Kevi**: Northern river, flows from Vokas Pendl through Elorshianod to the coast
- **Kilgre Apnuk**: Central river with many tributaries, feeds the capital at Jien Asari
- **Kilgre Thovrys**: Southern river, feeding the isolated port of Imyena Edhil

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Jien Asari (inland, on the Kilgre Apnuk)

**Major Cities**:
- **Eilren Thalas**: Primary port, southwestern coast. Handles most foreign trade.
- **Imyena Edhil**: Southern port on Kilgre Thovrys. Deliberately isolated—see below.

**Coastal Settlements**: Galaena Ancalen, Imsegroth, Ufa Caelora (along Kilgre Kevi from Vokas Pendl to ocean)

**Forest Settlements**: Numerous towns within Elorshianod along both major rivers

**Eastern Settlements**: Agricultural villages in the fertile plains before the Kelder foothills

### Imyena Edhil — The City of the Grieving

Imyena Edhil has no roads connecting it to the rest of Lenora. The only access is by river (Kilgre Thovrys) or sea. This is deliberate.

The city is home to the **Grieving**—elves who have committed the sin of forgetting a true name. In elven belief, when you forget someone's true name, their spirit is lost forever to the void beyond Celestia. The most permanent death possible. If you are responsible for this loss—through negligence, malice, or simple failure of memory—you have committed something unforgivable.

The Grieving are exiled to Imyena Edhil. They may never leave. The walls around the city aren't to keep enemies out—they're to keep the Grieving in. These elves are considered spiritually contaminated. Prolonged contact with them might cause others to forget.

But Imyena Edhil is still a city, not a prison camp:
- The Grieving have children, and those children inherit the stain
