- The city has existed for millennia; thousands of elves live there now
- They've built their own society—fishing, river trade within limits, crafts
- Ships can dock at the river mouth, but outsiders rarely enter the walls

The city is not a hellhole. The Grieving have festivals, families, art, love. But there's a weight to the place. Everyone knows why they're there. Everyone knows they'll never leave. Visitors describe Imyena Edhil as beautiful but heavy—like the whole city is holding its breath.

Anyone who flees Imyena Edhil is hunted. Not killed—returned. The other cities don't want the Grieving among them.

### Relations

**Illron**: Tense. Lenora has the population and wealth; Illron has tradition and the sacred forest. The confederation's one-kingdom-one-voice voting structure favors Illron, and Lenora resents it. But neither side wants to break the alliance—the ancestors who signed the Three Kingdoms Treaty are still watching at Laeroth Esori.

**Deo Esari**: Cordial but distant. Lenora needs Deo Esari's titan-bone ritual objects for certain ceremonies; Deo Esari needs Lenora's food. Neither particularly likes the other. Deo Esari views Lenora as vulgar and too concerned with growth; Lenora views Deo Esari as hidebound and self-righteous.

**Rakite**: Formal trade relations with the buffalo-herding elves to the east. The Rakiteans don't participate in the confederation, but they're still kin. Trade is regulated, ritualized, and generally peaceful.

### Primary Conflicts

- **The Succession Question**: Which families control the High Steward rotation? No resolution in sight.
- **Confederation Representation**: Should Lenora's larger population mean more votes at Laeroth Esori? The ancestors said no. But the ancestors didn't anticipate Lenora growing to four times Illron's population.
- **Imyena Edhil**: Some younger Lenorans question whether the Grieving truly deserve eternal exile. This is considered a dangerous opinion to hold publicly.

### History

The elves of what would become Lenora arrived in the Farlands roughly 5,000 years ago, part of a larger migration from the east. They settled the river valleys, cleared portions of Elorshianod for farming, and gradually pushed the forest's original inhabitants—various fae creatures—southward into what is now Amholia Greras.

The Three Kingdoms Treaty, signed at Laeroth Esori approximately 4,000 years ago, established the confederation and ended centuries of border warfare between the elven polities. Lenora has honored the treaty since, though not always happily.

The founding of Imyena Edhil is not recorded in official histories. The city simply exists, and always has, in the memories of living elves. Some scholars believe it predates the confederation itself—that the Grieving were being exiled even before the three kingdoms formally united.

## Lethos

Tags: state, shacklands

A waterlogged jungle peninsula that exists in the shadow of Gorath—and in the shadow of something older, something the jungle itself remembers.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: The Vigil (open council)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Faeja, Darklings, Gillykin, Druids

**Population**: ~5,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**:
- Secrecy: What happens in Lethos stays in Lethos
- Maintenance: The Binding must be tended, always
- Consensus: No single voice can speak for all seven villages
- Memory: The dead must be remembered, lest they remember themselves

The Vigil is less a government than a coordination mechanism. Representatives from each of Lethos's seven villages meet at Xyrionn twice yearly—more often during Hollownight or times of crisis—to ensure the Binding remains stable. Decisions require consensus from at least five villages, making Lethos slow to act but nearly impossible to subvert from within.

Each village handles its own internal affairs. There are no laws that apply across all of Lethos, no shared taxation, no unified justice system. What binds the villages together is not politics but necessity: if the Binding fails, everyone dies.

### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Undeveloped

**Primary Exports**: Rare herbs, poisons, certain jungle hardwoods

**Primary Imports**: Metal goods, preserved foods, salt

Lethos has almost no economy in the conventional sense. The villages are largely self-sufficient, living on fish, jungle game, cultivated roots, and gathered plants. What trade exists happens at Altimarloch, the village closest to the Gorathi border, where a few brave merchants bring metal tools, salt, and other goods unavailable in the deep jungle.

In return, Lethos exports rare medicinal plants and poisons that grow nowhere else—perhaps because of the Binding's influence on the local flora. These command high prices among Gorathi alchemists and physicians, creating a small but consistent flow of trade. The Faeja village of Mangaone specializes in this cultivation.

The Gillykin of Qlyp Augo participate in no trade at all.

### Military

**Size**: None (formal); Very Small (informal)

**Quality**: Very High (asymmetric)

**Enrollment**: None (voluntary specialists)

Lethos has no army. It doesn't need one.

The closest thing to a military force is the hunters of Ohoka, a Darkling village deep in the jungle. When threats arise—bandits who think they can prey on isolated villagers, Gorathi deserters seeking to disappear, occasionally something worse—Ohoka's hunters move through the supernatural darkness to eliminate them. They are very good at this. Bodies are rarely found.

But Lethos's true defense is the Binding itself. An invading army would face a jungle that actively resists them: paths that change, water that poisons, darkness that conceals enemies and disorients allies. Soldiers who die in Lethos do not stay dead—they become part of what they were fighting. The longer an occupation lasts, the stronger the jungle's resistance grows.

Gorath learned this three centuries ago. They have not forgotten.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern peninsula extending into the Sea of Sighs, eastern Shacklands

**Biomes**: Flooded jungle, mangrove swamp, brackish wetlands

**Named Entities**: Xyrionn (sacred stump), Qlyp Augo (ruins)

**Rivers & Lakes**: Multiple unnamed waterways; the land is more water than earth

**Adjacent Waters**: Sea of Sighs (south, east); Sherivreen Jungle coast (northeast)

Lethos is barely land at all. The jungle grows from standing water; paths between villages are canoe routes; buildings are constructed on stilts or floating platforms. Seasonal floods reshape the terrain constantly. Only a few areas of solid high ground exist, and these are significant—Erua is built on one, Xyrionn rises from another.

The climate is hot, humid, and oppressive. Rain falls almost daily. Insects are constant companions. Outsiders find the environment exhausting even before accounting for the Binding's effects.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None (Xyrionn serves as meeting place)

**Cities**: None

**Towns**: None

**Villages**:
- Tanekala (south, fishing)
- Altimarloch (west, trade/contact)
- Mangaone (interior, herbalism)
- Erua (east, druids)
- Ohoka (deep jungle, Darklings)
- Mintai (center, memory-keepers)
- Eptos (northeast, Gillykin monitors)

### Primary Conflicts

**Internal**:
- Gradual weakening of the Binding (requires increasing effort to maintain)
- Tension between isolation and survival (some argue for more outside contact)
- The Gillykin's increasing silence (they've grown more withdrawn recently)
- Succession at Erua (the druids are finally dying; who will anchor the Binding?)

**External**:
- The Binding may be spreading into the Sea of Sighs (ships disappearing)
- Gorathi interest is stirring (descendants of the Fourth Legion seek answers)
- Unknown pressure from beneath Qlyp Augo (the Gillykin warn of something awakening)

### History

**412 PR** — Gorath's Fourth Legion crosses into Lethos; occupation begins

**414 PR** — Provincial capital established at Qlyp Augo; garrison of 3,000

**~420 PR** — Druids begin the Binding ritual; first Gorathi dead woven into jungle

**452 PR** — Night of Roots; Qlyp Augo consumed; Gillykin emerge; Gorath withdraws

**453 PR** — Gorathi archives sealed; "tribute exemption" established

**~550 PR** — Village of Eptos founded to monitor Qlyp Augo

**Present** — Binding weakening; Veleth (druid of Xyrionn) aging; Gillykin increasingly withdrawn

### Relations

**Gorath**: Cold non-interference. Gorath maintains the "tribute exemption" and forbids military operations in Lethos. Most Gorathi officials don't know why. Those who do don't ask.

**Other Shacklands States**: Minimal contact. Lethos participates in no regional politics, signs no treaties, and asks nothing from its neighbors.

**The Sea of Sighs**: Complicated. Something in the water around Lethos is dangerous, and it may be getting worse.

## Lier

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Merchant republic

**Primary Inhabitants**: Humans

**Population**: 45,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**: Trade, pragmatism, discretion, profit

- Lier is governed by a council of merchant families who've accumulated enough wealth to buy seats. Politics is transactional—votes are traded, favors are currency, and everyone understands that principle matters less than profit.
- The population is predominantly human, though the port of Hessam attracts visitors and residents from across the Middle Sea. Diversity is tolerated as long as it's profitable.
- Lier serves as the commercial hub of Erpeus Island, trading Mkinti's clockwork and gems to the wider world while supplying the gnomes with goods they can't produce underground.
- The culture values discretion. Lier's merchants have learned that asking too many questions loses customers, and everyone has secrets worth keeping.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Re-exported Mkinti goods, agricultural products, fish, ships

**Primary Imports**: Grain, luxury goods, raw materials for Mkinti

- Lier's economy runs on being the middleman. Mkinti's gnomes don't want to deal with outsiders; Lier's merchants are happy to do it for a cut.
- The fertile hills produce grain, olives, and wine that feed both Lier and supplement Mkinti's non-gnomish population.
- Fishing fleets work the surrounding waters, and shipyards in Hessam build vessels for Middle Sea trade.
- The Bay of Whispers draws a peculiar sort of tourism—those seeking lost secrets or trying to speak across time.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (mercenary-heavy)

- Lier maintains a small professional military supplemented by mercenaries when needed. The merchant families prefer hiring soldiers to raising them.
- The navy is more substantial—protecting trade routes matters more than defending borders on an island this size.
- The real defense is economic. Attacking Lier would disrupt trade across Erpeus, and the gnomes of Mkinti have made clear they'd respond to threats to their supply chain.

### Geography

**Location**: Central Erpeus Island, in the Middle Sea

**Biomes**: Rolling hills, coastal lowlands, rocky highlands

**Named Entities**: Hills of Soromun, Murder Hills, Devil's Crest, Bay of Whispers

**Rivers & Lakes**: Several rivers flowing from the highlands to the coast

**Adjacent Waters**: The Middle Sea (east and west coasts)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Hessam

**Cities**: Hessam

**Towns**: Lucsha, Dozna, Coyzevan, Tothpa, Oychka, Gam

**Villages**: Numerous farming and fishing villages

### Primary Conflicts

**The Gnomish Question**

Lier's prosperity depends on Mkinti, but the gnomes' internal crisis threatens everything. If House Wizzik loses control, if civil war breaks out, if the clockwork trade stops—Lier's economy collapses. The merchant council is desperately trying to maintain good relations with all factions without picking sides.

**The Bay of Whispers**

The supernatural bay outside Hessam attracts seekers of forbidden knowledge—and the trouble that follows them. Strange cults, obsessed scholars, people trying to undo the past or learn the future. The council tolerates the tourism revenue but worries about what secrets might emerge.

**The Murder Hills**

The highlands got their name honestly. Bandits, fugitives, and things that don't like sunlight shelter in the Murder Hills, preying on travelers between Lier and Azuros. The council sends occasional expeditions to clean them out; the hills refill within months.

**Azuros Relations**

The monks to the north are harmless but unhelpful. They won't trade, won't fight, won't engage with the world in ways Lier's merchants understand. The council mostly ignores them—but some worry about what would happen if the monastery's isolation ever broke down.

### History

Lier was founded as a fishing village that grew into a trading post. Its position on Erpeus made it the natural intermediary between the gnomes of the mountains and the human world beyond.

The merchant families emerged gradually, accumulating wealth through generations of careful dealing. They formalized their power into the council system, where money determines influence and old blood means nothing without new gold.

The discovery of the Bay of Whispers changed Lier's character. The supernatural phenomenon drew attention—scholars, mystics, the desperate, and the curious. The merchants adapted, charging fees for access, selling supplies to seekers, and learning which questions not to ask.

Modern Lier is prosperous, pragmatic, and perpetually anxious. The gnomish crisis threatens everything. The Bay of Whispers generates opportunity and danger in equal measure. The Murder Hills resist all attempts at civilization. But the merchants keep trading, the ships keep sailing, and the money keeps flowing—for now.

## Linas

Tags: citystate, state, trade, dye

The dye capital of Alaria, built on a coast that would otherwise be unremarkable. Linas exists because a shipwrecked merchant discovered that the local shellfish—starfish, snails, and clams—contained pigments of extraordinary brilliance. That merchant built a fortune, and that fortune became a city.

Today, hundreds of ships leave Linas harbor each morning, heading for the Starfish Isles to harvest the shellfish that sustain the city's economy. The dye they produce—a brilliant orange-gold that holds its color through washing, sunlight, and years of wear—commands premium prices across Alaria. Nobles, merchants, and anyone who wants to signal wealth and taste pays handsomely for Linas pigments.

### Government

Linas is ruled by the **Dye Guilds**—the major merchant houses that control harvesting rights, processing facilities, and export networks. The guild masters collectively form a council that handles city governance, though "governance" mostly means protecting the dye trade and ensuring no single house gains too much advantage over the others.

The current balance of power involves five major guilds and perhaps a dozen minor ones. The major guilds control the best harvesting territories in the Starfish Isles, the largest processing facilities, and the most lucrative export contracts. The minor guilds fight for scraps—secondary harvesting grounds, subcontracting work, niche markets the majors don't bother with.

Guild politics are cutthroat but rarely violent. Reputation matters too much for open warfare. Instead, houses compete through quality, efficiency, and careful manipulation of harvesting quotas and trade agreements. The occasional poisoning or convenient accident happens, of course, but discreetly.

### The Starfish Isles

The foundation of Linas's wealth lies in the shallow waters north of the city. The Starfish Isles—a maze of sandbars, reefs, and tidal flats—contain the richest shellfish beds on the Seacleft Coast. The creatures that live there have shells saturated with pigment, concentrated in ways found nowhere else.

Linas claims exclusive harvesting rights to the isles, enforced by armed guild vessels that patrol the major channels. The dye guilds divide the harvesting grounds among themselves according to ancient agreements and more recent negotiations. Poaching is constant—the waters are too vast for complete control—but the guilds catch enough interlopers to make the risks real.

The harvesting season runs from late spring through early autumn. During peak months, the harbor empties each morning as the fleet heads out, and fills each evening with boats heavy with shellfish. The processing facilities run day and night, extracting pigment before the catch spoils.

### The Tide-Singers

On the outer reaches of the Starfish Isles, where the harvesting grounds give way to the Walker Depths, a small religious order maintains weathered shrines. The **Tide-Singers** perform ceremonies for the drowned dead who walk the seafloor below—singing names into the water to release trapped ancestors.

Most Linas harvesters consider the Singers harmless eccentrics. The guilds tolerate them because they provide shelter to boats caught in storms and seem to calm the waters near their shrines. A few old captains speak of them with genuine respect, claiming the Singers know things about the isles that no chart records.

The Singers accept no payment in coin. Families seeking their services must bring objects that belonged to the deceased, which the Singers weight with stones and drop into the depths. Whether the rituals work is a matter of faith.

### Economy

Dye dominates everything. The pigment extracted from Starfish Isles shellfish accounts for the vast majority of Linas's exports and tax revenue. Secondary industries exist—boat-building, net-making, barrel production, food services for the harvesting fleet—but all of them ultimately serve the dye trade.

The finished product travels across Alaria through legitimate trade networks and less legitimate ones. Linas merchants sell directly to textile producers in the Middle Sea states, to luxury goods traders in Tarkhon, to anyone with coin and a desire for color. The dye guilds maintain offices in major ports across the continent.

What the guilds don't openly acknowledge is how much of their product moves through Mirko. The lawless city-state to the south provides access to markets that respectable merchants can't reach—smuggling routes, black markets, customers who prefer not to leave paper trails. Linas dye ends up in places the guilds officially disapprove of, and everyone involved profits.

### Relations

**With Enavadi**: Cold indifference, occasionally warming to cold irritation. Enavadi fishermen work the northern edges of Linas's claimed waters, and the two groups have different ideas about where the boundaries lie. Conflicts are rare but sharp when they occur. The Enavadi consider Linas harvesters clumsy amateurs who mistake money for skill; Linas considers the Enavadi primitive isolationists with an inflated sense of their own importance.

**With Mirko**: Officially proper, practically intimate. Mirko's criminal networks distribute Linas dye to markets that guild merchants can't or won't touch directly. The guilds pretend their product ends up only in legitimate hands; Mirko pretends they're simple merchants. Everyone makes money.

Mirko's guilds also provide "protection" for Linas harvesting ships in the Starfish Isles—for a fee. This protection is remarkably effective, primarily because the main threat to harvesters is Mirko-affiliated pirates who know which ships have paid.

**With Everyone Else**: Linas trades with anyone who has coin. The dye guilds maintain commercial relationships across the Seacleft Coast and beyond, sending their product wherever there's demand. Politically, Linas prefers to stay neutral in regional conflicts—war is bad for business, and the city has no territorial ambitions beyond protecting its harvesting grounds.

### Culture

Linas is a merchant city, and merchant values dominate. Success is measured in coin, status in guild standing, respectability in the quality of one's business relationships. The city is cosmopolitan by necessity—traders from across Alaria pass through, and the guilds hire talent wherever they find it.

The dye trade shapes everything, including fashion. Wearing Linas orange is a statement of wealth across Alaria, but in Linas itself, the color is common enough to be unremarkable. The truly wealthy distinguish themselves through *other* colors—blues, purples, greens that must be imported at great expense—or through the subtle quality differences that mark master-grade dye from standard production.

The city smells of salt, shellfish, and the chemical tang of pigment extraction. New visitors find it overwhelming. Residents stop noticing within a week.

### Player Hooks

- **Harvesting Rights**: A minor guild wants to expand into waters claimed by a major house. They need evidence that the major house's charter doesn't actually cover the disputed area—evidence that might be found in old records, or might need to be manufactured.

- **The Vermilion Legacy**: Aldos Vermilion's fortune was supposedly distributed to the families of the Birthday Wreck victims. Some of it was. Not all. Someone knows where the rest went, and someone else wants to find out.

- **Tide-Singer Secrets**: The Singers know something about the Walker Depths—something the guilds would pay well to learn, or pay well to suppress. A dying Singer wants to pass on knowledge to someone outside the order.

- **Mirko Complications**: A Linas guild's Mirko contacts have become liabilities. The criminals know too much, want too large a cut, or have attracted attention the guild can't afford. The problem needs to be solved quietly, far from Linas, by people who can't be traced back.

## M'Svyla

Tags: state

Home to the Ix'Lorett lizardfolk. Located just south of the Crystal Mountains in Central Aboyinzu.

## Mez

Tags: state

Links: States

Mez is not a nation in any sense the surface world would recognize. It is a territory—a claimed domain beneath the southwestern Widebarrow Mountains where the Lyzine hold absolute sway. To call it a "state" insults the concept; to ignore it would be suicide. The spider-folk have ruled these depths for millennia, and they consider the entire surface world a temporary inconvenience on land that is rightfully theirs.

The Lyzine are fallen nobility, or so they insist. Their origin myths speak of vast surface kingdoms, elegant courts, and a glorious civilization brought low by jealous lesser races who conspired to drive them underground. Whether any of this is true—scholars outside Ve have never heard of these supposed kingdoms—matters less than the Lyzine belief in it. They consider themselves exiled royalty, and they intend to reclaim their throne. Every raid is a step toward restoration. Every human they kill or enslave is a correction of historical injustice.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Anarchy (Competing Queendoms)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Lyzine

**Population**: 20,000 (estimated; actual numbers unknown)

**Stability**: Very Unstable

**Core Values**: Supremacy, revenge, pleasure, legacy

- There is no central authority in Mez. Power rests with the spider queens—breeding females who command absolute loyalty from their respective hordes. Each queen rules her own domain within the warren complex, competing with rivals for territory, resources, and males.
- Succession is violent. A queen holds power until a challenger defeats her—either through combat, assassination, or the slower method of starving out her horde. The average reign lasts perhaps fifty years; some queens have endured for centuries.
- Males exist to serve. They fight, they build, they raid—and the most successful are rewarded with the privilege of attempting to mate with a queen. This is not the honor it sounds like; the process kills most participants. Those who survive contribute their bloodline to the next generation and are granted status as "consort-elect," a protected class with limited authority.
- Lyzine society is rigidly hierarchical beneath its chaotic surface. Within a horde, ranks are absolute. The strong dominate the weak. The clever exploit the foolish. Movement between ranks is possible—through combat, through cunning, through proving value—but the hierarchy itself is never questioned.

### Economy

**Size**: None (raid-based)

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Lyzine silk (involuntary)

**Primary Imports**: Slaves, surface goods (taken by force)

- The Lyzine have no economy as surface dwellers understand it. Wealth exists—silk, captured treasures, slaves—but it circulates through gift-giving, tribute, and theft rather than trade.
- Lyzine silk is the most valuable substance to leave Mez, and the Lyzine hate that it does. The silk is harvested from abandoned webs and slain Lyzine by Dernish hunters; no living Lyzine would sell it. The silk is extraordinarily strong, lighter than cotton, and takes dye beautifully. Fashion houses in Shyona pay fortunes for it. The Lyzine consider the trade a desecration.
- Slaves are the true currency of Mez. Captured surface dwellers—humans, primarily, taken in raids—serve as laborers, entertainment, and food. A queen's power is measured partly by the size of her slave pens. Well-treated slaves can survive for years; poorly treated ones last weeks. The Lyzine don't particularly care either way.
- Some inter-horde trade occurs: silk for ore, slaves for breeding rights, information for future considerations. These transactions happen through intermediaries and are always temporary alliances rather than permanent arrangements.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (but refusal is death)

- Every adult Lyzine is a warrior. Their exoskeletons provide natural armor equivalent to mail; their venom paralyzes; their web glands allow them to control terrain in ways no surface army can match. A single Lyzine is worth three human soldiers. A coordinated horde is a nightmare.
- Raids are the primary military activity. A queen sends her warriors to the surface to capture slaves, seize resources, and remind the humans above who the true masters are. Raids follow seasonal patterns—heavier in winter when Dernish patrols are thinner, lighter in summer when the surface is more vigilant.
- Large-scale assaults happen every few decades, when a particularly ambitious queen unites multiple hordes for a major push. These are the events that reshape borders and fill the Boneswamps with new dead. The last major offensive was forty years ago; the next could come tomorrow.
- Lyzine fight with natural weapons—venom, claws, webs—supplemented by captured or crafted arms. Their weapons tend toward the elegant: thin rapiers, curved daggers, weighted nets. Heavy armor is rare; their exoskeletons suffice, and mobility matters more than protection in tunnel fighting.

### Geography

**Location**: Beneath the southwestern Widebarrow Mountains, extending under Dern's territory and parts of the Blood Mountains

**Biomes**: Subterranean caverns, artificial warrens, fungal forests

**Named Entities**: The Deep Warren (primary Lyzine territory), various queen-claimed domains

**Rivers & Lakes**: Underground rivers and lakes (named in Lyzine tongue; no common translations)

**Adjacent Waters**: None (subterranean)

- The true extent of Mez is unknown. Dernish maps show sealed tunnels and known passages, but the Lyzine dig constantly, and their domain certainly extends far beyond what surface cartographers have charted.
- The upper levels, closest to the surface, are contested ground—the zone where Lyzine raids launch and Dernish patrols venture. Below that, the tunnels open into vast cavern systems where the Lyzine have dwelt for millennia.
- Fungal forests light the depths with bioluminescence. The Lyzine cultivate certain species for food; others grow wild in conditions no surface crop could survive. These forests are beautiful in a terrible way—soft glowing lights, delicate formations, and always the sense of being watched.
- Each queen claims a territory within the warren complex. Borders are marked with pheromones and web-patterns that surface dwellers can't perceive but Lyzine read like maps. Crossing another queen's territory uninvited is war.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Glik (primary queen's domain)
**Cities**: None (the concept doesn't translate)
**Towns**: Tnu Kiin
**Villages**: Tsuicht

*Note: These "settlements" are not equivalent to surface towns. They are domains—territories claimed by specific queens or powerful subordinates, with populations that shift as allegiances change.*

**Glik** — The domain of Queen Thessivara, currently the most powerful spider queen in Mez. Glik is less a city than a vast cave system draped in silk, its chambers connected by web-bridges and tunnel-warrens that would drive a human mad trying to navigate. Perhaps 8,000 Lyzine dwell here, along with several hundred slaves.

Thessivara has held power for 180 years, an extraordinary span. She's survived seven assassination attempts, three succession wars, and countless minor challenges. Her longevity comes from ruthless cunning: she plays rivals against each other, keeps her consorts weak, and maintains the largest slave population in Mez as both labor force and hostage pool. Other queens hate her; none have managed to bring her down.

The great silk-halls of Glik are said to be magnificent—vast chambers lined with woven tapestries depicting Lyzine history, lit by phosphorescent fungi, and centered on the Queen's Throne where Thessivara receives supplicants. Survivors who've escaped Glik speak of terrible beauty.

**Tnu Kiin** — A secondary domain controlled by Queen Vexara, Thessivara's most dangerous rival. Tnu Kiin occupies a warren complex in the southern reaches of Mez, closer to the Blood Mountains and further from human interference. Perhaps 5,000 Lyzine call it home.

Vexara is younger than Thessivara—a mere 90 years—but ambitious beyond measure. She's been cultivating alliances with lesser queens, building a coalition that could challenge Glik's dominance. The politics are complex: some queens ally with Vexara from genuine ambition; others do so hoping to weaken Thessivara enough to create opportunity for themselves.

Tnu Kiin is more militaristic than Glik, focused on raids and warfare rather than silk-weaving and court intrigue. Vexara's warriors are the most feared in Mez—better trained, better equipped, and driven by their queen's burning desire to prove herself. If a major offensive comes, Tnu Kiin will likely lead it.

**Tsuicht** — A minor domain, almost a footnote. Controlled by Queen Mylassia, an old queen who has retreated from active competition. Tsuicht is where aging Lyzine go when they've lost the strength to fight and the cunning to scheme—a quiet backwater by Lyzine standards.

Perhaps 2,000 Lyzine live here, including a disproportionate number of elderly. Mylassia tolerates them; she has no ambition left except survival, and a large population of dependents discourages rivals from bothering to conquer her. The domain produces nothing of value and launches no raids of consequence.

Curiously, Tsuicht maintains the closest thing Mez has to contact with the surface. A handful of humans—former slaves who earned freedom through unknown service—dwell in the outer tunnels, serving as intermediaries for the rare occasions when Lyzine and surface dwellers must communicate. The Dernish know these "tunnel-speakers" exist but officially deny it.

### Primary Conflicts

- **Dern and the Surface**: The eternal war of reclamation. The Lyzine will never stop. The surface, they believe, belongs to them. Every raid is a step toward restoration.
- **The Thessivara-Vexara Rivalry**: The two most powerful queens in Mez are heading toward confrontation. When it comes—not if—it will reshape Lyzine politics for generations. The question is whether they'll fight each other first, or unite for a surface offensive that allows both to prove themselves.
- **The Blood Mountains Question**: Beneath the Blood Mountains dwell the Bloodlings—vampiric gnomes with their own subterranean realm. The Lyzine and Bloodlings have skirmished for centuries. Neither has gained lasting advantage. Some queens advocate alliance against the surface; others see the Bloodlings as prey.
- **Succession Instability**: Thessivara is 180 years old. By Lyzine standards, that's ancient. Her death—by assassination, by challenger, by simple time—will throw Mez into chaos. Every ambitious queen is positioning for the aftermath.

### History

The Lyzine claim their exile happened in an age before human memory—that they ruled great surface kingdoms when the current races were still learning to walk upright. Shyonan historians dismiss this as myth. Chimean legends, interestingly, speak of "spider-lords" who ruled parts of Ve in the distant past before being driven underground by a coalition of gods and heroes. Which version is true—or whether either is—remains unknown.

What's certain: the Lyzine have dwelt beneath the Widebarrow for at least 3,000 years. Archaeological evidence from ancient mines shows Lyzine webbing in strata predating human settlement by millennia. They were here first. They intend to be here last.

The First Breach (approximately 380 years ago) marked the beginning of sustained Lyzine-human conflict. Before that, surface dwellers and Lyzine existed in a state of mutual ignorance—the Lyzine in their depths, the humans on their mountains, neither particularly aware of the other. Deep mining changed that. Human prospectors tunneled into Lyzine territory; the Lyzine responded.

The centuries since have seen countless raids, several major offensives, and no resolution. The pattern is consistent: the Lyzine attack, the humans fortify, the attacks grow until a major offensive either succeeds or breaks against Dernish defenses. Then follows a period of relative quiet while the queens rebuild their strength, until the cycle begins again.

The last major offensive, the Silk War (40 years ago), saw three queens unite for a coordinated assault that briefly captured the Dernish mining town of Greyvein. They held it for two months before Dernish reinforcements drove them back underground. Greyvein was never rebuilt; its ruins are now considered cursed ground.

### Hooks

- **The Thessivara Succession**: The old queen is weakening. Every power in Mez—and some on the surface—has an interest in who replaces her. Players could be hired to assassinate her, protect her, or manipulate the succession for outside interests.
- **The Tunnel-Speakers**: The human intermediaries in Tsuicht know more about Lyzine society than any other surface dwellers. What would it take to meet one? What do they know? What do they want?
- **Vexara's Offensive**: Intelligence suggests Tnu Kiin is preparing something big. The Dernish want to know what—and if possible, to stop it before it starts. They're hiring specialists.
- **The Bloodling Alliance**: Rumors suggest a faction of Lyzine and a faction of Bloodlings are negotiating. If the subterranean powers unite, the surface world is in serious trouble. Someone needs to confirm or deny—and if true, to disrupt.
- **Escaped**: A group of slaves has made it to the surface, claiming knowledge of Lyzine defensive weaknesses that could change the war. The Tunnel Watch wants to debrief them. The Lyzine want them silenced. The players are in the middle.

## Mirko

Tags: citystate, state

A very vertical city. Built in the space between the tide and the hills. Deep canals lead into the depths of the city. Highest murder rates of anywhere.

Home to humans, chargon, nyolci, karchon, and elves. Very active mafia, and some of biggest gambling rings in the world. Multiple high-power thief and assassin guilds have their headquarters located here. The city is conveniently located, ignores demands of its neighbors, has no laws, and contacts everywhere.

Origins lie from when they offered protection to some pirates fleeing privateers. The pirate repaid them with a small fortune, granting the city the money to grow, and decided to make the place his permanent residence.

## Mkinti

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Oligarchy (formerly five houses, now one)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Ohblex (Gem Gnomes)

**Population**: 25,000

**Stability**: Very Unstable

**Core Values**: Secrecy, scheming, craftsmanship, family loyalty

- Until recently, Mkinti was controlled by five major gnome houses locked in endless political games—alliances, betrayals, marriages, assassinations. Then four of the five house heads died in quick succession. Now only House Wizzik remains.
- The Ohblex are crystalline gnomes born from the gems of the Gemstone Peaks. Their skin reflects precious stone colors, and they sustain themselves by consuming gems rather than food.
- They are the sole keepers of clockmaking secrets on Alaria, crafting incredible mechanical devices and jewelry that fetch astronomical prices abroad.
- The gnomes live in elaborate underground mazes filled with mechanical traps, their cities expanding ever deeper into the mountains. They value secrecy, mystery, and complication above all else.

### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Very Developed

**Primary Exports**: Clockwork devices, jewelry, gems, mechanical components

**Primary Imports**: Food (for non-Ohblex residents), metals, raw materials

- Mkinti's clockwork is unmatched. No one else on Alaria has cracked the secrets of precise mechanical timekeeping, and the Ohblex intend to keep it that way.
- Gem trade is tightly controlled. The Ohblex consume gems as sustenance, which gives them intimate knowledge of every stone's quality and magical properties—making them impossible to deceive in gem trades.
- The economy runs on schemes. Every transaction has hidden layers, every contract contains clauses within clauses. Outsiders who try to do business in Mkinti without gnomish advisors invariably get fleeced.
- The recent political upheaval has disrupted trade. House Wizzik controls everything now, but the transition has been chaotic.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (house guards)

- The Ohblex don't maintain a traditional army. Each house maintained its own guards, and now those guards serve whoever pays them—mostly House Wizzik.
- The real defense is the terrain. The Gemstone Peaks are treacherous, the underground cities are mazes, and every corridor is trapped. Invading Mkinti would be a nightmare.
- The gnomes prefer assassination and sabotage to open warfare. Why fight battles when you can remove problems quietly?

### Geography

**Location**: Southwestern Erpeus Island, in the Middle Sea

**Biomes**: Mountains, underground caverns, gem-veined tunnels

**Named Entities**: Gemstone Peaks, Bejeweled Palace

**Rivers & Lakes**: Underground rivers and pools

**Adjacent Waters**: The Middle Sea (west)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Wizzik (formerly shared among the five house seats)

**Cities**: Wizzik, Zunuki

**Towns**: Uzikni, Elfekin, Tiknurtir

**Villages**: Numerous small settlements in the mountains and underground

### Primary Conflicts

**The Succession Crisis**

Four house heads are dead. House Wizzik claims they had nothing to do with it—and perhaps they didn't. Someone orchestrated the assassinations, and no one knows who or why. The surviving Wizzik leadership is consolidating power rapidly, but the other houses' remnants are scheming in the shadows. Civil war seems inevitable unless Wizzik can establish legitimacy fast.

**The Bejeweled Palace**

The cursed palace of an ancient Mkinti king sits abandoned in the mountains, its treasury legendary and its dangers worse. Gnomes don't dare approach it—too many have tried and never returned. But with the political chaos, some desperate factions are considering whether the palace's treasures might be worth the risk.

**The Clockwork Monopoly**

Other nations would pay anything to learn Ohblex clockmaking secrets. Spies infiltrate constantly. The gnomes have responded with increasingly elaborate counter-intelligence operations, but the pressure never stops. If the secrets ever leaked, Mkinti's economic dominance would collapse.

**Relations with Neighbors**

Mkinti shares Erpeus with Lier and Azuros, neither of which the Ohblex particularly like. Lier's humans are too straightforward, Azuros's monks are too honest. The gnomes trade with both while scheming against both, as is their nature.

### History

The Ohblex emerged from the Gemstone Peaks in an age before human records, born from the gems themselves through processes the gnomes don't discuss with outsiders. They discovered their clockwork aptitude early and realized its value—and its danger if shared.

For centuries, the five great houses competed for dominance through intricate political games. Wars were rare; assassinations were common but carefully targeted. The system was stable in its instability, each house checking the others' ambitions.

Then someone broke the rules. Four house heads died within a single month—poison, accidents, mysterious disappearances. The survivors pointed fingers at each other, then at House Wizzik, then at unknown outside forces. No one proved anything.

House Wizzik moved fastest, seizing control of the shared institutions while other houses scrambled to choose new leadership. Now they rule Mkinti alone, but their grip is tenuous. The old game of five has become a game of one against many, and the many are regrouping in the shadows.

## Moigos

Tags: state, kappa, crime

A state ruled by criminals who don't bother pretending otherwise. Moigos occupies the swampy northern reaches of Petrona Septia Island, where the Kappa — massive, toad-like crime lords — have built an empire of debts, favors, and quiet violence. The official government exists on paper; real power flows through the counting houses and backroom deals where the Kappa conduct their business.

The arrangement works, after a fashion. Moigos is stable, prosperous by regional standards, and safe enough for those who understand the rules. The rules are simple: the Kappa get their cut, debts are paid, and no one asks questions about matters that don't concern them. Those who follow the rules live comfortable lives. Those who don't... don't.

### The Kappa

The toad-folk who dominate Moigos are not numerous — perhaps a few hundred in the entire state — but they don't need numbers. They have patience, intelligence, and an institutional memory that spans generations. A Kappa never forgets a debt. A Kappa never forgives a slight. And a Kappa never acts hastily when slow, methodical destruction will serve better.

The Kappa operate through networks of obligation. They lend money, provide services, solve problems — always at a price, always creating debts that can be called in later. Over generations, they've woven themselves into every aspect of Moigos commerce and politics. The merchant families who sit on Thetin's council owe their positions to Kappa backing. The river captains who move goods owe their boats to Kappa loans. Everyone owes something, and the Kappa keep meticulous records.

Violence is rare because it's rarely necessary. When a debtor fails to pay, the Kappa don't send thugs — they call in other debts, arrange for business to dry up, ensure that the defaulter's reputation is destroyed. By the time the process is complete, the target has lost everything without a single blow being struck. The occasional example is made, of course, pour encourager les autres. But the Kappa prefer financial ruin to physical violence. It's more profitable and less messy.

### Government

Moigos has a council of merchant families that meets in Thetin, deliberates on matters of policy, and issues decrees that everyone politely ignores. The council exists because states are supposed to have governments, and the Kappa find it useful to have a body that can sign treaties, receive ambassadors, and handle the tedious business of appearing legitimate.

The merchant families who serve on the council are not puppets, exactly. They have genuine interests, genuine wealth, and genuine influence within their spheres. But they hold their positions because the Kappa permit it, and they make decisions that the Kappa have already approved. The system works because everyone understands the arrangement and no one has any interest in disrupting it.

Real governance happens through the Kappa networks. Disputes are settled by whichever Kappa has the strongest claim on the parties involved. Infrastructure is maintained by whoever profits from it. Order is kept by the shared understanding that disorder is bad for business.

### Economy

Moigos produces timber from the Alegheim forest, agricultural products from the river valleys, and various goods processed in its towns. None of this is remarkable. What makes Moigos economically significant is its position as a hub for less legitimate commerce.

The Kappa networks extend far beyond Petrona Septia. Goods that can't be sold openly elsewhere find buyers in Moigos. Services that can't be procured legally can be arranged through the right contacts. Information flows through Kappa channels, and secrets are bought and sold like any other commodity.

The Isle of the Night adds another dimension. The Kappa don't control Blackpoint's supernatural darkness, but they control access to it. Treasure hunters seeking the island's rumored wealth must outfit their expeditions in Moigos, hire guides through Kappa-connected brokers, and often finance the whole venture with Kappa loans. Most expeditions fail. The Kappa profit either way.

### Culture

Life in Moigos is ordinary for those who stay within the rules. Farmers farm, merchants trade, craftspeople practice their crafts. The Kappa presence is felt but not seen — a background awareness that certain people are not to be crossed, certain questions are not to be asked, certain debts must always be paid.

The human and other non-Kappa inhabitants of Moigos have adapted to their situation. They're practical, cautious, and skilled at reading the subtle signs that indicate which way power is flowing. They don't resent the Kappa, exactly — the arrangement provides stability and opportunity for those who work within it. But they don't love them either. The relationship is transactional, and everyone knows it.

Religion is muted. The Kappa have no use for gods, and the faiths that flourish elsewhere find less purchase in a society built on earthly debts rather than divine ones. People observe the forms, mark the festivals, and keep their spiritual lives private.

### Relations

**With Ios**: Limited contact across the Screech Peaks. Ios has little that Moigos wants, and Moigos has little that Ios can afford. The mountain passes see some trade, handled through Calentia, but neither state has significant interest in the other.

**With Rilyn**: The Splinkreen city-state is too small and too strange to be worth the Kappa's attention. Rilyn's woodland people keep to their forest; the Kappa keep to their swamps. Occasional trade happens, but no significant relationship exists.

**With the Tuktuk**: Complicated. The turtle-folk of the Tyvern Isles are fierce, honorable, and not easily intimidated — qualities that make them poor targets for Kappa manipulation. But they're also practical, and some commerce flows between Moigos and Vitruk village. The Kappa treat the Tuktuk with more respect than they show most outsiders, recognizing that these are not people who can be safely exploited.

**With everyone else**: The Kappa networks extend wherever trade reaches. Moigos has contacts, agents, and debtors across the Seacleft Coast and beyond. The state itself is unimportant; the networks are everything.

### Notable Locations

- **Thetin**: Capital on the Qulyintes River; center of Kappa power
- **Calentia**: Upstream town handling mountain trade with Ios; gateway to the Urun
- **Ragusa**: Skraigos River town processing timber and agriculture
- **Hepetas**: Upper Skraigos town at the edge of navigable water
- **Alegheim**: Swampy forest covering northern Moigos; Kappa territory
- **Urun Mountains**: Eastern range separating Moigos from Fernius Sound; mining camps, hidden caches, and older things above the treeline
- **Isle of the Night**: Supernatural darkness; Blackpoint treasure island

### Player Hooks

- **Debt Collection**: The players owe a Kappa — or someone they care about does. The debt isn't money; it's a service, a secret, or something worse. The Kappa is patient. The Kappa can wait. But eventually, the debt comes due.

- **Blackpoint Expedition**: Someone wants to reach the lightless island and is willing to pay well. The Kappa will arrange everything — guides, equipment, financing. All they ask is a percentage of whatever's found. And if the expedition fails, well, the debt passes to the survivors. Or their families.

- **Network Disruption**: Someone powerful wants the Kappa networks damaged. Not destroyed — that's impossible — but disrupted enough to create opportunities. The players are deniable assets, and the target is a Kappa operation that's grown too ambitious.

- **The Honest Merchant**: A trader in Calentia has managed to operate outside Kappa influence for years. Now they want to expand to Thetin, and they need protection. The Kappa have noticed. Everyone's about to learn whether independence is possible in Moigos.

- **Don't Dig Too Deep**: A debtor escaped the Urun mining camps and made it to the players with a wild story — something in the upper peaks, something the Kappa are afraid of. The toad-folk want the escapee back, badly. They're offering to clear substantial debts, no questions asked. What did the debtor see that's worth that much silence?

## Morgnor's Cradle

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Dragon tyranny

**Primary Inhabitants**: Etherweaver Goblins

**Population**: 8,000

**Stability**: Very Stable (under the dragon's claw)

**Core Values**: Obedience, mining, enchantment, survival

- Morgnor's Cradle is ruled absolutely by **Morgnor**, an ancient earth-attuned dragon. His word is law. Disobedience is death.
- The Etherweaver goblins serve the dragon—mining metals from the earth and enchanting them to his specifications. They are skilled with non-basic elements (time, void, force, light, dark) and use this magic in their crafting.
- Life under the dragon is harsh but predictable. Serve well and live. Fail and die. The goblins have adapted.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed (magical metallurgy)

**Primary Exports**: None (Morgnor hoards everything)

**Primary Imports**: None (self-sufficient through dragon mandate)

- The goblins mine gold, silver, and other precious metals from the Hills of Red Gold and the surrounding mountains.
- They enchant these metals using their Etherweaver magic, creating items of considerable power.
- Nothing leaves the Cradle. Morgnor keeps it all. His hoard is legendary—and growing.

### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Mandatory (everyone serves the dragon)

- The goblins maintain a guard force, but Morgnor himself is the real military. An earth-attuned dragon in his own mountains is nearly unstoppable.
- Thespia paid tribute for generations rather than face him. Queen Akta's refusal to continue is either brave or suicidal.

### Geography

**Location**: Mountains northwest of Thespia, west of Glorin, containing the Hills of Red Gold

**Biomes**: Mountain peaks, mining valleys, underground complexes

**Climate**: Alpine—harsh winters, brief summers

**Named Features**: Morgnor's Cradle (central mountains), Hills of Red Gold (southern slopes), Mt. Kazlos, Mt. Thryke

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Morgnor (the dragon's lair, also the name of the central settlement)

**Cities**: None (all settlements serve the lair)

**Towns**: Lagunas, Shegaat, Dir, Addis, Norwun, Scuilli, Telleni, Goldir

### The Hills of Red Gold

The southern slopes of Morgnor's domain, rich with gold and other precious metals. This is where most mining occurs. The earth-attuned dragon can sense every vein, every nugget, every grain of gold in his territory. Nothing escapes his notice.

### The Etherweaver Goblins

Unlike their Scalawag cousins, Etherweavers are reserved, cautious, and born with attunements to non-basic elements. Morgnor conquered their ancestors generations ago and put their magical talents to work. They enchant the metals he hoards, creating items of power that he adds to his collection.

The goblins don't love their master, but they've made peace with their situation. Some even take pride in their craftsmanship, even if Morgnor claims all credit.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Thespian Situation**

Queen Akta has stopped paying tribute. Morgnor hasn't responded yet—perhaps he's considering his options, perhaps he's amused, perhaps he's planning something elaborate. When he does respond, it will be devastating.

**Glorin's Gold**

Morgnor knows about Glorin's hidden wealth. He knows the Glorindian dwarves have been quietly mining gold for centuries. He wants it. The only question is when and how he'll take it.

**The Hoard**

Morgnor's greed is bottomless. No amount of gold, enchanted metal, or magical items is ever enough. The goblins mine constantly, enchant constantly, and it's never enough. The dragon always wants more.

### History

Morgnor claimed these mountains long ago, driving out or subjugating whoever lived there before. When he discovered the Etherweaver goblins' talent for magical enchantment, he conquered them rather than destroying them. A useful slave is better than a dead enemy.

For generations, the goblins have mined and enchanted under the dragon's rule. Thespia paid tribute to avoid his wrath. Glorin hid and hoped he wouldn't notice them. The arrangement was stable.

Now Akta has disrupted the tribute. Glorin's wealth has caught his attention. Morgnor is greedy, patient, and very, very powerful. Something is going to change.

## Movasi

Tags: state, theocracy, pilgrimage, velwey, greenwater-isles

Links: States, Greenwater Isles

The island-state of Movasi is a theocratic society governed by the Velwey—dwarves who've built their entire civilization around the Dream Jungle's psychedelic properties and the Pool of Names at their capital. While technically a nation, Movasi functions more like a sacred institution: its economy, laws, and culture exist to facilitate the ritual that reveals pilgrims' true names.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Theocratic Council

**Primary Inhabitants**: Velwey (dwarves), with significant human, elf, and halfling minorities

**Population**: ~19,000 permanent residents, plus 500-2,000 transient pilgrims at any time

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**:
- Sacred hospitality toward those who seek truth
- Absolute confidentiality regarding what is learned in ritual
- The Pool's mystery is to be served, not solved

The **Council of Guides** governs Movasi—a body of senior priest-guides who've conducted thousands of rituals and demonstrated both spiritual authority and administrative competence. There is no single ruler; decisions require consensus among the seven council members. Succession happens through recognition rather than election: when a seat opens, the remaining council members identify who among the priests has earned it through decades of service.

The Velwey make up roughly two-thirds of the permanent population and hold nearly all religious and governmental authority. Non-Velwey residents serve in supporting roles: dock workers, merchants, harvesters, laborers. They're treated fairly—the Velwey need them—but full citizenship and participation in governance requires being Velwey.

**Religious Structure**: The priest-guides form a hierarchical order with ranks based on experience and demonstrated ability. Junior priests assist with preparation rituals; only those who've undergone decades of training conduct Pool rituals. The highest-ranked guides are said to have glimpsed truths beyond mere names during their own journeys—though what those truths might be, they don't discuss.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

**Primary Exports**:
- Pilgrimage services (revelation of true names)
- Refined psychoactive preparations (heavily regulated)
- Clarity tonics and preparation compounds
- Crystallized dream-matter (rare, expensive)

**Primary Imports**:
- Food (the jungle isn't particularly edible)
- Construction materials
- Luxury goods for wealthy pilgrims
- Skilled labor

Movasi's economy runs on pilgrimage. The ritual to learn one's true name is expensive—the preparation alone costs more than most commoners earn in a year, and the full process can take weeks of supported stay. The Velwey don't apologize for this. The service they offer is literally invaluable: eternal survival versus permanent annihilation.

**The Pilgrim Trade**: Wealthy nobles, paranoid mages, dying merchants, ambitious heirs—anyone with enough resources and enough need makes the journey. The pilgrims bring gold, and that gold pays for everything Movasi can't produce itself.

**Regulated Exports**: The psychoactive compounds from the Dream Jungle are valuable beyond the island, but the Velwey control their distribution carefully. Sacramental preparations are never sold—they're only used in authorized rituals. Lesser preparations and clarity tonics can be purchased, but export quantities are limited. The Velwey have no interest in competing with recreational drug markets; they're protecting a sacred industry.

**The Harvest**: Banguna village processes the raw materials, and this work employs a significant portion of the non-Velwey population. The labor is skilled but dangerous, and workers are compensated well enough that volunteers continue arriving despite the known risks.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

The Velwey maintain no standing army. They don't need one.

**The Jungle Defense**: Any force attempting to invade Movasi would have to march through the Dream Jungle. Without Velwey guidance and preparation, the psychedelic effects would shatter unit cohesion within days. Soldiers would turn on each other, wander off into the deep jungle, or simply sit down and stop responding. The jungle itself is the island's primary defense.

**The Pilgrim Shield**: Movasi's clientele includes the wealthy and powerful from every nation in the known world. An attack on the island would be an attack on the interests of nobles, merchants, and rulers who've invested heavily in their own true names—or plan to. No one wants to be the kingdom that interrupted the sacred pilgrimage of a dozen other nations' elite.

**Temple Guards**: The Velwey do maintain a small force of trained guards to handle disputes, protect the Pool, and occasionally deal with pilgrims who become dangerous during bad trips. These guards are equipped with clarity tonics that let them function at full capacity in the jungle's influence—a significant advantage over anyone else on the island.

### Geography

**Location**: Southeastern Greenwater Isles, between the Gulf of Norag and the Sea of Spiders

**Biomes**: Tropical psychedelic forest (Dream Jungle), coastal mangroves, swampy meadows

**Named Entities**: The Dream Jungle, The Pool of Names, Telugai Bay

**Rivers & Lakes**: The Pool of Names (depth unknown), various small streams

**Adjacent Waters**: Gulf of Norag (northeast), Sea of Spiders (southeast), Telugai Bay (south)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Moura
**Cities**: None (Moura is the only major settlement)
**Towns**: Holik
**Villages**: Banguna, Kung, Dreftwaf, Trusiin

The island's political geography follows the pilgrim circuit rather than conventional settlement patterns. Holik serves as the entry point, Dreftwaf as preparation grounds, Moura as the sacred center, and Trusiin as the integration site. Banguna exists to support the harvest; Kung exists in deliberate separation from the system.

### Relations

**Trade Partners**: Movasi trades with anyone who comes to trade. Ships from across the Greenwater Isles, from Upoceax, from Rimihuica—all dock at Holik. The Velwey maintain strict neutrality in external conflicts; they serve anyone who can pay.

**Protected Status**: No nation openly threatens Movasi. Too many powerful people have too much invested in maintaining access. This isn't formal diplomacy—it's the natural result of building a service that the elite of every nation desperately want.

**Roule**: The nearest neighbor, the halfling island to the southwest. Roule handles much of the shipping that connects Movasi to the wider world. Relations are cordial and commercially valuable to both sides.

**The Wider Greenwater Isles**: Movasi participates minimally in regional politics. They attend councils when invited, maintain diplomatic courtesies, and otherwise stay out of the archipelago's conflicts.

### Primary Conflicts

**The Solis Question**: The ruins on the northeast peninsula predate the Velwey. Something there understands true names differently—or was the original source of the Pool's power. The Council of Guides doesn't discuss Solis, but younger priests increasingly ask questions. What did the Velwey really find when they came to Movasi? What did they take? What did they disturb?

**The Kung Heresy**: The skeptics in Kung practice older methods of seeking true names—longer, harder, without Velwey intermediaries. They claim the Pool ritual has been corrupted, optimized for throughput rather than truth. The Council dismisses them as reckless mystics, but their existence is an ongoing challenge to Velwey authority. If names can be found without the Pool, what does that mean for the system built around it?

**The Confidentiality Paradox**: The ritual is designed so priests never learn pilgrims' true names. But some pilgrims suspect this is a comforting lie. The paranoid ones speak their revealed name to no one—defeating the purpose of learning it. Others wonder: if the Pool itself "knows" every name ever revealed in it, what would happen if someone found a way to ask it?

**The One-in-Fifty**: The Pool claims approximately one in fifty pilgrims. Their bodies are never recovered. The Velwey call these deaths "acceptable risk" and point out that pilgrims are warned. But families sometimes want answers. Where do the lost ones go? Are they dead, or somewhere else?

### History

The Velwey arrived on Movasi roughly 1,200 years ago, though their own records are vague about where they came from or why they left. Their founding stories speak of being "called" to the island, of discovering the Pool and recognizing its sacred purpose.

What the founding stories don't explain is Solis—the ruins on the northeast peninsula that predate the Velwey by unknown centuries. Someone or something was here before. The Velwey either don't know what happened to the original inhabitants, or don't discuss it.

The modern ritual developed over centuries of experimentation. Early attempts were more dangerous—the one-in-fifty rate was once one-in-ten. The Velwey refined their preparations, their guidance techniques, their understanding of how to navigate the Pool's depths safely. Or as safely as such a thing can be.

Movasi has never been successfully invaded. The three attempts—by Upoceaxan treasure hunters in 412 SD, by Rimihuican expansionists in 891 SD, and by unknown parties in 1,744 SD—all ended the same way: the invading forces entered the jungle and never emerged.

### The Pilgrim's Journey

A complete pilgrimage to Movasi follows a traditional structure:

1. **Arrival at Holik** (3+ days): Acclimation to the island's ambient effects. Registration with brokers, selection of a priest-guide, preliminary medical and psychological evaluation.

2. **Journey to Dreftwaf** (1-2 days): Guided travel through the jungle to the preparation grounds.

3. **Preparation at Dreftwaf** (5-10 days): Fasting, lesser journeys, confession of known names, mental and spiritual preparation for the Pool.

4. **Travel to Moura** (1 day): The final approach to the capital.

5. **The Ritual** (1 night): Consumption of the sacrament, submersion in the Pool, the revelation (or not).

6. **Recovery and Speaking** (1-3 days): Physical recovery, first speaking of the name in the Speaking Houses.

7. **Integration at Trusiin** (3-30 days): Processing the implications, deciding who to tell, possibly commissioning protective bindings or oaths.

8. **Return to Holik and departure**: Exit through the jungle, departure by ship.

The entire process takes a minimum of two weeks; many pilgrims stay for a month or more. Rushing is dangerous and discouraged.

### What Pilgrims Should Know

**The Cost**: Full pilgrimage services start at approximately 500 gold crowns—equivalent to several years' wages for skilled laborers. Luxury accommodations, extended stays, and additional services increase the price significantly. The Velwey offer no discounts, but they do operate on a strict pay-what-you-can basis for those who can demonstrate genuine need and pure intention. These subsidized pilgrimages are rare.

**The Risk**: One in fifty pilgrims doesn't return from the Pool. This is not hidden. Every pilgrim signs acknowledgment of this risk. The Velwey maintain that the Pool takes only those who weren't ready—but they can't explain how to be ready, or what "ready" means.

**The Choice**: Learning your true name creates a paradox. Keep it secret, and you're safe from Deoric control but condemned to eventual oblivion. Share it, and you might survive death forever—but anyone who knows it can puppet you while you live. The Velwey help pilgrims think through this, but the choice is always the pilgrim's own.

**The Change**: Many pilgrims report that the ritual changed them in ways beyond the name itself. Some describe enhanced perception, persistent visions, or a lingering sense of connection to the jungle. Others experience personality shifts, altered priorities, or the feeling that they left something behind in the Pool and brought something else back. The Velwey don't promise that you'll return unchanged. They promise that you'll return knowing who you really are.

## Mueras

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Oligarchy (Council of Merchant-Kings)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Drachma, Yngli

**Population**: 100,000

**Stability**: Stable (through mutual deterrence)

**Core Values**: Style, instincts, cunning

Mueras is governed by merchant-kings who hold powerful monopolies over different aspects of trade—not goods themselves, but the means of moving them. The true currency of power in Mueras is navigation: control of the pilot guilds that guide ships through Labyrinth Sound, the treacherous waterway that defines the coast. Without licensed pilots, ships wreck. The merchant-kings who control the pilots control the trade.

Seven major houses hold seats on the Council, which meets in chambers carved into the highest cliff of Bazz. Assassination between houses is common but rarely successful—the merchant-kings are wealthy enough to afford extensive protections. More effective is killing a rival's pilots, sabotaging their navigation charts, or bribing their harbor masters. The competition is constant, vicious, and conducted with impeccable style.

The Drachma dominate the upper echelons of Mueran society—their cultural emphasis on panache, instinct, and personal style aligns perfectly with merchant-king values. The Yngli occupy a more complicated position: their magical silence makes them ideal for keeping secrets, and many serve as pilots, dock workers, and navigators. Some houses treat their Yngli well; others exploit them ruthlessly. The Yngli communities of the Taflex Forest maintain independence from all houses, trading forest products but answering to no merchant-king.

#### The Seven Houses

**House Hammren** (Hammer Coast): Controls the northern sea approach via the twin fortress-ports. The oldest house, predating even Kasper's rise. Conservative, militaristic, closest thing to old nobility.

**House Vethros** (Kelwin): Controls the Kelwin Throat, the most technically demanding passage. Their pilots are the finest in Mueras. Also controls most of the Taflex Forest logging operations.

**House Marsova** (Hennipin): Controls the southern passage into Corpse Bay. Eternal rivals to Vethros. Extensive warehousing operations that undercut Bazz's storage monopoly.

**House Korreth** (Bazz): Controls the Council Chambers and the central markets of Bazz. No navigation monopoly, but their position at the junction of Sound and Bay makes them the natural meeting point for all trade.

**House Velani** (Bazz): Specialists in insurance, banking, and contract enforcement. No ships of their own, but every ship that trades in Mueras eventually needs Velani services.

**House Dreth** (Port Perith): Controls trade with the haunted zone—Kaspion salvage, Cliffdrop pilgrimage fees, and the darker commerce that flows from death-touched regions.

**House Yoros** (Grimreach): The newest house, elevated to the Council only two generations ago. Controls the shadow-touched trade from the Darkened Coast. Other houses consider them barely civilized.

### Economy

**Size**: Very large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Fish, sea products, pilot services, shadow-touched materials, timber

**Primary Imports**: Everything else

The Mueran economy reaches across the world despite—or because of—the region's treacherous waters. Ships must pass through Mueran-controlled channels to access the Agreben Sea and the southern trade routes. The pilot fees alone generate enormous wealth, and the warehousing, insurance, and support services multiply it further.

Mueras lacks astral airways, cutting it off from the fastest magical trade routes. The merchant-kings have turned this into advantage: goods that can't be trusted to astral transport (fragile, perishable, politically sensitive, or simply too large) flow through Mueran ports instead.

The shadow-touched trade from Grimreach and the Darkened Coast adds a darker revenue stream. Void-eel oil, shadow-ray leather, ghost-touched artifacts from Kaspion—these command premium prices in markets that don't ask questions about provenance.

**The Pilot Guilds**: The true engine of Mueran wealth. Each major passage through Labyrinth Sound requires pilots who know the specific currents, rocks, and hazards. These pilots are bound to specific houses through guild contracts, oaths, and sometimes more coercive measures. A pilot who betrays guild secrets faces consequences that don't end with death.

### Military

**Size**: Medium (state forces) / Large (combined house fleets)

**Quality**: Medium (state) / Variable (houses)

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (state) / Contractual (houses)

The official Mueran military is deliberately modest—the Council fears any force strong enough to threaten merchant-king autonomy. State forces maintain coastal defense, suppress piracy, and occasionally intervene when house conflicts threaten trade.

The real military power lies in the individual house fleets. Each merchant-king maintains private naval forces, marines, and shore garrisons. House Hammren's forces are the most conventionally military; House Yoros fields specialized monster-hunters; House Dreth employs practitioners comfortable with death magic. Combined, the house fleets could challenge any navy in the region—but they will never combine.

**The Compact of Bazz**: An ancient agreement prevents house forces from operating within Bazz itself or attacking the Council Chambers. Violations are punished by the combined action of all other houses. This creates the one neutral zone in Mueras where merchant-kings can negotiate without fear of assassination.

### Geography

**Location**: Western coast of Rimihuica, Westrim region

**Biomes**: Coastal cliffs, arid plateau, temperate forest, shadow-touched coast

**Named Entities**: Labyrinth Sound, Corpse Bay, Walker Depths, Dustwind Plateau, Mueras Highlands, Taflex Forest, Grimreach, Darkened Coast, The Tarns, Beak of Mueras, Sal'emri Bay, Watchkeep Isles, Brunas Inlet

**Rivers & Lakes**: The Tarns (highland lakes), seasonal streams from Dustwind Plateau

**Adjacent Waters**: Agreben Sea (south/southwest), Walker Depths (west)

The geography created the politics. Mueras occupies roughly 400 miles of coastline, but the actual navigable waterway through Labyrinth Sound stretches over 200 miles of treacherous channels. The interior Dustwind Plateau isolates the coast from the eastern reaches. The Darkened Coast in the southeast adds supernatural hazard to natural difficulty.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Bazz (de facto; no formal capital designated)

**Cities**: Bazz (40,000), Kelwin (12,000), Hennipin (10,000)

**Towns**: Kaspion (abandoned/haunted), Port Perith (5,000), Port Echo (4,000), Al Khamilah (5,000), Cursius (3,000), Fulon (3,000), Eddiwood (2,500), Yorx (2,000), Indus (2,000)

**Villages**: Hickton, Saron, Sha'ra, Blof Point, Freed, Giorga, Sherly, Lieler, Al Muqlib, Am Batr, N. Hammer Point, S. Hammer Point (fortress-garrisons)

**Special Zones**:
- **The Haunted Zone**: Kaspion and surroundings, officially forbidden, unofficially exploited
- **Sal'emri Bay**: Tarkhon trading enclaves, nominally under House Hammren protection but effectively autonomous
- **Deep Taflex**: Yngli territory, no house authority recognized

### Primary Conflicts

**House Vethros vs. House Marsova**: The eternal rivalry. Control of the central Sound passages means control of the most profitable trade routes. Both houses constantly probe for advantage through price wars, pilot sabotage, and political maneuvering in the Council.

**The Haunted Zone**: House Dreth profits from Kaspion's curse; other houses want the ghost-city cleansed or at least contained. The curse may be spreading—the Darkened Coast has deepened since Kasper's death, and some blame the unresolved spiritual damage.

**Yngli Rights**: The Yngli cannot speak to advocate for themselves. Some houses treat them fairly; others exploit their silence. The free Yngli of the Taflex Forest occasionally act against the worst offenders, but they lack the organization for sustained resistance.

**Tarkhon Relations**: The Sal'emri Bay enclaves are useful but also potential Tarkhon footholds. House Hammren profits from protecting them; other houses wonder if the Tarkhon presence will eventually threaten Mueran independence.

**The Succession Problem**: Merchant-king families rise and fall. House Yoros joined the Council only recently; older houses resent their presence. If a house falls, who claims their seat? The Compact of Bazz doesn't address this clearly, and the ambiguity creates tension.

### History

**The Age of Petty Lords**: Before Kasper, Mueras was fragmented among dozens of minor lords, each controlling a single cove or passage. Trade was nearly impossible; piracy was endemic.

**Kasper's Consolidation** (approximately 150 years ago): A merchant named Kasper of Kaspion began unifying the coast through conquest, assassination, and forced marriage. Over three decades, he eliminated or absorbed nearly all rivals, establishing himself as the sole ruler of Mueras.

**The Tyranny** (32 years): Kasper ruled from his palace at Kaspion, demanding absolute loyalty. Those who displeased him were thrown from Cliffdrop. He centralized all pilot guilds under royal control and taxed trade heavily to fund his court and military.

**The Rebellion** (approximately 118 years ago): A coalition of surviving merchant families, funded partly by Tarkhon interests, raised forces against Kasper. The rebellion succeeded in breaching Kaspion's defenses—but Kasper had prepared.

**The Death-Curse**: Rather than surrender, Kasper triggered a curse that killed every living person in Kaspion instantly—approximately 12,000 souls. The curse bound the dead to the city, creating the haunted zone that persists today.

**The Compact of Bazz** (shortly after the curse): The surviving rebel leaders, horrified by what their victory had unleashed, negotiated the Compact—an agreement to share power through a Council, to maintain Bazz as neutral ground, and to prevent any single house from accumulating Kasper-like authority.

**The Modern Era**: 118 years of Council rule. Houses have risen and fallen; House Yoros joined the Council 40 years ago after their shadow-trade operations became too profitable to ignore. The curse has not faded. The Darkened Coast has deepened. And some say the merchant-kings secretly prefer it this way—the danger keeps outsiders from competing.

### Culture & Customs

**Style as Virtue**: Drachma influence permeates Mueran culture. How you do something matters as much as what you do. A merchant-king who wins through clumsy methods loses face; one who loses gracefully may gain respect.

**The Salt Greeting**: Visitors to a Mueran home are offered salt and water—symbols of the sea that sustains the nation. Refusing is a grave insult; accepting creates a limited guest-right that prohibits violence until the visitor leaves.

**Pilot Oaths**: Pilots swear complex oaths binding them to their house and guild. Breaking these oaths is believed to curse the oathbreaker's navigation sense—a superstition that may have magical truth.

**The Cremation Custom**: Bodies are cremated in Mueras, never buried. The reason is practical: buried bodies in cursed ground may rise. Crematoriums burn constantly in major ports.

**Silence as Trust**: Due to Yngli influence, Muerans are comfortable with silence in negotiation. A Mueran who fills every pause with words is considered untrustworthy—someone hiding something.

### Player Hooks

**Pilot Guild Intrigue**: A house wants to poach pilots from a rival. The party must recruit, bribe, or extract pilots while avoiding retaliation.

**Kaspion Salvage**: Something valuable lies in the ghost-city. House Dreth will pay well for retrieval—but the dead don't appreciate visitors.

**Yngli Liberation**: Free Yngli from the Taflex want to rescue kin from an abusive house. The party must infiltrate, extract, and escape without starting a house war.

**The Deepening Shadow**: The Darkened Coast is expanding. Someone needs to investigate why—and whether Kasper's curse is to blame.

**Tarkhon Tensions**: A Sal'emri merchant has been murdered. House Hammren wants it solved quietly before Tarkhon takes notice. The killer may be from another house—or from Tarkhon itself.

**Succession Crisis**: A merchant-king has died without clear heir. Multiple claimants are preparing for conflict. The party can tip the balance—for the right price.

## Murth

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Dual governance (shared authority between two capitals)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Rhea (Eynmore), Humans (Vastilin)

**Population**: ~25,000 (Eynmore ~8,000; Vastilin ~12,000; scattered communities ~5,000)

**Stability**: Stable but tense

**Core Values**: Knowledge and commerce, focus and profit, the binding of opposites

- Murth is governed by two capitals on two mountains: **Eynmore** on Mt. Disyus (Rhea scholars) and **Vastilin** on Mt. Arn (human merchants). Neither rules the other. Major decisions require agreement from both.
