## Qintas

Tags: state

A coastal state in Central Aboyinzu, home to a significant Amverela high elf population and famous across Alaria for Sondoros wine—a vintage so legendary that it has shaped the state's economy, politics, and international relations for millennia.

### Government

Qintas is governed by councils that embody Amverela philosophy: deliberate, patient, and frustratingly slow to outsiders. The High Council in Constancias meets quarterly to address major policy; lesser councils handle administrative matters; emergency procedures exist for crises but have been invoked perhaps twice in living memory.

Representation is weighted toward longevity. Amverela who have served for centuries hold more influence than human delegates who die after a few decades. This isn't formal policy—it simply reflects how council dynamics evolve over time. Humans participate but rarely dominate.

The government's approach to problems is to have anticipated them generations ago. When this works, Qintas appears preternaturally prepared. When it fails—when something genuinely new arises—the response can be catastrophically slow.

### Population

**Amverela High Elves**: The cultural and political elite, though not numerically dominant. They hold most council positions, own most significant property, and set the tone for Qintan society. They are patient, wise, and sometimes paralyzed by their own caution.

**Humans**: The majority population, concentrated in cities and agricultural regions. They do most of the manual labor, including the physical work of the wine harvest. Relations with the Amverela are cordial if condescending—the elves view humans as useful but short-lived; humans view elves as impressive but insufferable.

**Others**: Qintas welcomes traders and visitors from across Alaria, particularly in Mataojo. A cosmopolitan port population has developed there, distinct from the more homogeneous interior.

### Economy

**Sondoros Wine**: The foundation of everything. The legendary wine produced from the Sondoros forest's unique vines cannot be replicated elsewhere—many have tried, all have failed. This monopoly has made Qintas wealthy and given the Amverela leverage far exceeding their military power.

Wine exports fund imports of everything else. Qintas produces food, wool, and crafts for domestic consumption, but its international significance rests entirely on Sondoros.

**Trade**: Mataojo serves as the state's commercial gateway, welcoming ships from across Alaria eager to purchase wine and whatever else the high elves will sell. The Amverela prefer to let others handle the actual sailing.

### Military

Qintas maintains professional guards rather than a true army—enough to patrol roads, protect wine shipments, and maintain order, not enough to fight a war. The Amverela philosophy holds that wars are won through diplomacy, economics, and patience, not through soldiers.

This philosophy has worked so far. Whether it will survive contact with Chaal Nazzerox's undead hordes remains to be seen. The Amverela are preparing—they've been preparing for centuries—but their preparations are characteristically opaque.

### Culture

Amverela culture values patience, beauty, and long-term thinking. Buildings are designed to last millennia. Decisions are made slowly and implemented gradually. Art is created with the understanding that the artist may not live to see it completed, but the work will outlast everyone.

Humans in Qintas have adapted to this pace or learned to work around it. The port city of Mataojo operates on human time; the rest of the state operates on elven time. The contrast can be jarring.

### Relations

**Grustos**: Friendly neighbor to the west. The Amverela consider Grustani slightly uncouth but appreciate their role as buffer against the undead. Trade flows freely; borders exist mainly on maps.

**Chaal Nazzerox**: The existential threat. If Grustos falls, Qintas is next. The Amverela have plans—centuries of plans—but no one outside their councils knows what those plans entail.

**Broader Alaria**: Qintas maintains cordial relations with anyone willing to buy wine, which is everyone. The state's soft power far exceeds its size.

### What Will Go Wrong

- **The wine could fail**: If something happens to the Sondoros vines—blight, magical corruption, climate change—Qintas loses its only real asset.
- **Ojos Goros**: The watching forest in the south. The Amverela avoid it and won't explain why. Whatever's in there has been watching for a long time.
- **Too slow to respond**: Amverela patience is a strength until it's a fatal weakness. Some threats don't wait for quarterly council meetings.
- **The undead are coming**: Chaal Nazzerox will eventually break out. The Amverela's long-term plans had better be good.

## Rakite

Tags: state, elven, tribal

The great plains of the eastern Farlands, where the grass grows taller than a man and the Rakiten elves hunt buffalo beneath an endless sky. This is not a kingdom in any conventional sense—there's no capital, no standing army, no tax collectors. Just a loose confederation of tribes bound by shared blood, shared custom, and an absolute refusal to bow to anyone.

The Rakiten are the tallest elves in the known world, bred over generations to see over the six-foot grasses that dominate their homeland. They dress in auburn and rust, colors that blend with the autumn plains. They hunt the great herds that migrate between Tyror Miig and Tyror Weklis with a patience and skill that makes other hunters look like amateurs.

And now strangers are burning their grasslands to plant farms, and the Rakiten are deciding whether to disappear or fight back.

### Overview

Rakite sprawls across approximately 50,000 square miles of grassland, bounded by the Vokas Enrisikna mountains to the west, the Vokas Pendi range to the south, and the sea to the east. The land is mostly flat—gentle swells hiding the horizon, river valleys cutting through the plains, occasional copses of trees where the land dips enough to shelter them from the constant wind.

The grass is the defining feature. Tyror grass grows six feet tall in summer, higher in wet years, and moves in waves like a golden ocean. From ground level, visibility extends maybe twenty feet. From atop a horse—or from the height of a Rakiten elf—you can see for miles, tracking herds by the wake they leave in the grass.

There are no cities. The Rakiten live in movable camps, following the buffalo and the seasons. The only permanent structures are sacred sites, the Palace of Flickering Lights, and—increasingly—the Roule colonial villages sprouting on the eastern peninsula.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Tribal confederation

**Primary Inhabitants**: Rakiten elves

**Population**: ~10,000

**Stability**: Very Stable (internally), Threatened (externally)

**Core Values**:

- Generosity as dominance—the greatest gift-giver commands the most respect
- Confrontation is shameful; disputes are resolved through competitive giving
- The land belongs to no one; the herds belong to everyone
- Outsiders are tolerated but never trusted

The Rakiten have no unified government. Each tribe—there are perhaps thirty, ranging from a few dozen to several hundred members—governs itself according to its own customs. Some follow hereditary chiefs, others elect leaders, others defer to their elders or their shamans.

Once a year, at the autumn equinox, tribal leaders gather at the **Kro Shiik Convocation**—a meeting on the shores of the central lake where matters affecting all Rakite are discussed. Decisions are made by consensus or not at all. In practice, this means most decisions aren't made, and each tribe handles its own affairs.

The gift-giving tradition is central to Rakiten culture. Disputes between individuals, families, or tribes are settled not by violence or arbitration, but by gift competition. Each party gives the other gifts of increasing value until one side presents something so generous, so self-sacrificing, that the other is obligated to accept it—and with it, the giver's position in the dispute.

This sounds peaceful. It is, mostly. But it can also be brutal: the pressure to give more than you can afford, the shame of accepting a gift you can't reciprocate, the slow impoverishment of a family that keeps losing gift-battles. The Rakiten avoid open conflict, but they're capable of destroying each other with generosity.

### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Undeveloped

**Primary Exports**:
- Buffalo hides and horn (highly valued for quality)
- Tyror grass rope (unusually strong and water-resistant)
- Bone carvings (ceremonial and decorative)

**Primary Imports**:
- Metal tools and weapons (they have no smithing tradition)
- Salt (essential for preserving meat)
- Occasionally, luxuries for gift-giving competitions

The Rakiten economy is fundamentally different from settled peoples. There's no currency; wealth is measured in horses, buffalo, and the quality of your crafts. Trade with outsiders happens at the margins—literally at the edges of Rakiten territory—and is handled by specific families who've specialized in dealing with foreigners.

Most Rakiten go their entire lives without using money. Everything they need, they make, hunt, or receive as gifts.

### Military

**Size**: Small (but can mobilize quickly)

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Universal (all adults are trained hunters and warriors)

The Rakiten have no standing army because they don't need one. Every adult is a hunter, and every hunter is a warrior. They fight from horseback with bows, javelins, and long knives. They don't form ranks or follow orders—each warrior fights as an individual, using the grass for concealment and the plains for maneuver.

In open battle, they would lose to any organized army. They never fight open battles. Rakiten warfare is about ambush, harassment, and attrition. They appear from the grass, strike, and vanish. They poison water sources. They stampede herds through enemy camps. They burn the grass behind advancing forces and wait for starvation to do their work.

No army has ever conquered Rakite. Several have tried. The grass swallowed them all.

### Geography

**Location**: Eastern Farlands, between the Vokas Enrisikna (west) and Vokas Pendi (south) mountain ranges and the sea

**Biomes**: Tallgrass prairie, river valleys, scattered woodland

**Named Entities**:
- **Tyror Miig**: Northern plains, primary summer grazing
- **Tyror Weklis**: Southern plains, winter territory
- **Vokas Enrisikna**: Western mountain barrier
- **Vokas Pendi**: Southern mountain barrier
- **Ektolmni Grif**: Hills between the plains and western mountains
- **Extolmni Furis**: Forested hills in the central region
- **Drakwald Forest**: Dense woodland in the southwest
- **Palace of Flickering Lights**: Ancient Sarakiel structure in the north

**Rivers & Lakes**:
- **Kro Shiik**: Central lake, site of annual convocation
- **Ver Kanis**: Northern river, flows from Vokas Enrisikna
- **Ver Pes**: Central river
- **Ver Neles**: Eastern river, flows to the sea

**Adjacent Waters**: Bejeweled Sea (east coast)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None (the Kro Shiik gathering site serves as a temporary capital once yearly)

**Cities**: None

**Towns**: None (permanent Rakiten settlements don't exist)

**Villages**:
- **Kilik**: Roule colonial village, ~20 years old
- **Ilyasca**: Roule colonial village, eastern peninsula
- **Golyayla**: Roule colonial village, eastern peninsula

### The Roule Problem

Twenty years ago, halflings from Roule began settling the eastern peninsula. They called the land empty because they couldn't see anyone using it. They burned the grass to plant crops. They built permanent villages. They named the land and claimed to own it.

The Rakiten watched. They are culturally averse to confrontation, and the strangers were far from the core grazing lands. When the buffalo avoided the burned areas, the tribes simply adjusted their migration patterns. When Roule settlers started finding old hearths and stone foundations beneath their fields, they dismissed them as ancient history.

But the burning continues. Each year, a little more grassland becomes farmland. Each year, the buffalo have less range. Each year, the Rakiten lose ground without ever fighting a battle.

The tribes are divided. Some argue for continued withdrawal—the plains are vast, and there's room to disappear. Others say the withdrawal will never end until they fight. A few have begun quiet acts of resistance: herds "accidentally" stampeding through Roule fields, fires "somehow" spreading to colonial buildings. So far, the Roule don't even know the Rakiten exist. They think the plains are haunted.

That ignorance won't last forever.

### Primary Conflicts

- **Roule Expansion**: The colonial villages are growing. The burning continues. Something will break.
- **Internal Division**: The tribes can't agree on whether to fight, flee, or ignore the problem. The annual convocation grows more tense each year.
- **The Palace Question**: The Sarakiel in the Palace of Flickering Lights have lived among the Rakiten for centuries without interaction. Recently, the angels have been seen at the palace windows, watching the plains. Watching the Roule. No one knows what they're thinking.
- **The Light Leyline**: A major leyline of light energy passes through eastern Rakite. The Roule villages sit directly on it. Some Rakiten shamans believe this isn't coincidence—that the halflings are drawn to the power without knowing why, and that waking the leyline could have consequences.

### History

The Rakiten have lived on these plains longer than anyone can remember. Their oral histories stretch back dozens of generations, describing a time before the Palace, before the elven kingdoms to the south, before the mountains had names. The plains were always here. The buffalo were always here. The Rakiten were always here.

They've weathered droughts, harsh winters, and occasional incursions from the Vokas Enrisikna. They've traded with Lenora to the south and occasionally raided the coastal settlements that existed before Roule. They've never been conquered because no one has ever wanted the plains badly enough to pay the cost of taking them.

The Sarakiel arrived perhaps a thousand years ago—a squadron of fallen angels who built their marble palace and retreated inside. The Rakiten left them alone. The angels left the Rakiten alone. It's been a comfortable arrangement.

Now, for the first time, something is changing. The Roule colonists don't understand the rules. They don't know about gift-giving or sacred territories or the old arrangements with the Palace. They just see empty land and opportunity.

The Rakiten are about to learn whether their culture can adapt—or whether their refusal to fight will be the end of them.

### Player Hooks

- **The Charcoal Discovery**: A Roule farmer in Kilik digs up charcoal and stone foundations in his field. He reports it to the mayor. Someone needs to investigate whether the land was occupied before—and what it means if it was.

- **The Gift-War**: Two Rakiten families are locked in a ruinous gift competition. They've beggared themselves and are now offering things that shouldn't be offered—sacred objects, family members, promises that will destroy them. A neutral party might be able to mediate, if they can understand the rules.

- **The Angel's Offer**: A Sarakiel from the Palace has broken the ancient prohibition and walked into the plains. She seeks outsiders—not Rakiten, not Roule—for a task the angels cannot do themselves. She won't say what. She'll pay in light.

- **The Leyline Waking**: Strange lights have been seen at night near Golyayla. The Roule colonists think it's ghost-fire. The Rakiten shamans know better: something is stirring in the leyline, and the villages are at the center of it.

- **The Buffalo War**: A Roule hunting party killed a sacred white buffalo, not knowing what it was. The Rakiten are divided between those who want blood payment and those who want to give the colonists gifts until they understand what they've done. Both approaches could end in disaster.

## Roule

Tags: state, halfling, colonial, greenwater isles

A colonial halfling nation built on emptied land and sustained by honey, slavery, and selective memory. Roule is wealthy, orderly, and ruthless—a civilization that has perfected the art of not seeing what it doesn't want to see.

Three centuries ago, halfling colonists arrived on these shores. Within two generations, a plague had swept through the indigenous human population. The halflings called it providence. The survivors fled into the interior forest and have never forgotten. Today, Roule is one of the most prosperous nations in the Greenwater Isles, its flower fields and honey trade funding an expanding colonial enterprise. The price of that prosperity is paid by slaves, subjugated peoples, and the ghosts of those who came before.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Merchant Oligarchy (Parliamentary Facade)

The Parliament of Flowers meets in Fozyader and passes laws with great ceremony. Real power rests with the Honey Lords—the dozen or so merchant families who control flower cultivation, honey processing, and the slave trade. The Parliament elects a Prime Consul every five years; the Honey Lords ensure their preferred candidate wins.

The system is stable because it's profitable. Every Honey Lord has enough wealth to be content, and competition is channeled into social status rather than political conflict. When disputes arise, they're settled in private clubs over honeyed wine, not in the streets.

**Primary Inhabitants**: Halflings

Roule is overwhelmingly halfling—roughly 85% of the population. The remainder includes human merchants and sailors, a small gnomish artisan community in Kabilcuz, and slaves of various races. The Velani humans of Murderwood are not counted in any census.

**Population**: ~180,000

**Stability**: Stable

The colonial economy generates enough wealth to keep most halflings comfortable. Dissent is rare and quickly addressed. The major instabilities are external: the Velani in Murderwood, Nylos resistance, tensions with Xoth, and the ongoing Rakite expansion.

**Core Values**:

- **Commerce above all**: Profit is virtue. Successful merchants are respected; poverty is a moral failing.
- **Respectability**: What matters is appearance. Private sins are tolerated; public scandal is unforgivable.
- **Progress**: Roule is civilization advancing into wilderness. This belief justifies everything.
- **Selective memory**: The past is what we say it is. Uncomfortable truths are simply not discussed.

### Culture

Roulean halflings are polite, industrious, and quietly vicious. Social interaction follows elaborate rules of courtesy that everyone understands and no one violates publicly. Insults are delivered through praise; rejection comes wrapped in apology. Outsiders often mistake this for friendliness and learn otherwise too late.

Status is everything. Hat styles indicate profession and rank. Flower pins show family affiliation. Seating arrangements at public events communicate subtle hierarchies. A halfling can read another's entire social position at a glance and adjust their behavior accordingly.

The wind is central to Roulean identity. Greetings wish "fair winds." Curses wish "still air." Weather vanes top every building. Children learn wind-reading before letters. This obsession is practical—Roule's constant winds power mills, fill sails, and shape agriculture—but it's also cultural. Movement is virtue; stagnation is death.

**Food & Drink**: Honey features in everything. Honeyed meats, honey-glazed vegetables, honey wines, honey-sweetened teas. Visitors find it cloying; locals can't imagine food without it. The cuisine otherwise emphasizes preserved fish, root vegetables, and dense breads suitable for the windy plains.

**Religion**: Roule's official faith centers on Gythry, daemon of commerce and fair exchange—interpreted locally as blessing profit and punishing those who interfere with trade. Shrines to Gythry appear in every marketplace. The Honey Lords fund elaborate temples while ignoring the irony of a "fair exchange" deity blessing their slave markets.

**Taboos**: Never mention the previous inhabitants. Never suggest the plague wasn't natural. Never imply Murderwood contains anything but animals. These silences are enforced through social pressure rather than law—violators find themselves uninvited to gatherings, passed over for contracts, quietly excluded from polite society.

### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Developed

Roule punches above its weight economically. The honey trade alone would make it prosperous; combined with fishing, shipbuilding, and the slave trade, it's genuinely wealthy.

**Primary Exports**:
- **Taberthine honey**: Sharpens mental acuity (+1 cunning). Prized by scholars, merchants, and anyone facing important negotiations.
- **Zindwatt honey**: Induces gentle euphoria. Technically regulated; practically available to anyone with coin.
- **Preserved fish**: Roule's fishing fleets supply much of the Greenwater Isles.
- **Ships**: Kabilcuz and Teldib yards produce quality vessels.
- **Slaves**: Officially "modest domestic trade." Practically, a significant export to nations with fewer scruples.

**Primary Imports**:
- Iron and steel (limited local mining)
- Luxury goods for the Honey Lords
- Timber (Murderwood makes local logging... complicated)
- Exotic spices and goods from Xoth, Urok, and beyond

**Currency**: The Roulean Bloom—gold coins stamped with a stylized flower. Silver "petals" and copper "seeds" serve smaller transactions.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (with strong social pressure)

Roule's military is primarily naval. The fleet based at Teldib is the largest in the southern Greenwater Isles—fast patrol vessels, heavy warships, and the specialized prison-tender ships that supply Sunbeam Prison. Land forces are smaller but well-trained, focused on garrison duty and colonial expansion rather than large-scale warfare.

The navy operates with significant autonomy under Admiral Tessara Brightwater, a politically ambitious officer who has expanded her mandate steadily over the past decade. Her loyalty to the Honey Lords is... conditional.

**Notable Forces**:
- **The Teldib Fleet**: Main naval force. Approximately 40 combat vessels plus support ships.
- **Fairn's Pride**: Elite squadron named for the admiral who broke Xoth's naval power. Handles the most sensitive missions.
- **The Colonial Rangers**: Land forces specializing in "frontier security." Currently deployed in Rakite.
- **The Bay Watch**: Patrol force that guards Sunbeam Prison and monitors the Bay of Roule.

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Greenwater Isles

**Biomes**: Temperate plains, old-growth forest (Murderwood), coastal waters

**Named Entities**: Murderwood, Arins Plains

**Rivers & Lakes**: Hanli River, Ezcelmez Lake, Hakö Lake

**Adjacent Waters**: Bay of Roule, Inside Passage, Fairn Passage, Roule Channel

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Fozyader

**Cities**: Kabilcuz, Tezina, Teldib, Ovabasi, Samandoken, Recnaf

**Towns**: Esslekiv, Wunipek, Gavuragili, Dikilkaya, Nebil, Boke, Dimetoka, Gevenlik, Atamer, Bachesaray, Kyullyuk, Varhal, Karamehmetler, Mahmu

**Rakite Colonial Settlements**: Kilik, Ilyasca, Golyayla

*Note: Kyullyuk is listed in official records but is actually a Velani settlement in Murderwood that Roule does not control.*

### Primary Conflicts

**The Velani Problem**

The survivors of the plague live in Murderwood. They call themselves the Velani—"the remaining"—and they have not forgotten what the halflings did. For three centuries they've held the forest against every attempt at pacification. The Honey Lords publicly maintain that Murderwood contains only dangerous animals. Privately, military planners know the truth and have no solution.

The Velani kill halflings on sight. They trade occasionally with outsiders through hidden intermediaries, accepting iron and salt in exchange for forest goods. Whether they could ever threaten Roule directly is unknown—but the possibility haunts those who know the real history.

**Nylos Subjugation**

The islands of Nylos were once independent. Now they're a Roulean territory, their people taxed, their fishing rights controlled, their resistance fighters tried in Samandoken and executed in the public square. The Nylosi haven't forgotten independence, and the younger generation is increasingly willing to fight for it.

Roule maintains order through military presence, economic control, and exemplary punishment. So far, it's been enough. But the occupation is expensive, the Nylosi are patient, and every execution creates new martyrs.

**Xoth Tensions**

Two centuries ago, Admiral Fairn sank a Xothian fleet in the passage that now bears his name. Relations have never recovered. The nations maintain formal peace, but naval "incidents" occur regularly, trade negotiations are hostile, and both sides assume the other is planning something.

The conflict is partly territorial (disputed island claims), partly economic (competing trade routes), and partly pure historical grudge. Neither side wants open war, but neither side will back down.

**Rakite Expansion**

Roule is currently colonizing Rakite—burning grasslands for farms, establishing settlements, pushing ever westward. The colonists insist the land is empty. It isn't. The Rakiten elves have lived there for millennia, and they're beginning to realize that retreat won't save them.

Back in Fozyader, the Honey Lords know only what the Colonial Rangers tell them: progress is good, expansion is profitable, the land is available. The truth—that Roule is repeating its founding crime—hasn't reached them yet. When it does, they'll probably find a way not to see it.

**The Colonial Villages**

Three settlements now dot the eastern Rakite peninsula, all drawing water from the Ves Neles river system:

*Kilik* is the oldest and largest—twenty years established, grown from a frontier outpost into something approaching a proper town. Wooden buildings cluster around a central square, a small Gythry temple serves the faithful, and the original ford across the Ves Neles is now a proper bridge. Population around 400. The Colonial Rangers maintain their regional headquarters here.

*Ilyasca* lies downstream in low hills, chosen for its defensible position. The settlers here are the most nervous of the three villages—they insist something watches them from the tall grass. Livestock goes missing. Fires start without explanation. The Rangers dismiss it as frontier paranoia. Population around 200.

*Golyayla* occupies the river mouth where the Ves Neles meets the sea. Primarily a fishing village, it receives supply ships from Roule proper at its small dock. The colonists here spend more time looking seaward than inland, which may be why they sleep better than their neighbors. Population around 150.

A trail connects all three villages, maintained by the Rangers for supply runs and patrol routes. The colonists believe they're civilizing empty wilderness. They don't know the Rakiten exist. They don't know they're being watched.

### History

**The Arrival (c. 3076 SD)**: Halfling colonists from [eastern origins] make landfall at what becomes Kabilcuz. They find a populated land—humans living in coastal villages and the interior plains.

**The Plague (3080-3095 SD)**: A devastating disease sweeps through the human population. Nine in ten die. The halflings are largely immune. Whether the plague was natural, carried unknowingly by the colonists, or something worse has never been established. Survivors flee into the interior forest.

**The Claiming (3095-3150 SD)**: Halflings expand across the emptied land. The first Honey Lords establish flower cultivation. Attempts to clear Murderwood fail repeatedly. The colonial narrative crystallizes: providence gave them this land; what came before doesn't matter.

**The Nylos Conquest (c. 3180 SD)**: Roule subjugates the Nylos islands, adding their fishing grounds and pearl beds to the colonial economy. Resistance is crushed. The first generation of Nylosi born under occupation knows no other life.

**The Fairn Victory (c. 3200 SD)**: Admiral Fairn destroys a Xothian fleet attempting to challenge Roulean control of the northern passage. Roule becomes the dominant naval power in the southern Greenwater Isles. Xoth never forgives.

**The Modern Era (3200-present)**: Roule consolidates, grows wealthy, and begins expanding into Rakite. The Honey Lords rule through commerce. The Parliament provides legitimacy. The navy ensures security. Everything is prosperous and stable and built on bones.

### Notable Figures

**Prime Consul Aldric Thornberry**: The current head of government—a mild, agreeable halfling who does exactly what the Honey Lords tell him. He's genuinely popular because he's genuinely harmless.

**Admiral Tessara Brightwater**: Commander of the Teldib Fleet. Brilliant, ambitious, and increasingly independent. She's built a power base within the navy that rivals the Honey Lords' economic influence. What she wants is unclear; that she wants *something* is obvious.

**Lady Primrose Goldpetal**: Head of the largest Honey Lord family. Ancient, ruthless, and possessed of a memory for slights that spans decades. She's outlived three husbands, five rivals, and countless enemies. The other Lords fear her.

**The Keeper of Thorns**: The Velani's leader, if they have one. Roulean intelligence has never confirmed this figure exists, but prisoners taken at the forest's edge sometimes whisper the title before dying. The name suggests someone who remembers the original massacre.

### Adventure Hooks

- A Honey Lord hires the party to "survey" Murderwood—actually to find proof the Velani exist, so he can argue for renewed military action.
- Nylos resistance fighters approach the party for help smuggling weapons through the Inside Passage.
- Strange honey appears on the market—Taberthine that grants visions instead of clarity. The source is somewhere in the forest.
- A Rakiten elf reaches Fozyader seeking allies against colonial expansion. No one will listen—except perhaps outsiders with nothing to lose.
- Something has killed three patrol ships near Sunbeam Prison. The navy wants it handled quietly.

## Sennos

Tags: citystate, state

Inhabited by Sennites—the lake dwarves. The city formed when the dwarves were driven out of Argysis thousands of years ago for betraying the dwarves the first time. Dwarves hate the water, but such was the vengeance of the Hephake and Argysis dwarves, they had no choice but to flee literally into the water. There, they made a new home.

Huge, dark, underwater palace.

Area surrounded by Rusalka. The Sennites in constant war with them.

## Sestros

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Absolute monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Shailin

**Population**: 100,000

**Stability**: Very stable

**Core Values**: Glory, the graces of their god Talressses, do as your told, blind faith

- The king of Sestros is believed to have a direct link with their god Talresses.
- Believe magic is horrible, and is thus forbidden (Talresses' influence).
- Very warm towards outsiders.
- The world is very authoritarian, though they do not feel it. It is rude to ask questions.


### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Undeveloped

**Primary Exports**: Silks, fine textiles

**Primary Imports**: Technology

- Sestros boasts the finest textile production of anywhere in the world, attracting wealthy merchants and nobility form all over to purshase form their fine selection.
- Most people work as ranchers, herding sheep and other domestic animals across the plains.


### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Low

**Enrollment**: Mandatory

- The whisperings of Talresses encourage the Shailin to always hold onto a standing army. Why they must do this, they are unclear, but they do not ask. 


### Geography

**Location**: Western Ve, south of Edari, west of Shyona and the Hills of Dolor

**Biomes**: Savannah plains (central), Red Desert (north), forest (south)

**Named Entities**: Red Desert, Shepherds' Stones (northern mountains), Tukiwood (southern forest), Glory River

**Rivers & Lakes**: Glory River (central, flows east toward Shyona)

**Adjacent Waters**: Nuin Bay (southwest coast)


### Political Geography

**Capital**: Mu (southwest coast, on Nuin Bay)

**Major Towns (Northern Route)**: Eidu, Dangbanr, Kanasra, Hindi Kot, Nao Goth

**Major Towns (Central Route)**: Lolani, Panjizai, Ujato, Phuklean, Wadala Sindhuan, Banda Lamman, Karborai, Jhun Tar, Bel, Wariyah, Dangara, Kothian, Basti Wahni

**Major Towns (Western)**: Philoki, Chirianwala, Kohali, Goth Kilch


### Primary Conflicts

- As the Sestros military grows, Sestros' neighbors grow wary. They wonder if war is coming.
- Innovation slows, and scholarship remains frozen in the past, as asking questions is fronwned upon, and new ideas are rare.
- The Shailin are being deluded by a prideful daemon.


### History

## Seyiki

Tags: state, gnome, westwilds

A distributed nation of dragonfly-riding gnomes living among the Chakatann pillars—the massive stone monuments left by the fallen Postronamas Empire in the western Piktiniti Desert.

### Overview

Seyiki isn't a single city but a network of pillar-communities, each hosting between 200 and 2,000 gnomes in carved chambers along the pillar sides. The pillars rise hundreds of feet from the desert floor, and the Seyiki have adapted to vertical life: children learn to fall (safely, using wingsuit-robes) before they learn to climb.

Below many pillars lie saltwater lakes—remnants of ancient seas now trapped in desert basins. The giant dragonflies the Seyiki ride have adapted to tolerate saltwater, using the lakes as breeding grounds.

### Government

Seyiki governance is loose and distributed. Each pillar-community is largely autonomous, governed by a council of elders, successful merchants, and veteran riders. The pillars cooperate through periodic gatherings—"Sky Moots"—where representatives from across the network meet to resolve disputes, coordinate defense, and arrange marriages between communities.

No central authority exists. Attempts to create one have historically failed; the Seyiki are too independent, too scattered, and too suspicious of concentrated power (they remember, mythologically, what happened to the Postronamas).

### Culture

**Vertical Life:** Status correlates with elevation. Pillar-tops are prestigious; base-dwellers are low-caste. Everything important happens high up.

**The Robes:** All Seyiki wear flowing garments that convert to wingsuits when falling. This isn't fashion—it's survival. A Seyiki without their robe is naked in a way that has nothing to do with modesty.

**Dragonfly Bonds:** The relationship between gnome and mount is intimate and lifelong. Losing a dragonfly is a genuine tragedy; bonding with a new one takes months of patient work.

**The Reaping:** The seven-year locust swarms are the most important cultural event. Seyiki don't hide—they hunt, riding into the swarms to harvest locusts by the ton. A gnome's worth is measured partly by how many Reapings they've survived.

### Economy

Seyiki export crystal fragments (twyl from depleted pillars), dragonfly chitin, and locust products. They import metal, textiles, and luxuries through Jüt. Trade caravans are rare; most commerce happens via dragonfly-back, with hauler-breeds carrying cargo between pillars and to the coast.

### Military

Seyiki warriors are fantastic fighters despite their small size. Their combat doctrine emphasizes three-dimensional movement: dropping from above, striking from unexpected angles, retreating upward or into the air. On their home pillars, they're nearly impossible to defeat; enemies can't pursue them vertically, and the gnomes know every passage and platform.

The dragonfly-mounted cavalry is feared across the region. War-dancer breeds are fast, maneuverable, and aggressive. A Seyiki warband can harry an army indefinitely, picking off stragglers, raiding supply lines, and never committing to a fight they might lose.

### Relations

**Jüt:** Primary trading partner. The coastal city is the Seyiki's window to the wider world. Relations are commercial and cordial.

**The Orc States:** Distant but wary. The orcs control the inland Westwilds; the Seyiki control the desert pillars. Neither has much the other wants, so conflict is rare.

**Treasure Hunters:** A constant irritation. Outsiders seeking crystal cores are tolerated if they ask permission; those who try to claim pillars by force discover why the Seyiki have held this territory for millennia.

### Notable Locations

See atlas entry for Chakatann. The pillars themselves are the notable locations—each one a vertical village with its own character, history, and specializations.

### Player Hooks

- Hire Seyiki guides to cross the Piktiniti safely
- Seek Postronamas artifacts in the ruins near Enimogos (the Seyiki won't help, but they might not stop you)
- Join a Reaping—outsiders who prove themselves worthy earn Seyiki respect
- Investigate a pillar whose crystal core has begun humming *wrong*
- Mediate a dispute between pillar-communities threatening to escalate into inter-pillar war

## Shazuihn

Tags: state, orc, westwilds

They come from the east on giant boars, screaming, axes raised, and when they leave there's nothing but blood and burning. The Shazuihni don't build. They don't negotiate. They don't farm. They raid, they take, and they vanish back into the forest before anyone can retaliate.

The other orc states call them savages. The Shazuihni call themselves free.

### Overview

The Shazuihni are everything the other orc states have evolved past—or so the other states like to believe. They're enormous, hairy, and violent. They ride massive boars that can gore a horse to death. They wield axes that need two ordinary orcs to lift. They attack without warning, take what they want, and disappear.

But dismissing them as simple brutes is a mistake. The Shazuihni have survived for centuries in the hostile borderlands between forest and steppe. They've resisted every attempt to conquer or civilize them. They maintain a complex relationship with their boar herds that borders on spiritual. And their "primitive" raids have bled the other orc states for generations.

They're not sophisticated. They don't need to be. They're doing fine.

### Government

**Type:** War-Chief Dominance

The Shazuihni don't have formal government. They have whoever is strongest. A War-Chief rules until someone kills them or proves themselves more worthy of leadership—usually by killing them.

**Current Leadership:**
- **War-Chief Grokthul the Boar-Father** — Massive even by Shazuihni standards, Grokthul has ruled for fifteen years by being too dangerous to challenge. His boar, Skull-Splitter, has killed more orcs than most warriors.
- **The Blood-Riders** — A loose council of the most successful raiders. They advise Grokthul (when he asks, which is rarely) and lead their own warbands independently.
- **The Boar-Speakers** — Shamans who maintain the bond between riders and mounts. They hold surprising influence because without healthy boars, there are no raids.

Leadership changes frequently. Three War-Chiefs in the last generation died in raids; two were killed by rivals; one was trampled by his own boar. The Shazuihni consider this natural. The strong rule until they're not strong anymore.

### Culture

**The Shazuihni** are the largest of the Wildwood orcs—easily seven feet tall, covered in coarse hair, with prominent tusks and small, cunning eyes. They look like someone crossed an orc with an ogre, which may not be entirely wrong. Their intelligence is... different. Not absent, but focused. They don't think abstractly. They don't plan far ahead. They react, adapt, and survive.

**Core Values:**
- **Strength Is Everything** — The strong take. The weak give. This is how the world works.
- **The Boar Is Brother** — A rider and boar are bonded for life. Harming someone's boar is worse than harming them directly.
- **Take What You Need** — Property belongs to whoever can hold it. Theft is just a test of strength.
- **Die Well** — Death in battle is glorious. Death from old age or sickness is shameful. The best deaths involve taking enemies with you.

**Society:** Shazuihni society is organized around boar-bonds and war-bands. A young orc bonds with a boar around age ten—a dangerous process that kills many aspirants. Those who survive become riders; those who fail become servants, cooks, or corpses.

War-bands form around successful raiders. A rider who brings back good plunder attracts followers. A rider who fails too often finds themselves alone, which in the Shazuihni world is a death sentence.

Women raid alongside men. Children learn to ride before they learn to walk. The elderly—the few who reach old age—are quietly abandoned when they can no longer keep up. The Shazuihni don't discuss this. It simply happens.

**The Boars:** Giant boars, bred for size and aggression over centuries. A full-grown Shazuihni war-boar stands five feet at the shoulder, weighs over a thousand pounds, and has tusks that can punch through wooden shields. They're not horses—they're partners. The bond between rider and boar is genuine and profound, the one area where the Shazuihni show something like tenderness.

### Economy

**Size:** Very Small (formal economy)
**Tech:** Undeveloped

**Primary Exports:**
- Nothing (they don't trade)

**Primary Imports:**
- Nothing (they take what they need)

The Shazuihni don't have an economy in any meaningful sense. They raid for what they need—food, weapons, captives, anything useful. What they can't raid for, they do without.

Between raids, they hunt in the forests and steppes. The boars root for tubers and mushrooms. Captured slaves do the work the Shazuihni find beneath them. It's not sustainable in any civilized sense, but it's sustained them for centuries.

**The Captive Question:** The Shazuihni take slaves in their raids. Some are worked to death. Some are ransomed back. Some eventually earn acceptance by proving useful. A few have even become riders, though this is rare. The other orc states condemn this practice while quietly engaging in their own forms of bondage.

### Military

**Size:** Medium
**Quality:** High (in specific contexts)
**Enrollment:** Universal (all riders raid)

The Shazuihni military is simple: riders on boars with big axes. There's no infantry, no archers, no siege equipment. Just speed, mass, and overwhelming violence.

**The Raid:** A typical Shazuihni raid involves twenty to fifty riders appearing without warning, hitting a target hard, grabbing everything portable, and vanishing before defenders can organize. They don't hold territory. They don't fight pitched battles. They strike and run.

**Boar Charge:** The signature Shazuihni tactic. Fifty war-boars charging in formation create a wall of tusks, hooves, and screaming riders that can break any conventional defensive line. The charge is nearly unstoppable—but it only works once per battle. If the first charge doesn't break the enemy, the Shazuihni retreat to try again another day.

**Weaknesses:**
- Terrible at defense. They don't build fortifications and don't know how to hold ground.
- Useless in confined spaces. The boars need room to move.
- No ranged capability worth mentioning.
- Dependent on boar health. Sick boars mean no raids.
- Can't sustain long campaigns. They hit and run; they don't siege.

### Geography

**Location:** Eastern Wildwood edge, where forest meets the Steppe of Aziirn

**Biomes:** Forest margins, scattered woodland, open steppe

**Key Locations:**
- **The Rooting Grounds** — Semi-permanent camps where the boar herds graze and breed. Sacred to the Shazuihni.
- **The Blood Road** — The main raiding path west into Hedroscobb territory. Generations of raids have worn it into the land.
- **The Scorches** — Geothermal region to the north. Too dangerous for regular habitation but used as a retreat when enemies pursue.
- **Dragon Spout** — Where void and fire meet. The Shazuihni avoid it but use it as a boundary marker.

**Borders:**
- West: Hedroscobb (primary raiding target)
- Northwest: Tarn (secondary raiding target)
- North: The Scorches, eventually the northern steppes
- East: Steppe of Aziirn (mostly empty; occasional contact with steppe peoples)
- South: Wurmspine Mountains region

### Relations

**Hedroscobb:** Primary target. The Black Orcs have things the Shazuihni want—metal, food, slaves. The algae-iron armor is a problem, but the Shazuihni have learned to avoid the armored infantry and hit softer targets. This war has continued for generations with no end in sight.

**Tarn:** Secondary target. The Tarni spirit warriors are individually dangerous—nearly impossible to kill quickly—but they're few in number. Hit-and-run tactics work well against them. The river territory offers good plunder.

**Glivornax:** No direct contact. Tarn lies between Shazuihn and the Glivornaxi.

**Uhionai:** The Shazuihni learned the hard way not to raid the Wooden Men. The Ryunimir Forest is too dense for boars, and the Uihonaii make anyone who enters pay dearly. One catastrophic defeat was enough.

**Grieb:** No raids. Two generations ago, War-Chief Grulnak led fifty riders against the lakemen. Three came back. The boars won't go near water now—they remember something the riders won't talk about. The territory might be valuable, but nobody's willing to pay that price again.

**Vogenfeld:** The Shazuihni would love to raid the dwarves, but Hedroscobb is in the way. By the time raiders fight through Black Orc territory, they're too depleted to challenge mountain fortresses.

**The Orc Moot:** The Shazuihni attend irregularly. When they come, they demand tribute from the other states for "protection" (from themselves). This rarely works, but they keep trying.

### Conflicts

**The Eternal Raid:** The Shazuihni are always raiding someone. It's not a conflict that can be won or lost—it's a permanent state of affairs. They raid to survive. They survive to raid.

**The Boar Plague:** A sickness swept through the herds two years ago, killing a third of the war-boars. The Shazuihni are still recovering. Raids have been smaller and less frequent, but this won't last. The herds are growing again.

**The Eastern Question:** The steppe peoples beyond Aziirn have their own horse-riders and their own ambitions. Occasional clashes have occurred. If the steppe tribes ever unified, the Shazuihni would face a serious threat—but the steppes are even more fragmented than the Wildwood.

### History

Where did the Shazuihni come from? No one's sure. They were raiding the Wildwood before the other orc states formed. They might be descendants of ogre-orc crossbreeding. They might be an ancient orc lineage that never "civilized." They might have wandered in from the eastern steppes generations ago.

They don't keep records. They don't tell origin stories. They raid, they survive, they die in battle if they're lucky. That's enough history for them.

The other orc states have tried everything to stop them. Alliances, punitive expeditions, assassination attempts, even bribery. Nothing has worked. The Shazuihni absorb losses, retreat into the steppes, breed more boars, and come back. They're a fact of life in the Wildwood, as constant as the seasons and twice as destructive.

### Player Hooks

- Survive being captured in a Shazuihni raid
- Negotiate with a War-Chief for passage through their territory (bring gifts)
- Bond with a Shazuihni war-boar (extremely dangerous; possibly lethal)
- Investigate the boar plague—was it natural or something else?
- Hire Shazuihni mercenaries (they'll work for enough plunder)
- Unite the other orc states against the eternal raids (probably impossible)
- Convince the Shazuihni to raid somewhere truly dangerous (like Vogenfeld)

## Sheîr

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Feudal monarchy (nominal), island autonomy (actual)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Bledreon Islanders (humans with dual-lid eyes), various mainland humans

**Population**: ~35,000 (capital ~8,000; scattered island populations ~27,000)

**Stability**: Stable in chaos (the system is dysfunction, but it functions)

**Core Values**: Naval freedom, island pride, ancestral territory, the hunt

Sheîr is two nations wearing one crown. The capital—called simply **The Seat**—is a proper kingdom: courts, nobles, laws, tax collectors. The islands are something else entirely. Bledreon sailors acknowledge the king exists. They ignore everything he says.

The Bledreon Islanders are immediately recognizable: their eyes have dual lids, an inner membrane that protects against salt spray and harsh sun. They can see underwater with surprising clarity. Some claim the trait comes from interbreeding with sea spirits; the Bledreon say their ancestors simply learned to live at sea until the sea lived in them.

The capital's nobles are a different breed—mainlanders who married into island families or received land grants from forgotten kings. They ride **raptors**—swift, vicious creatures somewhere between lizard and bird—and maintain the deadliest land army in the Western Isles. When Sheîr's raptors land on an island, everyone on that island dies.

### The Ghost Treaty

Sheîr's relationship with Tarkhon is... complicated.

Five hundred years ago—before the Tarkhon Empire existed as such—the Neferati fire-bloods made a pact with Sheîr's ancestors. The terms were specific: Sheîr would provide naval support to whoever held the Evertorch in Tarkhetan. In exchange, the Neferati would never claim Sheîr as territory, would guarantee its independence, and would support it against external threats.

The pact was sworn on the Evertorch itself. It cannot be broken by either party—only renegotiated by mutual agreement.

When the Severance happened and humans inherited Tarkhon, they inherited the pact too. The current emperor claims this makes Sheîr a vassal—bound to support whoever holds the Evertorch. Sheîr's king argues the pact makes them *equals*—allies, not subjects, with no obligation beyond what they choose to give.

Both interpretations are legally defensible. Neither side wants to test the pact's limits.

In practice: Sheîr sends ships when Tarkhon calls—sometimes. Sheîr's nobles occasionally lend their raptor cavalry to Tarkhon's campaigns—when they feel like it. Tarkhon doesn't try to tax Sheîr or station troops there. Everyone pretends this is a functioning relationship.

The Kingdoms of Fire find the whole arrangement darkly amusing. The pact was with the Neferati. Perhaps the *original* holders of the Evertorch could interpret it differently...

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Standard, advanced shipbuilding

**Primary Exports**: Ships, sailors, fish, sea products, raptor mounts (rarely)

**Primary Imports**: Grain, metals, luxury goods, mainlander goods

Sheîr builds the best ships in the Western Isles. The Bledreon have been sailing these waters for centuries; they know what works. Shipyards on the larger islands produce vessels for clients across the Shattered Sea.

The islands themselves produce fish, salt, and various sea products. Some islands have valuable timber; others have little but rock and stubbornness. The capital controls most overland trade with Tarkhon.

Raptor breeding is controlled exclusively by noble families. The creatures are too dangerous and too valuable to sell casually. Occasionally a foreign noble will pay an obscene price for a trained mount—but they rarely survive long without Sheîr handlers.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Naval excellence, elite cavalry

**Enrollment**: Island volunteers (navy), noble retinues (cavalry)

Sheîr's military has two branches that barely interact:

**The Island Fleets**

Every significant island maintains its own ships and crews. There's no unified navy—each captain answers to their island elder. When Sheîr needs a fleet, the king sends word and captains who feel like responding show up. This sounds chaotic. It is. But it also means enemy raiders never know how many ships they'll face.

The Bledreon fight from ships with devastating effectiveness. Their dual-lid eyes let them see in spray and sun. They can dive overboard, swim to enemy vessels, and attack from below. Most naval powers fear engaging them.

**The Raptor Nobility**

The capital's nobles breed and ride raptors—pack-hunting predators standing taller than a man. The creatures are stupid, vicious, and terrifyingly fast. They hunt by running prey to exhaustion, then tearing it apart.

On open ground, raptor cavalry is nearly unstoppable. The problem is reaching open ground—Sheîr is mostly islands and water. But when the nobles land their raptors on an enemy island, the result is massacre. This happens rarely, but the threat keeps rival islands from pushing too hard.

### Geography

**Location**: Eastern Western Isles, jutting into the Shattered Sea from the Tarkhon coast

**Biomes**: Island archipelago, coastal mainland territory

**Climate**: Warm maritime, frequent storms

**Named Features**: The Seat (capital), Phyndarr Sound (northern waters), numerous unnamed islands

Sheîr straddles the boundary between the Western Isles and Tarkhon proper. The mainland portion is small—a coastal strip with the capital city. The real territory is the islands: dozens of them spreading west into the Shattered Sea.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: The Seat (~8,000)

**Major Islands**: Named by their elders, too numerous to list. Larger islands have populations of 500-2,000; smaller ones might have a few families.

### The Seat

The capital is everything the islands aren't: orderly, hierarchical, concerned with appearances. Stone buildings line planned streets. Nobles maintain townhouses and compete for proximity to the palace. The royal court observes elaborate protocols that islanders consider laughable.

**King Varos III** rules from the Driftwood Throne—carved from timbers of the ship that supposedly carried the first Bledreon to these shores. He's thirty-seven, competent, and increasingly frustrated by his inability to actually control the islands that are nominally his.

The Seat's power comes from three sources: control of Tarkhon trade, the raptor cavalry, and the legal fiction that the king speaks for all Sheîr. The first two are real. The third is useful mainly for negotiating with foreigners who expect someone to be in charge.

### The Islands

The islands govern themselves through tradition. Each has an elder or council; decisions are made by consensus or force. The king has no authority there. He can request assistance, but islanders respond only to direct threats or opportunities for profit.

Island culture values freedom above all. A Bledreon sailor answers to their captain, their captain answers to their elder, and their elder answers to no one. This makes coordinating anything nearly impossible—but it also means no foreign power can conquer Sheîr by taking the capital.

**Key Island Groups:**
- **The Inner Ring**: Islands closest to the mainland, most influenced by capital politics
- **The Drift**: Central island clusters where the true Bledreon traditions survive
- **The Far Reach**: Western islands barely connected to Sheîr at all—practically independent states

### Primary Conflicts

**King vs. Islands**

King Varos wants to actually rule his kingdom. The islands want to be left alone. This tension has existed since Sheîr's founding and will probably never resolve. Varos's recent attempts to collect island taxes have been met with selective deafness.

**The Ghost Treaty Question**

What exactly does Sheîr owe Tarkhon? The empire keeps pushing for more active support. Sheîr keeps finding reasons to provide less. Eventually someone will force the issue—and then the ancient pact will be tested in ways no one can predict.

**The Fire Kingdom Interest**

The Kingdoms of Fire have started asking questions about the Ghost Treaty. The pact was with the Neferati who held the Evertorch. What if the original holders reclaimed their flame? Would the treaty transfer? Some in Sheîr are curious. Others are terrified of being caught between Tarkhon and the Fire Kingdoms.

**Raptor Succession**

The noble families who breed raptors are dying out. Three of the seven breeding bloodlines have ended in the past century. The remaining families guard their secrets jealously—but secrets don't help if there's no one to inherit them.

### Notable Figures

**King Varos III** — Ruler of Sheîr in name, frustrated administrator in practice. Competent, ambitious, and increasingly willing to take risks to expand his actual authority.

**Elder Kresh of the Drift** — Unofficial leader of the island faction. Eighty years old, sailor since childhood, respected across the archipelago. When Kresh speaks, islanders listen—which is more than the king can say.

**Duchess Seravyn** — Leader of the raptor cavalry and head of the oldest breeding family. Cold, competent, and deeply concerned about her family's fading bloodline. Has three daughters; none have the talent for raptor bonding.

**Captain Drell Stormwake** — Most famous Bledreon sailor alive. Commands a fleet of twelve ships that he's built up through piracy, trade, and strategic marriages. Theoretically loyal to Elder Kresh; practically loyal to profit.

### History

**The First Bledreon**: The islands were settled long ago by humans who developed—or acquired—their distinctive eye trait. Origin stories vary; the Bledreon don't much care which is true.

**The Neferati Pact** (~500 years ago): When the Neferati were building what would become Tarkhon, they needed naval support. They couldn't conquer the Bledreon, so they made a deal. The Ghost Treaty has bound Sheîr to whoever holds the Evertorch ever since.

**The Severance**: When humans inherited Tarkhon, they inherited the pact. The relationship has been contentious ever since—Sheîr honoring the letter while ignoring the spirit.

**The Present**: A kingdom divided against itself, bound by an ancient treaty to an empire it doesn't respect, and increasingly caught between Tarkhon and the Fire Kingdoms. Something will break eventually.

## Shykasas

Tags: state, shacklands, alekroin

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Absolute monarchy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Alekroin

**Population**: 10,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Patience, territorial dominance, ancestral tradition, predatory hierarchy

The King of Shykasas rules over the six noble houses from the Palace of the Six Jaws in Vila Bonsa. Royal succession follows ancient ritual: an egg is selected from the King's brood and incubated in isolation at precise temperatures, watched by keepers who will never see it hatch. The chosen heir grows larger, stronger, and more cunning than any other Alekroin. The process is not explained to outsiders.

Each noble house controls territory within the archipelago and maintains its own hunting traditions, but all defer to the crown in matters of foreign policy and territorial defense. Internal disputes are settled through ritualized challenges—rarely lethal, but always decisive.

Shykasas has no allies because it wants none. The Alekroin view diplomatic relationships as a form of weakness, an admission that one cannot survive alone. They trade when convenient but make no treaties, sign no pacts, and join no coalitions.

### Economy

**Size**: Medium

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Medicinal plants, bioluminescent algae, cured hides, rare shells, aquaculture products

**Primary Imports**: Metals, worked steel, luxury goods

The swamps provide abundantly for those who know how to harvest them. The Alekroin have developed sophisticated aquaculture, cultivating shellfish and medicinal plants in managed pools throughout the archipelago. Their bioluminescent algae—harvested from the deeper channels—fetches high prices in foreign markets for both decorative and alchemical purposes.

Trade happens on Alekroin terms. Foreign merchants are restricted to Bixesarri on the outer islands. The Alekroin do not haggle, do not negotiate, and do not explain their prices. Traders who complain are free to leave and never return.

Metal remains the critical import. The swamps offer no ore deposits, and the Alekroin require steel for tools, weapons, and construction. This dependency is their only significant vulnerability, and they maintain carefully hidden stockpiles against the possibility of trade disruption.

### Military

**Size**: Very small

**Quality**: Exceptional

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (effectively universal)

Every adult Alekroin is a capable combatant—their predatory physiology and lifelong hunting experience make formal military training almost redundant. The "military" consists primarily of the coastwatch stationed at Erez and similar posts, who track vessel movements and coordinate responses to intrusion.

When Shykasas goes to war, it does so with the patient, methodical lethality of its people. They do not march in formations or hold battle lines. They wait in water that looks empty until it isn't. Three Gorathi survey vessels learned this lesson. Gorath has not sent a fourth.

An individual Alekroin can engage four or more typical soldiers with reasonable odds of victory. They are ambush predators by nature and training, striking from unexpected angles and using terrain that advantages them and disadvantages everyone else.

### Geography

**Location**: Southwestern Shacklands, between the Sea of Sighs and open ocean

**Biomes**: Saltwater swamp, tidal marshland, mangrove forest, coastal archipelago

**Climate**: Hot, humid, monsoon seasons

**Named Entities**:
- Turiik Forest (northern mangrove boundary)
- Soso Mari (southern tidal bay)
- Thizun (taboo ruins, northwest island)

**Rivers & Lakes**: Numerous tidal channels; no major freshwater rivers

**Adjacent Waters**: Sea of Sighs (north), open ocean (south and west)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Vila Bonsa

**Cities**: None (Vila Bonsa is the only major settlement)

**Towns**: Bixesarri (trade), Ansalonga (hunting), Molleres (eastern border), Arinsal (aquaculture), Erez (coastwatch)

**Villages**: Sornas (fishing/pearl diving)

### Relations

**Gorath**: Cold indifference. Gorath wants shipping access through Soso Mari; Shykasas has not responded to diplomatic overtures. Three survey vessels have entered the bay; none returned. The matter remains unresolved.

**Lyoki Ingi Ingi**: Neutral non-interference. The Mudzwiti goblins and Alekroin share a border at Molleres. Both peoples are territorial and respect boundaries. Trade is minimal but not hostile.

**Kyagos**: No meaningful contact. The mountain island's recent revolution has not affected Shykasas in any way.

**Slave Trade**: No participation. The Alekroin have no interest in slavery—they consider the practice inefficient and demeaning to the enslaved (who become prey) and the enslaver (who relies on prey for labor).

### Primary Conflicts

**The Soso Mari Question**: Gorath's interest in the bay shipping lanes remains unresolved. The Alekroin's continued silence forces Gorath to choose between accepting the status quo and risking another expensive lesson.

**Thizun**: The taboo ruins occasionally attract treasure hunters. The Alekroin do not interfere with these expeditions, but neither do they assist. Something in those ruins prevents most explorers from returning, and the Alekroin seem content to let this natural defense continue.

**Succession Tensions**: The ritual selection of royal heirs occasionally generates resentment among noble houses whose eggs were passed over. These tensions rarely escalate beyond pointed comments and careful watching.

### History

The Alekroin have occupied these swamps for millennia. Their oral histories speak of arriving from elsewhere—"when the waters were different"—but the details have been worn smooth by generations of retelling. What remains is the certainty that they belong here and always have.

Thizun predates them. The ruins were already ancient and already taboo when the first Alekroin settled the archipelago. The prohibition against visiting the site has been maintained without explanation for as long as anyone can remember.

Shykasas has never been conquered. Several powers have considered the attempt over the centuries. Those who proceeded learned why the others did not.

## Silwyn

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Council of Herd-Chiefs

**Primary Inhabitants**: Silzar (plains ogres)

**Population**: ~4,000

**Stability**: Somewhat Stable

**Core Values**: Kindness, strength, toughness, grit, connection to the plains

The Silzar have no permanent leadership. When the mammoth herds converge at seasonal watering grounds, the herd-chiefs meet in council to decide matters affecting all Silwyn. Decisions require broad consensus—but any chief who disagrees strongly enough can simply leave with their herd. This makes Silzar politics slow and frustrating to outsiders, but it has kept them unified through decades of war.

The entire culture rests on the Mammoth Covenant: a spiritual pact formed generations ago between the Silzar and the great mammoth herds. The mammoths are not beasts of burden or war animals—they are partners. A Silzar who mistreats a mammoth is exiled. One who kills a mammoth without dire need is killed in turn. In exchange, the mammoths carry the Silzar to war, share their strength, and grant them dominion over the open plains.

This covenant defines what the Silzar are. It also limits what they can become.


### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

**Primary Exports**: Mammoth ivory, cured leather, bone-craft

**Primary Imports**: Metal, timber, rope and fiber

The Silzar economy runs on three things: what the mammoths provide, what the plains provide, and what they take from Iylovia.

Mammoth ivory, leather, and bone form the basis of Silzar craftsmanship. They're skilled workers—their bone carvings and leather goods are prized by traders willing to make the journey. But they have no metal deposits, no forests, no fiber crops. Everything that isn't meat, milk, leather, or bone must come from outside.

Timber and rope come from Iylovia, taken in raids. Metal is harder. The Silzar trade with merchants who travel up from the southern desert routes—exchanging ivory and leather for iron and bronze. It's never enough. Every ballistae bolt represents precious metal that can't be replaced easily.

This scarcity shapes everything. The Silzar don't waste. They don't build permanent structures. They repair and reuse until nothing remains. And they raid Iylovia not from cruelty but from genuine need—the forests have what the plains lack.


### Military

**Size**: Small

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

The Silzar field perhaps 800 warriors at full muster—a tiny force by any standard. But those 800 ride war mammoths carrying ballistae platforms, and on open ground, nothing in the region can stand against them.

A mammoth charge breaks formations. The ballistae—massive torsion crossbows firing bolts the length of a man's arm—shatter shield walls and fortifications from range. And the trumpeting... the coordinated trumpeting of a mammoth war-line drowns out commands, drowns out thought, drowns out the whispers of the Gyv forest-magic.

The Silzar don't fight in organized ranks. Each mammoth is a mobile fortress crewed by three to five warriors—a driver, a ballistae operator, and flankers with bows or javelins. They coordinate through horn signals and generations of shared instinct. On the plains, they're unstoppable.

In the forests, they're helpless. Mammoths can't maneuver between dense trees. Ballistae can't aim through canopy. The whispers creep in despite the trumpeting. Every attempt to push into Iylovia's forests has ended in disaster—warriors stumbling out days later, half-mad, having killed their own companions in the confusion.

This is the heart of the Silzar dilemma. Their greatest strength is useless against the enemy they most need to defeat.


### Geography

**Location**: Landlocked plains in northwestern Rimihuica

**Biomes**: Temperate grassland, savanna

**Named Entities**: The Thundering Way, Grandmother's Rest, The Scar, Avil Lakes, seasonal gathering grounds

**Rivers & Lakes**: The Silwyn River (flows north through the plains into Iylovia), Avil Lakes (southern plains, trade meeting point)

**Adjacent Waters**: None (landlocked)

Silwyn occupies a pocket of grassland surrounded by impassable barriers. To the north lies Eros—the haunted forest of the Nemo, which no Silzar will enter under any circumstances. To the east stretches Iylovia, enemy territory, dense forest where mammoths cannot go. To the south and west, desert—death for any creature the size of a mammoth.

The Silzar are trapped in their own homeland. They dominate everything they can reach and can reach nothing worth dominating.

The Silwyn River flows from springs in the southern highlands, running north through the heart of the plains before crossing into Iylovia. This river is the lifeblood of the region—and a source of bitter internal debate. The Silzar could dam it, poison it, use it as a weapon against the Gyv. Some younger warriors argue they should. The elders refuse. Water is sacred under the Mammoth Covenant; it belongs to all living things. To weaponize it would be to become something less than Silzar.

The argument continues. The river flows on.


### Political Geography

**Capital**: None

**Cities**: None

**Towns**: None

**Villages**: None (seasonal gathering grounds only)

The Silzar build nothing permanent. They follow the mammoth herds along the Thundering Way—the ancient migration route that loops through the plains with the seasons. Where the herds stop, the Silzar stop. Where the herds move, the Silzar move.

Seasonal gathering grounds serve as temporary centers of Silzar life. The largest is where the river bends sharply east—here, in summer, as many as half the Silzar converge when multiple herds come to drink. Chiefs meet, disputes are settled, young people from different herds meet and marry, and traders from outside know they can find customers.

Grandmother's Rest is different—a lone rocky hill rising from the plains where, according to tradition, the first mammoth to accept the Covenant died of old age. The Silzar leave offerings here: carved bone, braided grass, small treasures. No one camps at Grandmother's Rest. It is for the dead.


### Primary Conflicts

**The War with Iylovia**

For forty-odd years, the Silzar have been driving the Gyv back. What was once Gyv territory from the forest to the desert is now Silwyn—plains that the Silzar took field by field, settlement by settlement, until the Gyv retreated into forests too dense to follow.

Now the war has stalled. The Silzar control everything they can reach. The Gyv hold everything the Silzar can't take. Raiding continues along the forest edge—the Silzar take timber, rope, food, whatever they can carry. The Gyv ambush anyone who pushes too deep. Neither side can end it.

**The Internal Schism**

The real threat to Silwyn isn't the Gyv. It's the question of what the Silzar are willing to become to win.

Traditionalists—mostly older chiefs who remember the days before the Covenant made them strong—insist that victory means nothing if it costs them their souls. The Covenant has rules. Water is sacred. The mammoths must be honored. There are lines that cannot be crossed.

Progressives—mostly younger warriors who have never known anything but war—argue that rules are luxuries. Dam the river. Send warriors in on foot. Burn the forest if necessary. End this, whatever it takes, and worry about the Covenant afterward.

This schism cuts through every herd, every family. Chiefs who were allies for decades now eye each other with suspicion. The next council gathering may not end in consensus. It may end in blood.

**The Water Question**

The river flows from Silwyn into Iylovia. The Gyv need it. The Silzar could take it.

Every few years, someone proposes damming the river. Every few years, the elders refuse. But the pressure grows. The war grinds on. Young warriors ask why their friends are dying in forest ambushes when they could end the Gyv in a single season by turning off the water.

The elders say: because we are not monsters. Because the mammoths would know. Because some victories cost more than defeat.

The young warriors say: the mammoths don't care about Gyv children. The Covenant is about us and them, not us and the enemy. Stop hiding behind tradition and win this war.

No one has acted. Yet.


### History

**The Old Days**

Before the Covenant, the Silzar were nothing. Scattered bands of plains ogres, hunted by predators, raided by the Gyv, barely surviving in the grasslands no one else wanted. The Gyv controlled the entire region—plains and forest both—and considered the ogres vermin to be culled when their numbers grew troublesome.

Silzar elders still tell stories of those days. Hiding in gullies while Gyv hunting parties rode past. Watching children starve because the Gyv had burned the grasslands for sport. Being captured and made to work until they died. The Silzar remember. They will never forget.

**The Mammoth Covenant**

How the Covenant began is sacred knowledge, shared only in certain ceremonies. What outsiders know is this: somehow, the Silzar formed an alliance with the great mammoth herds. Not domestication—partnership. The mammoths accepted Silzar riders in exchange for protection, guidance, and respect.

Within two generations, everything changed. Mammoth-mounted Silzar could cross the plains in days instead of weeks. They could carry supplies, shelter behind mammoth bodies, strike and retreat before enemies could respond. And when they learned to mount ballistae on mammoth platforms...

The Gyv didn't understand the threat until it was far too late.

**The Turning**

The first Silzar victories were small. A Gyv outpost overrun. A hunting party slaughtered. The Gyv sent punitive expeditions; the Silzar ambushed them on open ground and left no survivors.

Then the Silzar stopped hiding. They rode in force against Gyv settlements on the plains—and the settlements fell. One after another. Year after year. The Gyv retreated toward the forests, burning what they couldn't carry, but the Silzar just kept coming.

In a single generation, the Silzar took the plains. Gyv who had owned this land for centuries fled east or died where they stood.

**The Stall**

And then the Silzar reached the treeline, and everything stopped.

The first assault into the forest was a disaster. Two hundred warriors went in. Thirty came out, raving about trees that moved and voices that came from everywhere and friends who turned on friends. The mammoths had refused to enter at all.

The second assault went worse. The Silzar tried to cut paths, to burn clearings, to force the forest open. The forest fought back. Warriors vanished. Supply lines collapsed. Chiefs died to their own warriors' hands, driven mad by whispers they couldn't escape.

Now the Silzar raid the forest's edge and go no deeper. They've won everything the mammoths can reach. They've lost every battle the mammoths can't fight.

The war continues. Neither side can end it.


### Relations

**Iylovia**: Active war. The Silzar raid constantly; the Gyv defend desperately. Neither side negotiates. Both sides hate.

**Eros**: Terror. The Silzar will not enter the haunted forest under any circumstances. They don't speak of the Nemo. They pretend the northern border doesn't exist.

**Southern Traders**: Wary commerce. Merchants from the desert routes trade metal for ivory and leather. They don't stay long.

**Everyone Else**: Silwyn is too isolated for most nations to care about. Those who know of the Silzar know only that they're dangerous mammoth-riders in a distant grassland, fighting some forest people for reasons no one bothers to understand.


### Player Hooks

**The Schism**: The next council gathering could tear Silwyn apart. Both sides might seek outside help—mercenaries, advisors, assassins. Players could tip the balance either way.

**The Water Decision**: Someone might actually try to dam the river. What happens then? Do the players help, stop them, or deal with the consequences?

**Breaking the Stalemate**: Either side might hire outsiders to do what they cannot. The Silzar might want specialists who can fight in forests. The Gyv might want someone to sabotage the mammoth herds. Players could end this war—for better or worse.

**The Mammoth Plague**: A sickness spreading through the herds. The Silzar are desperate for a cure. Without the mammoths, they're nothing—prey again, like in the old days.

**What Lies North**: Someone needs to go into Eros. The Silzar won't do it. They'll pay anything—anything—for someone else to face what they cannot.

## Stipen

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Gerontocracy

**Primary Inhabitants**: Ezuri (Tree Gnomes)

**Population**: 9,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Truth, honor, creativity, beauty

- Settlements are built into massive trees—interconnected homes supported by living wood.
- The Ezuri here live up to 500 years, their longevity sustained by the trees themselves. To leave the forest is to give that up.
- Upon reaching elder years, you join the Stipen Council, which makes all decisions for the state.
- Fully integrated with the trees—they can speak with them and are expert gardeners.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Specialty flora

**Primary Imports**: None

- Largely self-sufficient. People grow their own gardens in the trees, kept alive and producing through accelerated growth magic.
- People rarely leave—their longevity is tied to the forest.
- Trade is minimal; outsiders are tolerated but not encouraged.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: Very High

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- Although not formally trained, the Stipen people are incredibly adept at working with nature to fight back against enemies.
- All their might is defensive. Outside the forest, they lose their connection and their power.
- The forest itself fights for them—trees shift, roots trip, branches strike.

### Geography

**Location**: Middle Sea coast along the Chedwin Strait, east of Thespia, north of Zintacas

**Biomes**: Ancient forest (entirely forested)

**Climate**: Temperate coastal—mild, humid, perfect for trees

**Named Features**: Teleriwood (western forest), Schousterwood (eastern forest)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Atra

**Cities**: None (Atra is the closest thing)

**Towns**: Shamwet, Kestrel, and scattered tree-settlements throughout the forest

### Primary Conflicts

**The Generational Divide**

There is growing tension between the young and old. The council of elders makes all decisions, but young Ezuri—who may be a century old and still considered "youth"—feel they lack any representation. Elders dismiss their concerns as impatience; youth see elders as out of touch with a changing world.

**The Drug Question**
