## Watalina

Tags: state, city

pronounced "wah-tah-LEE-nah"

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Merchant oligarchy (Council of Pearls)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Kendor (primarily Pelaendor wanderers)

**Population**: 3,000 permanent residents, plus transient traders

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Commerce, neutrality, discretion, beauty

- Watalina is governed by the Council of Pearls, seven Kendor merchant-houses who rotate leadership annually
- The settlement maintains strict neutrality in Atriik's internal politics—they trade with all four city-states equally
- Violence is not just illegal but socially unthinkable; disputes are resolved through elaborate arbitration rituals
- Non-Kendor visitors are welcome but expected to conduct themselves with decorum

### Economy

**Size**: Small but influential

**Tech**: Sophisticated (underwater engineering, shipbuilding)

**Primary Exports**: Pearl cultivation, pilotage services, shipbuilding, preserved seafood

**Primary Imports**: Timber (from Sprenwood), freshwater, surface-world luxuries

- Watalina controls all maritime traffic in Shark Bay through their monopoly on pilotage
- Their shipbuilding combines Kendor design principles with Sprenwood hardwoods
- Pearl cultivation is a closely guarded secret; Watalina pearls command premium prices across Westrim
- The settlement serves as the primary interface between Kendor society and the Atriik city-states

### Military

**Size**: None

**Quality**: n/a

**Enrollment**: n/a

- The Kendor consider organized violence vulgar and maintain no military force
- Defense relies on the settlement's underwater location (inaccessible to land forces), maritime leverage (no one attacks the only pilots who know the channels), and diplomatic relationships with all parties
- In emergencies, Kendor can summon aquatic creatures or simply withdraw to deeper waters

### Geography

**Location**: Southern Shark Bay, at the interface between coastal shallows and open water

**Biomes**: Submerged coral structures, floating platforms, tidal zones

**Named Entities**: The Pearl Halls (council chambers), the Pilot's Ledge (training center), the Market Platforms

**Rivers & Lakes**: n/a

**Adjacent Waters**: Shark Bay, Agreben Sea approaches

### Physical Structure

Watalina is not a conventional city. The settlement consists of:

- **Submerged Architecture**: Coral-grown buildings in the shallows, accessible only by swimming or diving. This is where most Kendor live and conduct private business.
- **Floating Platforms**: Moored structures where surface-dwellers can meet with Kendor traders. Markets, hostels, and negotiation chambers for those who cannot breathe water.
- **Tidal Gardens**: Cultivated oyster beds and pearl-producing mollusk colonies in the bay's shallows, maintained by Kendor aquaculture.

### Political Geography

**Capital**: The Pearl Halls (submerged council chambers)

**Districts**:
- The Shallows (residential, submerged)
- The Market Platforms (commercial, floating)
- The Outer Anchorage (visiting ships)

### Primary Conflicts

- The Kendor's strict neutrality occasionally frustrates Atriik's city-states, who would prefer Watalina to favor their interests
- Some younger Kendor chafe at the isolation from the wider Kendor fortress-cities to the south
- Surface traders sometimes resent Kendor price-setting on essential maritime services

### Relations

**Positive**: All Atriik city-states (trading partners), Mueras merchant-kings (distant trade contacts)

**Neutral**: Northern states (limited contact)

**Negative**: None—the Kendor cultivate relationships, not enemies

### History

Watalina was established generations ago when Pelaendor wanderers recognized Shark Bay's strategic value. The settlement grew as Atriik developed, providing maritime services that the land-bound city-states could not supply themselves. The relationship is symbiotic: Atriik needs the sea access; Watalina needs the trade goods.

The Council of Pearls has governed since the settlement's founding, with remarkable consistency in policy despite annual leadership rotations. The Kendor view the surface-dwellers with the patient amusement of beings who measure time in centuries.

## Watar

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Oligarchy (trading families)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Dwarblin

**Population**: 40,000

**Stability**: Stable

**Core Values**: Commerce, innovation, self-reliance, ambition

- Watar is ruled by an oligarchy of powerful trading families. Three families dominate: the Soleri (capital), the Gravo, and the Madero. Each controls significant territory and competes fiercely for market share.
- Dwarblin culture is defined by rejection. Hated by both dwarves and goblins, they built their own nation from nothing. This shared experience of being outcasts has forged a scrappy, entrepreneurial people.
- Very forward-thinking, almost tech-bro energy. They're always looking for the next opportunity, the next innovation, the next market to corner. Some smugness has crept in as they've succeeded, but the scrappy underdog mentality remains.

### Economy

**Size**: Large

**Tech**: Developed

**Primary Exports**: Trade goods (everything—they're middlemen), practical craftsmanship, ships

**Primary Imports**: Raw materials, luxury goods, anything they can flip for profit

- Members of the Aldriktch Trade Alliance, though they're increasingly aggressive about controlling trade routes.
- Dwarblin are master traders, bridging markets that would never otherwise interact. A Dwarblin merchant can sell sand to desert dwellers.
- Their craftsmanship is practical rather than beautiful. Dwarblin-made tools aren't pretty, but they never break.

### Military

**Size**: Medium

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary (mercenary-supplemented)

- Watar has a real navy—merchant vessels that double as warships, plus dedicated military ships protecting trade routes.
- Ground forces are adequate. They hire mercenaries when needed; why waste Dwarblin on fighting when you can pay someone else?
- The military exists to protect trade. Period.

### Geography

**Location**: Peninsula in the western Middle Sea, northwest of the Telphineas Strait, south of Erasnus, with Echea at the peninsula's tip

**Biomes**: Grassland, temperate forest, coastal regions, mountains to the west

**Climate**: Mediterranean—mild winters, warm summers, good sailing weather

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Solera

**Cities**: Solera, Gravo, Madero

**Towns**: Various port towns and trading posts along the coast

### Primary Conflicts

**The Telphineas Strait**

Watar wants control of the strait. It's the key to dominating Middle Sea trade—whoever controls passage can set tariffs and squeeze competitors. Bestacia also wants it, but they're on the wrong side of the mountains with no navy.

Watar has the ships and the willingness to use them. Tensions are escalating. The Trade Alliance technically prohibits members from blocking each other's shipping, but Watar is looking for loopholes or reasons to renegotiate.

**Family Politics**

The three great families—Soleri, Gravo, and Madero—cooperate when facing external threats but compete ruthlessly otherwise. Corporate espionage, market manipulation, and occasional "accidents" are all part of doing business. The oligarchy is stable because no single family can dominate the others, but the balance could shift.

**Alliance Strain**

The Aldriktch Trade Alliance is fracturing. Watar joined for the trade benefits but increasingly sees the alliance as a constraint on their ambitions. If they could control the strait independently, why share?

### History

The Dwarblin came to this peninsula as refugees—rejected by dwarf and goblin societies alike, with nowhere else to go. They found an empty stretch of coast and started trading.

Within a few generations, they'd built a merchant empire. What they lacked in magical aptitude or social acceptance, they made up for in relentless work ethic and shrewd dealing. The trading families emerged from the most successful early merchants, consolidating power through wealth rather than warfare.

Now Watar is an up-and-coming power. They've got ships, money, and ambition. The older powers of the Middle Sea are starting to notice—some with interest, others with concern.


Also just to clarify, the atlas files should focus more on high level stuff, interactions between regions, and geography, whereas the state files should be more about the actual inner workings of that state. There's lots of overlap, but the juicy specifics belong in the state file

## Windor

Tags: state, nation

Windor is a halfling nation occupying the northern slopes of Terrenia, wedged between the Green Mountains and the cold northern coast. It's a quiet country by most standards—pastoral, prosperous, well-organized—but the quietness is earned. The Windorf have built something comfortable in the shadow of troll-infested peaks, and they maintain it through luck, vigilance, and a practical understanding of acceptable losses.

### People

The Windorf are the dominant population: halflings who've lived here long enough that no one remembers arriving. They're small, clever, and blessed with an unusual relationship to probability. Fortune-magic runs in Windorf blood—not dramatic sorcery, but a subtle attunement to chance. Dice fall their way more often than statistics suggest. Dangers miss by inches. Opportunities appear at convenient moments.

This isn't universal or reliable. Most Windorf experience it as good instincts, lucky breaks, a sense of when to push and when to fold. A minority—those with genuine talent—can cultivate it into something more deliberate. These luck-blessers are valued throughout Terrenia and beyond.

Other peoples live in Windor—human traders, dwarf craftsmen, the occasional wanderer who settled down—but they're a small minority. Windorf culture dominates, and outsiders either adapt or move on.

### Government

Windor is governed by the Council of Chairs, a representative body that meets in Nylen. Each settlement sends delegates based on population and contribution, with seats weighted accordingly. The capital and Roye hold the most influence; villages share seats or rotate representation.

There's no king, no hereditary aristocracy. Local nobility exists—old families with wealth and reputation—but their authority comes from economic and social standing, not legal privilege. They serve on the Council like anyone else, and they can be voted out.

The Council handles defense coordination, trade agreements, taxation, and disputes between settlements. Local matters stay local. The system works because Windor is small enough for it to work, and because the Windorf temperament favors negotiation over confrontation.

**The Bureau of Fortune** operates under Council oversight, regulating luck-blessing services, training practitioners, and preventing the worst abuses. Fortune-magic for hire is legal and common—merchants pay for favorable winds, gamblers for loaded odds, parents for safe childbirths. The Bureau takes a cut and maintains standards. Unlicensed practice isn't illegal exactly, but the Bureau makes life difficult for competitors.

### Economy

Windor is modestly prosperous without being rich. The economy rests on four pillars:

**Agriculture**: The lowlands produce grain, dairy, and fruit—especially apples. Windorf cider is exported throughout Terrenia. Sheep and goats graze the hill country, providing wool and meat.

**Fishing**: The coastal villages work the cold northern waters. Cod, herring, and crab move inland; grain and goods move out. It's not glamorous work, but it's steady.

**Fortune Services**: Luck-blessing brings outside money into Windor. Merchants, nobles, and adventurers travel to Nylen seeking favorable odds. The Bureau of Fortune charges well for its services, and independent practitioners find plenty of clients despite official discouragement.

**Troll Materials**: An uncomfortable but profitable trade. Troll blood, bone, and hide have alchemical and magical applications. The Wardens sell what they harvest. Buyers don't ask too many questions about sourcing.

### Military

Windor doesn't maintain a standing army—there's no one to fight except the trolls, and trolls don't respond to conventional warfare. Instead, the nation relies on the Warden system.

**The Wardens** are professional troll-hunters, maintained by Council funding and stationed at posts along the mountain fringe. Each post covers a section of the border, running patrols, maintaining signal towers, and responding to raids. Wardens aren't soldiers; they're specialists. They know troll behavior, troll vulnerabilities, and troll territory better than anyone.

Warden service is voluntary but well-compensated. Recruits train for two years before field deployment. Casualties are expected—not catastrophic, but steady. The work attracts a certain type: people who want purpose, danger, and the respect that comes from doing something most can't.

**Village Militias** supplement the Wardens. Every mountain-fringe settlement maintains a watch, trains its adults in basic defense, and keeps weapons accessible. They're not expected to fight trolls directly—their job is to sound the alarm, evacuate civilians, and delay until Wardens arrive.

**Signal Towers** dot the mountain rim, each within sight of at least two others. Fire by night, smoke by day, specific patterns for different threats. When a tower lights, the message can reach Nylen within hours.

### Relations

**Aal Salma** (south): The human nation on Windor's southern border. Relations are civil but distant. Trade flows through Gorstraat. Aal Salma views Windorf fortune-magic with a mix of suspicion and envy—useful but untrustworthy, the way humans often view halfling advantages.

**Poum Titch** (across the mountains): The Scalawag territory beyond the Green Mountains is currently in civil war. Cross-mountain trade has collapsed. Refugees occasionally make the dangerous passage, bringing news that rarely improves.

**Broader Terrenia**: Windor is a small player in regional politics. It trades, it maintains neutrality, it avoids entanglements. The Council's priority is always internal—keep the trolls contained, keep the harvests coming, keep the luck flowing.

### Tensions and Hooks

**Something's Stirring**: Troll activity has increased over the past two years. Not dramatically, but noticeably. Raids are more frequent. New warrens are appearing in areas previously clear. The Wardens don't know why, and the Council doesn't want to admit they're concerned.

**The Deep Caves**: The mountains are riddled with unexplored cave systems. The trolls emerge from somewhere. What else lives down there? Occasional reports mention things that aren't trolls—larger, stranger, glimpsed in thermal fog or sulfur-steam. The Wardens don't investigate deep. Maybe they should.

**Bureau Politics**: The Bureau of Fortune holds significant power over a significant industry. Not everyone is happy about that. Independent luck-blessers chafe at regulation. Council members question the Bureau's growing treasury. Rumors of corruption circulate—favoritism, price-fixing, occasionally darker things. The Bureau denies everything.

**Clawsnatch**: The lake hides something. The Wardens know more than they say. Someone will eventually want answers.

**The Old Workings**: Pre-Windorf ruins dot the mountains. Who built them? What happened to them? The mines went deep before they were abandoned. Some shafts connect to places the Windorf have never mapped.

### Visiting Windor

Windor welcomes travelers and traders. The roads are good, the inns are comfortable, and the Windorf are hospitable in a reserved sort of way. They'll sell you cider, rent you a room, answer your questions politely. They won't invite you home for dinner unless they've known you for years.

The coast is cold. The lowlands are pleasant in summer. The mountains are off-limits unless you have business there and the sense to register with the Wardens.

Fortune-blessing services are available in Nylen through the Bureau or through less official channels. Prices vary. Results vary more. The Bureau guarantees satisfaction within defined parameters. Independent practitioners guarantee nothing but offer discounts.

Don't insult a Windorf's luck. It's the closest thing they have to a cultural taboo. Suggesting that their fortune is unearned or manipulated is deeply offensive—even when it's obviously true.

## Wykakas

Tags: city-state

Home to many Sharabha families. Strict, casted society. Nobility have arranged marriages. Large standing army to defend against Naruaghin in the south. Army has lots of talk about "honor" and "bravery" but they've not had to fight in 100 years, so the army has gone soft and is full of bs.

## Xi Chin

Tags: citystate

The Timekeeper city rises from a high plateau in the Tethryn Mountains of SW Urok, its white marble architecture stark against the dark volcanic stone. Xi Chin is home to the greatest astronomers in Alaria—scholars who track not just the movements of sun and moons, but the cycles of stars, the drift of planets, and patterns in the sky that most people never notice. When Xi Chin issues a prediction, wise rulers listen.

### Overview

Xi Chin exists because of a question: *What time is it?*

The question sounds simple. It isn't. Alaria's dual moons create a calendar of staggering complexity—Auris and Nyxara cycling at different rates, their alignments creating holidays that drift through the seasons, their light affecting magic in ways scholars still don't fully understand. Someone had to track it all. Someone had to maintain the authoritative calendar that merchants, priests, and kings could rely on.

That someone became Xi Chin.

The city was founded over two thousand years ago by an alliance of Uline Dwarven clockmakers and human astronomers who recognized that precision timekeeping required both mechanical expertise and observational patience. The dwarves built instruments; the humans watched the sky. Together, they created something neither could have managed alone: an institution dedicated to measuring time itself.

Today, Xi Chin remains cosmopolitan in a way the other dwarven city-states of SW Urok are not. Humans and dwarves share the city equally, with smaller populations of gnomes, elves, and others who've come for the knowledge and stayed for the culture. The marble architecture reflects this blend—dwarven precision in the stonework, human grandeur in the scale.

### The Great Clocktower

At the city's heart stands the Clocktower, visible from anywhere in Xi Chin and from ships in the Gulf of Xoth on clear days. The tower rises three hundred feet, its upper reaches housing the great orrery—a mechanical model of Alaria's sky that tracks the positions of sun, moons, and major celestial bodies with terrifying accuracy.

The Clocktower chimes the hours. Not just for Xi Chin—the sound carries across the mountains, and smaller towers throughout Alaria are synchronized to its rhythm. When the Clocktower strikes midnight at year's end, the new year officially begins. No other authority is recognized.

The tower's lower levels house the Chroniclers' archives: records of every astronomical observation made in Xi Chin's history, predictions issued and verified, calendars maintained for every nation that subscribes to the service. The archives are open to scholars, for a fee. The observation decks are not—those are reserved for the Chroniclers themselves.

### The Chroniclers' Compact

Xi Chin's neutrality is not an accident. It's a treaty.

The Chroniclers' Compact, signed over fifteen centuries ago and renewed every generation since, guarantees Xi Chin's independence. No nation may attack the Timekeepers. No army may march on the city. No trade embargo may starve it. In exchange, Xi Chin shares its calendrical services with all signatories equally, plays no favorites in political disputes, and issues predictions without bias.

The Compact has held because everyone needs it to hold. A war that destroyed Xi Chin's archives would plunge Alaria into calendrical chaos—festivals celebrated on wrong days, contracts with date-dependent clauses thrown into dispute, magical rituals timed to celestial events suddenly unreliable. The cost of destroying Xi Chin would exceed the benefit of conquering it.

This makes the city neutral ground. Diplomats meet here when they can't meet anywhere else. Treaties are signed in the shadow of the Clocktower. Enemies dine together in Xi Chin's restaurants, knowing that violence within the city walls would turn every signatory against the aggressor.

### Prediction as Power

The Chroniclers don't prophesy. They calculate.

Their predictions emerge from mathematical models of extraordinary sophistication—patterns identified in centuries of observations, correlations between celestial events and earthly consequences that most people would dismiss as coincidence. When Xi Chin predicted the Red Tide plague seven years before it arrived, they weren't reading omens. They were tracking a cyclical pattern in stellar positions that had preceded similar plagues twice before in recorded history.

Not every prediction proves accurate. The Chroniclers maintain careful records of their successes and failures, adjusting their models accordingly. But their track record is impressive enough that a formal warning from Xi Chin sends nations into preparation. They've predicted famines, volcanic eruptions, wars, and what they call "convergences"—moments when multiple celestial cycles align in ways that historically correlate with dramatic change.

The next major convergence, according to current models, is less than a decade away. The Chroniclers have not yet announced what they expect to happen. The silence makes people nervous.

### Government

Xi Chin is governed by the **Council of Hours**—twelve Chroniclers, each responsible for one aspect of the city's timekeeping mission. The Council selects its own members through a process involving peer review, demonstrated accuracy, and what they call "temporal intuition"—an ability to see patterns that others miss.

The Council has no hereditary component. Chroniclers come from any race, any background, any nation—what matters is the work. This meritocracy is genuine but brutal; failed predictions end careers, and the pressure to maintain accuracy drives some Chroniclers to obsession or madness.

Below the Council, the city operates through guilds: the Clockmakers who maintain the instruments, the Observers who watch the sky, the Calculators who crunch the numbers, the Archivists who preserve the records. Each guild has its own hierarchy, its own traditions, its own fierce pride in its contribution to the mission.

### The Orrery-City

Xi Chin's layout is not accidental. The city was designed as a functional astronomical instrument.

Streets align with solstice sunrises. Plazas mark the positions where specific stars rise over the eastern peaks. The Clocktower stands at the exact center of a geometric pattern that, viewed from above, maps the orbital paths of Auris and Nyxara. Walking through Xi Chin is walking through a model of the heavens.

This means the city cannot grow organically. Every new building must be approved by the Council to ensure it doesn't block sight-lines or disrupt the pattern. The result is a city of unusual order—beautiful, precise, and slightly unsettling to visitors used to the chaos of normal urban growth.

### Culture

Life in Xi Chin revolves around time. Appointments are kept to the minute. Lateness is a moral failing. The city's music features complex polyrhythms that mirror celestial cycles; its poetry counts syllables with mathematical precision; its cuisine emphasizes dishes that must be prepared at exact moments or they fail.

Outsiders find it exhausting. Residents find everywhere else sloppy.

The city celebrates time-based holidays that most of Alaria has forgotten—ancient observances tied to stellar positions rather than the common lunar calendar. Some of these celebrations are open to visitors; others are private, conducted in observatories at moments when specific stars align.

### Relations with Neighbors

Xi Chin maintains formal neutrality, but geography creates practical relationships.

**Wister Valley** feeds the city, and the valley folk resent their dependent status even as they profit from it. Tensions simmer but never boil—Xi Chin needs the food, and Wister needs the protection the Compact provides.

**Maun and Wisgarrd** are trading partners. Xi Chin's precision instruments often incorporate Wisgarrd gems; Maun's musicians perform at Chronicler ceremonies. The relationship is commercial rather than warm—the dwarven city-states find Xi Chin's mixed population vaguely distasteful, and Xi Chin finds their artistic obsessions frivolous.

**Xoth** is the complication. The Compact requires Xi Chin to serve all signatories equally, and Xoth is a signatory. This means providing calendrical services to a nation whose daemon-aided military makes everyone else nervous. Some Chroniclers argue the Compact should be revised to exclude Xoth; others argue that would destroy the principle of neutrality entirely. The debate has lasted generations without resolution.

### Player Hooks

- **The Silent Prediction**: The Chroniclers have identified something in their models—something they're not announcing publicly. Rumors suggest it involves Xoth, or the Shattered Court beneath Maun and Wisgarrd, or something older. Someone needs to find out what they know and why they're keeping quiet.

- **Temporal Anomaly**: A Chronicler has gone missing, along with records of specific observations. The Council wants the records recovered discreetly—whatever the Chronicler discovered, they don't want it becoming public knowledge.

- **The Convergence**: With a major celestial convergence approaching, various factions are positioning themselves. Some want to prevent whatever's predicted; others want to exploit it. Xi Chin is the only place with enough information to understand what's actually coming.

- **Compact Crisis**: A signatory nation has violated the Compact's terms—attacking a Xi Chin citizen, or pressuring Chroniclers for favorable predictions. The Council must respond, but harsh action might trigger the very conflict the Compact was designed to prevent.

## Xoth

Tags: state

Xoth is not a nation in the traditional sense. It is a covenant—a religious contract between mortals and the daemons of Celestia that became a state. What makes someone Xothic isn't blood or ancestry; it's faith. Dwarves, humans, gnomes, and others live side by side in Xoth, bound not by ethnicity but by shared worship of the Seven Patrons.

The result is one of the most formidable powers on Urok: wealthy, disciplined, and backed by divine forces that other nations can only envy or fear.

### Overview

Centuries ago, the peoples of southeastern Urok lived in scattered communities—dwarven mining camps, human farming villages, gnomish trading posts—competing and warring in the usual fashion. Then came the Covenant.

The details are sacred history now, embellished by generations of priests. What's certain is that a prophet (whose name is spoken only in temple) claimed the daemons of Celestia would protect any who worshipped them properly. Skeptics laughed. Then the faithful started winning battles they shouldn't have won. Crops flourished where they should have failed. Plagues turned aside at village boundaries.

Word spread. More joined. Those who refused were conquered or fled. Over generations, the covenant became a nation.

Today, Xoth spans the southeastern third of Urok—plains, forests, desert, and coast. Its population is diverse: you'll find dwarven smiths working alongside human farmers, gnomish merchants in the same temple as orcish soldiers, children of mixed heritage everywhere. Race matters less than observance. The question isn't "what are you?" but "do you keep the covenant?"

### The Seven Patrons

Xoth practices henotheistic daemon worship—acknowledging many daemons exist but organizing around seven who answered the original covenant. Each patron governs an aspect of life, and each demands constant worship.

**Theros, the Blazing Eye**
Daemon of the sun, truth, and revelation. The first to answer the prophet's call and chief among the Seven. Theros sees all deception and burns away falsehood. His temples face east; his rituals occur at dawn. The Hierophant of Theros is the closest thing Xoth has to a head of state.

**Kethros, the Shaper**
Daemon of craft, creation, and the work of hands. Kethros blesses forges, workshops, and artisans. Items made with proper ritual carry his minor enchantments—Xothic metalwork and stonework commands premium prices across Alaria. His temples smell of smoke and ring with hammers at all hours.

**Makhros, the Iron Voice**
Daemon of war, discipline, and righteous violence. Makhros does not love slaughter for its own sake; he loves *victory*—the precise application of force to achieve divine purpose. His priests lead soldiers in battle, and his blessings make Xothic armies terrifyingly effective.

**Nestros, the Rememberer**
Daemon of ancestors, oaths, and the honored dead. Nestros ensures that the fallen are not forgotten and that promises are kept across generations. His temples house genealogies stretching back centuries; his priests conduct funerals and witness contracts. Breaking an oath sworn before Nestros is spiritual suicide.

**Dikros, the Scale**
Daemon of law, hierarchy, and proper conduct. Dikros defines what is permitted and what is forbidden. His priests serve as judges, and his temples double as courthouses. Xothic law is harsh but consistent—everyone knows the rules and the consequences.

**Petranos, the Foundation**
Daemon of endurance, patience, and the earth itself. Petranos is the slow god, the one who waits. He blesses farmers, miners, and builders. His temples are the sturdiest structures in any Xothic settlement, often doubling as storehouses and shelters.

**Lykanos, the Guide**
Daemon of journeys, trade, and safe passage. Lykanos protects travelers, blesses merchants, and ensures that goods flow. His shrines stand at crossroads and harbor entrances. Xothic caravans carry his symbol, and bandits who rob them tend to meet unfortunate ends.

### The Covenant's Price

Daemons are not gods in the traditional sense. They are spirits sustained by belief—literally fed by worship. The Seven are powerful because Xoth feeds them constantly. Dawn prayers, dusk rituals, weekly observances, seasonal festivals, life-event ceremonies. The entire nation is a belief-engine, generating the faith that keeps the daemons strong.

This creates mutual dependency. If Xoth fell, the Seven would fade—not die, perhaps, but diminish into shadows of their current power. If the Seven faded, Xoth would lose the divine protections that make it formidable. Neither side can afford to let the other weaken.

The Xothic understand this arrangement clearly. They are not naive worshippers hoping for divine favor; they are partners in a contract. They give belief; they receive power. The relationship is transactional, even mercenary—but it works.

### The Hierophancy

Xoth is governed by the **Hierophancy**—a council of seven High Priests, one for each patron. Religious and civic authority are identical; there is no secular government.

**The Hierophant of Theros** serves as spiritual leader and final arbiter in disputes between the other six. In practice, this makes them the head of state, though the title is never used.

**The Hierophant of Makhros** commands the military. All soldiers answer ultimately to this office, and military strategy is as much religious doctrine as tactical planning.

**The Hierophant of Dikros** administers justice. The courts are temples; judges are priests; trials are rituals.

**The Hierophant of Kethros** oversees craft guilds, workshops, and production. Quality standards are religious standards—shoddy work offends the Shaper.

**The Hierophant of Lykanos** manages trade, diplomacy, and foreign relations. Ambassadors are priests; trade agreements are sacred contracts.

**The Hierophant of Petranos** controls agriculture, mining, and infrastructure. The roads, the granaries, the mines—all fall under this office.

**The Hierophant of Nestros** maintains genealogies, cemeteries, and the temples themselves. This hierophant also oversees the Rite of Binding for new converts.

The Hierophancy meets in **Umlas**, the holy capital, in the **Hall of Convergence** at the center of the seven great temples. Decisions typically require consensus among at least five of the seven, though the Hierophant of Theros can break ties.

### Conversion and Citizenship

Anyone can become Xothic.

The **Rite of Binding** is open to any adult who seeks it. The process takes seven days—one for each patron. The convert fasts, receives instruction in doctrine and ritual, and undergoes purification ceremonies. On the seventh night, they hold vigil in a temple until dawn, when a priest brands or tattoos the **Seven Marks** on their forearms: one symbol for each patron.

Once bound, you're Xothic. Full rights, full obligations. Your previous identity doesn't vanish—Xothic dwarves still have dwarven customs, Xothic humans still celebrate human holidays—but your primary identity is now the covenant. You will worship. You will serve. You will belong.

Children of Xothic citizens undergo a simpler version of the Rite at adolescence, confirming the covenant their parents made on their behalf at birth.

**Apostasy** is the worst crime in Xoth. Those who abandon the faith aren't merely exiled—they are **unmade**. Their names are stricken from genealogies, their families forbidden to speak of them, their existence erased from official records. Property reverts to the temples. Former friends and family must treat them as strangers—or risk being investigated for sympathies.

The punishment isn't death. It's worse: nonexistence. To the Xothic, an apostate literally stops being a person.

### The Daemon-Aided Military

Xoth's military advantage is real and terrifying. The army is large, well-equipped, and professionally trained—but so are other nations' armies. What makes Xoth different is divine support.

**The Theophoroi** ("god-bearers") are priest-soldiers who channel daemon power in battle. Every Xothic military unit includes at least one Theophoros, often more. Their role is to maintain constant communion with the patrons while their comrades fight.

The blessings are tangible:
- **Makhros** grants enhanced strength, reflexes, and coordination to faithful soldiers nearby
- **Theros** provides clarity—resistance to illusions, fear, and confusion
- **Petranos** offers endurance, reducing fatigue and helping soldiers hold positions longer
- **Lykanos** aids movement, helping units navigate terrain and maintain supply lines

In extremis, a Theophoros can invoke **manifestation**—channeling so much divine power that the daemon briefly takes physical form through them. This is devastating but dangerous; many Theophoroi die from the strain.

**The cost**: These blessings only work for the faithful. Mercenaries, allies, and conscripts from outside the covenant receive nothing. And if the Theophoroi are killed or their rituals disrupted, the blessings end immediately. Savvy enemies target the priests—but the Theophoroi are trained soldiers themselves, and Xothic units protect them ferociously.

**Mandatory service**: Every Xothic citizen serves two years in the military between ages 18 and 25. This isn't just defense—it's religious formation. Soldiers learn to fight *and* to pray. They witness the blessings firsthand. Veterans return to civilian life as true believers, having seen the covenant's power with their own eyes.

### Economy

Xoth is wealthy, and the wealth comes from multiple sources:

**Agriculture**: The central plains are fertile and well-managed. Petranos's blessings help, but so does simple competence—Xothic farmers are organized, their irrigation maintained, their harvests stored properly.

**Timber**: Kumi Wood produces exceptional lumber. Every tree felled requires ritual permission, which sounds like an obstacle but actually ensures sustainable harvesting. Xothic hardwood is prized across Alaria.

**Craft goods**: Items made in Kethros's name carry minor enchantments—tools that don't dull as quickly, armor that fits more comfortably, jewelry that catches light strangely. The premium isn't huge, but it's consistent.

**Religious services**: Xothic priests can petition the Seven on behalf of outsiders. Healing, divination, blessings for journeys or business ventures—all available for a price. Desperate people across Alaria pay for Xothic intercession, and the coin flows back to the temples.

**Trade position**: Xoth controls the Gulf of Xoth and significant portions of Urok's eastern coast. Lykanos blesses commerce, and Xothic merchants have a reputation for honoring contracts—Nestros ensures it.

### Culture and Daily Life

Life in Xoth is structured around observance. Dawn prayers. Dusk prayers. Weekly temple attendance. Seasonal festivals. Blessing meals before eating. Invoking the appropriate patron before beginning work. The rituals aren't optional; they're the fabric of society.

This sounds oppressive to outsiders, and it can be. But most Xothic don't experience it as burden—it's simply how life works. The rituals are familiar, even comforting. They provide structure. They connect you to your neighbors (who are performing the same rituals) and to the divine (who notice when you skip).

**Race relations** in Xoth are complicated. In theory, all faithful are equal. In practice, old prejudices linger—a dwarven family might look down on their human neighbors, or vice versa. But these prejudices must remain private. Public discrimination based on race is both illegal (Dikros forbids it) and impious (the covenant transcends blood). Intermarriage is common and unremarkable; mixed-heritage children are ordinary.

**Social status** derives from piety and service. The most respected Xothic aren't the wealthiest—they're the most devout. Veterans are honored. Priests are revered. Those who've made major temple donations or performed exceptional service gain status that wealth alone cannot buy.

**Art and architecture** in Xoth tends toward the monumental. Temples dominate every settlement. Public buildings are built to last centuries. Art depicts the Seven, scenes from sacred history, and abstract representations of divine concepts. Private art exists but rarely challenges religious themes—the patrons are watching.

### Relations with Neighbors

**Xi Chin** maintains correct diplomatic relations. The Chroniclers' Compact requires Xi Chin to serve all nations equally, and Xoth is a signatory. This means Xoth receives calendrical services despite making Xi Chin's other clients nervous. Some Xi Chin scholars privately question whether serving a daemon-worshipping theocracy violates the Compact's spirit; the official position is that neutrality means neutrality.

**Maun and Wisgarrd** are wary. The dwarven city-states don't share Xoth's faith and have no interest in joining. The three maintain trade relations—Xothic grain and timber for Maun's instruments and Wisgarrd's jewelry—but there's always tension. Xothic missionaries occasionally appear in both cities; they're tolerated but watched. The Shattered Court beneath the mountains concerns Xoth's Hierophancy less than it concerns the city-states; whatever's down there doesn't threaten the covenant.

**The Order of the Falling Leaf** is a wound that hasn't healed. When Xoth demanded the Order's submission centuries ago, they weren't asking for political allegiance—they were demanding the monks worship the Seven. The Order refused. They saw what happens when daemons are fed too much belief: they grow demanding, jealous, potentially dangerous. The Order's philosophy was self-mastery; the covenant requires perpetual submission.

The monks chose death-in-armor over spiritual slavery. Xoth still wants the Order's texts destroyed—they contain arguments against daemon-worship and techniques for resisting daemonic influence. Those ideas are dangerous to a society built on daemon-dependence.

### Geography

Xoth occupies southeastern Urok, bounded by the Pearly Mountains to the northwest, the Siddaneas River to the west, the Gulf of Xoth to the south, and the Bay of Lords to the northeast.

**Capital**: Umlas
**Sacred Cities**: Ku'or, Speusippos, Hippotas, Qo'koven, Sicule'os, Gryllus, Iphikrates
**Major Regions**: Kumi Wood (north), Dunes of Tithonius (northeast), Pyrgion Forest (west), Central Plains, Stepsidae Peninsula (southeast)
**Rivers**: Meilaenias, Tipholes, upper Siddaneas
**Adjacent Waters**: Gulf of Xoth, Bay of Lords

See the atlas entry for detailed geographic information.

### History

**The Scattering** (ancient): Before the covenant, southeastern Urok was a patchwork of competing communities—dwarven mining camps, human farming villages, gnomish traders, and others. No unity. Constant small wars.

**The First Covenant** (centuries ago): A prophet whose name is now sacred claimed the daemons of Celestia offered protection to those who would worship them properly. The first followers were few, desperate, and mocked. Then they started winning. Battles turned. Harvests flourished. The faithful grew.

**The Unification** (generations): As the covenant spread, it absorbed communities through conversion and conquest. Those who wouldn't join were defeated; their children often converted anyway. What began as a religious movement became a territorial power.

**The Order's Rejection**: When Xoth encountered the Order of the Falling Leaf—Uline Dwarven warrior-monks who had rejected their own people's growing interest in daemon-pacts—they demanded submission. The Order refused. Rather than fight a losing war or accept spiritual slavery, the masters bound their souls to their armor and sent their students away. Xoth has never forgotten this defiance.

**The Modern Era**: Xoth is secure, wealthy, and patient. The Hierophancy doesn't need to conquer—the faith spreads on its own. Converts trickle in from neighboring regions, drawn by Xoth's prosperity and protection. The covenant grows. The Seven grow stronger. The cycle continues.

### Player Hooks

- **The Binding**: A player character considers conversion—or is pressured into it. The Rite of Binding is genuine; the Seven are real; the blessings work. But the covenant is forever. Is divine power worth eternal obligation?

- **The Apostate**: Someone has fled Xoth after abandoning the faith. The Hierophancy wants them returned—not for punishment, but for *unmakng*. The apostate knows something dangerous, and the temples want them silenced before they can spread it.

- **The Missionary**: Xothic priests have arrived in [neighboring location], offering healing and blessings. They're gaining converts. Local authorities are concerned but unsure how to respond—the missionaries aren't breaking any laws. Yet.

- **The Seventh Text**: Rumors persist of an eighth daemon who was part of the original covenant but was somehow excised. Finding proof would shake Xothic theology to its foundations—or reveal something the Hierophancy desperately wants hidden.

- **Beneath Tithonius**: The Dunes of Tithonius hide ruins older than the covenant. Something's been uncovered—something that predates the Seven. The Hierophancy is mobilizing scholars and soldiers, and they're not explaining why.

- **The Order's Legacy**: Evidence suggests that students of the Falling Leaf escaped to Maun, carrying their masters' philosophy. Some of that philosophy—techniques for resisting daemon influence—may have survived in the School of Music's martial traditions. Xoth would pay well to recover or destroy it. Maun would pay well to protect it.

## Yuki

Tags: faction

Sister city of Garlow.

Located at (172, 82). 

Both cities, due to anomalies in the void are overlaced on top of each other. It is difficult to know which city you are in at any particular moment.

Primarily populated by etherweaver goblins, who have trapped the shit out of the city to protect it from the Krell.

## Zelidia

Tags: state

The jungle has teeth, and they are crimson-winged.

Zelidia is a theocratic matriarchy of Zelle pixies, nestled within the Kameesi Jungle on Urok's northwestern peninsula. To outsiders, it is a death sentence. The Zelle view all non-pixies as either threats to be eliminated or offerings to be sacrificed—and they have perfected both arts over centuries of isolation.

The kingdom's power flows from its High Priestesses, who commune with the jungle itself through blood-magic and ritual sacrifice. Every Zelle is raised from emergence as both predator and protector, trained in the arts of ambush, poison, and the sacred rites that keep their realm hidden from those who would conquer it. They have survived countless invasion attempts through ruthless cunning and the divine wards that shroud their territory.

Zelidia does not trade. It does not negotiate. It does not explain itself. It simply endures, feeding its hungry gods and waiting for the next fool to wander into its domain.

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Theocratic Matriarchy

The High Priestesses rule absolutely, their authority derived from direct communion with the jungle-spirits. There is no formal council, no system of laws—only the priestesses' interpretation of divine will. What the jungle wants, Zelidia does.

The current hierarchy includes seven High Priestesses, each responsible for a different aspect of Zelidian life: war, sacrifice, agriculture, prophecy, warding, judgment, and memory. They convene in the Temple of the Hungry Canopy to settle disputes, interpret omens, and determine which outsiders live and which die.

Succession is by demonstration. When a High Priestess dies (or is killed), any Zelle may attempt to commune with the jungle in her place. Those who succeed inherit the title. Those who fail rarely survive the attempt.

**Primary Inhabitants**: Zelle pixies

**Population**: Perhaps eight thousand Zelle across all settlements. Exact numbers are impossible to determine—the Zelle do not conduct censuses, and outsiders cannot count what they cannot see.

**Stability**: Stable

The theocracy has maintained its essential form for centuries. Internal power struggles exist—the High Priestesses compete for influence, and the temple factions have their own agendas—but the system itself is unquestioned. The jungle demands blood, and the Zelle provide it.

**Core Values**:

- The jungle is god; it must be fed
- Outsiders are either threats or offerings
- Survival justifies any cruelty
- The blood-debt must be paid

### Religion

The Zelle do not worship personified gods. They worship the jungle itself—an entity they experience as raw, impersonal hunger. Predation, rot, venom, growth: these are not metaphors but literal divine forces that the High Priestesses channel through blood-magic.

Every sacrifice feeds the jungle. Every death strengthens the wards. The Zelle believe they exist in a covenant: they provide blood, and the jungle provides protection. This covenant has held for centuries. The one time they tried to transcend it—the Great Working at Iirmos—the results were catastrophic.

The jungle-spirits themselves rarely manifest directly. Instead, they speak through omens: the pattern of falling leaves, the behavior of venomous creatures, the dreams that visit High Priestesses during ritual communion. Interpreting these signs is the priestesses' sacred duty.

### Economy

**Size**: Small

**Tech**: Somewhat Developed

Zelidia's economy is largely internal. The Zelle cultivate the jungle's bounty—sacred fungi, prophetic fruits, ritual materials—and craft what they need from local resources. They have no currency; goods flow according to need and religious duty.

**Primary Exports**: Technically none. However, through the shadow-market at Nox, certain goods reach the outside world: jungle venoms, alchemical ingredients, preserved specimens, and occasionally... services. The Zelle are exceptional assassins, and there are whispers that certain deaths in distant lands were arranged through intermediaries.

**Primary Imports**: Metal (the jungle provides little), glass, certain textiles, and captives. The last is the most important. Zelidian blood-magic requires blood, and the Zelle prefer not to spill their own.

### Military

**Size**: Small (by external standards); the entire population (by internal ones)

**Quality**: Extremely High

**Enrollment**: Universal

Every Zelle is trained from emergence to be a warrior. There is no distinction between civilian and soldier—only between those currently fighting and those preparing to fight. The jungle teaches: every Zelle knows which frogs are poison, which vines conceal ambush points, which calls summon allies and which warn of danger.

Formal military units do not exist. Instead, the Zelle fight as the jungle fights: through ambush, poison, psychological warfare, and overwhelming local force. Invading armies find themselves picked apart by enemies they never see, their supplies spoiled, their water poisoned, their sleep denied. The few who reach Zelidian settlements find them empty—the population simply vanishes into the canopy until the threat passes.

The wards are the greatest defense. Blood-magic has woven protection throughout Zelidian territory: concealment that makes paths loop back on themselves, confusion that scrambles compasses and memories, and worse things that trigger when invaders persist.

### Geography

**Location**: Northwestern Urok, within the Kameesi Jungle

**Biomes**: Tropical rainforest

**Named Entities**: Kameesi Jungle, Iirmos (ruined former capital)

**Rivers & Lakes**: Sovia River (central waterway connecting all settlements)

**Adjacent Waters**: Shamerei Bay (west, technically outside Zelidian control but within their reach)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: Glezamos

**Cities**: None (Glezamos is more a large town than a true city by external standards)

**Towns**: None

**Villages**: Nox (shadow-market), Katat (agricultural), Sookap (fishing), Yift (artisan)

### Relations

**Pyonokraus**: The cursed city-state in the Phalkrene Mountains is the closest thing Zelidia has to a neighbor. The relationship is one of mutual wariness and complete non-interference. The Zelle have no interest in entering a city from which none can leave, and whatever dwells in Pyonokraus has no interest in the jungle. Occasionally, Zelle hunters spot Ayblek at the forest's edge, watching with too many arms and inscrutable purpose. They do not approach each other.

**Outsiders**: All outsiders are presumed hostile until proven otherwise—and proving otherwise is nearly impossible. The few exceptions are traders who have spent decades building trust through the Nox shadow-market, and even they are never permitted beyond the eastern fringe. Any outsider found in Zelidian territory without authorization is captured for sacrifice.

### Primary Conflicts

- **The Sealed City**: Iirmos remains a wound in Zelidian consciousness. The wards contain what emerged from the botched ritual, but they require constant maintenance, and some priestesses argue they are weakening. Others whisper that what dwells in Iirmos could be bargained with, used as a weapon against Zelidia's enemies. The faction that controls Iirmos—or survives its opening—would control Zelidia.

- **The Blood Debt**: The jungle's hunger grows. Each generation, the sacrifices must increase to maintain the wards at their current strength. Some High Priestesses advocate expansion—claiming new territory to find new sources of blood. Others argue for deeper communion with the jungle-spirits, seeking more efficient sacrifices. The tension is theological, but the stakes are existential.

- **The Outside World**: Zelidia's isolation cannot last forever. Ships pass closer each decade. Explorers probe deeper. Somewhere, someone is mapping the peninsula, counting the Zelle's defenses, looking for weaknesses. The High Priestesses know invasion will come eventually. They disagree on how to prepare.

### History

The Zelle have inhabited the Kameesi Jungle since before human memory. They claim to have always been here—that they emerged from the jungle itself, born from moonlight and flower-nectar when the world was young. Other races dispute this; the Zelle do not care.

**The Founding of Iirmos**: The first capital was established in the jungle's heart, where the Sovia River's sources converge. For generations, Iirmos served as the center of Zelle civilization—such as it was. The Temple of the First Hunger stood at its center, and the High Priestesses conducted their rites in what they believed was perfect safety.

**The Great Working**: Centuries ago, faced with yet another invasion attempt, the High Priestesses decided to end the threat permanently. They would work a ritual so powerful that no outsider could ever enter Zelidia again—a ward powered by more blood than had ever been offered. They gathered captives by the thousands. They sacrificed entire villages in a single night.

Something answered. It was not the jungle.

What came through the tear in reality consumed Iirmos in hours. The survivors—barely a third of the population—fled eastward, eventually founding Glezamos. The High Priestesses sealed Iirmos behind wards of a different kind, and forbade any Zelle from ever returning.

**The Present Era**: Glezamos has been the capital for centuries now. The trauma of Iirmos has hardened Zelidian isolationism into religious doctrine. The jungle's hunger has grown; the sacrifices have increased; the wards have strengthened. Zelidia is more dangerous now than at any point in its history—and more paranoid, more xenophobic, more certain that every outsider represents an existential threat.

The High Priestesses watch the outside world through omens and the reports of the few traders permitted at Nox. They see the great powers rising and falling beyond the jungle. They prepare for the day when someone comes for them again.

When that day arrives, the jungle will be hungry.

## Zintacas

Tags: state

### Government & Peoples

**Government Type**: Kritarchy (tribal council)

**Primary Inhabitants**: Gozi (Goshwen variant)

**Population**: 3,000

**Stability**: Very Stable

**Core Values**: Universal goodness, afterlife, kindness

- Tribal society—each village is sovereign, but tribes come together for celebrations, trade, and diplomacy.
- No formal laws. Issues are brought before a tribal council and decided collectively. The worst punishment is banishment from the tribe.
- They fly on **large raptors** that hunt the gorges. Tribes are oriented around the breeding and training of these birds.
- The most honored position in society is **raptor pilot**—those who bond with and ride the great birds.

### Economy

**Size**: Very Small

**Tech**: Very Undeveloped

**Primary Exports**: None

**Primary Imports**: None

- Very little trade with the outside world. The Gozi are deeply isolated and prefer it that way.
- The raptors are sacred—not for sale. To hunt them is strictly forbidden and would bring the wrath of all tribes.
- Internal trade happens at **Rompur Liko**, the winter bazaar.

### Military

**Size**: Very Small

**Quality**: Medium

**Enrollment**: Voluntary

- No formal military, but the raptors make for devastating aerial cavalry when the gorges need defending.
- The terrain itself is the best defense—outsiders who enter the canyons without guides rarely survive.

### Geography

**Location**: South of Stipen, north of Kazül, in a region of deep gorges and stone spires

**Biomes**: Canyon country—red rock gorges, stone spires, mesas, sparse desert scrubland

**Climate**: Arid high desert—hot days, cold nights, little rainfall

**Named Features**: Sighing Canyons (central), Clay Hills (east), The Lizard's Back (northern border), Tootby Hills (northwest), Dzobi Lands (southwest)

### Political Geography

**Capital**: None (tribal—no central authority)

**Cities**: None

**Villages**: Nod, Tilik, Pil, Kilam, Shraz, Fimli, Hechaw, Shyat, and others scattered through the canyons

### Rompur Liko

A temporary winter bazaar where all the tribes of Zintacas gather to trade with one another. The entire fair takes place on a small island cliff connected to its surroundings via long rope bridges. For one week each winter, the isolated tribes become a single community—then scatter back to their gorges until next year.

### Primary Conflicts

**Isolationism**

Zintacas has massive prejudice against outside cultures and technology. They don't understand the outside world, don't want to understand it, and violently reject attempts to change their ways. Outsiders who enter uninvited are driven out—or worse.

**The Raptors**

Occasionally, outsiders try to capture or kill the great raptors. This unites all tribes in furious retaliation. Several would-be poachers have been dropped from great heights.

### History

The Gozi have lived in these canyons for as long as anyone remembers. They bonded with the raptors, learned to fly the gorges, and built their society around the birds and the stone. The outside world passed them by, and they were content.

They remain content. The canyons provide everything they need. The raptors are their partners, not their property. The tribes are family. Why would anyone want anything else?
